

# **B O U L D E R****Municipal Government History**

**1985 - 1990**

**COMPILED BY**  
**KELLY JO NELSON**



## Preface

This historical account of Boulder Municipal history from 1985 to 1990 was made possible through a grant from the Boulder Public Library Foundation. The purpose of this work is simply to summarize key events or issues of Boulder's municipal history from 1985 to 1990 and to serve as an index to the municipal government files located in the reference department of the Boulder Public Library. Obviously, not every event can be covered. This historical account presents the most compelling events or issues based not only on public interest as observed through the prodigious number of articles and references generated, but also on the advice of Reference Librarian Connie Walker. Additional information can be obtained from these municipal government files other than what's presented in this summary.

Sources utilized for this project primarily were the newspaper clippings files of the Municipal Government Reference Center. In most instances, the articles cited as the source can be found in the file of the same subject heading or in the Boulder City Council files for that year. When citing an article from some file other than the subject heading, the compiler has attempted to note the name of the file where the article can be found. In addition to the newspaper clippings files, the compiler used microfilmed newspapers, city council minutes and talked with city personnel to fill in gaps of events or issues insufficiently covered in files. The compiler would like to take this opportunity to thank those city personnel for filling in the gaps.

The format of this historical account is straightforward; subjects and issues are presented congruently and chronologically for ease of reading and understanding. For instance, one issue or subject is covered in its entirety rather than covering all issues or subjects for one given year then the next year and so forth.

## CHAPTER 1 BOULDER CITY ELECTIONS AND GOVERNMENT

This first chapter focuses on the municipal elections and government from 1985 to 1990. Specifically, the election of city council members, the local ballots or referendums passed and the special elections taking place during that period. In addition to the general elections coverage, four components of city government will be presented. Those four components of city government presented include city council, the city manager, the city attorney and the municipal court. The analysis of city government will include the personalities in office and some key happenings occurring during this time frame. Particularly those happenings that impacted Boulder's history, but don't necessarily fit within the context of the six remaining chapters. Major issues or events taking shape that fit categorically within the proceeding six chapters will be covered in those chapters. For example, environmental, transportation, land use, recreational, housing and public safety issues will be addressed separately in the other six chapters. Before presenting the history of Boulder's general elections and government, let's review Boulder's form of government and the election process.

Boulder operates under a council-manager form of government. City council acts as the legislative body of the city of Boulder. A total of nine council members are elected to office from the city at large. The election of Boulder City Council members occurs in the fall of every odd numbered year. Five council seats are always open each election year. The four candidates who acquire the most votes serves a four-year term each, while the candidate receiving the least number of votes serves a two-year term. Following the swearing in ceremony, city council elects the mayor of Boulder from among the council members. Between 1985 and 1990, Boulder City Council elections took place the following years: 1985, 1987 and 1989. In contrast to city council members, the other three components of city government are not elected from the city at large. Both the city manager and the city attorney are appointed by city council to serve at council's pleasure. Council also appoints municipal court judges. The city manager serves as the chief executive and administrative

officer of the city and oversees the city's affairs. The city manager not only sees that all city laws and ordinances are enforced, but that council remains apprised of all the city's affairs including the financial status of the city. The city attorney serves as the city's legal advisor. Disputes between the city of Boulder and members of the community are handled by the city attorney's office. The municipal court has jurisdiction over all criminal and civil cases arising under the city charter, code and ordinances. The municipal court administers punishment to violators. Punishment includes imposing fines and penalties as authorized by the city code and ordinances. Now that we have reviewed Boulder's government, let's proceed to look at the history of Boulder's elections and government from 1985 to 1990.

### **City Elections and Ballot Issues**

A special election was held April 30, 1985. Voters were asked to decide if the city's sales tax should increase by 0.1 percent over 13 1/2 years to fund the building of a new cultural center. Boulder voters had turned down two cultural center proposals before, once in 1970 and again in 1980. After some debate over the actual costs, city council presented a \$11.9 million Cultural Center proposal before Boulder voters in 1985. The proposal included building a new 65,000 square-foot performing arts building for \$9.9 million on the northern half of the block bounded by 13th and 14th Streets, Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe Avenue. Another \$1.6 million would buy and renovate the Midland Federal Savings and Loan building located at 1495 Canyon Boulevard into conference space. In addition, this proposal included another \$450,000 to renovate the existing Boulder Center for the Visual Arts at 1750 13th Street. Left out of the council's proposal this time, unlike the 1980 cultural center proposal, was the Boulder Theater at 2032 14th Street. According to Councilwoman Linda Jourgensen, the Cultural Center Planning Committee had considered including the Boulder Theater, but after further consideration the committee rejected its inclusion. The building did not provide the space needed by performing artists.<sup>1</sup>

---

<sup>1</sup>Boulder Daily Camera, 21 April 1985 and 13 February 1985.

For a third time, voters turned down the city's proposal for a cultural center. Those voting against the proposal outnumbered those in favor by 62.2 percent to 36 percent. However, only 23 percent of the eligible voters cast ballots. Opponents of the cultural center proposal believed the costs to build the cultural center would exceed the estimate and drain the city's general fund. That view prevailed over the proponents' view that the cultural center would not only revitalize the downtown area, but also generate money for the community and improve the artistic culture quality of Boulder.<sup>2</sup> Following the defeat of the cultural center at the polls, the city council unanimously agreed to do a cost analysis to figure out how much the city spent on the designs and promotion of the cultural center. Pressure for this analysis came not only from Dick MacLeod, a leader of the cultural center opposition, but also from Councilman Phil Stern. The analysis showed that the city of Boulder had spent \$91,619 of the \$100,000 budget allocated for the project in March 1984.<sup>3</sup> (For additional information on the cultural center proposal see Chapter 5.) A new system for counting votes was tested in the April special election. The Boulder County Commissioners invested \$25,000 in a new software package that was compatible with the city's IBM mainframe computer. The computer tallied the votes.<sup>4</sup>

Twelve candidates vied for the five open city council seats in the November 1985 election. The twelve candidates seeking election included incumbents Linda Jourgensen, Bob Greenlee, Homer Page and Roger Olson and eight newcomers. The newcomers were Cindy Carlisle, Steve Pomerance, Dick Harris, Bill Kamin, Tad Miller, Jody Stock, Jerry Van Sickle and Peter Williams. Three incumbents - Homer Page, Linda Jourgensen and Bob Greenlee were re-elected. Page received 8,326 votes, Jourgensen 7,556 votes and Greenlee

---

<sup>2</sup>Ibid., 1 May 1985.

<sup>3</sup>Boulder County Courant, 22-28 May 1985 and Camera, 29 May 1985.

<sup>4</sup>Camera, 28 April 1985.

6,345 votes. Two newcomers were elected to city council - Cindy Carlisle and Steve Pomerance. Carlisle received the third highest number of votes with 7,130 and Pomerance won the fourth seat with 6,480 votes.<sup>5</sup> The newly elected city council members took their council seat on January 1, 1986. The first task at hand was to elect a new mayor. Back in September 1985, Mayor Ruth Correll announced her decision to retire January 1 after serving eight consecutive years as Boulder's mayor. Correll was first elected to city council in 1973 and had served as Boulder's mayor since 1977. The new council selected Linda Jourgensen to replace Correll as mayor. In March 1986, the city held a dinner party to honor the former mayor, Ruth Correll. Correll was Boulder's first woman mayor.<sup>6</sup>

Five ballot questions, all initiated by the people, were presented to Boulder voters in November 1985. Those five ballot questions were as follows:

---

<sup>5</sup>Longmont Times-Call, 28 October 1985 and Camera, 20 October 1985 and 6 November 1985.

<sup>6</sup>Camera, 1 September 1985 and 1 March 1986.

### **BOULDER BALLOT QUESTIONS**

#### **Question No. 1**

Shall it be resolved that the following message be delivered to the President and Boulder's representatives in the Congress and the State Legislature: The people of Boulder demand that the U.S. renounce its declared policy (a willingness to be the first to use nuclear weapons in a conventional war in Europe, the Middle East, and elsewhere) and declare that it will not be the first to use nuclear weapons?

#### **Question No. 2**

Shall it be resolved that the City Council enact ordinances prohibiting: the production, transportation, storage, processing, or disposal of nuclear weapons; any facility, equipment, or substance used primarily for such purpose; any research or development of nuclear weapons; and any investment by the city in firms involved in nuclear weapons research, development, or production?

#### **Question No. 3**

Shall the citizens of Boulder call for cessation of private assistance to the "contras" of Nicaragua and the Salvadoran military?

#### **Question No. 4**

Shall the citizens of Boulder call for the United States Congress to prohibit private assistance to any belligerent which systematically violates human rights and international law?

#### **Question No. 5**

Shall an ordinance be initiated which provides that interest on security deposits is and remains the sole property of the tenant and may not be withheld except pursuant to the provisions of State law dealing with the withholding of security deposits, makes withholding interest on security deposits a violation of the City Code, provides for private enforcement, and prohibits any waiver in a rental agreement?

(Daily Camera, 27 October 1985)

Boulder voters passed all five ballots despite the light turnout at the polls. Only 28.4 percent of the registered voters exercised their right to vote.<sup>7</sup>

In 1986, no city council elections were held. However, one local ballot issue appeared on the November 1986 election ballot. Boulder voters were asked whether or not an amendment should be added to the Boulder City Charter that would establish permanent

---

<sup>7</sup>Ibid., 6 November 1985.

protections for open space land. The amendment, article 12, if passed would give the Open Space Board of Trustees more authority in managing the city's open space. Selling, leasing or trading of open space land would require approval not only from city council, but also from the Open Space Board of Trustees. In essence, the amendment would grant the Open Space Board of Trustees veto power. Also, the public would be given 60 days to petition for a public vote on any sale of open space land approved by council and the board, thus giving the public power to protect the city's greenbelt as well. The amendment to the charter was passed 25,674 votes to 6,801.<sup>8</sup>

The November 1987 election heated up by bringing two controversial issues to a vote—gay rights and taxation. The first ballot issue regarding gay rights asked Boulder voters to pass an ordinance that prohibited discrimination in housing, employment and public accommodations on the basis of a person's sexual orientation. A Boulder gay rights group called the Equal Protection Coalition gathered enough signatures to petition council to pass such an ordinance. Council voted 7 to 0 in favor of placing the issue on the November ballot to let the voters decide on a gay rights ordinance rather than council deciding the issue. Council's decision to circumvent the issue stemmed from an earlier incident whereby council had passed a similar ordinance back in 1974, only to have it repealed by voters. Voters not only repealed the 1974 gay rights ordinance, but also had one council member who had supported the ordinance recalled. In 1987, voters favored the ordinance prohibiting discrimination based on sexual orientation 7,916 votes to 7,621. With the passing of this ballot issue, Boulder became the second city in Colorado to have an anti-discrimination law based on sexual orientation. Aspen was the first Colorado city to pass such a measure. Frank Cernich, who ran for council on an anti-homosexual platform in the November 1987 election lost at the polls.<sup>9</sup>

---

<sup>8</sup>Ibid., 9 October 1986 and 5 November 1986.

The second ballot question heating up the 1987 November election was over the proposed city sales tax increase. Voters were asked to raise the city's sales tax by 0.38 percent and authorize the city to issue \$14 million in bonding to expand the main Boulder Public Library, build two branch libraries and to cover operating expenses. Initiating this second ballot question was the group, Citizens for the Library. Tired of council's inaction and the recent budget cuts, which forced the library to cut its hours of operation, Citizens for the Library pushed for placing the sales tax increase question on the November ballot. Council opposed a sales tax increase for just the library. Instead council proposed a lesser tax increase of 0.2 percent that would fund all of the city's operating expenses. A controversy ensued between council and the Citizens for the Library group over how much the sales tax should increase and whether or not the library should be the sole beneficiary of the revenue generated from the sales tax increase. (For more details on this sales tax controversy see Chapter 5.) In the end, a compromise was finally reached between council and the library group. In November 1987, voters were confronted with a two-part ballot question. The two-part ballot question asked voters not only to approve a 0.38 percent sales tax increase that would generate enough revenue to support \$14 million in capital improvement bonds for the library, but also whether or not all the money generated from the sales tax increase should be earmarked for the library. Voters approved the sales tax increase 9,857 votes to 5,962, but earmarking that tax revenue for the Boulder Public Library alone failed 7,073 votes to 8,566. Voters wanted the tax revenue, not needed to pay off the bonds, to go back to the city's general operating fund.<sup>10</sup>

Thirteen candidates vied for the 5 seats on city council in November 1987. Two of the five incumbents up for re-election chose not to run—Bev Sears and Phil Stern. The other

---

<sup>9</sup>Camera, 3 July 1987; 19 August 1987; 1 November 1987; Times Call, 14 November 1987; Camera, 4 November 1987.

<sup>10</sup>Boulder Courier, 21 May 1987; Camera, 4, 5 August 1987; Boulder Courier, 5 November 1987.

three incumbents—Spense Havlick, Annette Anderson and Bob Greenlee—were re-elected along with two newcomers—Sally Martin and Matt Appelbaum. Havlick received the most votes—10,323—followed by Anderson, then Greenlee. Martin beat out Appelbaum for the fourth four-year term seat on council. Appelbaum won a two-year term since he received the fifth lowest number of votes.<sup>11</sup>

A special election was held on December 6, 1988 to determine where to build the Boulder Public Library expansion. As you will recall, funding for the library's expansion was approved last November 1987 election. However, a controversy ensued over where to build the library expansion in 1988. Council was in favor of, and in fact voted for, building the expansion at the Watts-Hardy dairy site on 26th and Walnut Streets. A citizens group emerged to challenge council's decision. That group was called the Citizens to Expand the Boulder Creek Library. They favored building the library addition on Arapahoe, behind the current public library at 1000 Canyon Boulevard. Headed by John Spitzer, the group pushed the issue to a vote. Hence, a special election was held in December 1988 to resolve whether the library expansion should be built along Boulder Creek on Arapahoe or at the Watts-Hardy site. Voters decided by a substantial margin in favor of the Arapahoe site. More than 26 percent of the registered city voters turned out for the special election. The vote was 9,576 to 7,203 in favor of the Arapahoe library expansion.<sup>12</sup> (For more details on the library expansion controversy see Chapter 5.)

Four ballot questions were put before Boulder voters in the November 1989 election. Ballot question 1 was an amendment to the Boulder City Charter to remove unconstitutional restrictions on election expenditures. In essence, a repeal of Charter Section 36, which prohibits candidates from spending money on things like opinion surveys. Voters passed

---

<sup>11</sup>Camera, 13, 7 October 1987 and 4 November 1987.

<sup>12</sup>Camera, 4 December 1988 and Colorado Daily, 7 December 1988.

7,138 to 2,305 ballot question 1. Ballot question 2 called for paying council members \$100 per meeting, but compensation was not to exceed four meetings per month. In addition, payment for meetings was to increase based on the Consumer Price Index. Voters also approved this ballot proposal 7,156 to 4,198. Ballot question 3 proposed raising the city's sales tax by 0.33 cents per dollar to provide additional revenue for the acquisition, maintenance and preservation of open space land. Boulder voted overwhelmingly in favor of the open space sales tax. The vote was 8,783 to 2,827. The sales tax went into effect January 1, 1990 and is set to expire December 31, 2004. The sales tax increase for open space was prompted not only by the annexation wars waging between neighboring cities east of Boulder, but also by the recent defeat in May of a countywide open space sales tax. The countywide open space sales tax was overwhelmingly supported in Boulder, but failed to carry in other parts of the county. Ballot question 4 asked voters to exempt light poles at government-owned recreational facilities from the city's 55-foot height limit. In addition to exempting light poles from the height limit, the ballot asked voters to exempt light and traffic signal poles in the right-of-way and also utility poles. This ballot issue arose when the Stazio ball-fields were moved from university owned land at 47th Street to city owned land at 63rd Street and Valmont Avenue. The lights from the old Stazio ball-fields exceeded the city's 55-foot height limit. When the Stazio ball-fields were located on university owned land, the height of the lights was not an issue. Why? Because university owned land was not subject to city regulations. This fourth ballot issue also passed 7,400 to 3,666.<sup>13</sup>

In addition to voting on the four ballot issues, Boulder residents had to elect five individuals for the five open seats on city council in November 1989. Only six candidates vied for the seats on council. Four of the six candidates were incumbents, while the other two candidates were new comers—DeAnne Butterfield and John Spitzer. The four incumbents running for re-election were Linda Jourgensen, Matthew Appelbaum, Leslie

---

<sup>13</sup>Camera, 8 November 1989.

Durgin and Steve Pomerance. The fifth incumbent, Cindy Carlisle, chose not to run for re-election. Carlisle wished to spend more time with her family. All four incumbents were re-elected to council another four-year term. Butterfield beat Spitzer for the 5th council seat by only 48 votes.<sup>14</sup>

No city council elections or ballot issues in 1990.

### **City Council**

In 1985, the city took a moral stand against Apartheid. In July 1985, council supported a motion to have Boulder divest its holdings invested in U.S. corporations operating in the Republic of South Africa as a way to protest against segregation policies. Boulder was not alone in enacting disinvestment policies. As many as 44 other cities and at least 5 states, plus a dozen universities across the country had divested as well. Council held off taking formal action until city staff could complete a report on the effect divesting would have on the city's earning power. The report indicated the effect would be minimal or zero, since both the fire and police pension funds were exempt from the divestment requirement since they involved private funds. That August, council unanimously agreed to withdraw city-controlled money from all enterprises investing in South Africa, despite urgings from the city attorney to take a more moderate approach. City Attorney Joseph de Raismes attempted to convince council to withdraw its funds from only those companies that did not adhere to the Sullivan principles, a list of anti-segregationist policies. The Sullivan principles, named for Philadelphia activist Reverend Leon Sullivan, called for blacks to have equal pay and opportunities. To make the disinvestment policy more than just a token gesture, council asked that police and fire pension funds for employees hired before 1978 voluntarily divest since police and fire employees were guaranteed a level of payment and

---

<sup>14</sup>Ibid., 1 October 1989 and 30 September 1989 and 8 November 1989.

the city would have to pick up any lost income.<sup>15</sup>

In August 1985, Mayor Ruth Correll and CU President E. Gordon Gee unveiled a proposal to establish a joint Colorado University and city of Boulder working group. The joint CU-City working group would examine problems of mutual concern and propose solutions and make recommendations to Boulder City Council and the CU Board of Regents. The working group would not make decisions, only advise and guide city council and the university. Problems of mutual concern the joint working group would address included the housing situation in Boulder, particularly how the university students influxes affected Boulder's apartment supply, traffic control, the city's use of Macky Auditorium, the implications of CU's proposed Research Park and the city's admission tax on nonacademic events held at CU. The proposal to create a joint CU-City working group that would promote cooperation in community, economic and environmental areas of mutual concern was granted preliminary approval by council on August 20, 1985.

The first meeting of the joint working group took place on November 22, 1985. At that meeting, CU Board of Regents member Peter Dietze accused the city of Boulder of being anti-business. Dietze believed that anti-business image could cost Boulder the proposed CU Research Park. Mayor Ruth Correll criticized Dietze for his attack and denied the anti-business label attached to the city. Many of the policies and regulations in place regarding development and often construed as anti-business were in fact voted upon and put forth by Boulder residents. For example, the 55-foot height limit for buildings and the aggressive open space plan. Council did not deny that bureaucratic red-tape existed and that the city should re-examine some of its development procedures and regulations, but Dietze's attack was in poor taste. His attack was not a good way to start out a working relationship.

---

<sup>15</sup>Colorado Daily, 17-18 July 1985 and Camera, 21 August 1985.

Correll speculated that Dietze's attack was a way to shift blame on the city should the Board of Regents select Colorado Springs as the place to develop the research park. Dietze claimed his remarks made at the meeting were to solicit the city's help in establishing the research park in Boulder.<sup>16</sup>

Despite Dietze's surprise verbal assault at city officials at that first meeting, the city agreed to resurrect the joint working group relationship in April 1986. To prevent additional public attacks and to outline the goals of the CU-City working group, an agreement was drawn up. The agreement called for a sharing of power as well as cooperation and conciliation between differing view points. Two council members refused to support the agreement—Phil Stern and Steve Pomerance. Both Stern and Pomerance felt the agreement overemphasized the university's plans for a research park. The development of a research park was not the group's highest priority, but the agreement gave that impression. In May 1986, a second meeting of the joint CU-City working group was held. The group pledged cooperation towards developing a research park in Boulder.<sup>17</sup>

In February 1988, the CU-City working group discussed whether or not nonacademic events or performances at CU facilities were subject to a 5 percent admission tax by the city. Tickets for other events within the city limits such as movies, concerts and exhibits not held at CU facilities were already subject to such a tax. Why not nonacademic events held on campus? The city felt the tax should be imposed on campus events because the university not only boosted the cost of city government, but the students also intensified the housing shortage and traffic congestion in Boulder and therefore should be taxed. Since 1972, the subject of an admission tax had been a point of contention between the city and CU. The

---

<sup>16</sup>Camera, 13 August 1985; Colorado Daily, 23-24 August 1985; Camera, 23, 26 November 1985.

<sup>17</sup>Camera, 2 April 1986 and 29 May 1986.

university refused to collect the 5 percent admission tax at campus events. As a result, the city filed suit against the university in 1972. The Colorado Supreme Court ruled that although the tax on nonacademic events was valid, the city could not force the university to levy the tax. Now in 1988, the city and the university were addressing the issue again with the help of the CU-City working group. An agreement for implementing the city's 5 percent admission tax on nonacademic events held on campus was being discussed. Under the proposed agreement, CU was to retain 1.5 percent of the tax revenue in exchange for administering the collection of the tax. The university would also retain another 25 percent of the proceeds from the proposed admission tax. The city expected to collect \$200,000 a year from the admission tax at CU. The city was willing to phase in the tax as long as a definite date could be set for when the city would receive the revenue. Educational events such as music or theater performances were to be exempt from the tax. A list of events that would be subject to the tax was to be prepared.<sup>18</sup>

On January 1, 1986, council selected Deputy Mayor Linda Jourgensen for mayor over Councilman Homer Page. Council voted 6 to 3 in favor of Jourgensen. A last minute voting trade cost Page the mayorship. Page had struck a deal with Councilman Spenser Havlick. In exchange for Havlick's vote in the mayoral race, Page agreed to support Havlick for deputy mayor. When some council members learned of this voting deal, they shifted their support from Page to Jourgensen. Homer Page was selected as deputy mayor.<sup>19</sup> Starting in May 1986, city council meetings were to be televised for the first time. Boulder was one of the last Boulder County cities to televise its meetings. Talk of televising city council meetings was first discussed back in September 1985. The city had received \$140,000 payment from Boulder Cable TV. That money was used to buy basic studio set-up

---

<sup>18</sup>Colorado Daily, 25 February 1988 and Camera, 25 February 1988.

<sup>19</sup>Camera, 2 January 1986.

equipment. Boulder installed a remote-control system that utilized three cameras and monitors to televise city council meetings. The remote-control system was selected over a manual camera system so that the cameras would be less obtrusive and less noticeable in council chambers. Council did not want the cameras to intrude on their meetings or have council members play to the camera. The first city council meeting televised was May 20, 1986. The meetings aired on Channel 28 at 6pm the first and third Tuesdays of each month.<sup>20</sup>

In April 1986, council agreed to change the city's sign code regulations to allow Slick the pig to continue his grunts and snorts in conversational tones. The city's sign code regulations were changed to allow noise in connection with "artistic expressions" only, not commercial promotions. Debate over Slick the pig began in mid-February when Don Ray, owner of Oh Carolina! Pit Barbecue restaurant at 835 Walnut Street, erected the 1500 pound talking concrete pig in front of his restaurant. Slick would repeat a recorded conversational message every time the door of the restaurant opened. Slick said nothing to invite business or advertise. A controversy arose over whether Slick the pig was a sign or a piece of art. The city's sign code enforcement officers considered Slick to be a sign, while the owner considered Slick to be art. Under Boulder's sign code regulations, signs that talk were not allowed. The fate of Slick rested with council. A campaign to save Slick was mounted by Mapleton Elementary School third graders and as many as 1,721 people signed a petition in favor of Slick's rights. Slick's rights prevailed. Boulder's sign code regulations were changed.<sup>21</sup>

In May 1986, council considered a proposal to create a buffer zone for patients

---

<sup>20</sup>Daily Times Call, 12 March 1986 and Camera, 19 September 1985 and 20 May 1986.

<sup>21</sup>Camera, 9 April 1986.

entering establishments that perform abortions. Repeated harassment of patients entering the clinics by anti-abortionist groups prompted the Colorado National Rights Action League, the Boulder Valley Clinic and the Boulder Abortion Clinic to propose the abortion buffer zone ordinance. The ordinance under consideration would provide a 4-foot buffer zone between protesters and patients at health facilities. Council postponed taking action on the buffer zone ordinance. Instead, council directed the two opposing groups—pro-choice and anti-abortionists—to try and resolve their differences through mediation. To try and find a way for the anti-abortion demonstrations and the medical centers to peacefully coexist, council gave the two groups 120 days to resolve matters. If after that time no solution was reached, council would reconsider the buffer zone ordinance. Mediation efforts failed. Council addressed the issue again that fall. In October 1986, council voted unanimously to establish a buffer zone ordinance, despite possible repercussions like lawsuits over First Amendment rights. The ordinance prohibited protesters from coming within 4 feet of anyone entering a medical clinic without the patient's permission. Violators of the buffer zone ordinance faced a maximum 90-day jail sentence and \$300 fine. Boulder became the first city to enact such an ordinance. Council's actions sparked controversy. Anti-abortionists claimed the ordinance violated their constitutional freedom of speech. In November 1986, council voted to expand the buffer zone from 4-feet to 8-feet in order to protect patients from harassment better. A final vote on the 8-foot buffer zone was taken in December. Council voted 8 to 0 in favor of the measure. The buffer zone ordinance went into effect in 1987.<sup>22</sup> Controversy and protest over the buffer zone ordinance continued. Opponents of abortion used council's televised meetings as a forum to voice their views. The ordinance in itself increased the picketing, sidewalk counseling and pamphleteering of abortion protesters.<sup>23</sup>

---

<sup>22</sup>Camera, 21 May 1986 and 22 October 1986 and 10 December 1986.

<sup>23</sup>Camera, 26 April 1987 and Boulder Courier, 3 December 1987.

In August 1986, council rejected the Rocky Mountain Peace Center's proposal to fly flags at half-staff on days the United States tested nuclear weapons. Instead council voted to endorse a one-year moratorium on nuclear testing approved by the U.S. House of Representatives recently. Flying flags at half-staff usually was reserved for the death of a dignitary. Council continued its activism in foreign policy that fall. In September 1986, council declared Boulder a nuclear-free zone and asked Congress to halt nuclear testing. Peace activists had urged council to also ban the transportation of nuclear weapons through the city of Boulder, but council followed the advise of the city attorney not to include the transportation of nuclear weapons in its declaration. Council had no authority to enforce such a resolution since federal laws, not city, govern the transportation of hazardous materials. Instead, council agreed to work with the National League of Cities to tighten laws governing the transportation of nuclear weapons. Council's activism in foreign policy stemmed from a resolution passed by Boulder voters in November 1985 that instructed council to declare Boulder a nuclear-free zone. That resolution instructed council to make it illegal to research, manufacture or transport nuclear weapons in Boulder. However, since federal laws dictate the transportation of nuclear weapons, council was powerless to enforce such a resolution in regards to the transportation of nuclear weapons. Therefore, council's declaration making Boulder a nuclear-free zone did not address transportation. Council also exempted the city from liability for enforcing the ordinance declaring Boulder a nuclear-free zone. Members of the No First Use Nuclear Free Zone Committee, who had initiated the resolution in the 1985 November election, accused council of not keeping faith with Boulder voters. However, other peace activists praised council's actions.<sup>24</sup>

In February 1987, council unanimously adopted the city's first economic development plan that was prepared by the Assistant City Manager Dave Knapp. This plan contained guidelines aimed at restoring and improving the city's economy. Aspects of the

---

<sup>24</sup>Camera, 20, 7 August 1986 and 17 September 1986.

plan included improving the city's business climate by attracting new businesses and supporting existing ones, luring tourism, improving transportation, and helping CU develop its proposed research park. The economic development plan was a way to help the city shake its anti-business image.<sup>25</sup>

In September 1987, Councilman Phil Stern announced he would not seek re-election. Stern, who was a staunch environmentalist, served on council for 6 years. That fall, a change occurred as to when new council members elected would get sworn into office. The year 1987 marked the first year in which new council members would be sworn into office the third Tuesday in November. Actually, the city had changed the day the swearing in ceremony would take place back in 1981, but because switching the swearing in day before 1987 would have shortened the term of council members without warning, the switch was not made until then. In 1987, the terms of current council members were cut short by seven weeks, but since the charter to change the swearing in day was passed before they were elected, those council members had fair warning. Prior to the change, since 1917, council members had been sworn into office on New Year's Day or January 2 if New Year's Day fell on a Sunday. At the swearing in ceremony held in November, council unanimously elected Linda Jourgensen to a second term as mayor. Homer Page was elected as deputy mayor.<sup>26</sup>

In December 1988, council approved the creation of a task force to address the gift of a teahouse offered by the Soviet city of Dushanbe, one of Boulder's sister cities. The task force was to address the following questions: where the city should place the tea house?; what would building permits and other incidentals cost?; who would operate it? and what gift should the city give in return? The teahouse was estimated to be worth \$750,000. Dushanbe intended to send the teahouse in pieces to be reassembled by artisans from the

---

<sup>25</sup>Times-Call, 18 February 1987 and Camera, 18 February 1987.

<sup>26</sup>Camera, 30 September 1987 and 20 October 1987 and 18 November 1987.

sister city in 1989. Boulder Mayor Jourgensen tried to explain to Dushanbe officials, but to no avail, that Boulder would not be ready to accept the gift before 1990. The city first had to make arrangements to pay for the building site and foundation in Boulder. Not surprisingly, the gift of the teahouse created some controversy among Boulder residents. Some Boulder residents questioned the city accepting the gift not only because it could be construed as Boulder's acceptance of Soviet government and policies, but also because the gift was seen as too an expensive gift to accept from a poor Soviet area. Other residents felt the city should accept the beautiful gift graciously. The teahouse was a symbol of friendship after all. The 21-member task force, selected by Mayor Jourgensen in January 1989, took on the task of solving the problems and controversy surrounding the teahouse.<sup>27</sup>

Councilman Homer Page was elected to a seat on the Boulder County Commission in November 1988. Page resigned from his seat on council. Page had served on council since 1982 and held the title of deputy mayor for the last three of his years on council. With Page's resignation, council had to select someone to fill his vacated seat on council. Twenty-two people applied for Page's vacant council seat. Council selected five finalists in January 1989. The five finalists were DeAnne Butterfield, Leslie Durgin, Maggi Markey, Phil Shull and James Topping. On January 17, 1989, council selected Leslie Durgin to fill the vacant seat on council by a 7 to 1 vote. In addition to selecting a new council member, council had to elect a new deputy mayor. Council member Annette Anderson was elected as deputy mayor over Steve Pomerance by a 6 to 3 vote.<sup>28</sup>

Council's decision to place Leslie Durgin on city council was not well received by all members of the community. The Boulder Valley Right to Life Committee opposed council's selection of Durgin because she was the Executive Director of the Boulder Valley Women's

---

<sup>27</sup>Camera, 21 December 1988 and 16 January 1989.

<sup>28</sup>Colorado Daily, 10 November 1988 and Camera, 7, 18, 25 January 1989.

Health Center. They questioned whether or not Durgin could be impartial on abortion issues. Now that Durgin served on city council, the city's financial contributions to the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center, which performs abortions, also came into question. Specifically, was it legal under Colorado Constitutional Amendment 3, which prohibits direct or indirect public funding of abortions, for the city to contribute funds to such an organization as the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center. As a result of the public's concern, council asked the city attorney to investigate the legality of the city's contributions to the center in February 1989. Council was reluctant, but realized the issue would not go away unless the matter was investigated.<sup>29</sup>

That same year, council took an active role in foreign politics once again. In February 1989, council expressed concern over human rights abuses taking place in Boulder's sister city of Lhasa, Tibet. At issue was the Chinese oppression of Tibetans, particularly the shooting and jailing of Buddhist monks in Tibet. Mayor Jourgensen presented to council a draft of a letter to be sent to Lhasa Mayor Luo Ga. Jourgensen's letter was a mild statement that alluded to the human rights abuses and asked if there was any way Boulder could be of help. To many council members, Jourgensen's letter did not seem forceful enough. Council agreed that an additional statement should be included in the letter about the city's deep concern for human rights abuses, specifically news reports about the killing of Buddhist monks. Jourgensen redrafted the letter to contain a statement expressing Boulder's concern over human rights abuses. The more forceful letter was sent to the Lhasa mayor in March 1989. Continued reports of human rights abuses prompted the city to re-evaluate its sister city relationship with Lhasa. A motion to send another letter to the mayor of Lhasa, Tibet failed in February 1990. Council had voted in November 1989 to suspend relations with its sister city of Lhasa. As a result of the failed motion in February 1990,

---

<sup>29</sup>Camera, 9 February 1989.

council's decision to suspend relations stood.<sup>30</sup>

In April 1989, council initially approved amending the city's sign code to limit murals to 25 percent of a wall. Before adopting the amendment formally, council intended to hold a public hearing on the proposed changes in May. This proposed revision to Boulder's sign code regulations was the city's attempt to avoid problems distinguishing between art and commercial signs. Evidently, the city was vulnerable to lawsuits by regulating other non-commercial signs like political signs, but not murals or works of art. Not surprisingly, Boulder artists and muralists were not happy with the city's attempt to regulate art. A controversy ensued over this proposal to regulate wall murals. As a result, council tabled action on the proposed revision to the city's sign code. The city attorney was directed to negotiate a compromise with local artists. A compromise was finally reached in regards to wall murals in July 1989. Under the compromise, murals on commercial buildings would essentially be unregulated. However, business owners who did not want the murals to count towards a city mandated square footage limit on commercial signs would have to submit a review request to the city manager. The city manager would have 30 days to comment on the murals, but essentially be powerless to stop them. Council approved the revisions to the city's sign code that fall. Murals were considered an art form and therefore went unregulated.<sup>31</sup>

In June 1989, council decided to place on the November ballot a proposal that would allow city council members to receive pay. This proposal prepared by the city attorney, asked voters to pay council members \$100 per meeting for a maximum of 4 meetings per month. The pay was to escalate with the Consumer Price Index. Council had been

---

<sup>30</sup>Colorado Daily, 9 February 1989 and 23 March 1989 and Camera, 20, 23 February 1990. (To see a draft of that letter sent, see Camera article dated 3/23/89, p. 2C).

<sup>31</sup>Camera, 9 April 1989; 17 May 1989; 20 July 1989; 6 September 1989.

considering the pay issue since 1988. Voters had rejected proposals to pay council members twice before—once in 1983 and also in 1973. Boulder was one of few cities along the Front Range that did not pay its city council. Boulder voters passed this ballot proposal to pay its council members.<sup>32</sup>

Following the November 1989 election, council unanimously re-elected Linda Jourgensen as mayor. Two council members vied for the deputy mayor post—Steve Pomerance and Annette Anderson. The appointment of deputy mayor was postponed however, until December 5 since Councilwoman DeAnne Butterfield was absent at the swearing in ceremony held November 21, 1989. Butterfield was in Turkey on vacation at the time. Council reappointed Annette Anderson as deputy mayor by a 6 to 3 vote.<sup>33</sup> Jourgensen's reign as mayor was short-lived. Jourgensen resigned March 5, 1990, following her appointment to the Board of Boulder County Commissioners. With Jourgensen's resignation, council had to select a new mayor and someone to fill the vacant council seat. Council initially intended to hold off electing a new mayor till after a ninth council member was selected, but council changed its mind. A new mayor was selected from the 8 remaining council members. On March 6, 1990, council voted 6 to 2 in favor of appointing Councilwoman Leslie Durgin to the mayoral post over deputy mayor Annette Anderson. The majority of council favored Durgin over Anderson because of Durgin's voting record. Durgin was considered to be more open minded and concerned for the environment and social issues than Anderson. Only council members Bob Greenlee and Annette Anderson voted against Durgin for mayor. Greenlee questioned not only Durgin's quick rise to the mayorship after only serving one year on city council, but also council's decision to elect a mayor just a day after Jourgensen resigned.<sup>34</sup> In addition to electing a new mayor that

---

<sup>32</sup>Ibid., 28 June 1989 and 27 February 1989 and 8 November 1989.

<sup>33</sup>Colorado Daily, 22 November 1989 and Camera, 6 December 1989.

spring, council had to appoint a ninth council member to the vacant council seat. Seventeen people vied for the vacant seat on city council. Council narrowed the list to six finalists. On April 3, 1990, council selected Jim Topping to fill the vacant seat on council by a 5 to 3 vote. Topping, who was a University of Colorado budget director, was sworn into office on April 17.<sup>35</sup>

Council's selection of Durgin as mayor prompted criticism. The Boulder Valley Right to Life Committee, which had first opposed Durgin's appointment to council back in January 1989, criticized council's decision to elect Durgin as mayor. The Boulder Valley Right to Life Committee was not alone in condemning council's decision. A newly formed citizens' group called the Committee to Uphold the Public Trust also disapproved of Durgin as mayor. This new citizens' group demanded Durgin resign from city council. The opposition to Durgin as mayor centered primarily on the city's funding to the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center, where abortions were performed and Durgin served as director. But there were other grievances as well. Those other grievances with Durgin included her voting on the 1990 budget that included a \$32,000 contract with the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center, her responding and discussing that conflict of interest with other council members and her failing to provide the city with a written notice of her conflict of interest. Acting City Attorney Jane Greenfield cleared Mayor Leslie Durgin of all conflict of interest charges. Greenfield determined that the city's funding to the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center did not benefit Durgin personally and that the funding went towards specific services other than abortions. As for Durgin's voting on the 1990 city budget, Greenfield found no conflict of interest since the center only received a small portion of the budget—.04 percent. Furthermore, Durgin's vote on the budget was not influenced in any way by other council members' approval of the funding to the center. Greenfield also felt Durgin was

---

<sup>34</sup>Camera, 5, 7, 11 March 1990.

<sup>35</sup>Ibid., 17, 18 March 1990 and 4 April 1990.

correct in providing answers to questions raised by council members about the Boulder Valley Women's Health Center programs. In addition, Durgin had filed a disclosure statement with the city outlining her potential conflicts of interest. Council and Durgin were pleased that the city attorney had found no conflict of interest. Council members viewed the attack on Durgin by the Committee to Uphold the Public Trust as politically motivated, despite their claims to the contrary. The citizens group claimed their focus was ethics, not abortion. But one has to wonder when many of the officers of the Committee to Uphold the Public Trust were active in the anti-abortion movement.<sup>36</sup>

In May 1990, council considered a proposal from city staff that would require all city overhead lines—power, telephone and television cable—be buried. According to the preliminary proposal, customers would be taxed to pay for the cost of burying the power lines. The proposal to bury power lines stemmed from several issues including the public's concern about the possible health hazards the power lines posed and also the Public Service Company's plans to install an above ground transmission line to serve the IBM plant along the Diagonal Highway. Not surprisingly, Gunbarrel residents objected to the Public Service Company's plans. Gunbarrel residents were not the only ones complaining about overhead transmission lines. Grape Street residents complained about the transmission lines in existence in their neighborhood. Grape Street residents turned in petitions containing 3000 signatures demanding that the unsightly lines be buried not only for aesthetic reasons, but also for the possible health dangers electromagnetic fields posed. In addition, the city's 20-year franchise agreement with Public Service Company expired in August. This provided the city with the opportunity to explore new and different ideas in regards to how the franchise operated. The proposal to bury all city overhead lines did have some drawbacks. Buried lines were harder to repair and problems were more difficult to detect. But, the biggest obstacle was the cost of burying the lines. Since 1970, the Public Service Company had been

---

<sup>36</sup>Colorado Daily, 8 March 1990 and Camera, 11, 20 July 1990.

setting aside 1 percent of gross electric revenues from the city to pay for under grounding lines. Over \$4 million had been raised between 1970 and 1990, but that was still not enough money to get the lines buried in a reasonable time frame according to the director of environmental affairs, Stan Zemler.

Despite having some reservations about burying all lines because of the projected cost of the project—\$25 to \$50 million, council authorized city staff to proceed with a proposal to bury all power, phone and cable lines. Council was unanimously in favor of staff exploring the burying of the Grape Street transmission line. The cost of burying other smaller transmission lines was also to be explored by staff. City staff was to come up with actual cost estimates of the burial project and also methods of raising money to finance the project. Once the cost of the project was known, staff recommended council put the issue to a vote. Let the voters decide if they were willing to pay a sales tax on electric bills to underwrite the cost of burying the lines. City staff proceeded to study the burial of the Grape Street transmission line in June 1990. Staffed looked at various routes to bury the transmission line since burying the line along its current route would be difficult since it ran through several residential yards.<sup>37</sup> Over the next several years the controversy surrounding the cost of burying the power lines, specifically who should pay for the cost of the burial, continued.

In August 1990, council considered adopting a plan to stop the Halloween Mall Crawl. The main objective of the plan was to stop hordes of adult revelers from gathering on the downtown Pearl Street Mall. How did the city intend to stop the Halloween revelers? The city's plan intended to keep revelers from reaching the downtown area by restricting access to westbound streets. Other components of the plan to stop the Halloween Mall Crawl

---

<sup>37</sup>Camera, 13 May 1990; Colorado Daily, 15 May 1990; Camera, 16 May 1990 and 7 June 1990.

council was considering included the following: advertising the end of the Mall Crawl in schools and metro media; restricting people from parking downtown and towing violators; asking restaurant and bar owners to close early; providing no amenities on the mall such as food, beverages, music and portable toilets; raising the curfew age from 15 to 17; having the police strictly enforce city ordinances to discourage rowdiness; and having the State Patrol set up sobriety check points. By taking the fun out of attending the event, the city hoped to squash the event once and for all. A final vote on the plan was taken in October. At that time, Council was in favor of all aspects of the anti-Halloween Mall Crawl plan except for the sobriety checkpoints. The sobriety checkpoints posed constitutional issues that troubled council members. The matter of sobriety checkpoints became a mute point when the State Patrol informed the city that they would not have enough officers to set up the sobriety checkpoints. Relieved that the issue was taken out of their hands, council approved the other elements of the anti-Mall Crawl plan. Elements of the final plan adopted included closing city and church parking lots and towing violators, enforcing city ordinances, banning open and closed liquor containers on the mall, enforcing the 11pm curfew for children 15 years old and younger, removing amenities and limiting access to the downtown area by using roadblocks. Businesses in the downtown area were also asked to close early. The city also advertised its anti-Mall Crawl plan and asked that Boulder residents refrain from participating in the Halloween Mall Crawl.<sup>38</sup>

Concern over the annual Halloween Mall Crawl dates back to 1986. In November 1986, following the damage assessment done on the mall during the Mall Crawl that year, Councilman Homer Page urged the city to start fighting its image as the Halloween capital of the world. Council asked that a committee be appointed to assess the problems of the Halloween Mall Crawl and suggest solutions. In the 1986, the Halloween festivities cost the

---

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 20, 22 August 1990 and 17 October 1990 and Colorado Daily, 19-21 October 1990.

city \$14,500. Most of that money, \$12,000 in fact, went towards paying police officers overtime. The remaining sum paid for damage repairs to the mall and cleanup the day after. The police arrested 32 people and another 40 people were treated at the Boulder Community Hospital for injuries. The committee appointed by council concluded that there was little the city could do to control the annual Halloween rite. The committee proposed dispersing congestion on the mall by attracting people to other events held on the mall's fringes and increasing lighting on the mall. The committee also suggested the city persuade newspapers and television and radio stations to tone down their coverage of the Mall Crawl.<sup>39</sup>

In addition to the advise of the committee, the city received some advise from the Boulder Police Department on a course of action to take. To control or tame down the event, the Boulder Police Department suggested the city ban the possession of liquor, even unopened bottles or cans of beer, on the mall during Halloween. In October 1987, council voted 9 to 0 in favor of banning the possession of liquor on the mall. By banning unopened containers of alcohol as well as open ones, the police hoped to reduce alcohol consumption. Every Halloween 30 to 35 people were arrested for drunkenness or taken into protective custody by the police. Another 30 or so people were typically treated for drug and alcohol overdoses. The new ban allowed police officers to issue fines, seize liquor and force violators to leave the mall. The area bounded by Spruce Street, 15th Street, Walnut Street and 10th Street was affected by the ban.

Halloween 1987 marked the first time in a decade that the city attempted to curb the rowdiness associated with the Mall Crawl. Efforts to curb the rowdiness included a greater police presence on the mall along with the presence of fraternity traffic marshals, a ban on possessing alcohol containers and offering free coffee and alternative entertainment. The city

---

<sup>39</sup>Camera, 22 August 1990 and 5 November 1986 and 3 September 1987 (Boulder Events Halloween file).

diverted the crowd at midnight by having a jazz band play in the parking lot of United Bank located at the corner of Walnut Street and Broadway. The city's efforts to control the rowdiness was relatively successful even though the Halloween Mall Crawl attracted a record breaking crowd of 35,000 costume clad revelers. The crowd was more orderly than usual and the mall was less trashed by the event then in past years. Arrests were up over last year, but no serious crimes or injuries were reported. Fraternities from the university helped police control the crowd and assisted the city in cleaning up the mall afterwards.<sup>40</sup>

Due to the relative success of controlling the rowdiness in 1987, the city repeated those measures taken last Halloween the following year. The city also took some additional steps to further tame the event in 1988. Working with the Downtown Management Commission, the city increased activities and diversions to disperse the crowd on the mall. Activities offered included holding a costume parade and costume contest. Diversions included having local bands perform in various downtown parking lots and placing food tents throughout the downtown area. The name of the event was also changed from the Halloween Mall Crawl to "Boulder Boo." Once again the city's efforts were relatively successful. Arrests made in 1988 were down over last year. Only 21 people were arrested compared to 50 in 1987. In contrast, the 1989 Halloween celebration saw more arrests and fights and brawling. Police arrested 37 people and 100 people were treated for injuries. Alcohol and drug related problems were fewer than in previous years. Total cost to the city was about \$30,000. Given the more violent nature of the Halloween celebration in 1989, it should come to no surprise that council directed the City Manager Dave Knapp to devise ways for the city to get the event under control next year. Knapp was to report back to council in March.

---

<sup>40</sup>Camera, 21 October 1987; Boulder Courier, 29 October 1987; Camera, 2 November 1987.

A committee was organized to examine the event and to make recommendations on how to control it. The committee was made up of downtown business representatives, school personnel, city officials and police officers. The city's earlier efforts to tame the event and to create a more positive atmosphere had not worked overall. Therefore, in 1990, council adopted a plan put forth by the committee to stop the Mall Crawl. As indicated earlier, the whole idea behind the anti-Mall Crawl plan launched in 1990 was to prevent Halloween revelers from reaching the downtown area. Council was pleased with the results of the city's efforts to end the Mall Crawl. The crowd was reduced to 20,000 compared to nearly 40,000 last year. Only 25 arrests were made and only 12 people were treated for injuries by the emergency medical units on the scene. The police issued 85 municipal offense summons for infractions. The cost of the anti-Mall Crawl plan was more expensive than just holding the event, but the extra cost was deemed worth it if the Halloween Mall Crawl was eventually stopped. Total cost was approximately \$58,000. The extra costs associated with the event were a result of the advertising campaign discouraging people from attending the event and the traffic diversion plan, plus the increase in police patrols. The city intended to launch a similar anti-Mall Crawl plan in 1991.<sup>41</sup>

In November 1990, council tackled the issue of how to control building on slopes. At one point, council considered a moratorium that would have denied building permits on lots with more than a 15 percent overall grade, but council decided against the moratorium. Instead council proposed enacting a temporary ordinance, which would require the owners of such lots to provide an engineer's report demonstrating how the lot could be safely built upon. The temporary ordinance would allow but restrict building on slopes of 15 percent or greater. Council still wanted the option to say no to a development even if an engineer

---

<sup>41</sup>Colorado Daily, 30 September- 2 October 1988 and 30 October 1989; Camera, 15 November 1989 and 27 May 1990 and 22 August 1990; Colorado Daily, 20 November 1990.

approved the project. The city also wanted to be free of liability if any problems occurred. This temporary ordinance stood in place until city staff developed a comprehensive policy for building on slopes. Council's actions were spurred by a recent debate over plans to build houses on 3 lots in the Bellevue Vista subdivision near Chautauqua Park. The slope on those 3 lots was between 39 and 42 percent. Not surprisingly, neighbors objected to those building plans. They feared their homes located below the hill would be endangered if the 3 lots were built upon. They wanted council to pass a moratorium for building on slopes, but council opted for the temporary ordinance limiting building on slopes. The temporary ordinance was to expire July 1, 1991.<sup>42</sup>

### **City Manager**

Despite the economy, the City Manager Jim Piper believed the city should begin checking off a long-standing list of city building needs in 1986. Each year the city was forced to rent more and more office space because of overcrowded and deteriorating city facilities. With that in mind, Piper revealed to council his \$24.1 million building improvement plan in October 1985. Piper's plan included a 40,000 square-foot expansion of the public library, a new 51,285 square-foot police and fire department building, improvements to parks and recreation facilities, a new fire station to serve portions of east Boulder, a 1,800 square-foot addition to the senior center and improvements to city yards. Council acknowledged the city's building needs, but they wanted to ascertain the public's opinion before proceeding with Piper's building improvement plan. Council was still feeling the disappointment of the public's rejection of the proposed new cultural arts center last April. Therefore, council wanted to gauge the public's support. After all, Piper's capital improvement projects would be a costly undertaking. To carry out Piper's plan, the city would have to raise the city's sales tax. Piper proposed the city sell \$24 million in bonds to

---

<sup>42</sup>Camera, 14, 21 November 1990.

pay for the improvements. The city's sales tax would increase 0.2 percent through 1996 to service the bond. A permanent sales tax increase of 0.05 cents would finance the cost of city workers, utilities and supplies for the new city buildings proposed.<sup>43</sup>

A committee was formed to evaluate Piper's building improvement plan. The committee was comprised of several city officials from the various departments impacted by the proposed building improvements and also citizens at large. The committee endorsed Piper's plan, but recommended some additional building improvements. The committee proposed building a new senior center instead of expanding the existing one and making improvements to the human services offices.<sup>44</sup> Given the support and the need for improvements, council elected to have three building improvement packages presented to the public in the November 1986 election. Those three building improvement packages to be presented were as follows: a \$9 million public health and safety package that consisted of a new public safety and communications building, a fire training building and a Human Services building; a \$13.6 million library expansion package; and a \$5.7 million recreational center and senior services package that consisted of building a new east recreation center and ball-fields and expanding the senior center. In July 1986, just one month after council decided to place the building improvement packages on the November ballot, Piper recommended council withdraw the building improvement packages from the November election. Upon further investigation and evaluation, Piper discovered that the 1986 sales tax revenues were 3.9 percent less than projections. As a result, the city would, in all likelihood, have to make additional budget cuts and therefore could not justify the building improvements. Council initially disagreed with Piper's recommendation and proceeded with the plans to place the sales tax increase for financing the building

---

<sup>43</sup>Ibid., 13, 9 October 1985.

<sup>44</sup>Ibid., 27 March 1986.

improvements on the November ballot. However, upon further review, council voted the sales tax election down. The city's building improvements would have to wait.<sup>45</sup>

Piper asked council not to give him a raise in 1987 because of the city's budget troubles. Piper had been given a 7 percent raise in May 1986. The Assistant City Manager, Peggy Wrenn, resigned February 1, 1987. Piper chose not to fill the assistant city manager position due to the city's tight budget. After Wrenn's resignation, Piper was left with only one assistant city manager—Dave Knapp. It was not until January 1990 that council approved the hiring of a second assistant city manager. Former Boulder Parks and Recreation Director, Mike Segrest, was selected as the city's second assistant manager.<sup>46</sup>

In June 1987, Piper informed council that the city needed to either raise property taxes by 30 percent in 1989 or slash another \$1.5 million from the city's 1988 budget. The city had already cut \$1.5 million worth of services from the 1987 budget. Some of the services cut in 1987 included closing the Boulder Public Library one day a week and eliminating the spring trash haul. The city was hoping to restore those services in 1988, but the economy was stagnant. Until it picked up, the city needed to raise new revenue or cut back expenditures. A proposed sales tax increase in the upcoming November election would help finance capital projects but not operating expenses. Further action was needed to resolve the city's budget crisis. Piper made recommendations to council on what services to cut from the city's 1988 budget. Some of the services Piper recommended cutting included keeping the library closed one day a week, eliminating the spring trash haul another year, reducing grant funds for the arts, closing Channel 28, not filling the second assistant city manager position and reducing the money for council's travel expenses as well as eliminating the catered meals served to council members before meetings. Rather than following Piper's

---

<sup>45</sup>Ibid., 11 June 1986 (Elections file) and 15 July 1986 and 6 August 1986.

<sup>46</sup>Ibid., 5 November 1986 and 13 December 1986 and 21 January 1990.

advise, council decided to dip into the city's 1988 reserve fund to pay for some of the services Piper recommended cutting. Council added \$170,455 to Piper's proposed 1988 budget. Some of the services council restored included programming to Channel 28 and funding for their catered meals. Council also asked the Boulder Library Commission to use its reserve fund to keep the library open. Council's decision left only a 2.1 percent city reserve fund for next year. Council adopted a 1988 city budget of \$65.9 million, which was a 1.3 percent increase over the 1987 budget. The 1988 city budget relied heavier on property tax revenue—11.9 percent compared to 10.5 percent in 1987.<sup>47</sup>

After serving five years as Boulder's City Manager, Jim Piper resigned from his post to take a job in Tempe, Arizona in June 1989. Assistant City Manager, Dave Knapp, served as interim city manager until council selected a permanent replacement for Piper. Council's top choice in the search for a new city manager was Minnesotan James F. Miller. Miller had served as the city manager in Minnetonka for nine years. However, when Miller was offered the job in Boulder, he elected not to take the position for personal reasons. A disappointed council decided to select a city manager from the other three top contenders—Dave Knapp of Boulder, Linda M. Baron of Burnsville, Minnesota and Jan C. Perkins of Santa Ana, California. Council chose Knapp in the fall of 1989. As Boulder's new city manager, Dave Knapp earned a salary of \$83,000 a year. Knapp's reign as city manager was short lived. After only about six months, council suggested Knapp resign. Knapp took the advise of council and resigned in April 1990. The deteriorating relationship with council prompted Knapp's decision. According to Councilman Spense Havlick, the city demanded more than Knapp could deliver. Until Knapp's replacement was found, council selected City Attorney Joe de Raismes to serve as acting city manager on April 17, 1990. Council intended to take its time in selecting a new city manager. A national search for a new city manager was conducted. The consultants hired to conduct the search were to be given extensive directions

---

<sup>47</sup>Ibid., 14 June 1987; 6 August 1987; 23 September 1987; 21 October 1987.

as to what Boulder was looking for in a city manager. The city selected Tim Honey, a college administrator from Portland, Maine and former Portland city manager. Honey began serving as Boulder's city manager on April 8, 1991.<sup>48</sup>

### City Attorney

According to an article in Daily Camera dated June 7, 1987, the city was a popular target for negligence lawsuits. In 1986, fifteen negligence lawsuits were filed against the city of Boulder. Between April 1985 and April 1986, the city's insurance carrier paid \$142,538 in settlements. However, according to the City Attorney Joe de Raismes, the city's vulnerability to lawsuits should decrease because of reforms passed by the Legislature last year. The new reforms limited a city's responsibility. Under the new legislation, the types of cases in which a city could be held liable were limited and also the extent of a city's liability for damages were limited. A city could no longer be held liable for damages that exceeded the extent of its negligence in a particular case. City Attorney Joe de Raismes and his staff acknowledged that many lawsuits have a legitimate claim and were sympathetic in cases where someone suffered genuine injury, but other lawsuits groundless in claim, the city would not hesitate to fight.<sup>49</sup>

In June 1988, the city attorney's office went on record opposing a drug test ordinance that would restrict drug testing by private employers. The local chapter of the American Civil Liberties Union proposed the city pass a drug test ordinance. In a memo to city council, the Assistant City Attorney Jane Greenfield indicated that the city attorney's office found no evidence of drug testing abuses in Boulder and believed any drug testing

---

<sup>48</sup>Camera, 10 June 1989; 10, 20 September 1989; 4 October 1989; 10, 25 April 1990; Colorado Daily, 4 March 1991.

<sup>49</sup>Camera, 7 June 1987. (For specific instances of the lawsuits see the Camera article.)

legislation should come from the state level. Council initially followed the advise of the city attorney's office. However, persistent pressure by the local chapter of the American Civil Liberties Union, prompted council to adopt a drug testing ordinance the following year. In June 1989, council voted 6 to 1 in favor of adopting an ordinance that banned random drug testing and outlined procedures for all drug testing. The ordinance became law in 30 days. The ordinance prohibits employers from testing employees for drug use without cause, but does not prohibit employers testing job applicants. The ordinance adopted by council was a result of the city attorney's efforts. City Attorney Joe de Raismes had been directed by council to work with ACLU members and an ad hoc committee of the Chamber of Commerce to develop a compromise on a drug test ordinance that the city could accept. To enforce and inform employers and employees of the new ordinance, council authorized the hiring of a half-time employee.<sup>50</sup>

In November 1988, City Attorney Joe de Raismes was directed by Mayor Linda Jourgensen to prepare a memorandum for the police clarifying the city's "fighting words" ordinance. Specifically, to clarify under what circumstances racial slurs constitute "fighting words." Jourgensen wanted the public to know that racial slurs would not be tolerated in Boulder and that such racial insults could lead to prosecution. Boulder's "fighting words" ordinance states that "No person shall taunt or challenge another in a manner likely to provoke a disorderly response...."<sup>51</sup> Violation of the fighting words ordinance carried a \$300 fine and or 90 days in jail. The reason why the ordinance needed to be clarified stemmed from an incident on the Pearl Street Mall during the Halloween celebration. A Colorado University football player, J.J. Flannigan, was arrested for third-degree assault for slapping a woman after police broke up a fight between Flannigan and the woman's

---

<sup>50</sup>Ibid., 18 June 1988 and 14 June 1989.

<sup>51</sup>Times-Call, 24 November 1988.

companion. Many members of the Black Community Support Group felt the woman should have been ticketed under the fighting words ordinance because of her yelling racial slurs and obscenities at Flannigan. Penfield Tate, one of the group founders and former mayor, was concerned that Boulder police were not treating racial insults as fighting words when fighting incidents involving blacks and whites occurred. This was the first complaint the city had received about not ticketing someone for racial slurs. The city wanted to make sure the police applied the same standards for everyone and that no other allegation of double standards was ever made again. Granted the enforcement of the ordinance was a judgment call by officers, but racial slurs could and would be construed as fighting words under the ordinance. According to de Raismes, the city was "...not in the business of forbidding all insults, taunts or challenges,...but...[was] in the business of preventing breaches of the peace."<sup>52</sup>

In December 1988, the city attorney announced he would conduct a background check on Sina Simantob, a Boulder developer who had a deal with the city to build a new hotel at the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. (For details on the hotel deal Simantob had with the city see Chapter 4 on Land Use and Development.) The public and council were concerned over Simantob's past association with a convicted drug smuggler named Philippe DeMirza. Simantob was once partners with DeMirza in ownership of the Gold Lake Ranch. DeMirza used the ranch to launder his drug profits. Simantob was granted immunity for his testimony in the trial to convict DeMirza in Miami, Florida. Simantob testified that he was unaware of DeMirza's criminal activity. Knowing Simantob's past involvement with DeMirza, the city of Boulder wanted reassurance that Simantob was on the up and up before proceeding with the hotel deal with Simantob. City Attorney Joe de Raismes conducted the background check and reported his findings to council in July 1989. The city attorney found Simantob to be law abiding, but naive and lacking good judgment

---

<sup>52</sup>Colorado Daily, 22 November 1988 and Camera, 24 November 1988.

on occasion. Therefore, de Raismes concluded there was no reason why the city should not do business with him. The conclusion of that report was good news, but the key players in the hotel deal had changed. The principal financier and developer of the hotel project was now Barry McComic, a San Diego developer, not Simantob. Simantob remained involved in the hotel project as a consultant for McComic.<sup>53</sup>

In May 1990, Lori Gibbons, who lost her 7-year old son in an accident last December, filed suit against the city. Gibbons' son, Danny, was killed crossing the street at the intersection outside his school on December 11, 1989. Danny was crushed between two cars at the icy intersection. Gibbons suit against the city claimed the city of Boulder ignored several reports that the streets outside Whittier Elementary were very icy and needed sanding. Gibbons and her lawyer wanted the city to take responsibility for the accident and improve snow removal around schools. Gibbons asked for \$250,000 plus the cost of the boy's funeral and the lawsuit. At trial in March 1991, a jury found the city of Boulder 82 percent negligent in the boy's death and the driver of the car 18 percent negligent. The jury awarded Gibbons \$600,000. Gibbons collection of that money awarded proved challenging. In May 1991, Howard Bremer, the Denver attorney who represented the city at trial, asked that the awarded amount be reduced to \$18,145.25. City Attorney Joe de Raismes was surprised by Bremer's motion, but elected not to direct Bremer to change that motion. Bremer's motion to reduce the amount awarded to Gibbons was twofold. First, a state law limits the amount that can be awarded to someone suing local government to \$150,000. At the time of the trial, the jury was not informed of that state law. Secondly, Bremer cited another Colorado law that allows any money received in regards to the injury or damage sustained in an accident be deducted from the amount owed to the plaintiff. Bremer asked the judgment be reduced because of the money Gibbons had already received from other sources. Those other sources included money collected from Gibbons fellow employees at

---

<sup>53</sup>Colorado Daily, 15 December 1988 and Camera, 29 July 1989.

the J.B. Winberie Restaurant, pass the hat donations from Boulder Police officers and an insurance settlement totaling \$100,000. The boy's father, who Lisa Gibbons had divorced when Danny was an infant, received \$30,000 of that \$100,000 insurance settlement. On account of the insurance money and the other money Gibbons received, Bremer filed his motion to reduce the award amount to \$18, 145.25. Gibbons felt she was entitled to the full \$150,000 under the law. Council members and constituents were in agreement with Gibbons. Complaints of the city's actions to reduce the judgment and pressure from council, prompted the city of Boulder and its lawyer to rethink its position. Bremer proposed raising the amount awarded to \$123,000, which was 82 percent of the maximum amount of \$150,000 allowed by law. Bremer's motion was filed in Boulder District Court. In June 1991, District Court Judge Murray Richtel ruled the city owed the full \$150,000. Based on the advise from City Attorney Joe de Raismes, the city did not appeal judge Richtel's ruling.<sup>54</sup>

### **Municipal Court**

In an effort to curb serious traffic accidents, Municipal Court Judge Richard Hansen and a group of city officials proposed drastic increases in fines levied for traffic violations in June 1985. Judge Hansen and city officials recommended imposing a maximum fine of \$100 to \$300 for 17 different traffic violations.
