Prior to the proposed fine increase, the average fine amount for those 17 violations was only \$20. Why the proposed increase in fines? In the first five months of 1985, traffic fatalities had nearly tripled. Between 1982 and 1984, nearly 11,000 accidents were reported in Boulder. The percentage of accidents involving injuries had grown from 15.8 to 18.4. Eighteen people had been killed. The increasing number of accidents warranted action. As judge, Hansen could impose stiffer fines, but he chose not to without the approval of council and the public's backing. Judge Hansen wanted to heighten

---

<sup>54</sup>Camera, 3 May 1990 and 16, 31 May 1991 and 11 June 1991.

the public's awareness of traffic violations and accidents, as well as the increasing dangers on the road. A public hearing on whether or not to increase fines for traffic violations was held. Following that public hearing, council approved the resolution urging Municipal Judge Hansen to levy maximum fines.<sup>55</sup>

According to a Daily Camera article dated September 29, 1985, parking violators were escaping having their cars towed or booted for the past 18 months. Why? There was insufficient staffing in the municipal court offices to handle the backlog of computer-generated lists of parking violators. Without that list of parking violators, ticket writers could not ticket or boot cars. Municipal court administrator, Terry Aragon, and his four-person clerical staff were unable to match the daily incoming payments with the backlog of outstanding bills. Until Aragon and his staff had time to sort through the backlog, parking violators were not to be towed in fear that payment may have already been sent, but not entered into the computer database. The increased workload was the result of 3 new ticket-writing hires at the Boulder Police Department and also the growing number of violations of Colorado's 1984 metro-area emissions law. In 1984, 130,680 tickets were issued. Aragon hoped to hire two more clerical workers to straighten out the backlog, provided council approved his hiring more staff. The two new positions were not included in the city's 1986 budget draft, but Aragon intended to make a special supplement request. By mid-October, the backlog was straightened out. Aragon not only hired a temporary worker, but also had other people devote their time to sorting out that backlog. All payments received were entered into the computer. A list of the remaining parking violators was generated from the computer. Once again the city traffic enforcement officers were able to boot cars of flagrant parking violators for the first time in almost a year.<sup>56</sup>

---

<sup>55</sup>Ibid., 16, 19 June 1985.

<sup>56</sup>Ibid., 29 September 1985 and 15 October 1985.

Having one's car towed or booted was not the only punishment traffic violators had to contend with from the municipal court. Starting in January 1986, Boulder traffic offenders failing to appear in court would have to pay an additional \$30 on top of the traffic fine because of a new state law. Under that same new law, anyone failing to pay one's fine on time also resulted in an additional \$30 fine. The new law applied only to municipal and county court traffic offenses, not district court cases or parking violations. On average, 10 to 20 people per day fail to show up in court and many more fail to pay their fines on time. The clerk of Boulder's county and district courts, Patricia Patterman, had a computer installed in the courtroom so the deputy clerk could immediately record the court proceedings rather than waiting to log the information afterwards. Placing the entries into the computer during the court proceedings was more efficient use of time for Boulder's courts personnel, who were already overworked and understaffed.<sup>57</sup>

According to a January 15, 1987 article in the Niwot newspaper, Town & Country, January 1987 marked the beginning of Judge Richard F. Hansen's 24th consecutive year as the presiding judge of Boulder's municipal court. Hansen was appointed to the municipal court post in 1964. Hansen first arrived in Boulder as a young lawyer in 1958. His love of skiing first attracted Hansen to Colorado, plus the fact that his brother was a doctor here in Boulder. Hansen was born in Okmulgee, Oklahoma. At an early age, Hansen became acquainted with the law. His father served as Oklahoma's first assistant attorney general. As judge, Hansen was firm, but hospitable and always tried to make the defendant feel at ease by bringing a sense of humor to the courtroom. Each year approximately 15,000 defendants were tried in Hansen's court for disregarding traffic laws or municipal ordinances. During Hansen's years on the bench, Hansen helped to raise the fines for traffic violations and for

---

<sup>57</sup>Ibid., 12 January 1986.

bicyclists not properly equipped with front and rear lights.<sup>58</sup>

In March 1987, the city offered motorists with outstanding parking tickets partial amnesty. For the entire month of March, the city waived all late fees and other penalties from unpaid parking tickets once the ticket was paid in full along with the \$1 handling fee. Outstanding parking tickets issued between 1983 and 1986 numbered 163,000. If all those outstanding parking tickets were paid off during the amnesty program, the city would collect a minimum of \$978,000. By offering the amnesty, the city was hoping to clear up some of the backlog and to start producing better pickup lists of repeat violators. This was the first time Boulder offered an amnesty program. The city was disappointed with the results of its amnesty program. The city collected only \$8,856 from the 1,012 people who took advantage of the amnesty program. Following the month-long amnesty program, a crackdown on major offenders began.<sup>59</sup>

In June 1989, council passed an ordinance that made the municipal court a "court of record" for the offense of crossing picket lines. Prior to this ordinance, people convicted of crossing picket lines in municipal court could request a new trial in the County Court because the municipal court was not a court of record for that offense. Council's actions to make the municipal court a court of record stemmed from a recent protest at an abortion clinic in Boulder. At that protest, 120 people were arrested for crossing police lines and of those 120 people, 100 of them requested jury trials. The new ordinance did not affect those

---

<sup>58</sup>Town & Country, 15 January 1987.

<sup>59</sup>Camera, 8 February 1987 (Boulder Municipal Court file) and 28 March 1987 (Automobile Parking file).

people arrested in the abortion protest, only future protesters crossing picket lines.<sup>60</sup>

---

<sup>60</sup>Camera, 14 June 1989.

## CHAPTER 2 THE ENVIRONMENT

Boulder's concern for the environment prompted city council to adopt new or strengthen existing ordinances to further protect the environment and one's health. The ordinances enacted during this time frame, 1985 to 1990, dealt with a variety of environmental issues. For instance, reducing air pollution by banning wood burning on high pollution days, limiting ozone depletion from chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs), regulating pesticide applications and increasing recycling efforts. Other environmental concerns included regulating the transportation of nuclear wastes, protecting wetlands and safeguarding trees against Gypsy moths. In Boulder's war waged on air pollution, specifically carbon monoxide emissions, the city encouraged residents to use alternative transportation as part of the Better Air Campaign. Also during this time, Boulder had to address the closing of a landfill and leaching of toxic waste, improving the sewage treatment plant, selling excess raw water and conserving Boulder's treated water supply. Amidst all these environmental measures to contend with, Boulder city officials found time to enact measures to protect one's health as well, specifically the air we breathe indoors. Tougher ordinances regulating smoking were passed by city council during this time.

Nonetheless, all the steps taken by council to help save the environment during this time were necessary. The need for regulating toxic emissions for air, land and water was clearly demonstrated when the Citizen Action group released its environmental report on August 4, 1990. The report stated that Boulder County ranked fourth among Colorado's 63 counties for total toxic chemical emissions. In 1988, Boulder County companies released approximately 2 million pounds of toxic substances into the environment. Although the report's information was from data collected in 1988 and steps had already been taken since then to reduce toxic emissions, the concern for the environment brought Boulder County residents, city and county officials and industry representatives together to address the problem even further. Several measures to reduce the amount of toxic emissions were undertaken by the companies mentioned in the report and by the city itself. Companies like

Syntex and Storage Technology pledged to reduce toxic emissions by half of their 1988 levels reported<sup>1</sup>. Some measures taken by the city included enacting an ordinance limiting CFCs and improving the wastewater treatment process prior to releasing the water back into Boulder Creek. Let's look in greater detail at the city's effort to protect the environment.

### **Air Pollution**

In the summer of 1985, the Colorado Air Quality Control Commission adopted new air quality emission standards for wood stoves sold after January 1, 1987. Boulder City Council started working on ways to comply with the new air quality standards and eliminate carbon monoxide pollution. In October 1986, city council took the first steps towards enacting a new ordinance that would ban wood burning in residential stoves and fireplaces on high pollution days in the winter. Council's goal was to reduce the carbon monoxide emissions produced from fireplaces and stoves on poor air quality days. Wood smoke accounts for approximately 15 percent of the carbon monoxide and more than 25 percent of the particulate pollution in Boulder. To enforce such a measure, Councilman Phil Stern suggested imposing fines for violators of the ordinance. Stern recommended waiving the fines the first year the law was enacted, but after the first year fines would be issued to violators. Fines could amount to as much as \$300 for third-time offenders. Besides banning wood burning, council considered several other measures including banning the installation of fireplaces for one year, banning outright residential coal burning, and requiring older stoves to be upgraded for better emission control whenever a home with an old stove was sold. In addition, council considered offering incentives to encourage the use of more efficient stoves and granting exemptions to wood burning during power outages and to low-income households, which have only stoves or fireplaces for a heat source. City Energy Director Stan Zemler also proposed to council that Boulder ban all old, inefficient fireplaces

---

<sup>1</sup>Longmont Times-Call, 4 August 1990 and Boulder Daily Camera, 30 August 1990.

or wood stoves by 1994.<sup>2</sup>

In late November 1986, the ordinance to ban wood burning on high pollution days was unanimously enacted by city council. Boulder became the third Front Range city to ban wood burning on high pollution days. The power to declare high pollution alerts was granted to the Boulder City Manager. Boulder residents would be notified of the wood burning ban via local radio stations, newspapers or a city pollution alert hotline. Many of the other measures council had been considering were also enacted. Other measures passed included waiving fines the first year the wood burning ban was enacted, banning residential coal burning outright, and granting wood burning exemptions during power outages and to homes using wood as a primary heating source. The measure to ban the installation of new fireplaces for one year was not adopted by council. However, council required any installation of wood stoves after July 1, 1988 be certified by the Colorado Department of Health.<sup>3</sup>

Efforts to improve Boulder's air quality continued. In December 1986, the Public Service Company announced that its Valmont power plant east of Boulder would burn natural gas instead of coal on high pollution days. The power plant would switch to burning natural gas whenever the Colorado Department of Health called a high pollution day for the Denver metropolitan area as part of the Better Air Campaign. The Better Air Campaign, started back in 1983, was a program that aimed to limit and reduce air pollution along the Front Range. The program ran from November 1 to January 31 of each year. The Public Service Company's banning of coal burning was done more as a token since power plants don't generally produce much carbon monoxide. However, this power plant contributed

---

<sup>2</sup>Camera, 2 July 1985 and 8 October 1986 and 13, 10 November 1986.

<sup>3</sup>Ibid., 26 November 1986.

significantly to the visible brown cloud of pollution over Denver's skyline.<sup>4</sup> Therefore, the Public Service Company's efforts to reduce air pollution, if only reducing the brown cloud, were welcomed.

As part of the campaign to reduce air pollution, a stricter Colorado emission law was passed in June 1987. The emission law, House Bill 1113, required all registered motorists and any vehicles kept in Colorado over 90 days to pass an emission test. In Boulder, this new emissions law caused some confusion to motorists and even to some police officers. According to the new emission law, everyone registering vehicles in the eight Front Range counties had to present proof of a valid emission inspection at the time of registration. However, many motorists failed to present the necessary proof when mailing in the automobile registration. Also, some police officers were unaware that temporary residents like CU students and military personnel had to comply with the emissions requirements, thus hindering enforcement of the law to some degree.<sup>5</sup>

In other efforts to reduce air pollution, specifically carbon monoxide, the Colorado Air Quality Control Commission ordered the use of high-oxygenated fuels in vehicles along the Front Range starting January 1, 1988. Boulder City Council voted to comply with this order early. Many city vehicles such as police cars, fire trucks and city staff cars switched to high-oxygenated fuels. A blend of gasoline and methyl tertiary butyl ether (MTBE ) was used by the city. The use of high-oxygenated fuels was required each year from November 1 to March 1 since carbon monoxide levels tended to be higher during the winter months. This mandatory use of high-oxygenated fuels along the Front Range was the third component of Colorado's Better Air Campaign. The other components of the campaign included banning wood burning on high pollution days and reducing driving to work by using alternative

---

<sup>4</sup>Ibid., 13 December 1986.

<sup>5</sup>Camera, 6 August 1987 and Colorado Daily 14 January 1988.

transportation like buses. The heightened concern over carbon monoxide pollution centered around the health threats to the heart and central nervous system that carbon monoxide posed. These efforts to reduce carbon monoxide not only targeted the biggest contributors to the problem - automobiles and fireplaces or stoves, but also helped cities along the Front Range stay within the federal standards. The Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) allows 9 parts carbon monoxide per million. Boulder only violated federal standards once in 1987. In contrast, Denver violated federal standards 18 times in 1987.<sup>6</sup>

However, in the past five years, Boulder's air exceeded the maximum federal standards for carbon monoxide seven times. Boulder also violated national standards for the ozone eight times and for particulates eleven times. To better understand and solve Boulder's air pollution problems, City Energy Director Stan Zemler recommended the city conduct a study to pinpoint the causes and implement additional programs to alleviate the problem. Boulder City Council endorsed Zemler's study proposal and took Zemler's ideas to combat air pollution under advisement. Some of Zemler's program ideas council was considering included: requiring employers to organize and promote car-pooling, limiting the number of wood-burning appliances to one per structure, requiring all new fireplaces to be plumbed for natural gas and prohibiting vehicles from idling at drive-up windows. While council considered these additional measures, the study of Boulder's air got underway. The study, conducted jointly with Boulder County, University of Colorado at Boulder (CU), the Colorado Health Department, the National Center for Atmospheric Research (NCAR) and the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA), involved measuring and monitoring carbon monoxide, particulates and the ozone in the Boulder Valley. In addition to this quantitative analysis, this study visually assessed the smog levels and the movement

---

<sup>6</sup>Colorado Daily, 10 September 1987 and Camera, 2 November 1987 and 31 December 1987.

of air pollution.<sup>7</sup> Funding for this study was provided by Boulder, while many scientists from NCAR, NOAA, CU, and state and county Health Departments donated time and equipment. The monitoring and measuring of Boulder Valley's air quality got started October 15, 1988 and lasted till January 31, 1989. Compilation and analysis of the data took several months. Results of the study indicated that Boulder produces its own brown cloud—Denver was not to blame. In fact, in all likelihood Boulder's air pollution was contributing to the pollution of other cities east of Boulder. The frequent westerly winds not only helped clear the pollution from Boulder, but was the reason why Boulder tended to have less air pollution than other Front Range cities. The study also concluded that Boulder contributes more carbon monoxide to the metropolitan area than Boulder receives from that area.<sup>8</sup> For more details and results of Boulder's air quality study see the report entitled Results of the Boulder Air Quality Study in the Environmental Affairs Box found in the library's municipal government reference collection at the Boulder Public Library. As for Zemler's program ideas to further reduce air pollution, council enacted none of them at this time except for programs to encourage alternative transportation. In November 1988, council briefly considered asking businesses to close their drive-up windows on high pollution days. However, after further study council was unsure whether the measure would actually reduce pollution, therefore council motioned to drop the measure.<sup>9</sup>

Although council did not enact these specific measures, other measures to improve Boulder's air quality were undertaken by council. In August 1988, council unanimously voted to ban wood burning in fireplaces and stoves in Boulder on days when Denver called a high air pollution alert. Previously, Boulder's wood burning bans were ordered based on the

---

<sup>7</sup>Colorado Daily, 20-23 May 1988 and Camera, 25 May 1988.

<sup>8</sup>Camera, 3 December 1989.

<sup>9</sup>Ibid., 15 November 1988 and 16 November 1988.

air conditions in Boulder alone. Last winter, Boulder had 4 wood burning bans and only one the previous year.<sup>10</sup> By combining the no burn days with Denver's high pollution alerts, Boulder hoped to combat poor air quality more effectively. Air quality was seen more as a regional problem than local. However, limiting wood burning was not the only thing the city targeted in its efforts to improve air quality. The city of Boulder participated in Boulder County's campaign to reduce driving. According to a November 2, 1988 article in the Louisville Times, 1988 marked the first year Boulder County conducted its own campaign to clean up air pollution. Obviously, Boulder County cities had already taken steps to alleviate the problem in conjunction with Denver's Better Air Campaign, but now Boulder County organized its own campaign. The Boulder County Clean Air Consortium (BCCAC), formed in 1987, was "...a partnership between employers, the county, the municipalities and private entities all striving to reduce vehicle miles traveled during the campaign and to clean up the air."<sup>11</sup> The BCCAC's slogan was "Air. It was never meant to be seen." By informing employees of the immensity of the air pollution problem and by offering them incentives, the BCCAC hoped to decrease vehicle trips and increase employees' use of alternative transportation. During the 1989-90 BCCAC campaign, 47 percent of Boulder city employees participated and used alternative transportation.<sup>12</sup>

In the fall of 1990, additional measures to improve the air quality went into effect. Clean Air Colorado officials banned wood burning for all six counties along the Front Range whenever the brown cloud over Denver blocked out more than 7.6 percent of the light in a kilometer of air. Previously, only carbon monoxide and particulate levels would trigger a high pollution alert that would ban the use of wood stoves and fireplaces. The Clean Air

---

<sup>10</sup>Ibid., 17 August 1988.

<sup>11</sup>Louisville Times, 2 November 1988.

<sup>12</sup>Colorado Daily, 1 February 1990.

Colorado Campaign, formerly known as the Better Air Campaign, also extended the pollution control period from November 1 to April 30 instead of just November 1 to January 31 of each year. In addition, high pollution alerts could be declared seven days a week instead of just Monday through Friday.<sup>13</sup>

Boulder's efforts so far to reduce air pollution was commendable, but Boulder did not stop there. In addition to enacting measures that would protect our outdoor environment, the city also took steps to improve the air quality of the air we breathe indoors as well. National reports on the health dangers of second-hand smoke no doubt prompted council's course of action. During this time frame, 1985 to 1990, Boulder took several steps to reduce second-hand smoke pollution in the city. Toughening the city's 10-year old smoking ordinance was first on the agenda. Council approved for a third and final time in May 1985 to amend the smoking ordinance. The changes to the ordinance included requiring restaurants with 30 or more seating accommodations to provide a non-smoking section and ordering employers to provide a smoke-free area for nonsmoking employees. Fines for violating the new measures of the smoking ordinance increased from \$25 to \$100. The new amended smoking ordinance went into effect August 7, 1985. The following year, city council was forced to amend the smoking ordinance to prevent any possible loopholes. The ordinance was amended so that restaurant owners had to provide contiguous smoking sections, instead of scattering smoking tables throughout the restaurant. Another change to the smoking ordinance required "No Smoking" signs to be posted beside ashtrays in elevators and in no-smoking sections of Crossroads Mall and restaurants.<sup>14</sup>

Boulder County Commissioners followed suit and passed a county smoking ordinance in December 1987. The ordinance went into effect March 1988. The county set

---

<sup>13</sup>Camera, 21 October 1990 and Colorado Daily 23 October 1990.

<sup>14</sup>Camera, 8 May 1985 and 17 April 1985 and 2, 17 July 1986.

harsher penalties for violating the smoking law than the city. The maximum fine for violations could run as high as \$1000 and a year in jail. The county law restricted smoking in commercially public buildings like banks, stores, hotels, theaters, hospitals, nursing homes and restaurants with 30 or more seating. However, the county's smoking ordinance did not apply to several buildings such as bars, schools, churches and government owned buildings. Boulder County's two school districts joined in the smoking ban also. Smoking was banned inside school buildings as of August 1, 1989 and outside on school grounds January 1, 1990.<sup>15</sup>

Debate over the city's smoking ordinance resurfaced in 1988 when council considered placing even more limits on smoking. Proposals to amend and strengthen Boulder's smoking ordinance resulted from continual pressure from the Group to Alleviate Smoking Pollution (GASP). Evidently, Crossroads Mall failed to comply with the smoking ordinance on several occasions ever since the ordinance was first passed. Therefore, GASP wanted council to enforce and strengthen the smoking ordinance. Several changes to the smoking ordinance were under consideration. One proposed change would require restaurants with fewer than 30 seats to provide a non-smoking area. A second change would require restaurants to post signs directing customers to a non-smoking area. Other changes included requiring business owners to enforce the non-smoking ban, even if no one lodged a complaint, and banning smoking in lobbies and hallways of public buildings as well as areas adjacent to cash registers or service lines. The proposed changes even went as far as to extend the smoking ban in public building after it was closed to the public.<sup>16</sup> The R.J Reynolds Tobacco USA Company opposed city council's proposed changes to the smoking ordinance and urged smokers to speak out against the ordinance. The tobacco company was

---

<sup>15</sup>Ibid., 29 February 1988 and 25 August 1989.

<sup>16</sup>Ibid., 23 February 1988 and 3 December 1988.

unsuccessful. Council voted in April 1989 to strengthen the city's smoking ordinance. The revised ordinance went into effect May 18, 1989. The revised smoking ordinance now required restaurants with less than 30 seating accommodations to provide a non-smoking area and banned smoking throughout Crossroads Mall except for in the food court. In addition, ashtrays were banned from non-smoking areas of Crossroads Mall.<sup>17</sup>

Despite the passage of a new stricter smoking ordinance, Crossroads Mall continued to disregard the law. As a result, the anti-smoking group GASP threatened a lawsuit against Crossroads Mall. The point of contention was the mall's refusal to remove ashtrays from areas in the mall where smoking was prohibited. The ordinance limited ashtrays to the mall entrances and every 200 feet along the corridors. The mall contended that more ashtrays were needed to prevent smokers, who went well beyond the entrance, from dropping cigarettes on the floor or from placing cigarettes into the trash cans, where fires could start. The City Attorney Joe de Raismis attempted to negotiate a compromise between Crossroads Mall and GASP. Boulder City Council revised the smoking ordinance in regards to the placement location of ashtrays. Ashtrays were allowed in other areas of the mall besides the entrance as long as "No Smoking" signs were posted. However, council decided to ban smoking in the mall's food court. The new regulations went into effect January 1, 1990.<sup>18</sup>

### **Environmental Protection**

Concern for the environment prompted further efforts by Boulder City Council and the Environmental Protection Agency. In October 1985, the Environmental Protection Agency conducted preliminary tests for hazardous materials at 28th and Pearl Streets, near the Target Store. The reason why the EPA wished to conduct tests there was because

---

<sup>17</sup>Ibid., 18 April 1989 and 22 March 1989.

<sup>18</sup>Colorado Daily, 28-31 July 1989 and Camera, 29 November 1989.

Arapahoe Chemical once was located there until 1976. During its operation, the company was allowed to place hazardous wastes into concrete ponds or lagoons until the material could be disposed of properly. Therefore, the EPA wanted to ascertain whether the cleanup process was done correctly and whether any contamination still remained. Tests results showed no hazardous residues from Arapahoe Chemical. Traces of pollutants were found, but nothing dangerous or uncommon for an urbanized area.<sup>19</sup>

In October 1986, council was concerned over the transportation of hazardous waste materials through the city. Boulder government approved proposed amendments to the National Municipal Policy regarding hazardous material transportation. Boulder's proposed amendments clarified the rules of hazardous waste transportation. Specifically, the "...routing, frequency of transportation, local notification and financing protective measures."<sup>20</sup> Boulder wanted to include all shipments by the U.S. Department of Defense and the Department of Energy in the National League of Cities' policy on hazardous waste transportation. City Councilwoman Beverly Sears, who represented Boulder in the National League of Cities, presented Boulder's proposals to the league. The league would in turn lobby the federal government to endorse the proposed policies for the transportation of hazardous waste. Boulder City Council's involvement and interest in strengthening the transportation laws on this issue stemmed from the 1985 November election. During that election, Boulder voters passed a resolution declaring Boulder a nuclear-free zone. To honor that resolution spirit, council asked the National League of Cities to strengthen its nuclear waste transportation policy.<sup>21</sup>

---

<sup>19</sup>Camera, 20 October 1985 and Rocky Mountain Business Journal, 11 November 1985.

<sup>20</sup>Camera, 14 October 1986.

<sup>21</sup>Ibid.

In February 1987, city council started drafting pesticide regulations based on the premise: right to know. Two pesticide ordinances were under consideration—one for regulating the application of pesticides outdoors and the other for indoor application of pesticides. Both required property owners and employers to notify neighbors, tenants or employees prior to applying pesticides. The outdoor pesticide regulation was strongly criticized by pesticide spraying companies. Commercial pesticide applicators even threatened lawsuit against the city. Despite the criticism, council proceeded with the ordinances. Council gave initial approval to the two pesticide ordinances in November 1987. The indoor pesticide ordinance required a 48-hour written notice prior to applying pesticides. This ordinance went into effect the first of the new year. The second ordinance required at least a 24-hour notice before treating outdoor property with pesticides. Signs 4x5 inches in size had to be posted before each application, warning people of the pesticide spraying. Although council had approved this second pesticide ordinance 7 to 2, the ordinance could not be enforced until the state court ruled the ordinance legal. Apparently, a federal court ruling held that federal acts regulating pesticides preempts local regulations. However, the Colorado Supreme Court could give Boulder the authority as a home-rule city to enact local ordinances. Until the legal lines were clarified, Boulder postponed when the ordinance regulating pesticide applications outdoors went into effect.<sup>22</sup>

This caused Boulder some legal difficulties and confusion. Since Boulder had not put the ordinance into effect, the court dismissed the city's suit to clarify its authority on regulating outdoor pesticides applications. In light of this, council voted 5 to 1 to put the outdoor pesticide ordinance into effect August 5, 1988. Coinciding with Boulder's attempts to clarify its authority, the Colorado Pesticide Applicators for Responsible Regulation (COPARR) filed suit against Boulder to challenge the city's authority. The pesticide

---

<sup>22</sup>Boulder Courier, 12-18 November 1987; Camera, 2 December 1987; Colorado Daily, 12 November 1987.

applicators believed only the federal government had the authority to regulate pesticide applications. Finally, a decision regarding the application of pesticides was handed down in federal court in October 1989. Judge Richard Matsch ruled in favor of Boulder. Boulder had the authority to regulate pesticides. However, some parts of the city's ordinance, which incorporated federal and state regulations into Boulder's ordinance, was struck down by Matsch.<sup>23</sup> Thus, the ordinance requiring property owners to notify neighbors, tenants or employees before spraying pesticides became law in Boulder.

In the spring of 1988, an infestation of Gypsy moths plagued the Martin Acres area of Boulder. The moths were known to wreak serious havoc on forests and now threatened the same devastation to trees in Boulder. Gypsy moths were first discovered in the Boulder area in 1985. Every year since then the number of moths had increased, so action was required before too much damage was done. To rid the area of the infestation, the city chose to spray a bacterial pesticide - *Bacillus thuringiensis* (Bt). According to city park officials, the pesticide Bt was the most effective and safest environmental means of dealing with the bugs. Three aerial applications of the Bt pesticide were utilized in May 1988. The city's battle against the Gypsy moths was successful. Fewer moths were trapped the following year after the spraying of Bt pesticide. However, city foresters continued to observe and trap the moths to ensure complete eradication.<sup>24</sup>

In September 1988, the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) announced "...that radon gas in homes is more pervasive than originally thought."<sup>25</sup> The dangers of radon, namely lung cancer, prompted the EPA to recommend having all homes tested for radon. In

---

<sup>23</sup>Colorado Daily, 27-30 May 1988 and Camera, 22 June 1988 and 9 May 1988 and 7 October 1989.

<sup>24</sup>Camera, 6 March 1988 and 17 April 1989.

<sup>25</sup>Ibid., 13 September 1988.

a random state testing, two Boulder County homes ranked among the top ten for radon gas levels. A Jamestown residence was ranked third and a Boulder residence was ranked tenth. The number of Colorado homes tested was 2,112. Concern over this matter prompted the city's Energy Advisory Board to approach city council for money to conduct additional radon testing and surveys. Council denied the \$15,000 request. Council felt that such funding should be paid by the county or private agencies. Residents in Boulder were encouraged to test for radon, but the city was not going pay for such tests. Residents would have to pay the bill for radon testing in their own homes.<sup>26</sup>

Also in 1988, a new government report released names of places where hazardous chemicals were stored. The report was made public in order to comply with the Federal Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act. The report for Boulder County listed 21 locations where hazardous chemicals were stored. The list was made public to ensure that local emergency agencies could handle any accident or spill. Preparedness was key to keeping any emergency situation manageable. Boulder County's 25-member Hazardous Materials Response Team would take charge if ever a spill or accident occurred in the county.<sup>27</sup> (See Appendix A for that list of places where hazardous chemicals were stored.)

In August 1988, council considered developing a wetlands ordinance whereby the city would regulate its own wetlands instead of the Army Corps of Engineers and the Environmental Protection Agency. To take over regulating the city's wetlands, Boulder needed permission from the federal government. The wetlands ordinance under consideration covered all wetlands in the city, even the wetlands under one acre. The federal government does not regulate the disturbance of wetlands under one acre. Working with the

---

<sup>26</sup>Camera, 13 September 1988 and Colorado Daily, 27 April 1989.

<sup>27</sup>Camera, 6 November 1988.

EPA, Boulder identified 60 wetlands within the city. Boulder's heightened concern for wetlands was prompted by two violations of wetlands in recent years by Techpartnership developers and Markel homebuilders. Techpartnership had leveled and filled 2 acres of land located in Gunbarrel in order to build an industrial plant. That land leveled turned out to be wetlands. Therefore, the EPA ordered Techpartnership to remove the dirt dumped and to restore the land to its natural state. A fine for the destruction of wetlands was also issued to Techpartnership. The second violation of wetlands occurred when Michael Markel of Markel Homes filled 1.3 acres of wetlands in Gunbarrel to build condominiums between 1984 and 1987. Like Techpartnership, Markel was unaware that the land was actually wetlands. The EPA fined Markel \$20,000 and ordered restoration of 2.6 acres of dried-up land near Boulder Reservoir to its former state—wetlands. Markel was fortunate that the condos could remain and that wetlands elsewhere could be restored.<sup>28</sup>

Despite the heightened concern, the matter of enacting a wetlands ordinance was still undecided as of December 1990. Council planned on passing a wetlands ordinance, but before doing so council wanted to assess public opinion. Public reaction to the wetlands ordinance was surprisingly unfavorable in January 1991. The public criticized the wetlands ordinance because the city had too much discretion, the permit fees were too high and the definition for wetlands was too broad. Given the criticism, city council decided to review the ordinance and make changes. Council held a study session on the wetlands protection ordinance in June 1991, but no official action was taken. It wasn't until December 1, 1992, that council approved a wetlands protection ordinance. The ordinance required developers to mitigate any effects a building project would have on wetlands and also required the replacement of any wetlands destroyed. Under this wetlands protection ordinance, Boulder

---

<sup>28</sup>Colorado Daily, 9-16 August 1988 and Camera, 17 August 1990 and 19 October 1990.

protected all wetlands larger than 400 square-feet.<sup>29</sup>

In November 1989, Boulder started looking at ways the city could help protect and save the ozone layer. Some measures under consideration included banning the sale of foreign aerosol cans which contain chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs), offering incentives to encourage contractors to use insulation that does not contain CFCs, banning hospitals from using CFC products as sterilizers and requiring the use of vampire machines to service air conditioners of all city vehicles. One of those measures under consideration, the use of vampire machines, was already being utilized by Boulder RTD mechanic Howard Boehme. Vampire machines when attached to the air conditioning compressors of buses recycles the freon, thus preventing CFCs from entering the atmosphere. By utilizing the vampire machines, Boehme saves 9000 pounds of ozone-eating chlorofluorocarbons from entering the Earth's stratosphere. Talk of adopting an ordinance to ban chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs) continued in April 1990. At the April Boulder City Council meeting, council declared its intention to follow Denver's lead in passing such an ordinance, but action was not quick in forthcoming. To nudge city council along, members of the Clean Water Action (CWA) group canvassed Boulder streets collecting signatures to present to council. The Clean Water Action group also encouraged Boulder residents to send letters to council members indicating the need for a law against CFCs. Council saw the pressure from the CWA as unnecessary since the matter was scheduled for discussion at a later date. Council had every intention to pass an ordinance limiting CFCs just as soon as city staff members could concentrate solely on that issue.<sup>30</sup>

Concentrated efforts to protect the ozone occurred that fall. In October 1990, council

---

<sup>29</sup>Camera, 13 January 1991 and 24 May 1991 and 11 June 1991 and Colorado Daily, 3 December 1992.

<sup>30</sup>Camera, 26 November 1989 and 19 April 1990 and 25 May 1990.

met with local businesses to discuss regulating ozone depleting substances like CFCs. Council presented a draft ordinance for limiting CFCs for them to review and consider. Actual adoption of an ordinance limiting CFCs occurred in 1991. Council gave final approval of the ordinance at its February 5th meeting. The ordinance required CFCs from air conditioners and refrigerators to be recycled. Furthermore, the ordinance banned the manufacturing and sale of products with CFCs and limited the use of halon fire extinguishers.<sup>31</sup>

### **Refuse Disposal**

Three main concerns in regards to refuse disposal plagued the city during this time frame—1985 to 1990. Those three concerns included the closing of Marshall Landfill as it neared its full capacity, the cleanup costs of Marshall Landfill's toxic leaching and groundwater contamination problem and the city's questioning of its annual spring trash haul. In light of these pending refuse disposal problems, it's not surprising that the city increased its recycling efforts during this six-year period. More and more, the disposal of refuse transformed as concern for the environment heightened. Recycling was key to reducing waste. The city of Boulder concerned itself with reducing the amount of trash dumped in the landfill. This in turn, not only increased the length of time the Marshall Landfill remained open, but also reduced the chances of further toxic contamination. Both refuse disposal and recycling issues were key concerns of city council. Although the two subjects intertwine, presentation of each issue's history was done separately for simplicity sake.

In 1985, the Marshall Landfill was expected to reach its full capacity in the next two years. In addition, hazardous wastes and residues were leaching from trash in an old unused portion of the dump. Therefore, a search for an alternative trash dumping site was

---

<sup>31</sup>Ibid., 11 October 1990 and 6 February 1991.

paramount. Boulder County Commissioners discussed and searched for an alternative site, but no immediate solution was forthcoming. The closing of Boulder County's only dump site would bring a loss of funding for recycling and clean-up of wastes seeping from Marshall Landfill. In light of the pending Marshall Landfill closing, Western Disposal, the principal trash hauler for Boulder, considered purchasing a landfill near Erie just across the Boulder County line in Weld County. Western Disposal decided to go ahead and purchase its own landfill in Erie. This decision decreased the amount of trash and revenue at the Marshall Landfill.<sup>32</sup>

With the loss of business from Western Disposal, Landfill Inc., operator of the Marshall Landfill and subsidiary of the commercial trash hauling company Browning Ferris Industries, asked the county to raise the trash dumping fees for individuals and small haulers using the Marshall Landfill. For obvious reasons, Landfill Inc.'s rate increases did not include commercial outfits. The request to raise dumping fees was denied because Landfill Inc. failed to supply figures to Boulder County Commissioners' questions. Plus, the commissioners questioned Landfill Inc.'s not raising the fees for commercial outfits like Browning Ferris Industries, which dumps nearly 66 percent of the trash at the landfill. Even though less trash was being dumped at Marshall Landfill, the closing of Boulder's only landfill was inevitable. To prevent this from happening anytime soon, the operator of the landfill asked to expand the dump site in January 1986. Landfill Inc. wanted to raise the height of the landfill another 10 feet to keep the landfill from closing. The landfill operator's request to expand was denied. Why? Because of the landfill's problem of toxic leaching. Although the Environmental Protection Agency was negotiating with Browning Ferris Industries (BFI), owner of the Marshall Landfill, to cleanup the toxic area, no agreement had been reached. BFI was refusing to pay the entire cleanup costs. With BFI's refusal, the EPA had to decide its next course of action. Should the EPA "...order BFI to begin cleanup, refer

---

<sup>32</sup>Camera, 8 December 1985 and Louisville Times/Lafayette News, 19 March 1986.

the case to the Justice Department, or use federal money appropriated under Superfund legislation to cleanup Marshall."<sup>33</sup> Until the cleanup matter was resolved between the EPA and BFI, the Colorado Department of Health denied Browning Ferris Industries's request to expand.<sup>34</sup>

After further review and investigation, the EPA agreed with BFI over the cleanup costs. The EPA identified other parties responsible for a portion of the Marshall Landfill cleanup costs, including the city of Boulder. The city was named in October 1986. Boulder was named because the city had deposited sewage sludge, trash and industrial wastes at the landfill from 1969 to 1980. The city of Boulder's lack of action over the Marshall Landfill cleanup from October 1986 to April 1987 prompted the EPA to threaten the city with a lawsuit. Boulder asked the EPA for a three month extension to negotiate a settlement. The extension was granted—a lawsuit was averted. Boulder submitted a cleanup proposal to the EPA in August 1987.<sup>35</sup>

At the same time the Landfill Inc.'s request to expand was being considered and the negotiations over the cleanup was being discussed, talk of locating a new landfill in Boulder County resurfaced in February 1986. A previous study done in 1983, by the county's Solid Waste Advisory Committee, provided the basis of where to start looking for a new landfill. The search for a suitable landfill location got underway in October 1986. A committee was to identify and evaluate potential sites.<sup>36</sup> The study group charged with identifying potential landfill sites concluded that no suitable place exists within Boulder County. Several factors

---

<sup>33</sup>Times-Call, 31 January 1986 and Camera, 1 September 1986.

<sup>34</sup>Times-Call, 13 January 1987.

<sup>35</sup>Camera, 27 April 1987; 2 May 1987; 7 August 1987 (Environmental Protection file).

<sup>36</sup>Times-Call, 11 February 1986 and 21 October 1986.

were identified as to why no suitable site for a landfill existed in Boulder County. Those factors identified included geotechnical problems, inadequate top soil, zoning and proximity to residents or wildlife habitats. In essence, Boulder County should rely on landfills in the surrounding counties. Not surprisingly, the city of Boulder was not pleased with the study's conclusion.<sup>37</sup> In light of the study's conclusion, Western Disposal worked towards establishing a garbage transfer site in east Boulder. When Marshall Landfill closed, the transfer site would be utilized. Collected garbage would be dumped there and sorted for recyclables until larger trucks could haul the trash to Western Disposal's landfill in Weld County. Western Disposal's plans for a waste transfer site did not go unscathed. On two separate occasions, Western Disposal abandoned plans for a transfer site in 1987. One transfer site location abandoned was at 63rd Street and Valmont Road—the KOA Campground. This site was inhibited by floodplain restrictions. The second site selected was in the Ute Research Industrial Park area on 75th Street, but public outcry in that neighborhood stopped Western Disposal's owner Dan Souder from pursuing that site.<sup>38</sup> Eventually the necessity of a waste transfer site prevailed. Western Disposal was allowed to build its waste transfer station at the first location site considered—the KOA Campground site. Unlike the previous plan, the transfer station was to be built at the back of the property, placing it out of the floodplain. Also, a larger parcel of land was purchased to better accommodate the transfer station. The transfer station was expected to be operational by June 1988.<sup>39</sup>

With the negotiations for the cleanup of Marshall Landfill underway, the Boulder County Planning Commission approved the landfill operator's second request to expand

---

<sup>37</sup>Camera, 18 April 1987 and 28 April 1987.

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 25 June 1987 and Time-Call, 4 November 1987.

<sup>39</sup>Camera, 19 December 1987.

Marshall Landfill in February 1988. The expansion plan would allow the landfill to grow 10 feet taller on the condition that the dump shuts down in 1992. The Planning Commission stipulated additional conditions to the landfill operators expanding the landfill. Those other conditions included BFI's commitment to a cleanup plan and prevention of further toxic seeping as well as the blowing litter problem. BFI had already showed its commitment to the cleanup. Negotiations of how to split the estimated \$5 million cleanup costs between the city of Boulder and BFI were taking place. As for the other conditions, BFI agreed to control the blowing litter problem and prevent additional toxic waste leaching. Boulder favored the expansion plan of the Marshall Landfill. However, the expansion plan was not without opponents. Opponents of the expansion included the National Center for Atmospheric Research (NCAR) and the town of Superior. NCAR opposed because of its close proximity to the dump, while Superior objected because of the blowing trash problem. On windy days, the city of Superior was often subjected to trash raining down on its city. Final approval of the Marshall Landfill expansion rested with the Boulder County Commissioners. Permission to expand was granted by the commissioners, but they insisted that the landfill close in January 1992. The vote was 2 to 1 in favor of the expansion. The commissioners also placed some conditions on the landfill in exchange for granting permission to expand. Some of those conditions placed on the landfill included planting more vegetation, putting chemical dust suppressants on the haul roads to the landfill, and closing the landfill whenever winds reached 35 mph or if litter started blowing off the site.<sup>40</sup>

Progress over the Marshall Landfill cleanup was made. In April 1988, the city of Boulder reached an agreement with the EPA on Boulder's share of the cleanup costs. Boulder was to pay 30 percent of cleanup costs for depositing sewage sludge at the landfill from 1969 to 1980. Even though the city had followed standard procedures and laws in effect at the time, Boulder was still liable under the EPA's Superfund law. To help fund the

---

<sup>40</sup>Ibid., 18 February 1988 and 13 April 1988.

city's estimated \$1.2 million share of the cleanup, city council raised the sewer bills of Boulder residents. Besides the increase in utility bills, the cost of dumping trash at Marshall Landfill increased by nearly 30 percent. The rate increases were approved by the Boulder County Commissioners and went into effect September 1988. The rate for a carload of garbage increased from \$2.75 to \$4.00 and the rate for a pickup or van filled with trash increased from \$5.70 to \$9.00. Rates for commercial haulers also increased from \$2.20 to \$3.50 for each cubic foot.<sup>41</sup>

A final agreement on the cleanup costs was reached in April 1989. The EPA approved a \$4.4 million agreement for cleaning up the Marshall Landfill. Boulder agreed to pay \$1 million and Landfill Inc. agreed to pay the other \$3.4 million. An additional \$485,000 was to be paid to the Environmental Protection Agency by former users and operator of the landfill. The \$485,000 was reimbursement to the EPA for response cleanup costs. The two former companies contributing to the reimbursement fund were the Public Service Company of Colorado, which paid \$120,000 and AMF Inc., which paid \$125,000. The former operator of the landfill, James Kean and his company—Mesa Sand and Gravel, paid \$240,000.<sup>42</sup> The first stages of cleanup were scheduled to start in the fall. Cleanup involved making improvements to the landfill and providing groundwater collection, treatment and monitoring. The construction of the groundwater collection and treatment system was underway by the summer of 1990. A Longmont Times-Call article dated August 2, 1990, indicated that the Marshall Landfill cleanup would take at least another year. Plans to remove and treat all the groundwater was still in progress.<sup>43</sup>

As Boulder struggled with the Marshall Landfill issues, council addressed a third

---

<sup>41</sup>Ibid., 19 April 1988; 19 May 1988; 1 July 1988.

<sup>42</sup>Lafayette News, 12 April 1989 and Camera, 18 August 1989.

<sup>43</sup>Times-Call, 11 April 1989 and 2 August 1990 (Environmental Protection file).

refuse disposal concern. Although minor in comparison to the other two issues, the issue was a concern of Boulder City Council. The issue at hand was Boulder's annual spring trash haul. Council not only wrestled over whether or not to hold the annual trash haul, but also where to dump the trash collected. In the spring of 1986, a mini-trash war over Boulder's garbage was waged. During Boulder's annual spring trash haul, the city's trash was taken to Columbine Landfill near Erie instead of the Marshall Landfill. Why? Two reasons—high winds which had forced the Marshall Landfill to close temporarily and the higher cubic-yard flat rates and extra fees for dumping trash charged by the Marshall Landfill's operator. When Boulder was forced to look elsewhere for an alternative dump site because Marshall Landfill was temporarily closed due to high winds on May 5th, it found lower rates and no extra fees for things like mattresses and appliances at Columbine Landfill. To draw the city back to using Boulder's landfill, operators of Marshall Landfill lowered the cubic-yard rates and eliminated the extra charges. Offering a cheaper rate worked, Boulder returned to using Marshall Landfill.<sup>44</sup>

The following year, Boulder had to cancel the spring trash haul due to budget constraints. Canceling the event saved the Public Works Department budget about \$250,000. For 17 years the city had provided this free trash removal, but the need to cancel the long tradition was not opposed. Boulder residents took the cancellation in stride. In March 1988, council voted 7 to 2 to reinstate the city's annual trash haul for 1988. Not all council members welcomed reinstating the costly event. Some council members felt the \$250,000 cost of the spring trash haul could be better spent elsewhere. The two council members who voted against reinstating the event were Matthew Appelbaum and Spense Havlick.<sup>45</sup> The objections to the costly program were duly noted. Later that same year, talk of holding the

---

<sup>44</sup>Camera, 13 May 1986.

<sup>45</sup>Boulder Courier, 16 July 1987 and Camera, 2 March 1988.

trash haul every other year instead of annually gained favor among city council. Again, budget concerns prompted council to consider offering the trash service every other year. Council decided in September 1988 to offer the trash service every other year. However, public outcry prompted council to reverse that decision. Unlike in 1987, Boulder residents insisted the city provide the service annually. Therefore, in November 1988, council voted 5 to 2 in favor of sponsoring the free trash pickup on an annual basis. The sales tax increase approved by voters last fall would help finance the cost of the annual trash haul.<sup>46</sup>

### **Recycling**

The plight of the Marshall Landfill affected the recycling business and the city's role in recycling. The years 1985 and 1986 were marked by Eco-Cycle's struggle to keep afloat. Eco-Cycle, a non-profit organization charged with providing curbside pickup of recyclables once a month and recycling drop-off sites in Boulder and Longmont, was losing money in operating expenses. The price drop in recyclables and the loss of revenue from Marshall Landfill were the reasons for Eco-Cycle's financial troubles. Eco-Cycle expected to lose \$100,000 to \$250,000 by the 1986 fiscal year-end. Therefore, Eco-Cycle asked Boulder County and the city of Boulder for financial help.<sup>47</sup> In the past, both the county and city had helped sustain Eco-Cycle's operation. The county granted the request for continued support, but the amount was less than requested. Boulder County Commissioners allocated \$30,000 for Eco-Cycle's 1986 operating expenses. Eco-Cycle had asked for \$120,000 from the county's Resource Recovery Fund. Money in the Resource Recovery Fund came from fees charged at Marshall Landfill for dumping trash. Since Western Disposal stopped hauling trash to the Marshall Landfill, the amount of money in that recovery fund dropped. Boulder County Commissioners informed Eco-Cycle that the organization could spend the county's

---

<sup>46</sup>Camera, 16 September 1988 and 16 November 1988.

<sup>47</sup>Ibid., 2 October 1985.

\$70,000 contribution to Eco Cycle's building fund on operations instead. Eco-Cycle had plans to construct new facilities and therefore did not want to subtract any money from that fund for operating expenses. So far the organization had managed to save \$212,000 for building a new facility.<sup>48</sup> Boulder City Council granted Eco-Cycle \$60,000 for operations. Council also allocated \$12,000 to conduct a recycling and solid waste management study. Boulder wanted to expand its recycling efforts and establish a long-term recycling plan. Additional funding for the study was to come from Boulder County Commissioners, but the city's request was denied.<sup>49</sup> The need to divert as much waste material as possible from Marshall Landfill was evident given the toxic leaching and groundwater contamination problem there and also the fact that the landfill would inevitably close. Therefore, Boulder proceeded with its plan to establish a five-year comprehensive recycling plan. Council directed city staff to investigate the opportunities for establishing a long-term recycling program as well as funding for the program.

Boulder's efforts to establish a comprehensive recycling program included developing a request for a recycling proposal and then conducting a search for that recycling proposal contract and finding a way to support the recycling program financially. Boulder's plan was to provide weekly or biweekly-weekly curbside collection of recyclables citywide and increase community participation. According to Stan Zemler, city energy director, Boulder was " ...looking for a contract for specific recycling services for a fixed dollar amount."<sup>50</sup> Zemler suggested to council that the city should take bids on curbside collection, but retain using Eco-Cycle to handle the marketing and processing of recyclables. To help fund the recycling program, Zemler also suggested to council that an occupational tax be

---

<sup>48</sup>Ibid., 14 January 1986 and 1 September 1986.

<sup>49</sup>Boulder Courant, 16 April 1986 and Times-Call, 24 June 1986 (Refuse Disposal file).

<sup>50</sup>Camera, 19 October 1986.

levied on trash haulers. This idea drew criticism from trash haulers since collecting the fees would be administratively difficult. Given the criticism, Boulder City Council rejected this funding option. Just how to finance its recycling program remained an issue, but not the only one. Some council members also questioned giving the processing and selling of recyclables contract outright to Eco-Cycle. Bids for all aspects of Boulder's recycling plan should be taken, not just on the curbside pickup.<sup>51</sup> Council debated the issue for some time and at one point council directed the City Manager Jim Piper to negotiate with Eco-Cycle. However, when Pete Grogan, Eco-Cycle founder and director, announced he would retire in July 1987, council opted to take other bids for the processing end of the recycling contract as well as the recycling collection contract.<sup>52</sup> Interested parties presented bids to council.

Eco-Cycle and Western Disposal teamed up and presented a bid for the city's recycling contract. The bid proposal was as follows: Western Disposal would collect recyclables along with the trash weekly from Boulder residents; then Eco-Cycle would process the recyclable materials and market the recycling program to increase Boulder participation. Eco-Cycle and Western Disposal bid \$125,000 a year for the recycling contract. As it turned out, this was the only bid presented to city council. The contract was awarded to the two companies. A pact between the city of Boulder, Eco-Cycle and Western Disposal was reached in August 1988.<sup>53</sup> According to the agreement, Western Disposal would collect recyclables weekly while Eco-Cycle would handle the processing aspect and marketing of the program. In return, the city agreed to pay \$125,000 and provide plastic bins for recycling to Boulder residents. The city also agreed to continue leasing space to Eco-Cycle at 5030 Pearl Street near the City Yards. However, Eco-Cycle would now be charged

---

<sup>51</sup>Ibid., 28 January 1987.

<sup>52</sup>Times-Call, 4 February 1987 and Camera, 1 May 1987 and 29 July 1987.

<sup>53</sup>Camera, 27 March 1988 and 2 April 1988 and 13 August 1988.

\$1,300 a month instead of the usual nominal \$1 fee previously charged. Boulder would support the recycling program through an appropriation from the city's general fund. The new recycling program, which got started in October 1988, was called "Recycle Boulder." In the first year of operation, "Recycle Boulder" had a 60 percent participation rate. The success of the program was attributed to the convenience, regularity, publicity and public education. Curbside collection instead of drop-off sites was key to the program's success also.<sup>54</sup>

Boulder's commitment to recycling did not stop there. Boulder initiated other recycling projects including buying and encouraging the use of recycled paper, composting leaves, precycling and recycling old phonebooks. In January 1989, Boulder City Council desired to give recycled paper suppliers preference over other suppliers. To this end, council voted to have the city attorney prepare an ordinance allowing the city to purchase recycled paper. The ordinance written allowed the city to purchase recycled paper provided the cost did not exceed 5 percent over the usual paper expenditures. Council approved the ordinance in April 1989.<sup>55</sup> Boulder not only preferred buying recycled paper, but also encouraged newspapers to utilize recycled paper. A plummeted market for recycled paper was caused by a deluge of old newspapers. Too many people were recycling newspapers. Hence, the city tried to encourage and increase the use of recycled products. City officials wrote letters to local and national newspapers to access and urge the use of recycled paper. Boulder was not alone in its plea for using recycled paper. Many states and cities were considering or had already passed legislation requiring the use of recycled paper by newspapers. Boulder's plea did not fall on deaf ears. By the end of 1990, 50 percent of the Daily Camera was produced

---

<sup>54</sup>Colorado Daily, 17 August 1988 and Stan Zemler, "How One City Works to Reduce its Solid Waste with Recycling," Colorado Municipalities, July-August 1989, 10-15.

<sup>55</sup>Camera, 4 January 1989 and 5 April 1989.

from recycled paper.<sup>56</sup>

In other efforts, Boulder minimized the amount of trash dumped in the landfill by offering leaf composting in the fall of 1989. The city offered free curbside pickup of bagged leaves to 5,000 households in Boulder. Biodegradable bags were delivered to those homes to gather the leaves for the composting project. This pilot composting program was sponsored by the Environmental Protection Agency, Boulder County and of course the city of Boulder. The EPA awarded Boulder with a \$48,000 grant to fund the program. Both the county and city contributed as well. The county contributed \$15,000, while the city gave \$20,000 to support the composting program. Over 100 tons of leaves were diverted from the landfill for composting. The composted material was then made available for purchase to Boulder area residents.<sup>57</sup>

Another program developed in 1990 helped consumers make educated decisions on recycling. Products in local grocery stores were labeled to inform the buyer of the products recycling potential. The labels indicated whether the product was packaged in recycled material or if the material could be recycled and also if the product used minimal packaging to reduce waste. This "precycling" program was made possible through grants from the city and the EPA. Each contributed \$15,000 towards the labeling program. Boulder County contributed \$5,000. The city's precycling program was part of Boulder's three-step conservation plan—recycling, buying recycled materials and waste reduction through precycling.<sup>58</sup> The importance of reducing waste dumped in landfills inspired another pilot recycling program in Boulder—recycling old phonebooks. Western Disposal, King Soopers

---

<sup>56</sup>Camera, 4 September 1989; Colorado Daily, 15-17 September 1989; Camera, 9 December 1990.

<sup>57</sup>Colorado Daily, 4-7 August 1989 and 9 October 1989 and Camera, 17 November 1989 (Refuse Disposal file).

<sup>58</sup>Camera, 14 September 1990 and Colorado Daily 14-16 September 1990.

and U.S. West sponsored this program. King Soopers served as a drop-off site for old phonebooks. Western Disposal provided the containers at the drop-off sites for recycling the phonebooks.<sup>59</sup>

In June 1990, council awarded Eco-Cycle and Western Disposal additional funds beyond the contract amount, but not as much as both organizations had hoped. Extra money was needed to ease the strain of plummeting prices for recyclables in 1989, plus new equipment and additional staffing. Eco-Cycle had requested \$124,180 to help make ends meet, but only \$58,180 was awarded by the city. Western Disposal received \$71,300 instead of the \$160,598 requested.<sup>60</sup>

Boulder's commitment to recycling was reiterated at the July 17, 1990 city council meeting. Council not only discussed expanding its recycling programs to include curbside pick-up of recyclables for every resident instead of the 60 percent currently served, but also support of recycling for businesses in the form of education, information and references. Council even voiced support for mandatory recycling, but disagreed on when to initiate mandatory recycling. More importantly at this meeting, council discussed ways of financing the city's expanded recycling efforts. Ways to finance the city's recycling programs discussed included taxing or charging households a recycling fee, placing a sales tax on trash haulers revenue, or levying a mill tax or placing an occupation tax on trash haulers. Environmental Affairs staff Stan Zemler and Alison Peter were charged with developing more specific proposals for expanding the city's recycling program as well as finding funds to support the recycling efforts. In the fall, a plan on how to finance the expanded recycling efforts of the city was proposed. The proposal imposed a 6 percent sales tax on trash haulers to support recycling. This new recycling tax would raise an estimated \$450,000 annually for

---

<sup>59</sup>Camera, 30 November 1990.

<sup>60</sup>Ibid., 6 June 1990.

the city to expand its recycling program. Residents would pay 35 cents per garbage can each month and businesses would pay 20 cents per cubic yard of trash each collection. This new tax was an incentive to recycle and to reduce the amount of waste dumped in the landfill. On October 16, 1990, Boulder City Council gave preliminary approval of this new recycling tax. Final approval of the new recycling tax was granted on October 23, 1990. The new tax was to go effect November 5th, so that the tax would be exempt from the effects of a tax-limiting initiative on the November 6th ballot. However, since voters did not approve the tax limitation ordinance, council pushed back the starting date of the new recycling tax until January 1991. This allowed the means of collecting the new tax to be refined. <sup>61</sup>

### **Sewage Disposal and Water Supply**

In January 1985, Boulder City Council approved a 20 percent increase in the monthly sewage treatment fees for Boulder residents. The new rate went into effect March 1985. The increase was to help with the operating costs of the 75th Street wastewater treatment plant. The extra money generated by the fee increase also helped to establish a capital improvements fund so the wastewater treatment plant could meet effluent standards.<sup>62</sup> State effluent standards for the permissible level of ammonia discharged from the plant into Boulder Creek were tightened. Negotiations over when and how Boulder had to comply with the new standards were taking place. Boulder, for obvious reasons, requested that the state allow the city's sewage treatment plant to improve effluent standards gradually. An article in the Daily Camera dated January 9, 1986, indicated that the EPA was requiring all sewage treatment plants to comply with the stricter effluent standards by July 1988.<sup>63</sup> In

---

<sup>61</sup>Camera, 18 July 1990; Colorado Daily, 18 October 1990; Camera, 24 October 1990 and 19 November 1990.

<sup>62</sup>Camera, 7 January 1985.

<sup>63</sup>Ibid., 9 January 1986 (Water Pollution file).

light of this news, Boulder proceeded with making the necessary improvements. In October 1986, Boulder residents were informed that sewer rates would increase once again in 1987. As before, the increase was to help finance improvements at the sewage treatment plant so Boulder could comply with tougher state and federal quality standards on water released from the plant back into Boulder Creek.<sup>64</sup>

The cost of the improvements to the sewage treatment plant did not come cheap—\$21 million. The improvements were financed in part by a federal grant. The grant paid for more than half the cost of the improvements—\$14 million. The remaining \$7 million balance was paid by Boulder city residents and business owners in utility fees. Developers did not go unscathed. Developers' water and wastewater plant investment fees were increased as well. Developers paid these one-time fee charges when they hooked up new buildings to city utilities. In October 1987, council voted 9 to 0 to approve these fee increases for developers. The city also approved a rate restructuring plan for wastewater discharge permits. The city felt it was necessary to charge industries that produce excess-strength sewage more for their wastewater discharge permit to help offset the cost of the city's pretreatment process. The cost of a permit depended on the amount of wastewater industries released. For example, in the first year of the new rate plan, industries releasing 10,000 gallons a day would be charged \$863 for the discharge permit, while the release of 25,000 gallons would cost \$1,213. After the first year of implementing this new rate increase for discharge permits, the cost of permits were to double in 1990 and then increase once again in 1991 by 50 percent. With the money collected from the utility and permit fee increases, plus the federal grant money, the city was able to carry out its sewage treatment plant improvement plans.

Improvements to the sewage treatment plant included adding an extra step in the treatment process to reduce solids and installing a separate process to reduce ammonia

---

<sup>64</sup>Ibid., 29 October 1986 (Boulder Public Works/Utilities file).

levels. The city needed to meet the federal standard of 30 milligrams of suspended solids and organic materials per liter of treated water. With the new treatment processes, city utility director, Bob Wheeler, figured the city would easily stay within the federal standards. Summit Constructors of Westminster and Reid-Burton Construction of Fort Collins were hired to complete the improvement work. Improvements to Boulder's sewage treatment plant were completed by June 1990. With these improvements, Boulder was able to improve the quality of treated wastewater poured back into Boulder Creek by half compared to the quality two years ago.<sup>65</sup>

Amidst these improvements to the sewage treatment plant, another project was in the works—cogeneration. The process known as cogeneration converts methane gas, a byproduct of sewage, into electricity by utilizing the latest technology. To make this work, two 15-ton generators were installed at the sewage treatment plant at 4049 N. 75th Street in March 1986. The methane gas fueled the generators, which in turn produced electricity. The generators together could produce about 7200 kilowatt hours per day. The electricity produced was then sold by the city to the Public Service Company of Colorado. The city expected to earn approximately \$75,000 a year from the sale of electricity. According to June Heinrich, Public Works Department Project Manager, the cogeneration project cost about \$2 million, but a federal grant of nearly \$1.5 million helped cover most of the cost. The city held a dedication ceremony for this project on Thursday May 7, 1987.<sup>66</sup>

A second electricity producing project by the Public Works Department was completed during this time frame—1985 to 1990. The city harnessed the water pressure created from the mountain streams descent in elevation to produce energy. How? The water

---

<sup>65</sup>Camera, 3 November 1988 and 21 October 1987; Colorado Daily, 23-25 December 1988; Camera, 25 June 1990.

<sup>66</sup>Camera, 4 March 1986 and Colorado Daily, 8-9 May 1987.

pressure spins the turbines of the generators to produce electricity, hence hydroelectricity. Instrumental in convincing Boulder of the hydroelectric capabilities possible from the mountain streams was Councilman Phil Stern. The bond issue of \$3.6 million for hydroelectric power, which Boulder voters approved in 1984, became a reality the following year. In March 1985, Boulder's first hydroelectric station—the Maxwell Hydroelectric Plant—began operating. The Maxwell plant, located at Linden Avenue and Wonderland Hills Road, produced about 40,000 kilowatt hours of energy a month, which was enough energy to supply 80 homes with electricity. Four other hydroelectric plants started operating within the next two years. The four other hydroelectric stations created were Kohler, Orodell, Sunshine and Betasso. The Betasso Hydroelectric Plant, located west of Boulder at the Betasso Water Treatment Plant along Betasso and Sugarloaf Road, was the largest of the hydroelectric facilities created. The turbine generators used to generate electricity at the Betasso plant came from China. According to Project Manager June Busse, the Betasso Hydroelectric Plant started commercial operation on December 18, 1987. Hence, the city started earning money from the electricity produced and then sold to the Public Service Company of Colorado, although later than anticipated since the plant was not completed on time. The city held a dedication ceremony for the four hydroelectric stations on April 20 1988. The Maxwell Hydroelectric Station had already been christened back in June of 1985. The city expected to generate \$765,000 a year in revenue from selling the electricity produced by all four facilities. Plans for another hydroelectric plant known as Lakewood was in the works, whereby the city could earn even more revenue, but that hydroelectric plant never materialized. The city did end up receiving some additional money since the Betasso Hydroelectric Plant was not completed by August 25, 1987, the date specified for completion in the contract with the chief contractor, Summit Constructors Inc. of Northglenn. The city was awarded \$296,250 in May 1989.<sup>67</sup>

---

<sup>67</sup>Camera, 27 June 1985; Boulder Public Works and Utilities, Newsletter, January

City council addressed two issues concerning Boulder's water supply in 1990. Issues addressed by council included the conservation of Boulder's treated water supply to delay expansion needs of the cities two water treatment plants--the Betasso and the Boulder Reservoir--and the selling of its surplus raw water supply. Council's stepped-up concern for water conservation measures arose from when Boulder residents nearly tapped out its treated water supply one day back in July 1989. The city consumed over 50 million gallons of water. The city's maximum water delivery capabilities were 55 million gallons of treated water a day. Had an emergency occurred like a fire, the city would not of had enough water available. Such an event could not happen again. Obviously, Boulder had to expand its water treatment facilities in order to meet the growing water needs of Boulder residents. Therefore, council ordered a study to be conducted to determine the city's water situation and to help the city plan for the expansion of its water treatment facilities.

The study determined that implementing water conservation measures could delay the expansion needs of the city's water supply and treatment facilities for nearly 20 years. Not surprisingly, council favored implementing conservation measures to delay the expansion needs. A voluntary water conservation plan was preliminarily approved unanimously by city council in November 1990. Council held off final approval until 1991 in order to allow the Public Works and Utility Department time to refine its cost estimates and schedule for enacting the conservation plan. The conservation plan included providing the public and businesses free consultation on water saving measures, implementing low-water landscaping like xeriscape throughout the city, and approving an advanced plumbing code that requires new construction projects to use low-water-use toilets, showerheads, dishwashers and washing machines. It was projected that implementing this water conservation plan would reduce Boulder's peak day water demand by 11.8 million gallons. The water conservation plan initial cost estimates was \$72,000, which would result in a 1

---

1988; Camera 21 April 1988 and 25 May 1989.

percent increase in Boulder residents' water bill. The new water conservation plan was to begin in the spring of 1991.

Earlier conservation efforts started back in June 1990 were also to continue. Boulder had implemented a scientific approach to conserving water in June 1990. The public was informed daily of the evapotranspiration (ET) rate—the loss of water from soil due to evaporation—to maximize lawn watering efficiency. The Daily Camera as well as the local radio and television stations reported the ET rate so residents could determine the minimal of amount of water needed to keep one's lawn green. This voluntary watering conservation program helped save Boulder's treated water supply. Peak water demand in the summer of 1990 only reached 45 million gallons one day.<sup>68</sup>

Although helpful in conserving Boulder's treated water supply, implementing conservation measures alone was not enough. Therefore, the city intended to operate its second water treatment plant—the Boulder Reservoir Plant—year round. Normally, that water treatment facility would operate only during the summer, but over the past five years the Boulder Reservoir Plant had to be operational for eight or nine months out of a year in order to keep up with the water demand. Plus, if something happened with the Betasso facility, Boulder's water needs would still be met, but only if the Boulder Reservoir Plant operated all year long.
