Operating the Boulder Reservoir Plant year round would obviously mean additional costs for Boulder residents. Year round operational costs were estimated to be \$500,000. In addition to running the water treatment facility year round, the city wished to make needed repairs to the city's raw water transport system. Boulder relies on the both the Lakewood Pipeline and the Barker Reservoir Pipeline to transport raw water from the Lakewood and Barker Reservoirs for its municipal water supply. The city's principal concern

---

<sup>68</sup>Camera, 12 November 1990; Colorado Daily, 23-25 November 1990; Camera, 6 June 1990 and 31 May 1991.

was to replace the 10-mile Lakewood Pipeline. Boulder was losing its water delivery capacity since the Lakewood Pipeline was nearly 40 years old and leaking.

The city's efforts to replace the pipeline with a larger one date back to 1986-87. The city wanted a reliable water delivery system, one that would comply with federal water quality standards. However, Boulder's efforts to replace and realign the Lakewood Pipeline required approval from the United States Forest Service since the pipeline cuts across portions of National Forests. In November 1990, the U.S. Forest Service was in the process of conducting an environmental impact statement to determine the impact of the Lakewood Pipeline replacement project.<sup>69</sup> Public objection and concern over the environmental impact of the proposed new Lakewood Pipeline had prompted the U.S. Forest Service to announce back in March 1990, that they would conduct the environmental impact statement. Earlier environmental assessments conducted by the city had indicated no significant impact. The U.S. Forest Service had originally concurred with the city's environmental assessment findings that construction of the pipeline posed no significant impact. Construction of the Lakewood Pipeline replacement was to start in March 1989, but mountain property residents were unsatisfied and disagreed with the city's environmental assessment findings. As a result, Sugarloaf residents lodged an environmental appeal against the pipeline with the U.S. Forest Service, thus delaying construction of the pipeline. In March 1989, the U.S. Forest Service sided with Sugarloaf residents and directed the city of Boulder to conduct another environmental assessment study, whereby the concerns of Sugarloaf residents would be addressed.

The following year, the U.S. Forest Service decided an environmental impact statement should be conducted instead of environmental assessment in order to resolve public objections to the Lakewood Pipeline project. The environmental impact statement

---

<sup>69</sup>Camera, 12 November 1990 and Colorado Daily, 23-25 November 1990.

(EIS) was a more thorough and time consuming study, but also more costly. The city was to pay for the environmental impact statement. The city complied with the U.S. Forest Service's decision to conduct the EIS to ensure the public that the city was doing everything possible to analyze the environmental impact. Engineering Sciences of Denver was hired to prepare the environmental impact statement. Results of that environmental impact statement were still pending in November 1990. The delay of replacing the Lakewood Pipeline, not only impacted the city's efforts to improve water delivery, but also prevented the city from building its Lakewood Hydroelectric Plant as planned.<sup>70</sup> To this day, the Lakewood Pipeline remains a controversial topic and still has not been replaced.

Coinciding with the city's water conservation plans came the decision to sell surplus raw water to Broomfield. For years the city had been contemplating selling off rights to its Windy Gap water shares. Boulder has a surplus of raw water, unlike its treated water supply, which was very limited. In November 1990, council directed city staff members to begin conducting water sale negotiations with Broomfield. The water deal was finalized in February 1991. Broomfield paid Boulder \$21.5 million for approximately 1.4 billion gallons of water a year from the Windy Gap project. Payment was spread out over a two year period, beginning July 1, 1991. How the water would be delivered and where the water would be stored was not included in the sale agreement.<sup>71</sup>

---

<sup>70</sup>Colorado Daily, 27-29 March 1990 and Camera, 16 June 1988; 25 March 1989; 27 March 1990.

<sup>71</sup>Camera, 28 November 1990 and 20 February 1991.

## CHAPTER 3 TRANSPORTATION

Between 1985 and 1990, the usual transportation issues characterized Boulder's transportation history. For instance, providing adequate parking spaces and improving road conditions and intersections for safety and optimum traffic flow. During this time frame, Boulder built a new parking structure downtown, expanded existing parking structures and implemented a permit-parking zone ordinance to improve parking conditions downtown. In addition to parking improvements, Boulder made several road improvements to enhance safety as well as mobility and traffic flow. Boulder completed Foothills Parkway, replaced bridges, made improvements at the intersection of Baseline and Broadway and also along west Arapahoe Avenue.

Besides the usual transportation concerns, Boulder exhibited a strong desire to preserve Boulder's quality of life by minimizing the negative impacts of auto traffic such as air pollution, congestion and noise. To preserve Boulder's quality of life, the city encouraged alternative transportation. Alternative modes of transportation encouraged included bicycling, walking and riding the bus or trolley. The city encouraged Boulder residents to use alternative transportation by holding annual events such as Bike to Work Day, No Drive Day and the Pedestrian Conference or by offering free bus rides. The city's Bike to Work Day enticed employees to ride their bicycle to work by offering free breakfast and prizes to participants. Eventually Boulder's Bike to Work Day increased to a week long celebration. Boulder first sponsored a week long bicycle celebration in 1984. Boulder Bike to Work Day fell in the middle of Boulder Bike Week. In 1990, over 3,300 people participated. The year 1986 marked the city government's first No Drive Day. That program aimed to reduce traffic congestion and air pollution by asking employees to use alternative transportation at least once a month. The second Wednesday of every month was selected for the No Drive Day.<sup>1</sup> The building of new bicycle and pedestrian paths like the Boulder Creek Path and the

---

<sup>1</sup>Camera, 16 August 1985 and 26 July 1990 (Bicycling file) and 12 June 1986 (Boulder Transportation & Traffic Engineering file).

Boulder Canyon Trail, also demonstrates Boulder's commitment to alternative transportation.

Sponsoring alternative transportation programs or building new pathways was not the only way Boulder showed its support for alternative transportation. Analysis of Boulder's Transportation Master Plan adopted in 1989 clearly shows just how committed Boulder was to alternative transportation and in maintaining the city's quality of life. An ad-hoc committee appointed by council back in 1986 developed the plan. Key points or goals of Boulder's 1989 Transportation Master Plan were as follows: to cut single-occupant car trips by 15 percent over the next 20 years, to reduce money spent on roads and increase funding for bicycles, pedestrians and mass transit, and to combat auto use by working with businesses to develop transportation alternatives for their employees. The transportation plan earmarked \$129 million to revamp the city's transportation system over the next 20 years. The plan emphasized pedestrian, bicycle and mass transit options over street improvements. To help finance transportation projects, the plan recommended imposing a new building tax on developers. The new development fee or tax was phased in over the next two years. To help oversee research and development of a mass transit system and to monitor the progress made towards the plan's goal to reduce auto trips by 15 percent, council appointed a five-member transportation committee or board. After five-years, the committee would cease to exist unless council decided to renew the committee. In addition to the transportation board, the city appointed Bob Whitson as Boulder's new alternative modes coordinator. This newly created post within the Boulder Transportation Division would lead the city in implementing the Transportation Master Plan.<sup>2</sup>

Another example of Boulder's commitment to alternative transportation can be seen

---

<sup>2</sup>Colorado Daily, 26 January 1989; Camera, 3 June 1988 and 15 June 1989; Colorado Daily, 24-26 November 1989.

in council's reactions to a metro-Denver transportation plan developed by the Metropolitan Transportation Development Commission. In November 1989, Boulder City Council was asked to comment on the metro-Denver plan developed by the commission. Overall, council members were displeased with the metro-Denver transportation plan. Council believed if the plan had been developed by the city of Boulder, it would be very different from the plan proposal presented. Council member Sally Martin criticized the plan for not only the absence of an air quality statement, but also for the lack of bikeway planning and high-occupancy automobile incentives. Not enough attention was given to reducing automobile travel. Other criticisms of the metro-Denver plan by council included the ignoring of land-use issues, the inequity in the financing scheme, and the emphasis on highway construction instead of mass transit.<sup>3</sup>

The city wanted to ensure Boulder's quality of life was preserved not only by encouraging alternative modes of transportation, but also by opposing new highway construction. A clear example of Boulder's opposition to new highway construction was city council's resistance to the construction of the W-470 beltway. The W-470 beltway plan entailed building a highway circling Denver to the north. The proposed beltway would link Interstate 70 near Golden with Interstate 25 near Lafayette. A portion of the beltway would run through Boulder County depending on which route was selected. Council wanted to keep the beltway out of Boulder County. On several occasions Boulder City Council voiced opposition towards the W-470 beltway construction. For instance, in February 1985, council wanted a more thorough investigation conducted on the proposed W-470 beltway. Council informally agreed to contribute up to \$20,000 for additional study on the W-470 beltway, particularly the environmental impact of the proposed beltway. Again in December 1987, council went on record opposing any alignment of the W-470 beltway on the western side of Rocky Flats. Such an alignment would affect city open space. Council saw the eastern

---

<sup>3</sup>Colorado Daily, 30 November 1989.

alignment as the lesser of two evils. In October 1988, council passed a resolution disapproving the western alignment. The views of council were passed on to the Denver Regional Council of Government.<sup>4</sup> In February 1989, voters in Boulder, Adams and Jefferson counties defeated a W-470 Authority proposal to charge a \$10 vehicle registration fee to fund the W-470 beltway construction. The proposal was defeated by a 4 to 1 margin. It was Boulder's opposition to the W-470 beltway that helped to scuttle the W-470 project. A scaled down version of the W-470 beltway called the Northwest Parkway resurfaced in October 1990, but that plan eventually died as well. The W-470 Authority disbanded in 1992. Talk of building a Northwest Parkway resurfaced in 1995-1996 and persists today, but as yet has not been built.<sup>5</sup> With this overview of Boulder's transportation history in mind, lets look in more detail at what transpired during this time frame--specifically, the parking and road improvements made between 1985 and 1990 and also the city's efforts to encourage alternative modes of transportation.

### **Parking**

In August 1985, Transportation Director Robert Whitson proposed to council a plan for a permit-parking system. Under the proposed permit-parking plan, residents could purchase an annual parking permit for a designated area in their neighborhood. Non-residents would be limited to two-hour parking within that designated permit-parking zone. Anyone parking without a permit for more than two hours was subject to fines. This idea for a permit-parking system was devised after Goss-Grove residents complained about the lack of parking in their neighborhood. Goss-Grove residents were always competing for parking with Boulder High School students and downtown employees. Tired of competing, Goss-

---

<sup>4</sup>Times-Call, February 6 1985; Camera, 16 December 1987; Colorado Daily, 6 October 1988.

<sup>5</sup>Times-Call, 9 February 1989 and 18 October 1990; Camera, 19 July 1992; Louisville Times, 10 July 1996; Camera, 24 January 2001.

Grove residents pushed for the city to devise a permit-parking system. The permit-parking system would not only solve the parking problem faced by Goss-Grove residents, but also encourage the use of alternative transportation by downtown employees and high school students. Before the city could implement the permit-parking system in an area or neighborhood, 60 percent of the residents affected by the parking system had to sign a petition. Not surprisingly, the permit-parking system received strong opposition from Boulder High School students and their parents. In order to accommodate both parties, the city developed an ordinance that would mandate only a 50 percent permit-parking zone. Only one side of the effected streets would require a permit. The other side of the street would allow two-hour parking between 9:00am and 5:00pm weekdays. The ordinance also stipulated that the city manager had the authority to increase a particular permit-parking zone. The Boulder Planning Board unanimously approved the permit-parking system in January 1986. Council approved the plan in May 1986.<sup>6</sup>

After pushing for the city to develop the permit-parking system, Goss-Grove residents were not granted a permit-parking zone in their neighborhood. Why? Goss-Grove residents failed to collect enough signatures for the petition as required by law. The law required 60 percent of the residents affected by the permit-parking zone to sign a petition in support of creating the permit-parking zone in their neighborhood. Apartment residents of the Goss-Grove neighborhood were reluctant to sign the petition because they did not want to pay for parking. On the other hand, homeowners in the Goss-Grove neighborhood unanimously supported the petition to establish permit-parking zone in their neighborhood, but it was not enough.<sup>7</sup>

---

<sup>6</sup>Boulder Courant, 30 October 1985 and Camera, 14 November 1985 and 17 January 1986 and 28 May 1986.

<sup>7</sup>Camera, 16 November 1986.

In the spirit of Christmas, the city offered downtown shoppers free parking on the remaining four Saturdays in December 1985. Some council members opposed the idea on the grounds that free parking discourages the use of alternative transportation and provides no financial returns for the city. The two council members opposing the free parking for holiday shoppers were Spense Havlick and Phil Stern. Downtown businesses and shoppers, on the other hand, appreciated the city's offer of free parking.<sup>8</sup>

In 1986, the city considered adopting a six-year parking plan to solve downtown parking problems. Major components of the parking plan were as follows: increasing the hourly metered parking rate 20 cents, converting parallel parking to angle parking on sections of Walnut and 16th Streets, expanding the RTD parking garage at the bus station located at 14th and Walnut Streets and building two new parking lots. The location site of the two new proposed parking lots were 15th and Pearl Streets and 11th and Spruce Streets. The parking plan added approximately 890 more parking spaces downtown. In April 1986, council unanimously approved every aspect of the \$14 million parking plan except for increasing the hourly metered rate 20 cents. Had council supported that portion of the plan, the financial burden put on downtown property owners would have been less. Downtown property owners already paid a 7-mill property tax to finance parking improvements. Council's actions increased that property tax burden. Since downtown property owners and businesses would bear the brunt of financing the parking improvements, it was decided that an election would be held to determine if downtown property owners supported a tax hike to finance the parking improvements. Council voted 6 to 3 in favor of holding that election on March 24, 1987. Council members that cast dissenting votes had concerns with the overall picture of the parking improvement plan, not the parking projects themselves. Concerns expressed by some council members included the problem of air pollution and traffic congestion downtown. Adding parking spaces would only increase those problems. Council

---

<sup>8</sup>Ibid., 4 December 1985.

members casting dissenting votes also felt that some of the parking projects warranted further study. For instance, the architectural plans for building the new parking structure at 11th and Spruce Streets had not even been approved yet and the possibility of having RTD help finance the expansion of the RTD Transit Center parking structure was never investigated. At the special election held in March, downtown property owners were asked to approve raising the tax levy from 7 mills to 18 mills. Two categories of voters were eligible to vote in the special election—downtown residential and business property owners and registered voters of the Central Area General Improvement District (CAGID). The weight of a person's vote depended on the property value or worth of that person's downtown property.<sup>9</sup>

Three downtown parking improvement projects appeared on the March ballot. Those three parking projects were as follows: constructing a parking structure at the intersection of 11th and Spruce Streets, adding additional decks to the RTD parking structure at 14th and Walnut Streets, and purchasing the 274-space Randolph Center parking structure at Walnut Street and Broadway. Total cost of this parking plan was \$10.7 million. The parking proposals on the March ballot were defeated by only three votes. The registered voters of the CAGID defeated the measure. The close vote prompted the city to propose a less costly downtown parking plan that same year. A second election was held July 28, 1987. The main differences between the March ballot parking proposal and the July ballot proposal, besides the price tag of the improvements, was the number of parking projects proposed. The city's refinancing or purchasing of the Randolph Center parking garage at Walnut Street and Broadway for \$855,000 was dropped from the July proposal. Also, the private developer, Siena/Randolph Group, was no longer involved in financing the proposed parking structure at 11th and Spruce Streets. The cost of the July parking proposal was approximately \$9.4

---

<sup>9</sup>Camera, 31 January 1986 and 23 April 1986; Colorado Daily, 22 January 1987; Camera, 23 March 1987.

million. Downtown property owners approved the \$9.4 million parking plan. Under this parking plan, approximately 550 parking spaces were to be added to the downtown area. Construction of the parking garage at 11th and Spruce Streets and the expansion improvements to the RTD garage was scheduled to get underway in 1988.<sup>10</sup>

However, in January 1988 some Boulder Planning Board members and Boulder City Council members began to question the necessity of the downtown parking projects. Both parking plans came into question, but more particularly the parking structure planned for 11th and Spruce Streets. The plan to add parking spaces contradicted the city's goal of wanting to curtail automobile usage and pollution. By adding more parking spaces, the problems of traffic congestion and pollution in the downtown area would undoubtedly increase. Downtown business owners, on the other hand, believed the parking projects were vital to their economic survival. Failure to provide adequate parking downtown would cost businesses potential customers. In the end, the views of downtown business owners prevailed. In February 1988, council voted 5 to 3 in favor of the 11th and Spruce Streets parking garage. Council members that voted against the parking project were Spense Havlick, Steve Pomerance and Matthew Appelbaum. The Boulder Planning Board followed suit and voted 5 to 4 in favor of the 11th and Spruce Streets parking project in March 1988. In voting for the parking project, the board urged the city to prepare a plan that would reduce the need for additional parking downtown. With the approval of council and the planning board, construction of the two downtown parking projects went ahead as planned.<sup>11</sup>

The city hired Ringland Johnson Crowley of Boulder as the general contractor for both parking projects. The expansion of the RTD Transit Center parking was completed in

---

<sup>10</sup>Camera, 23, 25 March 1987 and 20 May 1987 and 26 July 1987 and Boulder Courier, 30 July 1987.

<sup>11</sup>Camera, 17 January 1988; Colorado Daily, 19 January 1988; Camera, 10 February 1988 and 4 March 1988.

late October 1988. The expansion provided 203 additional parking spaces. The parking garage at 11th and Spruce Streets was delayed until after the 1988 holiday season so not to hinder Christmas shoppers. Construction of the six-level, 390-space parking garage at 11th and Spruce Streets started in January 1989. The parking structure included office and retail space. The Boulder architectural firm, Downing, Thorpe & James Inc. designed the structure. During construction at 11th and Spruce Streets, five underground gasoline tanks were discovered or unearthed in January 1989. Gasoline from the tanks had leaked into the soil. Before construction could proceed, the tainted soil had to be removed as required by law. The cost of removing the gasoline tanks and tainted soil was \$250,000. Cleanup costs was paid from the project's contingency fund. Construction delay of the parking structure was minimal. Construction was only five weeks behind schedule. The new six-level parking garage and retail space at 11th and Spruce Streets opened in November 1989. The new parking facility increased the number of public off-street parking spaces by more than 50 percent.<sup>12</sup>

Coinciding with the development of the 11th and Spruce Streets parking facility and the expansion of RTD Transit Center's parking structure, the city closed the parking lot known as the "People's Parking Lot" in November 1988. For seventeen years the People's Parking Lot, located at the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard, provided free parking to the public. The lot was closed in preparation of a change in ownership. Developer Sina Simantob intended to purchase the land from Glacier Park Co., a subsidiary of Burlington Northern Railroad Company. Simantob planned to build a hotel and conference center at that site. A chain-link fence was placed around the old, unpaved parking lot in November 1988 in preparation for the transfer of ownership. Until construction of the hotel got underway, Simantob offered to lease the property back to the city for parking for \$1 per

---

<sup>12</sup>Camera, 6 January 1989 and 15 February 1989 and 16 March 1989 (Environmental Protection file) and 7 November 1989.

year. The city considered Simantob's offer and had every intention to grade the lot and reopen it as a pay lot. However, in the end the city chose not to reopen it as a pay lot. Why? Converting the lot was too complicated and expensive. The city would not only have to meet permit requirements and devise a plan that would be approved by the planning department, but also landscape the area and pave the lot. Complicating things further was the fact that a portion of the 1.7 acre lot was located in the Boulder Creek floodway, which meant the city would have to acquire a floodway permit before it could reopen the lot as a paved pay lot. This was not the first time the city had considered converting the People's free parking lot into metered or permit parking. The city had considered the idea once before back in 1986, but the city never carried out those plans in 1986. Those same complications stopping the city in 1988 from turning the lot into a paid parking lot also made the city reconsider its conversion plans in 1986. The costs to convert the People's lot just exceeded the city's plans. With the city abandoning its conversion plans, Simantob was willing to reopen the gates to the parking lot and offer free parking as soon as the land transfer was final. However, the land transfer to Simantob never was finalized. Simantob failed to come up with the finances needed to purchase the land and develop the hotel at that site himself. The People's Parking Lot remained closed; it was just a vacant lot.<sup>13</sup> (For additional details of the hotel development planned for the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard see Chapter 4.)

A plan to instigate uniform permit parking rates for downtown was proposed by the Downtown Management Commission in October 1989. Under the proposed plan, permits for covered parking structures would cost \$110 a quarter and permits for uncovered surface parking lots would cost \$60 a quarter. Overall, permit parking would increase by 10 percent according to the proposal. If approved by city council, the new rates were to go in effect

---

<sup>13</sup>Camera, 10, 24 November 1988 and 3, 15 December 1988 and Colorado Daily, 20 January 1994.

January 1, 1990. Council approved the increase in parking permits on October 4, 1989.<sup>14</sup> Raising permit parking rates for the downtown area was not the only parking increase instigated. Metered parking and the hourly rates for short-term parking lots also increased. The idea to increase the cost of downtown parking fees was first considered in February 1990. The parking fee increase would help finance maintenance and operation of downtown parking facilities and support alternative transportation programs like free bus passes for downtown employees. Council approved raising the downtown parking rates for metered and short term parking in July 1990. Two council members cast dissenting votes for the parking rate increases—Steve Pomerance and Matthew Appelbaum. Metered parking rates increased from 30 cents to 50 cents an hour and short term parking rates increased from 25 cents to 35 cents an hour for the first three hours. The new rates went into effect in October.<sup>15</sup>

### **Highways, Streets and Roads**

In April 1985, an agreement between the city of Boulder and the State Highway Commission was approved, whereby Foothills Parkway would be completed by 1988. Boulder agreed to pay \$4.2 million over the next three years, while the state agreed to increase its funding from \$8.5 million to \$12.2 million. State funds for other highway projects were to be diverted and used towards the Foothills project so the Foothills Parkway could be completed by 1988. Once the Foothills Parkway were completed, the state money diverted to that project would be returned to complete other highway projects. Both the city of Boulder and the state of Colorado were anxious to complete the Foothills Parkway project. Plans for the Foothills Parkway project date back over twenty years and construction of the project had been underway since the early 1970s. It was time the project got

---

<sup>14</sup>Camera, 3 October 1989 and Colorado Daily, 5 October 1989.

<sup>15</sup>Camera, 4 February 1990 and 10, 11 July 1990.

completed.

Work on the Foothills Parkway progressed. A new stretch of the parkway between Pearl Street and Valmont Road opened in May 1986. However, several elements of the construction project still remained. Those elements remaining included building a road embankment and drainage system, building three bridges, paving the parkway north from Valmont Road and installing road signs and signals. The 1988 completion date of the Foothills Parkway came in question briefly in 1986 when federal financing was nearly cut. However, by 1987 the threat of federal cuts dissipated. Congress had over rode President Reagan's veto of a federal highway bill that would of decreased federal funding. Federal funds for the Foothills Parkway project were made available again thanks to Congress. Completion date of the project was back on schedule.<sup>16</sup>

Building the last segment of the Foothills Parkway got underway in August 1987. The final segment of the highway project involved building three bridges and paving the road. Lawrence Construction Company was awarded the contract to build the three highway bridges. Location points of the three bridges were as follows: 47th Street, Diagonal Highway and Four Mile Creek, and over the Burlington-Northern Railroad tracks south of Iris. During the final phase of the Foothills Parkway construction project, free-standing, unbraced, 45-ton concrete girders fell over as a result of very strong winds. Despite this mishap, work on the Foothills Parkway project was not delayed. However, as a result of this accident it became standard practice to brace free-standing girders to prevent other accidents.<sup>17</sup>

After nearly 17 years, construction of the Foothills Parkway was completed in August 1988. The city held a ribbon cutting ceremony on August 26, 1988. City Manager

---

<sup>16</sup>Ibid., 19 April 1985 and 8 June 1987.

<sup>17</sup>Boulder Courier, 20 August 1987 and Camera, 27 January 1988 and 9 March 1988.

Jim Piper cut the ribbon and Mayor Linda Jourgensen drove the first car across the completed Foothills Parkway. Mayor Jourgensen's drive across the Parkway marked the opening of the northbound lanes of the Foothills Parkway. The southbound lanes had opened about two weeks earlier. The five-mile, four-lane Foothills Parkway project cost \$38.77 million. Most of the money for the project came from both the state and federal governments. The city of Boulder contributed approximately \$5 million towards the project, plus some right-of way land. Boulder County donated \$1 million towards the Foothills Parkway project. The Foothills Parkway connects the Boulder-Denver Turnpike and the Diagonal Highway.<sup>18</sup>

In addition to completing Foothills Parkway, several other road improvements were undertaken by the city during this time. In 1986, the Boulder Transportation Department planned to make intersection improvements at Baseline Road and Broadway. According to the Boulder Police Department, that intersection at Baseline Road and Broadway was the third most dangerous intersection in the city. The transportation department's improvement plans called for the following: eliminating the left-turn lane from westbound Baseline Road onto southbound Broadway, relocating the right-turn lane for motorists driving west on Baseline Road turning north onto Broadway, enlarging the right-turn lane for motorists heading east on Baseline Road and turning south on Broadway and creating two lanes of westbound traffic on Baseline that would merge back into one lane west of Broadway. In April 1986, the transportation department's plans to rebuild the intersection of Baseline Road and Broadway sparked some debate among council members. Council questioned one aspect of the transportation department's plan—the idea to create two lanes of westbound traffic on Baseline Road and then having those two lanes narrow and merge into one. The Boulder Planning Board was asked to review the transportation department's plans. The Boulder Planning Board endorsed the plan. Work on improving the intersection of Baseline Road and

---

<sup>18</sup>Camera, 25, 27 August 1988.

Broadway was to get underway in May 1986.

However, before work could begin, a sculpture located near the intersection had to be moved. The sculpture was by the artist Kim Fields. It was placed at that intersection in 1974 as part of Boulder's public art program. Since its placement there, the sculpture had sparked controversy. The Boulder Arts Commission was assigned the task of deciding what to do with the sculpture. The Boulder Arts Commission decided to have the sculpture moved to the Valmont Road entrance of the Boulder Municipal Airport. RG Ironworks was hired to move Fields' sculpture, plus another flower sculpture, which mysteriously appeared at that same intersection in the spring. With the sculptures removed, work on redesigning the intersection got started. Structures Unlimited of Denver was hired to redesign the intersection at Baseline Road and Broadway. By September 17, 1986, the right-turn lane from Baseline Road onto northbound Broadway was open to traffic. Other aspects of the intersection improvements, like widening Baseline Road west of Broadway into two lanes for a short distance and prohibiting westbound traffic on Baseline Road from turning left onto southbound Broadway, was nearly complete as well.<sup>19</sup>

In 1986, the Colorado Highway Department conducted an inspection of all bridges in the state. The state rated bridges throughout Colorado for sufficiency and load carrying capacity. Two of Boulder's bridges received poor ratings. The state ranked the bridge on Arapahoe Avenue over Boulder Creek between Broadway and 13th Street as the second worst bridge in Boulder. The worst bridge in Boulder was located along west Arapahoe near the mouth of the canyon. The city had plans to replace both bridges. The second worst bridge in Boulder was 63 years old and therefore needed to be replaced. The railings of the bridge were bad and the sidewalks were unsafe. As a result of the rating, the city received a federal and state grant of \$822,000 to replace the bridge. However, before the city could

---

<sup>19</sup>Ibid., 17, 19 April 1986 and 22 August 1986 and 17 September 1986.

replace the old bridge, Domino's Pizza located at 1230 Arapahoe Avenue would have to move. The Domino's foundation touched the foundation of the old bridge. There was no way to replace the foundation of the bridge without affecting the Domino's building. The new bridge would be 30-feet longer than the old and Arapahoe would be widened to make room for sidewalks and bike lanes. The longer bridge would increase the waterway opening and make it more attractive to traffic traveling on the bike path, which runs under the bridge. With the land vacated by Domino's Pizza, the city intended to expand the city park adjacent to the building. However, neither the property owner Jim Martin or Domino's business owner Joe Romano wanted to move or sell. That current location was key to Domino's strategy of delivering pizza any where in Boulder within 30 minutes. Nevertheless, the city forced Domino's to move from its location of 1230 Arapahoe Avenue. In November 1987, council passed an ordinance to acquire the Domino's property however it could. Work on building the new bridge began in May 1988. Arapahoe Avenue was closed between Broadway and 13th Street. The new bridge was completed and opened on July 30, 1988.<sup>20</sup>

During this same time period, efforts to improve the bridge ranked the worst in Boulder were taking place. However, the city's plans to replace the bridge encountered opposition from west Arapahoe residents. Instead of replacing the bridge, west Arapahoe residents wanted the bridge closed to motorists. In April 1987, many residents of west Arapahoe Avenue petitioned city officials to close the west end of Arapahoe Avenue to prevent motorists from using that street as a shortcut. Instead, residents wished to create a park and pedestrian bridge at the west end of the street, where Arapahoe heads toward the canyon. Motorists would be forced to use Canyon Boulevard, which was designed to carry heavy traffic, and not Arapahoe Avenue as a shortcut to and from the canyon. The idea to close Arapahoe at the canyon's mouth was endorsed by the Open Space Board of Trustees,

---

<sup>20</sup>Camera, 22 July 1987 and 8 August 1987; Colorado Daily, 12 November 1987; Camera, 7 May 1988 and 30 July 1988.

but approval was still needed by the planning board and city council. The plan to close the west end of Arapahoe was not well received by all. Some mountain residents located up the canyon were opposed, as were some business owners located along west Arapahoe Avenue. The Boulder Fire Department also questioned the closure, particularly how the response time would be affected by the possible road closure. Opposition to the plan led the city to propose a compromise. In September 1987, Boulder transportation planners proposed the following: replacing the west Arapahoe traffic bridge with a bridge for pedestrians and bicyclists, and building a new traffic bridge across from the Silver Saddle Motel located at 90 Arapahoe. This proposal not only prevented motorists from having a straight shot onto Arapahoe Avenue from the canyon, but also still allowed the public access to businesses located on Arapahoe. West Arapahoe residents were unsatisfied with the proposal.<sup>21</sup>

In November 1987, the Boulder Planning Board approved 4 to 3 a revised city plan to restrict vehicle traffic along a stretch of west Arapahoe Avenue. Besides replacing the existing bridge with a pedestrian bridge and building a new vehicle bridge near the Silver Saddle Motel, the plan called for extending Boulder Creek Path west from Eben G. Fine Park to the mouth of the canyon. The proposed vehicle bridge would require motorists to make a sharp turn to get from Canyon Boulevard to Arapahoe Avenue and therefore would discourage motorists from turning onto Arapahoe. City transportation planners estimated this curve in the road would decrease Arapahoe neighborhood traffic by 40 percent. The city's idea to place medians in the center of the street to slow traffic was rejected by the board. Residents also felt the medians would not work and refused to pay for their installation. The cost of building a new bridge was estimated at about \$600,000. The city received a \$482,000 state grant to finance the new bridge. Who should finance the remaining \$118,000 balance to build the new vehicle bridge was undecided. Until that was determined, construction of the new bridge was delayed. The delay also allowed the city's Ad-Hoc

---

<sup>21</sup>Camera, 24 April 1987 and 21 September 1987.

Transportation Committee time to determine if the new vehicle bridge was really needed. In February 1988, transportation officials concluded that the new vehicle bridge should be built. However, they recommended the city should hold off making other changes in the west Arapahoe neighborhood until they completed a character study on how west Arapahoe effects the entire Boulder transportation system.<sup>22</sup>

In April 1988, council voted 9 to 0 in favor of the West Arapahoe Transportation Project. All elements of the plan approved by council that April included replacing the old vehicle bridge with a pedestrian bridge, building a new vehicle bridge a quarter of a mile eastward from the old one, installing traffic circles on Arapahoe at both ends of a planned one-way section of Arapahoe in front of Eben G. Fine Park and building a pedestrian/bicycle underpass beneath Canyon Boulevard in order to extend the Boulder Creek Path. The project was to be financed by state bridge and trails grants, the city Transportation Fund and the Boulder Creek Fund. Construction was scheduled to begin in the fall. The bike path was to be built first, then the new vehicle bridge. Overseeing construction of the \$1 million project for the city was Jim Gendill. In March 1989, the bridge project was plagued by budget woes. Work on replacing the vehicle bridge was to start last month, but the bids by contractors exceeded the project budget of \$900,000 by almost \$300,000. All bids were refused by the city. Work on the project came to a halt while the city searched for ways to reduce the project's costs.<sup>23</sup>

Plans for the West Arapahoe Improvement Project were modified. Plans for the new vehicle bridge were redesigned as were the plans for the pedestrian/bicycle underpass beneath Canyon Boulevard. The underpass and path were reconfigured to improve safety and path connection. The underpass and path would cross over to the north side of Farmer's

---

<sup>22</sup>Boulder Courier, 19 November 1987 and Colorado Daily, 26 February 1988.

<sup>23</sup>Camera, 6 April 1988 and 19 September 1988 and Colorado Daily, 21 March 1989.

Ditch, north of Canyon Boulevard. The path would continue through property owned by the Boulder Housing Authority and through Settlers Park and then ramp down to Pearl Street east of Settlers Park parking lot. These modifications helped reduce the cost of the project. The city also requested and was granted additional bridge replacement funds from the State Highway Department. New bids were taken on the project. Nicholas Construction of Lakewood submitted the lowest bid. The low bid was less than estimated and within the revised project budget. The West Arapahoe Improvement Project was completed in 1990. A celebration marking the completion of the improvements to west Arapahoe was held June 29, 1990. The celebration included a dedication ceremony at Settlers Park, a ribbon cutting ceremony at the west end of Eben G. Fine Park along with a three-mile round trip tour of the new Boulder Canyon Trail and a west Arapahoe neighborhood block party.<sup>24</sup>

The intersection of Broadway and College Avenue was considered one of Boulder's most dangerous crossings. To provide a safer intersection, the Boulder Transportation Department intended to build an overpass or underpass at that intersection. The city's plans to develop either an underpass or overpass at the intersection of Broadway and College Avenue proceeded in February 1990. The project was to be funded jointly by the city, CU and the highway department. The city's 1991 budget devoted \$400,000 for the project. The relative merits of an underpass or overpass were still being weighed by the city, but the underpass was the favored choice by the city. The city's lean towards the building the underpass sparked debate. In fact, a controversy ensued over whether to build an underpass or an overpass. University Hill merchants worried an underpass would divert potential business away. Hill merchants were not against making the intersection of Broadway and College Avenue safer, but against the city's suggestion of an underpass to improve that

---

<sup>24</sup>West Arapahoe Improvement Project newsletter #5, 20 July 1989 and Camera, 29 June 1990.

intersection.<sup>25</sup>

Over the next several months the matter was debated. As late as September 1990, no final decision had been made. Design plans for both options were presented to the public late September. Both the city and the University of Colorado contributed to the design plans presented. The city's continued focus on an underpass drew criticism and opposition once again from Hill merchants. A letter of protest containing 80 signatures was sent to city council. In an attempt to address the public's concerns, Cindy Jatcko, a city transportation official, presented and discussed the city's proposals to a group of concerned citizens in November 1990. The attempt to squash merchants' concerns failed. As a result, the project was postponed. A new committee was to be organized to study the long-term plans for the Broadway corridor in 1991.<sup>26</sup> After some debate and study, the city's plan to build an underpass at Broadway and College Avenue prevailed. In February 1992, work crews began relocating utility lines and a drainage ditch in preparation for the construction of the underpass that was to start in May 1992. The pedestrian underpass at Broadway and College Avenue was to be completed by August 15, 1992, before students returned to the university.<sup>27</sup>

Another city road improvement effort that received public criticism was the city's plans to widen Table Mesa Drive. The city wanted to improve a six-tenths-of-a-mile section of Table Mesa Drive not only because of the growing traffic congestion there, but also because of the increasing accident rate along that stretch of Table Mesa Drive. The city also wanted to encourage alternative transportation by installing bike lanes, which Table Mesa

---

<sup>25</sup>Colorado Daily, 13 February 1990 and 23 August 1990.

<sup>26</sup>Camera, 25, 26 September 1990 and Colorado Daily, 9-11 November 1990 and 14-16 December 1990.

<sup>27</sup>Colorado Daily, 3 October 1991 and Camera, 4, 22 February 1992 and 17 May 1992.

Drive lacked. Plans to expand or widen Table Mesa Drive originated in 1985-1986. A 1985 South Boulder Traffic Study had recommended the city expand Table Mesa Drive by adding bike lanes and left-turning lanes for east and west bound traffic and right-turning lanes for north bound traffic on South Broadway. To carry out those widening plans for Table Mesa Drive, the study suggested demolishing some houses along that street. Initially, both the Boulder Planning Board and Boulder City Council approved the plan to widen Table Mesa Drive, but other road projects and commitments delayed the city from taking any further action on those recommendations until 1990.<sup>28</sup>

In the fall of 1990, the city was considering several options on how to expand Table Mesa Drive. Those options were presented to south Boulder residents in November 1990. One of the options under consideration entailed creating a boulevard by razing 14 to 18 homes along Table Mesa Drive east from Broadway, just past Moorhead Avenue, to Highway 36 exit, a six-tenths-of-a-mile section of Table Mesa Drive. The actual number of homes razed depended on which side—north or south—the city chose to expand Table Mesa Drive. The north side expansion would require 14 houses to be razed, while the south side would destroy 18 houses. Not surprisingly, some of those residents objected to the city razing their homes. Residents agreed there were traffic problems on that section of Table Mesa Drive including rush-hour traffic jams and numerous accidents, but many residents did not think tearing down their homes should be part of the solution. Other options presented that November included restriping the road so that it includes one westbound lane and one eastbound lane with a middle lane for cars turning left; expanding the road within the existing right of way to include bike lanes and a left-turn lane; and diverting traffic from Table Mesa Drive all together by building the controversial foothills extension that would connect Foothills Parkway with south Broadway. Residents were split over the options presented at that November meeting. The only consensus reached at that meeting was that

---

<sup>28</sup>Camera, 2 May 1985 and 21 June 1985 and 28 December 1987.

the city should decide a course of action soon. The Boulder Transportation Department was scheduled to present a preliminary proposal to city council on December 18, 1990, but that presentation was pushed back until January 8, 1991. South Boulder residents were dismayed over the delay of a decision. The fate of their homes was resting on that decision, particularly if the city decided on the boulevard proposal option. It was not until April 1991, that city council decided to abandon the boulevard proposal option for expanding Table Mesa Drive. Homes would not be demolished to solve the heavy traffic congestion and high accident rate along Table Mesa Drive between Broadway and Tantra Drive. However, just what the city would do to resolve those problems remained undecided now that the boulevard proposal was no longer an option. The only thing council was certain about was that the improvements made to Table Mesa Drive would include bike lanes and that the road would remain four-lane.<sup>29</sup>

### **Public Transit**

To encourage alternative transportation and to alleviate traffic congestion on Broadway, Regional Transportation District (RTD) offered free bus rides on bus routes 202 and 204. The free bus service on these two routes was offered January 1 through September 1985. The city of Boulder and RTD split the cost of providing the free bus service. In just the first two weeks, the number of riders on routes 202 and 204 was up by 60 percent. Further analysis of the free bus ride program in March 1985 revealed a continued increase in the number of people riding the bus, but the amount of automobile traffic on Broadway had not decreased as expected. In fact, it may have even increased over last year by 3 1/2 percent. The city was hoping for at least a 10 percent decrease in the number of cars

---

<sup>29</sup>Colorado Daily, 31 October 1990; 16 November 1990; 6 December 1990; 3, 5 March 1992.

traveling on Broadway.<sup>30</sup>

In April 1985, additional efforts to encourage the use of alternative transportation were undertaken. The Regional Transportation District proposed changes in Boulder's bus service to city council. Some of the proposed changes in bus service RTD presented to council were as follows: more frequent bus service on Arapahoe Avenue, Broadway and Baseline Road; streamlining route 205 service to Gunbarrel by cutting travel time 12 minutes; extending route 206 to Table Mesa Park-n-Ride; improving service between Crossroads Shopping Center, Downtown and the University of Colorado; and offering a new bus route from Boulder to downtown Denver. The aim of these changes was to provide faster, more frequent service between key places of activity in Boulder by making the bus routes simpler. By making the bus more convenient, the public would be more inclined to ride the bus. Council along with the Boulder Planning Board granted initial approval to the streamline changes in bus service. These streamline changes addressed the major public complaints and suggestions presented to RTD back in February 1985. At that time, Boulder residents and Planning Board members were given the opportunity to voice their opinion on RTD bus service. Complaints voiced centered on the fact that riding the bus was not enjoyable or fun because the buses were too big, too impersonal, too expensive, too noisy and too smelly. Suggestions to improve RTD service included more frequent service on major routes, offering free rides and using smaller buses. The public also suggested the city offer a shuttle service between Crossroads Mall, the University of Colorado and Downtown.<sup>31</sup>

Over the next several years, RTD attempted to implement the public's suggestions. In late August 1985, RTD started two new regional bus routes—the J-Gunbarrel/Denver via

---

<sup>30</sup>Camera, 13 January 1985 and 2 March 1985 and 22 April 1985.

<sup>31</sup>Ibid., 22, 24 April 1985 and 22 February 1985.

Foothills and the T-Boulder/Greenwood Plaza via Foothills. To promote these two new bus routes, RTD offered free rides through September 14 on these two new bus routes and on all other local routes as well. After September 14, 1985, the cost of riding these new routes was \$1.75 each way. To help finance other public suggestions for improving bus service, RTD increased bus fares in June 1987. This bus fare increase was the first increase since 1981. The biggest change in fares was that the off-peak hours fare of 35 cents was discontinued. Instead, a flat rate of 50 cents was charged for all local bus routes. The fare increase provided RTD with approximately \$2.4 million in additional funds. Starting in November 1987, the cost of a monthly RTD bus pass for seniors and disabled people was lowered. In Boulder, the price of a monthly bus pass changed from \$14.50 to \$12.00 for seniors and disabled people.<sup>32</sup>

New state legislation passed by the Colorado Legislature in 1988 required RTD to contract out 20 percent of its service to private companies by March 31, 1989. The new law "...was designed to introduce competition into the publicly supported transit system and make it more cost efficient."<sup>33</sup> Interested companies bidding for the contract were selected by RTD. RTD's selection process entailed more than just selecting the company with the lowest bid. RTD also wished to select a company that met not only RTD's safety and training standards, but also one that could accommodate the passengers of routes selected for privatization. The contract was awarded to Mayflower Contract Services of Kansas. A three-year contract for \$17.9 million was signed in December 1988. Mayflower also kept all the fares it collected. RTD retained the option to renew the contract each year. RTD routes farmed out to Mayflower for privatization were low-ridership and high-cost routes. Mayflower Contract Services would operate 18 routes, nine of which were local for RTD.

---

<sup>32</sup>Ibid., 30 August 1985 and 2 June 1987 and 12 October 1987.

<sup>33</sup>Ibid., 17 October 1988.

Once familiarized with Boulder routes, Mayflower Contract Services took over seven local routes in June 1989. Additional routes were turned over to Mayflower in the fall. Routes serviced by Mayflower Contract Services included the 200, 201, 202, 203, 204, 205, 206, 207, 208 and the 209. Mayflower utilized new smaller buses that were approximately 10 feet shorter and shaped more like schoolbuses. These smaller buses were painted the same colors and insignias as RTD's buses and the drivers wore the same RTD uniform, hence many passengers never noticed a change with the privatization.<sup>34</sup>

In April 1989, Colorado was awarded \$18.2 million for mass transit projects from the federal Urban Mass Transportation Administration. Cities all along the Front Range benefited from the grant money. In Boulder, the funds awarded to RTD were used to expand the Table Mesa Park-n-Ride facility. The grant money provided 80 percent of the necessary funds needed to expand the Table Mesa Park-n-Ride. The design plans, which would double the parking capacity of the Park-n-Ride, were still in the developmental stages in July 1989. The preliminary plans favored by RTD proposed building a parking garage over the air space above the Boulder-Denver Turnpike adjacent to the old Table Mesa Park-n-Ride. By building over the air space above the turnpike, existing parking would be less disrupted than building a new parking deck over the current lot. Not surprisingly, opposition to RTD's design plan of building over the Boulder-Denver Turnpike arose. As a result, the idea to build over the turnpike was abandoned by RTD. Instead, RTD decided to build a \$3.2 million multi-level parking garage over the current Park-n-Ride site. The new plan proposal included building a 2 1/2 story parking garage, creating a separate lane for buses to unload passengers and providing a 4-lane exit for automobiles. Construction of the Table Mesa Park-n-Ride expansion project was to get underway in the spring of 1991. The new parking structure would increase the number of parking spaces to about 500. Before construction improvements, the Table Mesa Park-n-Ride had approximately 300 parking spaces. During

---

<sup>34</sup>Ibid., 17 October 1988; 20 December 1988; 17 October 1989; 11 June 1989.

construction of the new parking structure, an empty lot at the intersection of South Boulder Road and South Boulder Circle was converted into a temporary parking lot for commuters. Commuters also utilized the parking lot of the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter Day Saints for parking. This expansion of the Table Mesa Park-n-Ride was the first of several construction improvements planned by RTD to increase the number of parking spaces at that park-n-ride facility.<sup>35</sup>

In July 1989, RTD announced the coming arrival of 15 new high-tech buses to Boulder, five of which would be powered by compressed natural gas. The five buses that would be powered by compressed natural gas were apart of RTD's alternative fuels program. The cost of each new bus was approximately \$85,000. The new 24-seater buses were to hit Boulder's streets late in 1989 or early 1990. The new buses would replace a portion of RTD's fleet, particularly those buses that were 11-year old buses. In Boulder, RTD maintained a fleet of about 25 buses, not including the privately operated buses by Mayflower Contract Services. The new buses to be delivered to Boulder were manufactured by a Houston based company. The new high-tech buses were about two-feet shorter than the buses they replaced.<sup>36</sup> That same year, RTD had no choice but to increase bus fares again. The state Legislature's demand for more revenue prompted the fare increase. Under a new pending transportation bill, RTD would have to recover 30 percent of its costs from fares. Other factors in the price hike included rising fuel costs and paying employees higher wages. Local fares for riding the bus increased 10 cents. The cost of one-way local bus fare changed from 50 cents to 60 cents. The new rate went into effect September 1, 1989. Rates for seniors and disabled people also increased. Seniors saw a 5 cent increase in cash bus fare, while disabled people saw a 15 cent increase. Commuters traveling on regional routes from Boulder to

---

<sup>35</sup>Ibid., 5 April 1989; 27 July 1989; 2 March 1990; 18 May 1991.

<sup>36</sup>Ibid., 27 July 1989.

Denver were also subjected to a fare increase. The increase for regional travelers was 50 cents more each way. A one-way ticket would now cost \$2.50 instead of \$2.00. However, to help cut the cost of rising bus fares RTD introduced a new "FareSaver" program. The purchase of a FareSaver 10-ride ticket book for \$4.75 saved commuters 30 percent over the single-trip cash fares. The ticket books were sold at RTD stations and local grocery stores.<sup>37</sup>

To further encourage the use of alternative transportation and to ease traffic and parking congestion, a new program, which would allow downtown employees free bus rides, was launched in July 1989. Initiated by the Downtown Management Commission, the pilot program offered downtown employees, including city and county employees, free access to RTD buses anywhere in Boulder until the end of the year. An employee could ride the bus for free after obtaining a photo identification card. The program was scheduled to run till the end of the year, but the popularity of the program prompted Bob Whitson, Boulder's Alternative Modes Coordinator, to extend the program funding for another month. The pilot program increased ridership on RTD buses by 7.7 percent. Plans to continue the program with city government funding was strongly being considered in 1990.<sup>38</sup> In August 1990, the city of Boulder announced its plan to offer all 900 permanent city employees a free pass on any RTD bus route in the metro area starting in September. The estimated cost was \$22 per employee. In November 1990, the city of Boulder decided to expand its free buss-pass program to employees of non-downtown businesses as well. Before, the free bus ride program was only offered to employees of downtown Boulder businesses. The new free bus-pass program was entitled "Mobility Pass Program." Business owners interested in the program would contribute funding to supply their employees with unlimited free bus rides on RTD buses anywhere in Boulder County and also to Denver and Stapleton Airport. The

---

<sup>37</sup>Ibid., 4 March 1989 and 7 May 1989 and 31 August 1989.

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 25 July 1989; Colorado Daily, 5 January 1990; Denver Post, 12 January 1990.

new program was scheduled to start in January 1991.<sup>39</sup>

In another attempt to encourage alternative transportation and to make it more convenient, the city worked with RTD on a plan to place bicycle racks on buses. Although RTD was in favor of placing bicycle racks on buses, RTD had some concerns. Concerns expressed by RTD included how to secure the bicycles from theft and how to minimize adding extra footage to the buses with the racks. Using locks to secure bicycles could cause schedule delays, while placing bicycle racks on buses would lengthen the buses by about two-feet, which could result in parking problems at the 14th Street bus station. The city acknowledged RTD's concerns and worked on resolving those concerns. The design plans were still being worked out in April 1990. The city was working on design ideas with Roaring Fork Transit of Aspen, which utilized bicycle racks on the front of their buses. The cost of placing the bicycle racks on RTD buses was estimated to be less than a \$1000 for installing 18 racks. The Alternative Modes of Transportation division of Boulder's Transportation Department planned to finance the project. Placing bicycle racks on buses was not the only way ridership on buses was encouraged. The Boulder RTD station, located at 14th and Walnut Streets, was remodeled, making the bus terminal more pleasant for commuters. Changes to the bus terminal included adding 20 bicycle lockers, a snack concession counter and offering a larger and brighter lobby along with expanded parking for commuters. The newly remodeled RTD bus station opened in February 1990. Commuting to Denver became easier in December 1990. The Regional Transportation District started a new bus route that traveled between Foothills Park-n-Ride in Boulder and Market Street Station in downtown Denver during morning and evening rush hours. The new route offered was Route F.<sup>40</sup>

---

<sup>39</sup>Rocky Mountain News, 19 August 1990 and Camera, 13 November 1990.

Riding the bus was not the only mass transit offered in Boulder during this time frame—1985 to 1990. You will recall how the public suggested that the city offer a shuttle system between Crossroads Mall and Downtown and the university campus at a public meeting evaluating the services of RTD back in February 1985. The city followed the advise of the public that spring. In May 1985, the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority (BURA) hired CRS Sirrinc Co. of Denver to conduct a feasibility study for a shuttle system. The study was to access a shuttle system between Crossroads Mall, Downtown and the University of Colorado campus. At first, the study was also going to analyze a shuttle system from remote parking lots to the downtown area, but that part of the study was dropped. BURA further scaled down the study to gauge only a shuttle system between Crossroads Mall and Downtown. When BURA decided to scale down the study, BURA asked CRS Sirrinc Co. to propose a lower bid than the original bid of \$28,000 to conduct the study. The Denver consulting firm proposed a bid of \$26,000 for the scaled-down study. Based on the new bid proposal and the fact that BURA was disappointed with the quality of work done so far on the study, Authority Board members dismissed CRS Sirrinc Co. consulting firm in July 1985. The CRS Sirrinc Co. was paid \$3,000 for work completed on the first phase of the feasibility study. Boulder's Transportation Department was to devise a way for the city to continue the study.<sup>41</sup>

In March 1986, Boulder Planning Director Ed Gawf proposed that merchant store owners and the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority split the cost of a shuttle system trial-run. Instead of a shuttle system between Crossroads Mall and Downtown, this trial-run shuttle system proposed having one or two trolley cars transport shoppers back and forth from the various shopping centers in Boulder including the Crossroads Mall, Target, Marshall's Plaza,

---

<sup>40</sup>Colorado Daily, 4 April 1990 and 15 February 1990 and Camera, 8 December 1990.

<sup>41</sup>Camera, 23 May 1985 and 20 June 1985 (BURA file) and 19 July 1985 (Boulder Transportation & Traffic Engineering file).

The Village Shopping Center and the Arapahoe Village Shopping Center. The trial-run was to take place that summer. Pat Kitowski, owner of Tivoli Trolleys, volunteered his trolleys for the shuttle trial-run. However, the plans to test the shuttle system never materialized the summer of 1986. Negotiations with merchant store owners were not completed in time to offer the trolley shuttle that summer. With negotiations unresolved, Pat Kitowski decided to use his trolleys for his routes in Denver instead.<sup>42</sup>

The following summer the trial-run trolley shuttle finally materialized. The new free trolley shuttle system was implemented on June 13, 1987 for a three month trial period. Funding for the shuttle system was paid for in part by BURA, city council and the four shopping centers—Crossroads Mall, Marshall's Plaza, The Village and Arapahoe Village. The trolley owner, Pat Kitowski, financed the remaining cost of the trial shuttle system, approximately 40 percent, by selling advertisement space on the trolley. Kitowski received \$27,500, approximately 60 percent of the actual cost of running the trolley for the three month trial-run. The four shopping centers pitched in \$1,000 each, the city \$10,000 and BURA contributed \$13,500. BURA's contribution to help finance the trial-run trolley shuttle was given with the understanding that they did not intend to keep subsidizing the trolley shuttle every year. BURA was not interested in entering into the transportation business on a permanent basis. With the financing needed to run the trolley shuttle in place, the trolley began operation in June 1987. Operation hours of the trolley were 11am to 6pm, Monday through Saturday and noon to 5pm on Sundays. After the three month trial-run, BURA was to analyze whether or not the trolley should continue operating permanently. During the three month trial-run, approximately 13,000 shoppers used the trolley. As a result, the trolley was brought back the following summer. Once again, the city, BURA and the shopping centers financed the trolley shuttle. A fifth shopping center—the Water Street shopping

---

<sup>42</sup>Camera, 6 March 1986 (Shopping Centers file) and 18 September 1986 (BURA file)

complex—was added to the shuttle system in 1988.<sup>43</sup>

In 1989, the trolley provided free rides to 34,000 passengers. Despite the popularity of the trolley during the summer months, the trolley did not operate in 1990. In January 1990, funding for operating the trolley shuttle system came in question. Businesses were not convinced that the financial benefits of the trolley outweighed the cost of funding the trolley. Both private and public agencies sponsoring the shuttle system conducted a search to find an inexpensive trolley operator that could continue providing the shuttle system. Evidently, the previous trolleys used proved problematic with chronic mechanical breakdowns. No affordable and reliable trolley operator was found. The city hoped to reinstate the trolley service or some other shuttle service in 1991. In December 1990, the city was considering a proposal for a year-round shuttle operating up to 12 hours a day. However, unlike the trolley, the new year-round shuttle would not be free to passengers. A nominal fee to ride the shuttle would be charged. However, before a year-round shuttle could be offered, the city would have to figure out a way to finance it. According to Alternative Modes Coordinator Bob Whitson, the cost of getting a year-round shuttle program going would be about \$600,000.<sup>44</sup>

### **Bicycles and Paths**

In March 1985, Boulder began building the Boulder Creek Path. After more than two years in the planning stage, construction of the new path finally got underway that spring. The city hired Gary Lacy to take charge of the path project. The path laid would run from Eben G. Fine Park, which was located at Arapahoe Avenue and 3rd Street, to 55th Street, approximately 4.5 miles. The official kick-off of the Boulder Creek Path project was held

---

<sup>43</sup>Ibid., 8 June 1987 (Shopping Centers file) and 24 May 1988 (BURA file).

<sup>44</sup>Colorado Daily, 5-11 January 1990 and Camera, 2 May 1990 and 7 December 1990.

Thursday March 14, 1985. Mayor Ruth Correll took part in the ground breaking ceremony. Boulder residents could help fund the project by buying a foot of land for the bikeway from the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department. The price tag of each foot of land was \$15.00. In return, the city would inscribe the donor's name in cement along the path. Other funding sources for the project included the Colorado State Lottery, grants, and a variety of Boulder city and county departments. The projected cost of the Boulder Creek Corridor project was \$2.5 million. Completion of the Boulder Creek Path was scheduled to take two years, so 1987.<sup>45</sup>

The first phase of the Boulder Creek Path and restoration was completed by October 1985. This first segment of the path project completed ran from Eben G. Fine Park to 28th Street. A celebration was held to announce the opening of the path's first segment that fall. The celebration entailed bicycling or hiking along the new path and serving refreshments at the Hilton Harvest House on 28th Street. Several improvements were made along the creek in conjunction with building the new bicycle and pedestrian Boulder Creek Path. For instance, landscaping and restoring disturbed areas along the creek, building pedestrian bridges at Eben G. Fine Park and near the Municipal Building and renovating the Kid's Fishing Pond located west of Ninth Street. The cost of this first phase of the Boulder Creek project was \$1 million. Over the next year and half, work on the Boulder Creek Path continued. The last segment of the path was finished in 1987. A grand opening ceremony marking the completion of the Boulder Creek Path was held on July 31, 1987. Mayor Linda Jourgensen officially opened the new trail extension in a ribbon cutting ceremony during Boulder Bike Week. The final segment laid ran from 28th Street to 55th Street. This new segment of the trail cost approximately \$361,000. Total cost of the Boulder Creek Corridor

---

<sup>45</sup>Camera, 10 March 1985; Times-Call, 15 March 1985; Denver Post, 20 March 1985.

Project was approximately \$3.6 million.<sup>46</sup>

Finishing touches to the new Boulder Creek Path were done in October 1987. A culvert placed beneath the railroad tracks between Foothills Parkway and Pearl created a tunnel, whereby bicyclists and pedestrians could bypass the tracks. Although the main trail was complete, other improvements still remained which would add to the functioning and beauty of the trail project. Additional improvements to the Boulder Creek corridor planned by the city included landscaping and developing more parks along the path, building a fish observatory along the creek near the Clarion Harvest House Hotel located at 1345 28th Street and extending Boulder Creek Path up Boulder Canyon. The Boulder Creek Fish Observatory opened formally on September 22, 1988. Four huge portholes in a subterranean amphitheater provided the public a view of Boulder Creek inhabitants like fish in their natural habitat. The Boulder Parks and Recreation Department, the Boulder Flycasters local chapter of Trout Unlimited and local businesses jointly funded the \$55,000 project. The idea for the fish observatory originated from Jay Wendell, biologist and vice president of Aquatic and Wetland Consultants of Boulder.<sup>47</sup> Work on extending the Boulder Creek Path began in October 1989. Boulder County and the city of Boulder combined forces to build the trail extension that ran west from Eben G. Fine Park up Boulder Canyon along side Boulder Creek and State Highway 119 to just east of Fourmile Canyon. The new trail dubbed Boulder Canyon Trail was a two-mile extension. The first one-third mile of the new trail was paved, while the remaining length was gravel. The packed gravel portion of the trail followed the old railroad bed. Work on the Boulder Canyon Trail was completed in June 1990 except for an underpass that would connect the Boulder Creek Path with the new

---

<sup>46</sup>Camera, 16 October 1985 (Boulder Creek Corridor file); Boulder Courier, 16 July 1987 (Boulder Creek Corridor file); Camera, 23 July 1987 (Bicycles file).

<sup>47</sup>Camera, 11 October 1987; 27 February 1988; 10 August 1988 and Denver Post, 19 September 1988.

Boulder Canyon Trail. The underpass was to be completed in September 1990. A ribbon-cutting ceremony marking the opening of the new trail was held June 29, 1990 at Settlers Park.<sup>48</sup>

Completion of the Boulder Creek Path produced two different responses. One response was concern and safety. The new path was very popular and heavily used. In fact, the path was so heavily used that city council decided the path should be monitored to determine a course of action for safety purposes. The path was overcrowded and narrow, thus posing a hazardous threat to users. To alleviate the danger, more signs were posted along the path indicating areas of poor visibility and reminding path users to keep on the right side of the path. Gary Lacy, coordinator of the Boulder Creek project, suggested that a separate pedestrian path parallel to the existing path be built. A separate pedestrian path already existed along some portions of the creek path, so all that remained was to connect those sections together. Lacy claimed a pedestrian path of crushed rock would be inexpensive and help solve the traffic congestion on the path. Lacy intended on working with city departments to get those pedestrian sections of the path linked together.<sup>49</sup> The second response to the Boulder Creek project was success and recognition. The city's Boulder Creek Corridor project received state recognition. The engineering firm, which designed the creek project, David J. Love & Associates, was awarded the Engineering Excellence Award in 1988. The award was from the Consulting Engineers Council of Colorado and the American Consulting Engineers Council. The success of the Boulder Creek Path served as a model for creating new trails not only to the city of Boulder, but also to other cities across the United States. Planners from such cities as San Francisco and New

---

<sup>48</sup>Colorado Daily, 6-8 October 1989 and 8-11 June 1990.

<sup>49</sup>Camera, 11 October 1987 and Colorado Daily, 8 October 1987.

York expressed interest in duplicating Boulder's greenways.<sup>50</sup>

The overall success of the Boulder Creek project prompted the city to develop a plan for additional bike and pedestrian paths along Boulder Creek's six tributaries. The new plan developed came to be known as the Tributary Greenways Program. Council first initiated the Tributary Greenways Program in December 1987, when they approved spending \$35,000 for a project coordinator who would analyze the costs and details of a trail network along Boulder Creek's tributaries. The Tributary Greenways project entailed building new trails and bike paths along each tributary and connecting those new trails with existing trails, planting new vegetation, and landscaping to preserve the area wildlife and to diminish the disturbed areas along the new proposed paths. The plan also called for improving water quality and controlling water flow. A consortium of city departments including Open Space, Parks and Recreation, Transportation and Flood Utility provided the funding for the Tributary Greenways Program. In January 1989, council endorsed the greenways plan for six tributary creeks: Fourmile Canyon Creek, Wonderland Creek, Goose Creek, Skunk Creek, Bear Canyon Creek and South Boulder Creek. The city planned on contributing \$400,000 to \$600,000 a year over a 5 to 10 year period until the project was completed.<sup>51</sup>

In 1989, the city had to find a new coordinator for the project. City Manager Jim Piper decided the new coordinator should be part of the Public Works Department instead of the Boulder Planning Department as Gary Lacy had been. Piper's decision to have the new coordinator be a part of the Public Works Department was because the Greenways project was more complex in nature. Lacy, who was the coordinator of the Boulder Creek project, resigned in part because of Piper's decision, but also because he wished to pursue a career as a freelance consultant for greenways projects nation-wide. Lacy returned two years later to

---

<sup>50</sup>Camera, 27 February 1988 and Denver Post, 24 April 1989.

<sup>51</sup>Camera, 27 December 1987 and 26 November 1988 and 4 January 1989.

head up the Greenways project. A refined Tributary Greenways master plan was voted on by city council in September 1990. The revised plan would add 15 miles of bike paths at a cost of \$3.5 million over a five-year period. Council voted 7 to 1 in favor of the revised plan. Councilman Bob Greenlee cast the dissenting vote. Funding for the greenways project was to be provided by the city's Transportation fund, Flood Control Utility and the state lottery. Council was to decide during its budget process how much money would be spent on the project each year. The plan integrated transportation needs and recreational concerns without damaging the environment. The plan also took into account floodplain management and aesthetic improvements.<sup>52</sup>

Not all of the city's efforts to develop bikeways was as successful as the Boulder Creek Path. For example, the city's plans to complete the missing trail link in east Boulder known as the Wellman Canal trail were unsuccessful. In July 1986, the Boulder County Planning Commission nixed the city's plans to build the missing trail link of the Wellman Canal bikeway. The Wellman Canal trail extended approximately 2.3 miles along the canal from the University of Colorado campus to Eisenhower Drive and from Merritt Drive to Dimmit Road. The trail was interrupted at Eisenhower Drive. Bicyclists had to travel along Pennsylvania Avenue to 55th Street in order to reconnect with the trail at Merritt Drive. The city wanted to build the missing link in the trail. Plans for the missing bikeway were part of the 1984 Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. Maps of the 1984 Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan showed the Wellman Canal missing path link running along the south side of the canal, through several backyards of homeowners between Eisenhower Drive and 55th Street. Not surprisingly, homeowners opposed the plan. They claimed the path would infringe on their privacy and endanger wildlife. They had asked the city to remove the bikeway from the comprehensive plan maps, but to no avail. Until the Boulder County

---

<sup>52</sup>Camera, 4 May 1989; Denver Post, 24 April 1989; Camera, 27 May 1998 and 17 September 1990; Colorado Daily, 20 September 1990.

Planning Commission vetoed the plans for the Wellman Canal missing link, the city had intended to build the bike path. The commission's power to veto the bikeway stemmed from an intergovernmental agreement between the city and the county. The Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan was subject to votes by four bodies of government—city council, the city planning board, the county commissioners and the county planning commission. The cancellation of the missing trail link meant the city would have to find other means of connecting the two segments of the Wellman Canal bike path already built.
