Homeowners in that area suggested creating a two-way bike lane on Pennsylvania Avenue. Residents also would consider giving up part of their front-yards for the bike path.<sup>53</sup>

Like the Boulder Creek Path, other trails within the city's mountain parks and open space were very popular during this time period. As with the Boulder Creek Path, the city had to take steps to resolve the conflicts between the different trail users of these other popular trails. In February 1987, the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board banned mountain bicyclists from two mountain paths in the city's park system—Tenderfoot Loop trail off of Flagstaff Road and Enchanted Mesa/Bluebell Road trail starting in Chautauqua Park. The ban was issued in order to prevent further destruction of the land and to end conflicts between mountain bicyclists and other trail users. Park rangers had received 102 complaints about mountain bicyclists in 1986, an increase of 500 percent over 1985 figures. Not surprisingly, avid bicyclists objected to the path closings. They claimed the problems would be better solved through educational programs and enforcement of rules as well as seasonal or rotational trail closures. Despite objections, the mountain paths remained closed to bicyclists. In March 1987, Boulder Open Space Board of Trustees followed suit. Mountain bicyclists were banned from using 12 of the city's 16 trails. Trails not closed to mountain bicyclists were Rudd Trail, West Rudd Trail, Doudy Draw and Wonderland Lake Trail. The closures were to be reviewed in six months. The review showed disregard for the ban by

---

<sup>53</sup>Camera, 24 July 1986 and 24 January 1986.

bicyclists. Despite the ban, bicycle related incidents and complaints were still high, especially in August and September after CU students returned to Boulder. Nonetheless, the ban remained in place. This banning of bicyclists from most of the city's recreational land carried a stiff fine for violators. Violation of the city regulation carried a \$100 fine for first-time offenders, while repeat offenders faced stiffer fines.<sup>54</sup>

Coinciding with the city's efforts to develop new paths, the city also concerned itself with promoting bicycle safety. Building new paths, whereby bicyclists and pedestrians were separated from motorists, was one way the city promoted safety, but more action was required. The increasing clash between bicyclists and motorists, along with a recent accident, whereby a bicyclist was killed, prompted action by the city. In 1985, the Boulder Transportation Department proposed to council a new ordinance on lighting requirements for bicyclists and also stricter law enforcement of traffic violations committed by bicyclists and motorists. The ordinance proposal under consideration would require bicyclists to have a frame-mounted headlight and rear reflector when riding at night. This proposed ordinance change created some debate. Some viewed the ordinance proposal as too imposing. As a result, council amended the ordinance proposal so that the light could be worn on the rider or helmet instead. However, after further consideration, council went with the original ordinance proposal that required a frame-mounted headlight. Allowing the light to be worn on the bicyclists rather than the bike was too similar to the current ordinance already in place and therefore council opted for the more stringent bicycle-mounted headlight. The stricter law enforcement policy requirements for bicyclists and motorists included raising the maximum fines for violators and giving more traffic violation tickets to both bicyclists and motorists. On June 18, 1985, council gave unanimous approval to the resolution urging Municipal Judge Richard Hansen to impose the maximum fines for traffic violations by

---

<sup>54</sup>Camera, 24 February 1987; Colorado Daily, 13-17 March 1987; Camera, 5 October 1987; Colorado Daily, 29 August 1989.

bicyclists and motorists. Council gave final approval to the bicycle-mounted headlight ordinance July 2, 1985.<sup>55</sup>

To further increase bicycle safety, SANE Transportation Network and the Boulder Transportation Department produced locally a film entitled "Ace of Cycling" in April 1986. SANE Network was a newly organized local group that promoted safe, accessible, non-violent and ecological transportation, hence the acronym SANE. The filming was part of Cycle Sunday, a bicycle safety festival sponsored by the city of Boulder Bicycle Program. The film starred Anthony Edwards and featured world-class cyclists Greg LeMond, Connie Carpenter-Phinney, Davis Phinney and Muriel Sharp. In addition to the filming, the new Arapahoe bikeway underpass was officially opened by Mayor Linda Jourgensen in a ribbon-cutting ceremony during the Cycle Sunday festival. The new underpass provided cyclists a safe way to cross one of Boulder's busiest streets.<sup>56</sup>

Working to promote bicycle safety within the city, Boulder had a bicycle coordinator. Since 1974, when the city first created the bicycle coordinator position, the city has attempted to make Boulder a safer environment for bicyclists. In August 1987, Steve Clark decided to step down as Boulder's bicycle coordinator. Clark planned on traveling across the country as a bikeways consultant. Clark had served as Boulder's bicycle coordinator since 1984. Due to his efforts, more educational programs on bicycle safety and instruction in schools were offered, more bike lanes on streets were created and more tickets for bicycle violators were given. Also under Clark, the Bike to Work Day program became institutionalized locally. Clark acknowledged Boulder's efforts and progress in getting its citizens to ride bicycles, but he still felt Boulder had a ways to go in comparison to European countries. Nancy Dutko was selected to replace Steve Clark as Boulder's bicycle program

---

<sup>55</sup>Camera, 26 May 1985 and 19 June 1985 and 3 July 1985.

<sup>56</sup>Camera, 18 February 1986 and Times Call, 28 April 1986.

coordinator.<sup>57</sup>

To provide a safer environment for bicyclists, the city of Boulder reviewed its bicycle registration and regulations in 1989. The increase in bicyclists compiled with a high incident of bicycle thefts in Boulder warranted action. To curb bicycle thefts, Boulder Police considered reinstating mandatory owner registration of bicycles. Boulder had abolished bicycle registration in 1979 due to the rising cost of licensing fees. The licensing program lost money and was inefficient. In 1988, approximately 600 bicycles were stolen in Boulder. While the city did not offer a bicycle registration program, the University of Colorado at Boulder did. The university required students to register their bicycles with the university bike/parking program. The university's bicycle registration program was open to residents of Boulder County as well. Despite the bicycle theft problem, the city implemented no mandatory bicycle registration system during this time frame. The city did consider a plan using microdot identifications on bicycles, but that plan was dropped because it was too expensive. The city hoped to implement a bicycle registration plan in the next couple of years. In the meantime, the police recommended the following to prevent bicycle theft: locking your bicycle securely with a heavy-duty lock to a stationary object, storing your bicycle out of sight whenever possible, and keeping a copy of the bicycle's serial number as proof of ownership or engraving your driver's license number on the bicycle if your bicycle comes without a serial number.<sup>58</sup>

Besides bicycle registration, the city considered adopting new traffic code regulations for bicyclists in October 1989. Proposed changes to Boulder's traffic code for bicyclists included imposing a 15 mile-per-hour speed limit on city bike paths and

---

<sup>57</sup>Rocky Mountain Sports & Fitness Magazine, August 1989; Colorado Daily, 27 August 1987; Camera, 12 December 1987.

<sup>58</sup>Colorado Daily, 24 April 1989; Camera, 25 May 1989 and 8 October 1989; Colorado Daily, 15 May 1990.

forbidding bicyclists from wearing headphones while riding. A group of Boulder bicyclists opposing the 15 mph speed limit drafted a letter to city officials demanding the city encourage bicycle use by not imposing too many rules. Despite opposition to the speed limit, Boulder City Council approved the 15 mph speed limit by a 5 to 3 vote. Council members Steve Pomerance and Spense Havlick opposed the measure along with mayor Linda Jourgensen. Overall, council had misgivings about over-regulating, but safety concerns prevailed in the end. The growing popularity and heavy use of the Boulder Creek bike path and other bicycle paths required some regulations to be implemented. The speed limit went into effect December 28, 1989.<sup>59</sup>

In addition to creating new bicycle paths and improving bicycle safety, Boulder's reputation as a bicycle community and proponent of alternative transportation was clearly demonstrated in its annual holding of a bicycle race known as the Coors International Bicycle Classic. The race began as the Red Zinger Classic back in 1975 with Celestial Seasonings Inc. as its sponsor. When the sponsors of the race changed, so did the name of the race. The Adolph Coors Company took over sponsorship in 1980. During this time frame, 1985 to 1990, Boulder's reputation as a bicycle community and mecca for world-class athletes hit an obstacle. In October 1988, the Adolph Coors Company, which had been sponsoring the race since 1980 announced it would no longer sponsor the Coors International Bicycle Classic. After nine years of sponsoring the event, Coors withdrew its support from the race on account of the company's changes in market strategies. The Coors Company was growing nationally and therefore the cost of local sponsorship now outweighed the company's benefits and its marketing strategies. Instead, the company wished to sponsor cycling nationwide. The loss of sponsorship from the Adolph Coors Company led to the race being canceled in 1989. The director of the Coors International Bicycle Classic,

---

<sup>59</sup>Colorado Daily, 3 October 1989; Camera, 7 October 1989; Colorado Daily, 30 November 1989.

Michael Aisner, was unable to find another sponsor in time for the race. Hence, the 15th annual International Bicycle Classic was never held that year, but Aisner was optimistic that the race would be back in 1990. However, Aisner acknowledged that the moving the event from Boulder was a possibility. With the race being canceled in 1989, the year 1988 marked the last year that the Coors International Bicycle Classic race was held in Boulder. That year, the race was moved from its usual North Boulder Park site between Ninth and Seventh Streets to the University of Colorado campus. Some residents of North Boulder were relieved the race had been moved from their neighborhood after years of dealing with crowd noise, littering and trespassing across their lawns. The race had outgrown the North Boulder Park site. Residential streets had to be closed for most of the day and parking was scarce. The move to the university campus was ideal. There was adequate parking, the campus was easily accessible and offered plenty of space for the event, plus the Events-Conference Center could house the bicycle exposition.<sup>60</sup>

### **Pedestrians**

Following a walk around the downtown area with signs of protest, a group of Boulder pedestrians sat down with transportation officials in September 1985. Together they discussed what the city was doing to make things better for pedestrians. City efforts to improve things for pedestrians included having an Alternative Transportation Coordinator, who would determine the needs of pedestrians, bicyclists and car-poolers. The city was also preparing a master transportation plan that emphasized alternative transportation including the needs of pedestrians. To help determine those needs, Boulder was having a CU student intern tour every city pavement to assess the city's walking experience. The intern's findings would then be entered into the city's computer system so that the Boulder Transportation Division could correct problem areas for pedestrians. In addition, the city distributed a

---

<sup>60</sup>Camera, 20 October 1988 and 7 July 1989 and 15 February 1988.

brochure on pedestrian safety for the first time in 1985. Despite the city's efforts to improve things for pedestrians, many pedestrians still found a lot to complain about to transportation officials at this meeting. Complaints of pedestrians included the city not providing long enough "Walk" signals at intersections or ticketing enough drivers that turn into the paths of pedestrians and not informing motorists that pedestrians have the right of way in an uncontrolled intersection with a crosswalk. Pedestrians also felt that the city was not repairing damaged sidewalks fast enough.<sup>61</sup> Clearly, the city was making strides in improving things for pedestrians, but more was needed according to pedestrians.

That same fall, Boulder's tradition of sponsoring the annual Pedestrian Conference continued. In September 1985, Boulder played host to the sixth annual Pedestrian Conference. It was the only conference in the United States dedicated solely to pedestrian planning and design. The main event of this sixth annual Pedestrian Conference was a design competition for a pedestrian and bicycle corridor along 13th Street in Boulder from Arapahoe Avenue to Pine Street. Other events of the conference included walking tours, discussions on pedestrian problems and solutions from around the world and a slide presentation on the health benefits of walking. The idea for the pedestrian/bicycle corridor along 13th Street was suggested as part of a downtown improvement plan prepared by a citizens committee back in 1984. (For other elements of that downtown improvement plan see Chapter 4.) The committee envisioned the 13th Street corridor stretching for 18 blocks between the North Boulder Recreation Center and Macky Auditorium. However, designers for the competition were instructed to focus on the downtown portion of the 13th Street corridor. Thirty-three designers submitted design plans. A panel of judges was to select the winner during the conference. Designs were narrowed to six finalists. At the conference, the judges selected the designs of Denver architects Kris and Nancy Gifford. Elements of the Giffords' design included a tree-lined parkway carrying one lane of traffic flanked by bicycle

---

<sup>61</sup>Ibid., 20 September 1985.

lanes and sidewalks and parallel parking along one side of the street, and a square, bricked plaza that bridges the Boulder and White Rock Ditch in front of the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts located at 1750 13th Street. At the intersection of Canyon Boulevard and 13th Street the Giffords proposed a circle of trees illuminated by lights at night and a "forest" of brick columns and linear pools at the Pearl Street intersection to mark the mall's entrance. As first place winners, the Giffords were awarded \$5,000 plus the chance to negotiate for a \$150,000 initial design contract with the city. At the time of the conference, Boulder had not yet set aside funds for construction of the 13th Street corridor.<sup>62</sup>

The following year, plans for the 13th Street corridor were put on hold because of a lack of money. An article in the Daily Camera dated July 29, 1986, indicated that it would probably be several years before the 13th Street corridor became a reality. The city did not have the funds for the project. Further analysis of the 13th Street corridor project by a panel at the seventh annual Pedestrian Conference held in September concluded that the demise of the 13th Street corridor was the result of poor communication. Planners neglected to talk with and inform business and property owners along the corridor route about the plans. Hence, some owners were not in favor of the project because they had no say in the project. Others objected to the plan because it reduced the number of parking spaces. Whatever the reason, elements of the 13th Street corridor plan like a bicycle lane were not developed until the early 90s. In May 1994, the city held a ribbon-cutting ceremony marking the official opening of the 13th Street bike lane. The 13th Street bike lane stretched from Pine Street to the Boulder Creek Path. Between Spruce Street and Canyon Boulevard, the bicycle lane was only for southbound bicyclists. These cyclists were separated from northbound motorists by large flower pots.<sup>63</sup>

---

<sup>62</sup>Ibid., 9, 15, 16, 22 September 1985.

<sup>63</sup>Ibid., 29 July 1986 and 19 September 1986 and 12 May 1994.

In May 1986, Councilman Spense Havlick proposed the city adopt a "Bill of Rights for pedestrians. Havlick criticized city policies and capital improvements that increase the rights and privileges of automobiles. Havlick believed the city had given more attention towards and funding for automobile facilities than the pedestrian. Under the proposed bill of rights, the pedestrian would be guaranteed "...safe, healthy, economic and agreeable alternative forms of transportation."<sup>64</sup> Suggestions to guarantee those rights of pedestrians included building overpasses or underpasses on highways, outlying parking lots for motorists to utilize before walking to social events and placing more benches, public art and fountains along walkways. Havlick suggested that priority zones for pedestrians be established throughout Boulder. Although council members agreed with Havlick's proposal, they felt the proposal should be refined. Council forwarded the pedestrian bill of rights proposal to the Boulder Transportation Department for review. That fall, Havlick presented his pedestrian bill of rights idea at the 1986 Pedestrian Conference. In 1989, efforts to have the city adopt as law the Pedestrian Bill of Rights continued. A letter drafted by the Boulder Pedestrian Safety Committee urging the city to adopt the pedestrian bill of rights was sent to the City Manager James Piper. As late as May 1990, the city still had not officially adopted the pedestrian bill of rights. According to a Daily Camera article dated May 22, 1990, the city already had a major plan to improve pedestrian conditions. The city's newly adopted Master Transportation Plan emphasized alternative modes of transportation including pedestrians. In other words, the city was already aspiring to protecting the rights of pedestrians and to improving the walking conditions in Boulder.<sup>65</sup>

In June 1987, the results of a study conducted by the Boulder Transportation Division revealed that the greatest hazard to pedestrians walking downtown city streets was

---

<sup>64</sup>Ibid., 21 May 1986.

<sup>65</sup>Camera, 21 May 1986; Boulder Pedestrian Safety Committee to James Piper, 2 February 1989, in Boulder Pedestrian file; Camera, 22 May 1990.

motorists failing to yield to pedestrians in crosswalks. For the study, the city examined six intersections to determine not only the causes and patterns of accidents, but also to figure out what preventive measures could be taken. The intersection with the highest percentage (14.7%) of accidents involving pedestrians or bicyclists was at Broadway and 16th Street. The city felt changing motorists' attitude was vital to pedestrian safety. Therefore, city council directed transportation officials and police officers to institute a policy of better educating the public about pedestrian rights, to improve street signs and markings at crosswalks, and to boost enforcement of laws protecting pedestrians. The study offered solution suggestions to curb the danger motorists posed to pedestrians. Some of the solutions suggested included holding public workshops on pedestrian safety and rights, having traffic offenders attend special classes, increasing the length of time "Walk" signals were displayed and using more automatically timed "Walk" signals. The city intended to pursue solutions on an intersection by intersection basis.<sup>66</sup>

While the city was figuring out solutions as well as preparing its transportation master plan, pedestrians took to the streets. On September 16, 1987, members of a citizens group called the Boulder Pedestrian Safety Committee held a demonstration at the intersection of Broadway and 16th Street. The demonstration was designed to remind motorists that pedestrians have the right of way, that the law requires motorists to yield/stop to pedestrians in crosswalks. The group of demonstrators planned to cross the intersection of Broadway and 16th Street over and over again for an hour to make their point. The demonstrators pointed out to motorists that they needed to pay more attention to people on foot. The group planned to hold similar demonstrations at other busy intersections in the future. In November 1987, a rough draft of the Boulder transportation master plan provided a list of long-range transportation needs for the city. The construction of underpasses or overpasses was seen as one solution to the growing problem of motorists failing to yield to

---

<sup>66</sup>Camera, 7 June 1987.

pedestrians in crosswalks. As instructed by city staff, the Ad-Hoc Transportation Committee, assisting the city with the transportation master plan, prepared a list of 40 potential crossing sites throughout Boulder, where underpasses or overpasses were needed. They then prioritized that list. The intersection of Broadway and College Avenue was rated number one on that priority list. According to the committee, an underpass or overpass for pedestrians and bicyclists at that intersection was needed more than anywhere else in the city. Other sites needing underpasses or overpasses included the Broadway and Euclid intersection, the Broadway and Regent intersection, U.S. Highway 36 and Bear Creek, and U.S. Highway 36 and Table Mesa Road. The sites were rated according to the number of accidents at the intersections, the volume of traffic and its location in relation to major centers of activity.<sup>67</sup>

Held in September 1989, the 10th annual Pedestrian Conference entitled "A Decade Reviewed, Commitment Renewed" highlighted the city's newly adopted Transportation Master Plan as a clear indicator of Boulder's commitment to alternative transportation. Elements of the transportation plan impacting pedestrians included the \$7 million dedicated for repairing sidewalks and building 20 overpasses or underpasses as well as sidewalk ramps at intersections, and creating parallel alternative routes for bicyclists along 13th and Ninth Streets in order to distance bicyclists from walkers on those streets. The pedestrian conference in itself was a clear indication of Boulder's commitment to alternative transportation. The conference started 10 years ago with a small group of citizens and planners gathering together in support of a pedestrian-oriented environment following city council's proclaiming "'the year of the pedestrian.'" Boulder's commitment to alternative transportation continued the following year. Once again Boulder played host to the annual Pedestrian Conference. In 1990, the 11th annual Pedestrian Conference focused on examining and discussing ways of ending Americans romance with their automobiles. The

---

<sup>67</sup>Camera, 16 September 1987 and Boulder Courier, 19 November 1987.

conference entitled "The Road less Traveled: Getting There by Other Means" offered some radical suggestions to curbing our reliance on the automobile. For instance, it was suggested that motorists be taxed for every mile they drive on city streets and that pedestrians should be given control of traffic signals and that motorists be the ones ticketed for interfering with pedestrians.<sup>68</sup>

---

<sup>68</sup>Camera, 6 August 1989 and 10 September 1989 and 30 September 1990.

## CHAPTER 4 LAND USE AND DEVELOPMENT

Several developments in Boulder today took shape during this time—1985 to 1990. For instance, new auto parks, hotels and shopping complexes and stores were built. Changes occurred throughout Boulder. We see the start of the University of Colorado Research Park, the building of a new east branch library and the altering as well as the destruction of buildings along Pearl Street. However, getting new developments off the ground proved difficult at times. In addition to needing city approval, developers had to contend with zoning regulations, finding funds to finance a project and sometimes public scrutiny. The difficulties in starting new development projects gave Boulder the reputation of being anti-business. Key to understanding the development history of Boulder, particularly the difficulties developers had, one must first look at the rules and procedures governing new development. For instance, the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, land use regulations, zoning and the development review process all governed Boulder's development. In essence, how Boulder established new developments.

Boulder was not just interested in new developments. Boulder was also interested in preserving the past, particularly buildings or homes of historical significance. Boulder cherished its historic beginnings and therefore took steps to preserve some of those links to the past. For instance, design guidelines for the downtown area, which emphasized blending new construction with older existing structures, were established. Historic houses and buildings were given landmark status to ensure their preservation. This chapter focuses on the changes taking shape in Boulder as well as the preservation efforts. For clarity, the land use and developments are categorized and presented in sections. Categories of land use and development include the downtown area, shopping centers and historic preservation. However, not all developments fall into those three categories. Developments not falling into the three categories will be covered separately. But before looking at some of the developments that took shape during this period, the rules and procedures governing land use and development will be presented first in this chapter.

### **Planning and Zoning**

In January 1985, the Boulder Planning Board proposed streamlining the city's building permit allocation system. Instead of a complicated and extensive application procedure, which judged the quality of a development solely on a point system, the new proposed permit system would simply allocate 200 permits four times a year. The permits would be distributed equally among all applicants after meeting basic standards. Developers would no longer be rewarded building permits just for offering things like energy conservation features or moderate-income units. This point system would become obsolete with the new permit allocation system. Each developer requesting permits would be offered an equal percentage of the requested units. According to the proposed allocation permit system, the number of permits made available would increase over a five-year period. The new permit system would still maintain the city's 2 percent limit on growth. Councilman Phil Stern disagreed with that conjecture. Nevertheless, city council approved the measure 8 to 1. Final approval by council for the new permit system was given on January 29, 1985. The first allocation of permits under the new system was given out February 28, 1985. At that time, developers requested 543 permits, but only 200 permits were issued in accordance with the new permit system. However, any time the permit requests were less than 200, like during a slump in the construction industry, the remaining permits would carry over to the next allocation of permits. Thus at times, more than 200 permits could be issued at the planning board's discretion.<sup>1</sup>

The following year, at its November meeting, Boulder City Council discussed raising the cost of new building development fees. The development fee increase would help finance services necessary to support Boulder's growing population. For instance, the fee

---

<sup>1</sup>Boulder County Courier, 9 January 1985 and Boulder Daily Camera, 16 January 1985 and 19 February 1985.

increases would help finance the cost of a new library addition and street maintenance, plus increase both fire and police protection. In essence, growth would pay for itself with these new development fee increases. A study conducted by the Denver consulting firm of Browne, Bortz and Coddington had found that development in Boulder was not paying its way. Under the proposed new fee schedule, building fees for a new home would increase from \$2,850 to \$4,383, while commercial development fees would increase from 18¢ to 79¢ a square foot. Council initially approved the building fee increases in May 1987. Council unanimously approved the measure at its June 16, 1987 meeting.<sup>2</sup>

In April 1987, the Boulder Planning Board and Department sought public input on the city's long-range plan. The city wanted a better understanding of current economic, demographic and social trends that would impact Boulder's future. This gathering of public input was needed in preparation for the city's 10-year update of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. The plan provides the city with a blueprint of how to approach growth and development in Boulder and the surrounding Boulder Valley. The plan directs future development and divides the valley into three areas: Area I was land inside the city limits; Area II was land just outside the city limits where development was expected within 15 years; and Area III was land in the county where no urban development was scheduled. Besides mapping development in Boulder, the plan proclaims the city's shared values and vision of the future. For this reason, the city held four panel discussions/workshops to gather public opinions at the University of Colorado Boulder Events Center. Questions or issues raised included whether or not to maintain the city's 2 percent per year growth limitation amidst the declining rate of economic growth, the aging of the population and the quality of life. Later in the year, city council and the planning board intended to start reviewing and making changes to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan for the 10-year update. Changes to the plan required not only the approval of Boulder City Council and the Boulder Planning

---

<sup>2</sup>Camera, 19 November 1987 and 6, 20 May 1987 and 17 June 1987.

Board, but also the Boulder County Long Range Planning Commission and Boulder County Commissioners since the plan was a joint project of both the city and county. Work on updating the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan took three years.

Revisions to the plan included strengthening the sections on the environment and economy and adding, for the first time, a section on human services and also a master transportation plan. As before, the plan still called for annexing Gunbarrel and Heatherwood. Making changes to the plan was not an easy task since the plan sparked considerable debate. One element of the plan that sparked the most debate was over defining the city's "service area." The service area was the area that the city eventually intended to annex, such as Gunbarrel. City planners recommended not expanding the city's service area.<sup>3</sup> They projected that Boulder Valley's population growth rate would slowly but steadily decline, and therefore believed enough vacant land existed to handle development through the year 2010. Developers and others, on the other hand, questioned not only the population projections of the city planners, but also the supply of land available for development. Builders and developers contended that a shortage of large parcels of vacant land already existed for residential, industrial and commercial development and therefore pushed to expand the city's service area. In addition, the goal to annex Gunbarrel was seen as unrealistic given the fact that many Gunbarrel residents were either ambivalent or outright opposed towards annexation.<sup>4</sup> The ambivalence and or opposition towards annexation stemmed from the Gunbarrel North housing development project approved by council in 1986 despite objections from neighboring residents. Even though the development remained unbuilt as of January 1988, many Gunbarrel residents were still angry over the Gunbarrel

---

<sup>3</sup>Ibid., 5 April 1987 (Boulder Planning Dept. file) and 3 April 1988 (Regional Planning-Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan file).

<sup>4</sup>Ibid., 12 February 1989, 6 January 1989 and 13 February 1989 (Regional Planning-BVCP file).

North housing project. Therefore, the city was reluctant to forcibly annex the area. For this reason, developers and builders wanted to expand the service area since the likelihood of annexing Gunbarrel seemed highly improbable. Nevertheless, the service area was not expanded beyond what was already indicated in the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. In February 1990, council preliminarily approved the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, which established the city's guidelines for growth and development for the next 20 years. However, council directed the planning department to update and clarify the maps included in the plan. Annexing Gunbarrel and the Heatherwood area still remained apart of the plan, but no date as to when the annexation would occur was given. Changes to the maps and portions of the text were made. Final approval of the plan by council occurred in May 1990.<sup>5</sup>

Following the approval of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan's 10-year update, city and county planners continued to assess and study Boulder's service area, specifically Area III of the plan. The debate over land availability that the plan update sparked warranted the reassessment. In May 1990, city and county planners started to conduct a study to determine whether or not parts of Area III should be prepared for development. In essence, expand the city's service area for future development by changing some of the land designations in the comprehensive plan from Area III to Area II. A Citizens Advisory Committee was organized and assigned to work with city and county planners on the study. The results of this study and others conducted were incorporated into the next major update of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan in 1996. Other studies conducted included such topics as Gunbarrel annexation, North Boulder Subcommunity Plan and the Integrated Planning Project.<sup>6</sup>

---

<sup>5</sup>Ibid., 29 January 1988 (Annexation Municipal Government-Gunbarrel file) and 7 February 1990 and 2 May 1990.

<sup>6</sup>Camera, 8, 10 May 1990 and City of Boulder Department of Community Design, Planning and Development and Boulder County Land Use Department, "Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan," 1996, 1.

In December 1990, in compliance with the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan, the city of Boulder extended its city limits by annexing 324 acres of land in north Boulder. The land annexed was a triangular piece of land boarded by Violet Avenue, Broadway and 28th Street. Initially, the city intended to annex approximately 383 acres, but not all property owners were enticed by the city's annexing offer. As an incentive to be annexed, the city and county offered to pay for half of the cost of extending city utilities to that area. Normally, the annexing property owners pay the entire cost. The properties that chose not to be annexed were left as county islands. Talk of annexing north Boulder first occurred nearly three years ago. A question that went unresolved at the time of annexation was how the city intended to zone 13.3 acres of the 324 acres for a proposed low-income housing project known as the White Owl. Council delayed the controversial zoning issue until March 1991. At that time, council voted 7 to 2 to zone those 13.3 acres for medium density.<sup>7</sup> (For additional information on this proposed low-income housing project see Chapter 6.)

Coinciding with the city's 10-year update of the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan came the need to review the city's land use regulations. A point of contention that persisted to plague builders and developers was the cumbersome and bureaucratic red tape of the city's development review process. The development review process was viewed as complex and at times inconsistent with the city's land use regulations, thus Boulder was often seen as anti-business. This anti-business label—Boulder not welcoming new businesses—was an image city council had to contend with often. Efforts to streamline the review procedures were first undertaken in the fall of 1985. City Manager Jim Piper worked with Planning Director Ed Gawf, Assistant City Manager Dave Knapp and Public Works Director David Rhodes on how to simplify and shorten the development review process. Some changes made to the development review process in 1985 included distributing explanatory materials describing

---

<sup>7</sup>Camera, 19 December 1990 and 6 March 1991.

the system, establishing clearer set schedules and instituting a monthly tracking system. Despite the efforts to streamline the procedures in 1985, the development review process was still heavily criticized in 1988. The problem rested with the city's complex land use regulations and rules. Hence, the city decided to hire a consultant to evaluate Boulder's land use regulations in the fall of 1988. The city hired Bob Freilich from the Kansas City, Missouri firm of Freilich, Leitner, Carlisle and Shortlidge to evaluate the land use regulations. The last time the regulations were reviewed was 17 years ago. A technical advisory committee was created to work with the consultant. That committee served as a guide and a liaison between the community and the city manager.<sup>8</sup>

Once again the public was asked for input. This time, input on Boulder's land use regulations and development review process was sought. That way the city could ascertain the community's land use goals and determine how the regulations should be revised to achieve those goals. Five meetings were held to gather public input in March 1989. Some of the issues addressed at the meetings included ways to simplify the development review process, adjusting zoning in order to meet community land use goals and ways to incorporate design into development. Upon review of Boulder's land use regulations, the consultant Freilich told council in July 1989 that the land use regulations were inadequate and outdated. Freilich identified and reported four problem areas to council. The four problem areas identified were as follows: neighborhood planning, the development review process, community design, and the current land use regulations not reflecting the goals or objectives of the 1986 Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan. According to Freilich, planners needed to focus on the unique characteristics of neighborhoods to achieve compatibility between existing and new planned homes. As for the development review process, he indicated that the role of the planning board needed to be clarified so that the board could review and evaluate more efficiently. New developments failed to meet the expectations and

---

<sup>8</sup>Ibid., 27 November 1985 and 12 April 1988 and 20 September 1988.

aspirations of the community. City council accepted the findings of Freilich in November 1989. Council asked that solutions to the city's land use regulation problems be found and developed. By November 1991, solutions and changes to the city's land use regulations were nearly complete. The changes would hopefully ease the concerns and frustrations both developers and residents had regarding Boulder's building rules. For developers, the project proposal to final approval would be a faster and less cumbersome process. As for residents, they would know for certainty what could and could not be built next door to them. More specific guidelines would lessen the confusion and uncertainty of the land use regulations. Both the Boulder Planning Board and Boulder City Council were to review the changes to the land use regulations in 1992.<sup>9</sup>

As stated earlier, several developments were taking shape during this period in addition to the changes in procedures and rules governing development. Now that the procedures and regulations governing development have been addressed, let's proceed with the new developments taking place during this time. Specifically, those developments taking form that give rise to the city of Boulder we see today. As indicated earlier in the introduction, developments and land use are divided into three categories—shopping centers, downtown and historic preservation—for clarity. However, there were developments taking shape that do not fall into those categories. Those developments not falling into the three categories will be presented here first. Then the developments and changes in land use taking place in the three categories will be addressed in the remainder of this chapter.

The auto park located east of the intersection of Pearl Parkway and 47th Street seen today was created between 1985 and 1987. Automobile dealers George McCaddon and Tom Lyons transformed 12.5 acres of undeveloped land into an auto park to house McCaddon's Oldsmobile-Cadillac dealership and Lyon's Toyota. However, before the auto park was built,

---

<sup>9</sup>Camera, 14 July 1989 and 17 November 1989 and 10 November 1991.

the threat of flooding from Boulder Creek had to be eliminated. The construction of drainage channels by Boulder's Transportation Department for the Pearl Parkway project allowed the plans for the auto park to proceed. Lyons relocated his car dealership to the auto park in October 1986 and McCaddon followed in March 1987. Both auto dealerships—Lyons Toyota and McCaddon Oldsmobile-Cadillac—were previously located at Canyon Boulevard between Folsom and 26th Streets. A new shopping complex was going to be developed in the area formerly occupied by the car dealerships.<sup>10</sup>

In April 1985, plans for another auto park located on Arapahoe Avenue across from the Cherryvale Road intersection was in the works. Boulder auto dealer, Bill Crouch, proposed building a 21 acre auto park that would house several auto dealerships on five separate lots, including Honda, Mercedes-Benz, Mazda, Mitsubishi and Ford. Development of the auto park was to be done in several phases. Crouch had submitted his proposal to the city's planning department in March and was just waiting for city approval. According to a Business Plus article of the Boulder Daily Camera dated April 9, 1985, the plans for the auto park had the support of the city's planning department. However, Crouch faced the same problem Lyons and McCaddon had to contend with—building in a floodplain. Part of the property Crouch intended to build his auto park on was located within the floodplain of South Boulder Creek. Therefore, construction of Crouch's auto park could not begin until the problem of building within the floodplain was resolved. That problem was resolved within a year. Bill Crouch opened his Mazda and Mercedes dealership in the new Boulder Auto Park at 6025 Arapahoe in October 1986. The first phase of the auto park was complete. The new auto park contained a 55,000 square-foot sales building. Crouch intended to relocate his other car dealerships at the new auto park as well. Both of these two new auto parks offered

---

<sup>10</sup>Boulder Daily Camera Business Plus, 2 April 1985 (Land Use file) and 28 October 1986 (Shopping Centers file).

customers larger showrooms, inventories and service areas.<sup>11</sup>

In 1986, plans to develop land northwest of the intersection of Lookout Road and Idylwild Trail for residential purposes created quite a stir. Even though plans for the Gunbarrel North project had already been approved by the city planning board in February 1986, council decided to exercise its right to review the proposal given the controversy surrounding the development. After nearly a year of debate, council voted 5 to 3 to annex and zone 43 acres for the proposed Gunbarrel North development in September 1986. The plan was given the go ahead from council despite opposition from Gunbarrel residents, specifically, residents from the neighboring Homestead and Gunbarrel Greens subdivisions. What development plans caused so much opposition? Developers Don Unkefer and George Williams planned to annex and rezone the development site from economic development to medium-density residential zoning. Once annexed and rezoned, the developers intended to build 325 residences on the site, both apartments and homes for middle and upper income families. Originally, the developers wanted to build housing for the elderly including a 163-unit retirement home, but those plans received strong opposition from Gunbarrel residents. The change in development plans did not alter the neighbors' animosity towards the project. Points of contention Gunbarrel residents had with the plans included the development's high density and the traffic increase such a high density development would bring to the neighborhood. Homeowners in that area also feared that the new development would decrease property value. Ownership of that land was also a concern of the residents. Evidently, the University of Colorado owned the land and had leased the land to the developers. Gunbarrel residents questioned who would be accountable if the project did not succeed.<sup>12</sup> Despite the opposition, council gave the go ahead for the development. As a

---

<sup>11</sup>Ibid., 9 April 1985 (Land Use file) and 29 October 1986 (Boulder Business Automobile Dealers file).

<sup>12</sup>Camera, 3 September 1986 and 22 February 1986 and 5 December 1985.

result, many Gunbarrel residents vowed to oppose the city's efforts to annex Gunbarrel into Boulder. In November 1988, Boulder city and county planners decided to let Gunbarrel remain unincorporated rather than force annexation.<sup>13</sup> To this day, Gunbarrel remains unannexed except for some portions of the area like the Gunbarrel North subdivision.

The plans for building a 150 room Courtyard Marriot Hotel just east of 47th Street and south of Pearl Street was another development that was not warmly received. This time, the opposition towards the project came from the city's planning board. Building a hotel at that location meant the city's land use regulations would have to be amended. The land was zoned for industrial development only. At first, the Boulder Planning Board rejected the idea of amending the city's land use regulations, but later the board reversed its decision after city council voiced its support for the hotel. A zoning change was made by council to allow the hotel to be built. Construction of the new hotel was scheduled to start in the summer of 1987. A tentative opening date for the new hotel was the fall or winter of 1988. The hotel was actually completed by August 1988. A grand opening ceremony was held on August 9, 1988. The hotel amenities included an indoor swimming pool, exercise room, a full-service restaurant and lounge and two small meeting rooms. The Courtyard Marriot was the first major hotel built in Boulder since 1986, when The Residence Inn started offering apartment style suites.<sup>14</sup>

Another development taking shape in 1986 was the Colorado University Research Park. The university proposal entailed creating a research campus on 200 acres of university owned land and then leasing that land to businesses interested in building research-related

---

<sup>13</sup>Times-Call, 5 October 1986 and Camera, 11 November 1988 (Annexation Municipal Government Gunbarrel file).

<sup>14</sup>Camera, 11, 12 September 1986 (Land Use file); Camera, 13 March 1987 (Boulder Planning Board file); Boulder Courier, 5-11 November 1987 (Land Use file); Camera, 9 August 1988 (Boulder Business file-Marriot Hotels).

facilities. The 200 acres was bounded on the north by Arapahoe Avenue and Colorado Avenue on the south between 30th Street and Foothills Parkway. The proposed venture would be developed over a 15 to 20 year period and would create approximately 5,000 jobs. In December 1986, Boulder City Council and the University of Colorado Board of Regents reached an agreement establishing the ground rules for developing the research park. Key points of the agreement were as follows:

### MAJOR POINTS OF CU-CITY AGREEMENT

These are some of the points outlined in the agreement between the city of Boulder and the University of Colorado:

- The University and city will cooperate so that the "spirit" of city land use regulations is followed.

- The University and city will equally share the costs of mutually agreed upon off-site transportation and utility improvements.

- The University will adhere to national building, fire, electrical, mechanical and plumbing codes and will "consult with the city" on its standards.

- The University park will include uses found on the main Boulder campus, including, without limitations, hotel and conference facilities, research and development facilities, incubator facilities for start-up businesses, office buildings and laboratories.

- The University will notify the city manager of developments the chancellor has approved. The City Council can formally request that any development be halted or changed.

- University-financed projects will be exempt from city fees and taxes, but developers who lease land from CU and build their own projects will have to pay the fees and taxes.

- The University will abide by state and federal flood-control standards. The agreement does not address the stricter city flood-control standards.

- The University will not be required to comply with the city's solar access requirements.

- The University will provide open space and parking consistent with a campus setting.

- The University will develop a strict set of covenants dealing with the types of buildings, style and materials that "will generally ensure that all buildings and facilities are of a high quality and will meet high aesthetic standards."

- Buildings will be subjected to the University's project design review process.

...  
(Daily Camera, 17 December 1986)

Council approved this agreement by a 7 to 1 vote. Councilman Steve Pomerance cast the dissenting vote. Council member Bev Sears abstained from voting since she was employed by the university. A last minute addendum to the agreement helped sway some council members to vote in favor of the agreement. The addendum called for the city and the

university to join forces to conduct an impact study. The impact study was to determine how much the research park would impact the city's transportation system, water and sewer utilities and also air and water quality. The agreement between the city and the university was significant since the land was state owned and therefore the university did not need the city's permission to build the research park or have to comply with the city's development regulations. However, in the spirit of cooperation and to avoid any future hassles, the university pledged to take city regulations into consideration. Nevertheless, opponents of this agreement contended that the city agreed to quickly and relinquished responsibility to oversee the park's development, since the city wanted to disperse with the "anti-business" stigma or image plaguing the city. Plus, many complained about how the agreement negotiations were conducted. Only the City Manager Jim Piper and City Attorney Joe de Raismes were involved in the initial negotiations with the university. Council was not made aware of the negotiations until very late in the negotiation agreement process. In spite of how the agreement was conceived, council approved the measure. The economic benefits that the research park would likely bring to the city won out.<sup>15</sup>

In February 1987, the Colorado University Board of Regents granted initial approval of the research park in concept. Now that approval had been granted by both the city and the university, the two parties set out to determine the impact the research park would have on the city as stipulated in the agreement. In April 1987, Wright Water Engineers Inc. of Denver was hired to conduct the impact study. The \$80,000 consulting fee was split between the city and the university. Results of the impact study were released in June of that same year. The study revealed that building the research park would impact both traffic and air pollution in Boulder, while the impact on the city's water, sewage and storm water would be minimal over the next 20 years. The consultants indicated that smart planning and additional funding could lessen the impact. To ease traffic congestion the consultants suggested

---

<sup>15</sup>Camera, 11, 17 December 1986 (Colorado University Research Park file).

building an interchange at Foothills Parkway and Arapahoe Avenue, adding traffic signals, widening Foothills Parkway and building turn lanes along the roads bounded by the research park. Suggestions to lessen the air pollution expected from the research park included the following: constructing buildings that will maximize air flow, promoting van pools and bus riding, placing limits on what type of research firms will be allowed, planting trees and staggering businesses' work hours of operation.<sup>16</sup>

Interest in the proposed research park by businesses was not lacking. The first to show interest in becoming a tenant at the research park was the Laboratory for Atmospheric Space Physics (LASP). In September 1987, LASP proposed constructing a 45,000 square-foot building to conduct research and develop instruments for space exploration. LASP was not alone in expressing interest of becoming a tenant. In December 1987, U.S. West Inc. of Englewood announced that it had selected the university's research park in Boulder for the location of its new research facility the company intended to build—U.S. West Advanced Technologies. U.S. West had examined several Front Range cities as possible sites for its new research facility, but Boulder was the city chosen. Boulder Mayor Linda Jourgensen and Governor Roy Romer were both ecstatic that Boulder was the site chosen for U.S. West's new facility. Initially, U.S. West intended to build a 500,000 square-foot facility, but a recent federal ruling forced U.S. West to scale down its new facility. Federal Judge Harold Greene ruled to restrict or limit the type of research competing telephone companies could conduct. Therefore, U.S. West scaled down its facility to 200,000 square feet. U.S. West would occupy approximately 35 acres of the 97-acre park.<sup>17</sup>

However, before any tenants or businesses could occupy the research park, the site area had to be safeguarded against flooding. Evidently, a large part of the site was located in

---

<sup>16</sup>Ibid., 25 April 1987 and 10 June 1987.

<sup>17</sup>Louisville Times, 30 September 1987 and Camera, 11 December 1987.

the floodplain and at risk of the 100 year flood from the surrounding waterways including Boulder Creek, Skunk Creek and Bear Canyon Creek as well as the Wellman and Empson irrigation canals. Therefore, the university devised a plan to mitigate the threat of floods. The plan called for elevating the base of every building site 2 to 4 feet above the 100 year floodplain. In addition, an aesthetic water feature to absorb surface water would be engineered. The aesthetic water innovation was made up of grass-lined channels, wetlands, ponds and underground drains. Boulder engineer David Love, with the help of CU students majoring in environmental design, devised the plan to safeguard the park.<sup>18</sup>

Additional measures had to be taken by U.S. West prior to proceeding with its plans to build a research facility. Skunk Creek had to be re-routed. U.S. West proposed rerouting Skunk Creek east of its proposed facility, but both the city and the university disliked that re-route idea. Instead the city and university proposed rerouting Skunk Creek back to its natural, historical alignment. However, this meant the creek would flow directly through the Stazio softball fields. Ironically, the ball-fields were created 9 years ago by diverting Skunk Creek. Now the city would be diverting the creek back to the way it was before the ball-fields were built. It was known that the ball-fields would eventually have to be eliminated due to the research park construction, but not this soon. To delay the inevitable at least one more year, Boulder City Manager Jim Piper proposed to council a way to save the ball-fields for another season. Piper proposed building a temporary 400-foot long wooden channel around Stazio field. This would not only save the fields for the 1989 ball season, but also allow the city more time to find and buy land for new softball fields. Council approved Piper's plan in May 1988 and directed him to find an economically feasible way to build the channel. Work on flood proofing the area and building the channel got underway that summer. Tarco Company was selected to safeguard the research park from flooding and to

---

<sup>18</sup>Colorado Daily, 14 April 1988.

reroute Skunk Creek.<sup>19</sup>

As work on flood proofing and building the channel progressed, a ground breaking ceremony to officially mark the construction of research park was held on Wednesday September 7, 1988. Governor Roy Romer, CU President Gordon Gee and Boulder Mayor Linda Jourgensen were in attendance and helped shovel the first spadefuls of dirt. At the ground breaking ceremony, U.S. West Advanced Technologies revealed its design plans for its new facility. The new U.S. West facility design reflected that of the University of Colorado buildings, specifically the pinkish sandstone walls and steep red tiled roofs.<sup>20</sup> In April 1989, U.S. West announced it would voluntarily seek formal city approval of its research facility. U.S. West chose to go through the city review process and seek a planned unit development permit from the city even though the company was not required since the facility was to be built on state owned land. Speculation as to why U.S. West chose to do this was so they could "...preclude any future challenges to the project by the city..."<sup>21</sup>

U.S. West submitted its building plans to the city planning board in May 1989. Design plans for phase one of development called for building a 271,000 square-foot facility with a height of 48.5 feet, which approached Boulder's building height limit of 55 feet. In Boulder constructing a building over 35 feet tall required special review. Nevertheless, the Boulder Planning Board unanimously approved U.S. West's building plans in July 1989. The height variance was approved since the building was to be set back 290 feet from Foothills Parkway and therefore would not block views of the Flatirons. The planning board also accepted U.S. West's plan to reduce the number of parking spaces built by 16 percent, since the company still provided one parking space per employee. U.S. West planned to expand its

---

<sup>19</sup>Camera, 17, 18 May 1988 and Colorado Daily, 9-16 August 1988.

<sup>20</sup>Colorado Daily, 8 September 1988 and Camera, 29 July 1988.

<sup>21</sup>Camera, 28 April 1989.

operation over the next five years by building a second facility.<sup>22</sup> Now that the city had granted approval to the project, all that remained was finalizing the lease agreement between U. S. West and the university. U.S. West and the university finally worked out details of the lease agreement that summer after nearly 18 months of wrangling. A 200-page 40-year lease was signed in July 1989. The terms of the lease agreement stipulated that U.S. West would pay the university \$559,417 a year for the next forty years. Actual construction of the U.S. West research facility did not get started until October 1989. The delay was a result of last minute negotiations with the university over landscaping. Completion of the new facility, phase one of U.S. West's project, was scheduled for July 1992.<sup>23</sup>

Amidst all the developmental changes taking place during this period, changes within the Boulder Planning Board and Department were happening as well. In July 1986, Bob Drake resigned his post on the Boulder Planning Board. Drake wished to devote more time on Boulder congressman Tim Wirth's campaign for the U.S. Senate. The increasing number of planning board meetings coupled with the length of those meetings prevented Drake from serving as Wirth's political director and therefore Drake resigned his post. Drake had served on the planning board since February 1984. Council interviewed ten applicants for the vacated post on the planning board in August 1986. Victor Fruehauf was selected from the 10 candidates interviewed to finish out Drake's term on the planning board. However, after serving only a little over a year on the Boulder Planning Board, Fruehauf resigned his post in December 1987. Council held off appointing Fruehauf's replacement until January 1988 when council intended to fill other vacancies on city boards and commissions. Architect Jerry Moore and scientist Tom VanZandt were appointed by council to fill the two vacated seats on the planning board.<sup>24</sup> In April 1990, Boulder Planning

---

<sup>22</sup>Camera, 10 May 1989 and 7 July 1989 and Colorado Daily, 7-10 July 1989.

<sup>23</sup>Camera, 8 July 1989 and 7 October 1989.

<sup>24</sup>Ibid., 8 July 1986 and 15 December 1987 and 20 January 1988.

Director Ed Gawf was named to a new position in the city manager's office—special projects coordinator. Gawf had served as the city's planning director since 1982. Until Gawf's replacement was selected, assistant planning director John Fernandez served as acting planning director. In October 1990, Garner Stoll of Lincoln, Nebraska was selected from 102 candidates to become Boulder's new planning director.<sup>25</sup>

### **Shopping Centers**

Phase II of the Crossroads Mall redevelopment was finally completed. The opening of the Mervyn's department store in 1986 marked the end of the Crossroads Mall redevelopment project that had started back in the early 1980s with the formation of the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority (BURA) in 1979. Appointed by city council, BURA was charged with overseeing the redevelopment of Crossroads. Wanting a shopping center that would better serve the community, BURA worked with the MaceRich Company, the developer selected for the mall's expansion project, to improve Crossroads Mall. The Crossroads Mall redevelopment project was done in two phases. Redevelopment plans entailed the following: building an addition to the mall east of the J.C. Penney store, remodeling the existing mall, and building a two-level addition with a new anchor department store—May D&F—on the north end of the mall, plus another fifth department store. Phase II opened in August 1983 without the fifth department store in place.<sup>26</sup> Finally, in April 1985, the development of the fifth major department store promised by the MaceRich Company as part of the mall's renovation got started. The department store built was Mervyn's, a California based chain department store that specialized in selling clothes, linens and housewares at discount prices. The new two-story store added 82,000 square-feet

---

<sup>25</sup>Ibid., 28 April 1990 and 24 October 1990.

<sup>26</sup>Crossroads Mall Advertising Supplement, 10 August 1983, in Boulder Daily Camera.

to the Crossroads Mall, bringing the mall's entire size over 800,000 square feet. Although two years later than expected, Mervyn's officially joined the other four department store anchors—May D&F, J.C. Penney, The Denver and Montgomery Wards—when it opened its doors on Friday February 28, 1986. The reason for the delay was on account of the mall owner and developer—MaceRich Company. MaceRich was slow in finding a fifth department store, because both MaceRich and the city were hoping to attract Sears to build at Crossroads. Only after Sears Roebuck & Co. declined did MaceRich approach Mervyn's in April 1984. This was after the other department anchors were in place and all other components of Phase II of the mall's expansion had been completed.<sup>27</sup>

The delay of the fifth anchor store prompted eight Crossroads Mall merchants to renew their lawsuit against MaceRich Company in April 1985. The eight merchants claimed a loss in business due to the lack of customer traffic along the mall's east side corridor since Mervyn's was not completed on time. The eight merchants suing MaceRich for \$50,000 were: The Yankee Doodle, Uptown Gallery, Uncommon Scents and Notions, Buckland's Ltd., Abo's Pizza, The Golden Pheasant Coffee and Tea, The Boulder Holding Company and the Brick Shirt House. An article in the July 15, 1985 edition of the Boulder Business Report indicated that the lawsuit was still pending and due to come to trial in January 1986. Only two of the eight Crossroads businesses suing were still in business at the Crossroads Mall—Abo's Pizza and The Golden Pheasant Coffee and Tea. The other six businesses were forced to close before going to trial.<sup>28</sup> The lack of follow-up articles on the lawsuit in the Municipal Government Reference Center files, prevents further analysis. Specifically, how the lawsuit settled.

Construction of Crossroad Commons located on Pearl Street between 28th and 30th

---

<sup>27</sup>Times-Call, 17 January 1985 and Camera, 26 April 1985 and 1 March 1986.

<sup>28</sup>Camera, 12 April 1985 and Boulder Business Report, 15 July 1985.

Streets got underway in 1986. The 162,000 square-foot retail center was built by developer Samuel Burger of Samuel Burger Realty Co. Burger borrowed \$10.1 million from First Interstate Bank of Denver for the project. Retail businesses located in the new shopping complex included Sound Warehouse and Best Products Inc. By 1990, 94 percent of the retail space was leased. Despite this fact, Burger filed for Chapter 11 in March 1990 when he was unable to repay the loan.<sup>29</sup>

That same year Crossroad Commons was taking shape, another retail project was in the works. In October 1986, plans to develop a retail and office plaza at 2425 Canyon Boulevard between 26th and Folsom Streets were revealed. Developer James Loftus of Boulder and Maxwell Bruce Drever of San Francisco intended to build a 108,000 square-foot retail and office center in two stages over the next year. The developers selected the historic street name Water Street for their new development. Water Street was the original street name of Canyon Boulevard before its name was changed. Stage one of the development involved constructing a four-story building where Tom Lyons Toyota use to be located before moving to the new auto park at 47th street and Pearl Parkway. The first floor would house specialty retail shops, while the remaining three floors would serve as office space. Stage two of the development entailed building a one-story retail center that would house more specialty shops and another two-story building, which would house not only more offices, but also some restaurants. The second phase of the development was to be located in the area occupied by McCaddon Oldsmobile-Cadillac. The McCaddon auto dealership planned to move to the same auto park as Tom Lyons Toyota in March 1987. In addition to the retail shops and offices, the Water Street development would furnish 315 parking spaces and an underground 50-space parking garage. The architectural firm, Zimmerman-Hand of Boulder was selected to design the center and Loftus-Walker

---

<sup>29</sup>Boulder Daily Camera Business Plus, 28 October 1986 and Boulder County Business Report, May 1990.

Construction Inc. was hired to build the Water Street complex. The Water Street development was to be completed by the fall of 1987.<sup>30</sup>

According to a Boulder Daily Camera article dated October 13, 1987, phase one of the development—the four-story building—was nearly complete. Ground had already been broken for phase two of the development and was scheduled for completion in March 1988. That same article indicated that Disk Connection, a computer and office supply store, would be the first retail tenant of the Water Street development. The Disk Connection opened in January 1988. By November 1988, 70 percent of Water Street's retail space and 40 percent of the office space was leased. In the fall of 1998, the Water Street complex celebrated its 10th anniversary.<sup>31</sup>

Additional changes to the Crossroads Mall took place over the next couple of years. In June 1987, Sears Roebuck & Co. announced that it would open a store in the Crossroads Mall. The new Sears store was scheduled to open in October 1987 in the space vacated by The Denver store. Over the past fifteen years, attempts had been made to establish a Sears store in Boulder either by the company or by the city, but to no avail until the summer of 1987. The grand opening of Sears in the Crossroads Mall was held October 26, 1987. Mayor Linda Jourgensen had the honor of cutting the ribbon to officially open the new store.<sup>32</sup>

---

<sup>30</sup>Boulder Daily Camera Business Plus, 28 October 1986.

<sup>32</sup>Camera, 24 June 1987 and 27 October 1987.

### SEARS HISTORY

1972 — The Boulder City Council turns down a proposal by Westcor Co. to build a shopping center plus Sears store east of 33rd Street and north of Arapahoe Avenue. The council thinks the site is too far east.

1975 — The Planning Department turns down a proposal by Ralph Biernbaum to build a Sears-anchor shopping center at Folsom and 28th streets because it is too small.

1977 — The council turns down a proposal by Ernest Hahn for a Sears-anchor shopping center at Arapahoe Avenue and 30th Street. The site is too far east.

1978 — Sears turns down a request from the city to build in downtown Boulder. The company proposes instead to build in Louisville as part of a large regional Centennial Valley Mall.

1979 — Boulder responds by proposing an expansion of Crossroads Mall and from 1979 to 1983 tries without success to persuade Sears to join the mall.

1983 — The expanded Crossroads Mall opens without Sears.

1987 — The Denver department store closes in Crossroads Mall and Sears takes over the space.

DAILY CAMERA OCT. 27, 1987

Another change to the Crossroads Mall took place in 1989. The Boulder Planning Board approved May D&F department store's request to expand its store. The 38,882 square-foot addition was to extend east of the current store. May D&F's plans to expand also included building a one level parking deck, which would provide 257 parking spaces. A pedestrian bridge would link the store's new addition to the parking deck built along 30th Street. A groundbreaking ceremony for the expansion of the May D&F store was held on

June 14, 1989.<sup>33</sup> Changes at the Crossroads Mall were not the only transformations taking shape in Boulder.

In the fall of 1988, construction of the retail complex known as The Meadows got underway. The Meadows would replace the old Thunderbird Shopping Center located on Baseline Road between Mohawk Drive and Foothills Parkway. Actual plans to redevelop and expand the Thunderbird Shopping Center were first made public in October 1986. Developers James R. Loftus and Maxwell Bruce Drever had approached the city of Boulder about the project nearly a year before it was made public. Loftus and Drever proposed a 552,000 square-foot development that would include retail shops, offices, a discount Cub supermarket, a five-story hotel, a six-screen movie theater, restaurants and a bank. According to the developers, the renovation and expansion of the Thunderbird Shopping Center would be done in three stages over a four year period. Land adjacent to the Thunderbird Shopping Center as well as the land occupied by the Thunderbird Bowling Alley was to be utilized for this project—a total of 31 acres in all. When the developers' plans were made public, the plans received considerable opposition from residents of the Baseline neighborhood. People of that neighborhood expressed concerns over the inevitable increase in traffic this project would bring, particularly because of the proposed six-plex movie theater and the Cub warehouse discount grocery store. People of that neighborhood feared the discount grocery store would attract customers outside of Boulder as far as 50 miles away, thus creating heavy traffic in the area. Residents also expressed concerns over the height of the proposed hotel.<sup>34</sup>

The concerns and opposition from residents not only delayed the redevelopment

---

<sup>33</sup>Ibid., 5 May 1989 and 15 June 1989.

<sup>34</sup>Boulder Daily Camera Business Plus, 2 March 1988; Camera, 17 October 1986 and 7 November 1986; Boulder Daily Camera Business Plus, 12 January 1988.

plans of the Thunderbird Shopping Center, but also prompted the Boulder Planning Board to request that the developers modify their proposal. Specifically, to scale down the height of the hotel from five-story to three-story and to install traffic lights to help control traffic. Developers also abandoned the ideas of a six-plex movie theater and the discount supermarket—Cub Foods—to appease the community. Instead of the movie theater, the developers decided to include an 8,000 square-foot library in The Meadows complex. Developer James R. Loftus donated the space for Boulder's new east branch library. The 0.38 percent sales tax approved by voters in November 1987 helped finance the interior construction costs of the branch library. In place of the warehouse grocery store, developers convinced Safeway to renew its lease and build a new store in The Meadows complex. A new 52,000 square-foot Safeway grocery store was built. The older Safeway store located in the Thunderbird Shopping Center was torn down. As a result of these changes in the redevelopment plan, construction of the \$45 million retail complex did not start until the fall of 1988. The first phase of construction was completed by August 1989. The new Safeway store opened then and the new Meadows Branch Boulder Public Library officially opened in December that same year. According to a newspaper article in the Boulder Daily Camera, dated February 20, 1990, 82 percent of the retail space was leased.<sup>35</sup> The new shopping complex was well on its way of taking the shape we see today.

#### **Downtown**

Between 1985 and 1990, the city made changes to the land use regulations to control downtown development and to ensure the visual quality of new developments. The city's efforts were prompted by downtown residents' complaints about how high density developments were destroying their neighborhood. In February 1985, the Boulder Planning Board discussed reducing the number of units allowed per lot without review. At that time,

---

<sup>35</sup>Camera, 3 April 1987 and 24 July 1988 and 20 February 1990.

most of the downtown area was zoned as high density. Thus, four units per lot were allowed without review by the planning board. To ensure well-conceived developments, the city planning staff considered cutting the number of units allowed without review in half. Talk of controlling or improving high density projects in the downtown area continued in April. At that time, city planner Peter Pollock and other city planning staff were directed to devise a new system to control development in the downtown area. A system that would combine quality review with reduced density. By the summer of 1985, a system was developed by the planning staff and was under consideration by council. The planning staff conceived the idea of creating an "overlay zone" in high density neighborhoods of downtown. The new zone required more review of in-fill development in high density neighborhoods and also limited the number of bedrooms to two per unit. By limiting the number of bedrooms without review, the city hoped to reduce the impact of in-fill developments since units of three or more bedrooms were often occupied by unrelated adults. Not surprisingly, developers objected to the overlay zone. They viewed the new zone as downzoning since the extra review would only encourage developers to build less dense projects. City planners, on the other hand, viewed the new overlay zone as a way to ensure quality development. The same number of units could still be built, but not without review first. Council approved the overlay zone in August 1985.<sup>36</sup>

In addition to developing and adopting a system to lessen the impact of in-fill developments downtown, the city was also considering an Urban Design Plan for the downtown area. Elements of the downtown plan included adding a pedestrian and bicycle route to 13th Street, improving pedestrian access, encouraging alternative transportation for downtown employees, restricting parking in downtown neighborhoods and developing architectural design guidelines for new downtown developments. This downtown plan originated from a fifteen-member citizen task force appointed by city council back in

---

<sup>36</sup>Ibid., 1 February 1985; 19 April 1985; 29 July 1985; 21 August 1985.

January 1984. Council instructed the Downtown Task Force to develop a plan that balanced commercial development with residential quality. The aim of the plan was to improve the commercial area, while protecting the surrounding residential areas. In January 1985, the downtown plan was presented to council. Upon review, council did not formally adopt the plan, but directed city staff to develop policies based on the concepts presented in the plan.<sup>37</sup>

As instructed, city staff worked on developing policies that adhered to the downtown plan. Work on developing the architectural design guidelines got underway in 1985. Numerous meetings were held to develop the architectural design guidelines. City departments involved in developing the guidelines included the Boulder Planning Board, the Downtown Mall Commission, the Central Area General Improvement District Board and the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board. With the help of architect Nore Winter, design guidelines were drawn up for both the historic and nonhistoric commercial areas of downtown. Guidelines for the interface area of downtown, the area where residential and commercial zones abut, were also defined. The proposed guidelines emphasized the preservation of original buildings whenever possible and also blending new buildings with existing buildings. By incorporating historic elements of the old buildings, like large storefront windows, tall and narrow upper windows, cornices, muted colors with bright color accents and discreet lighting, new buildings would blend with the old. The Boulder Planning Board adopted the downtown design guidelines in December 1985. The guidelines were to be voluntary, but new developments would face review by a newly created architectural review board that would be appointed by council. The review was mandatory, but compliance with the review board's recommendations was non-mandatory. Final approval of the downtown Urban Design Plan was granted by city council in January 1987. Only two council members voted against the plan—Bob Greenlee and Annette Anderson. In May 1987, council appointed the five-member Downtown Design Advisory Board that would review

---

<sup>37</sup>Ibid., 7, 9 January 1985.
