new downtown development proposals. In addition to adopting the Urban Design Plan, council approved other measures affecting the downtown area that were in compliance with the concepts of the downtown plan. The other land use regulations adopted included reducing the floor area allowed for principal buildings from 2.5 times the lot size to 1.7, banning most drive-up facilities downtown except along Canyon Boulevard and limiting street level parking lots. The ban on drive-up facilities was prompted by an accident near a bank's drive-up facility that resulted in a pedestrian being killed in 1985.<sup>38</sup> (For additional information on the other downtown plan concepts that focus primarily on transportation issues like the 13th Street corridor and alternative transportation see Chapter 3 of this work.)

A zoning change for portions of downtown neighborhoods was granted in 1989. By presenting a petition containing at least 160 signatures, residents of the Whittier area convinced both the Boulder Planning Board and Boulder City Council to downzone the Whittier area from medium to low density. Whittier residents wished to halt the pace of in-fill developments so to preserve the family atmosphere of their neighborhood. In September 1989, council voted unanimously to downzone a 13 block area along Mapleton Avenue between 17th and 21st Streets and also Bluff Street between 18th and 21st Streets. The low density zoning allowed one home on a 7,000 square-foot lot compared to the medium density zoning which allowed one home on a 3,500 square-foot lot.<sup>39</sup>

As we have seen, getting new developments off the ground was not always an easy task in Boulder. Attempts to build two new hotels in the downtown area were no exception. The two hotel proposals that encountered numerous obstacles were the Crystal Square Hotel planned for 15th and Spruce Streets and the Sheraton Hotel planned for Ninth Street and

---

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 19 July 1985; 20 December 1985; 7 January 1987; 7 May 1987 and Colorado Daily, 9-10 January 1987.

<sup>39</sup>Camera, 7 July 1989 and 6 September 1989.

Canyon Boulevard. Both hotel projects stirred considerable debate and controversy within the city during this time period. In the end, neither hotel was ever built. However, efforts to build a hotel at the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard still continue. Given the historical impact of these hotels during this time period and historical significance surrounding one of these hotel projects today, let's look at the attempts to build these two hotels in the downtown area.

Developer Wally Palmer first proposed building the Crystal Square Hotel in 1984. Originally, the plans involved building a 104-room hotel and two-floor retail complex in one, plus converting the historic Boulder Theater into conference space for the hotel. Council approved that proposal in October 1984 after the Boulder Planning Board had disapproved the project because of the hotel's massive size and the extra traffic the hotel would induce. In 1985, Palmer's plans for the hotel changed. Changes to the proposed hotel included dropping the renovation of the Boulder Theater from the plan and adding more rooms by reducing the amount of space used for retail. Those proposed changes lost Palmer council's support for the hotel project. Council declined to review the revised Crystal Square Hotel plans after the Boulder Planning Board rejected Palmer's latest proposal in April 1985. Undismayed by the rejection, Palmer presented amended hotel plans that summer. To regain council support, Palmer once again included renovating the Boulder Theater in his hotel plans. The amended plans also called for building a 142-room hotel with one floor of retail space and reducing the size of the fifth floor. Planning staff objected to the fifth floor of the hotel and demanded that the fifth floor be removed. City council, on the other hand, voted 5 to 2 in favor of the amended hotel plans in August 1985. The fifth floor plans remained intact. Palmer's decision to include the Boulder Theater no doubt helped sway city council. Council saw the renovations to the Boulder Theater as an alternative to the recent cultural center proposal that was rejected by Boulder voters.<sup>40</sup>

---

<sup>40</sup>Ibid., 19 January 1985; 18 April 1985; 18 July 1985; 7 August 1985.

Even though Palmer had been given the go-ahead to proceed, construction of the Crystal Square Hotel still had not started as late as February 1987. According to a February 26, 1987 article in the Town & Country Chronicle, Palmer had difficulty raising the finances for the project. That same article goes on to state that Palmer had until next spring to start construction. After such time, approval of the hotel development plans expired. The city code requires all planned unit developments be built within three years of approval or be granted an extension.<sup>41</sup> The Crystal Square Hotel was never built.

The second hotel proposed for Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard also has a turbulent history. Problems plaguing this hotel proposal included changing developers in mid-project, revising the design plans, public scandal and financing troubles. Plans to build a hotel at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard were first proposed by the Glacier Park Company, a subsidiary of Burlington Northern Railroad. Boulder City Council approved the proposed hotel of Glacier Park Company in April 1984, but that developer never built the proposed hotel at that location. In 1986, a new developer, Sina Simantob of Sinco International Investments Inc., expressed interest in developing a hotel at that same location. Simantob's plans for a hotel were revealed the following year. Simantob's hotel plans were not only larger in scale, but also different in shape compared to the 1984 hotel plan proposed by the Glacier Park Company. The 1984 hotel project proposed a 188,000 square-foot "V" shaped hotel, while Simantob proposed a 266,500 square-foot "zig-zag" shaped hotel. Simantob intended to build a five-story hotel with 275 rooms and a 440-space parking garage, plus conference facilities, a health club and retail shops. Building the proposed 266,500 square-foot development was estimated to cost \$27 million. To finance such a project, Simantob proposed a tax-increment financing plan, whereby the property taxes generated by the hotel would pay for portions of the project, specifically, the proposed 440-

---

<sup>41</sup>Town & Country Chronicle, 26 February 1987.

space parking garage, instead of funneling the revenue back into the city and county government. The city and county were not the only entities asked to surrender property tax revenue. Both the Central Area General Improvement District (CAGID) and the Boulder Valley School District were to surrender their property tax revenue as well. All these entities, with the exception of the Boulder Valley School District, were to donate 100 percent of the property tax revenue from the hotel towards the project for the next 20 years starting in 1989. The Boulder Valley School District was to contribute 78 percent for 20 years. Such a financial agreement obviously required the approval of all the parties involved. A three-mill property tax increase in the Central Area General Improvement District was also part of the financial plan Simantob proposed to help finance the project.<sup>42</sup>

Before Simantob could proceed with his plans, he first had to purchase the land from the previous developer and owner—Glacier Park Company/Burlington Northern Railroad. However, Simantob's option to purchase the land at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard from the Glacier Park Company expired August 10, 1987. The Glacier Park Company was free to negotiate with other potential buyers, but Simantob remained the favored party. In April 1988, Simantob managed to sign an option to purchase the 1.7 acres of land. Now that Simantob had struck a deal to buy the land, negotiations with the city were reopened, specifically, the tax-increment financing deal discussed in 1987. However, before the tax-increment financing deal could proceed any further, the city had to declare that property at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard blighted. According to state law, land must be declared legally blighted before it can qualify for tax-increment financing. Not surprisingly, the idea of designating the land blighted was not well received. Nevertheless, council approved declaring that land blighted in September 1988.<sup>43</sup> Plans for the hotel were progressing, but

---

<sup>42</sup>Camera, 31 August 1986; 16 April 1987; 30 July 1987 and Boulder Courier, 30 July 1987.

<sup>43</sup>Colorado Daily, 25 August 1987 and Camera, 3 October 1987; 6 April 1988; 21 July 1988; 7 September 1988.

within a month the plans for the hotel were threatened once again. Public scandal threatened Simantob's chances of finding financial backing for the hotel project.

On November 27, 1988, the Daily Camera printed an article linking Simantob with Phil DeMirza, a convicted criminal. Evidently, Simantob and DeMirza were once partners in Gold Lake Ranch, a business venture in which DeMirza used to launder some of his drug profits before the ranch was sold in 1984. DeMirza was convicted for racketeering, smuggling, operating a criminal enterprise and tax fraud by the U.S. District Court in Miami, Florida. At the trial, Simantob testified for the prosecution and was granted immunity. Simantob claimed he did not know DeMirza was a criminal. However, Simantob's testimony revealed that he had fronted for DeMirza's hidden interest in the ranch and was paid in cash. Not surprisingly, council was concerned with Simantob's association with DeMirza. Boulder would not use any "tainted money" to build a hotel. Therefore, in December 1988, council directed City Attorney Joe de Raismes to investigate Simantob.<sup>44</sup>

Surprisingly, Simantob managed to find financial backing for his hotel project in light of the Daily Camera article that tainted his character. Simantob secured financial backing from R. Barry McComic of San Diego. McComic paid Glacier Park Company \$2 million for 1.7 acres of land at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. McComic now assumed full financial responsibility for the hotel project. Simantob had a 120-day option to purchase the land from McComic, but he never exercised that option. Instead, Simantob and his partner Chet Winters served as development consultants for the hotel project. For their continued involvement in the hotel project, both Simantob and Winters were to receive a share of the hotel profits.<sup>45</sup>

---

<sup>44</sup>Camera, 29 November 1988 and 1, 14 December 1988.

<sup>45</sup>Ibid., 3, 15 December 1988.

Plans for the hotel progressed under McComic. In April 1989, McComic hired the San Francisco architectural firm of Bocken, Arrigoni and Ross to design the hotel. Before the project could proceed, approval of the hotel plans and financing deal from the various city entities was still needed. That summer, the Boulder Urban Renewal Authority (BURA) approved the hotel and parking garage tax-increment financing plan. BURA agreed to issue \$6.2 million in tax-increment bonds to pay for 340 spaces of the parking structure provided McComic secured financial backing for the hotel project. The developer, McComic, was to pay for the other 100 spaces of the parking garage. Once the hotel and parking garage were built, the property taxes generated by the hotel were to be used to repay the bonds issued by BURA. That same summer, City Attorney Joe de Raismes finished his investigation of Simantob. Council had delayed approval of the project to allow the city attorney time to investigate. The city attorney found no evidence of illegitimate business dealings. Simantob was reported to be trustworthy in past business dealings, but lacking good judgment on occasion. In November 1989, the Boulder Planning Board approved the plans for the 55 foot-tall, 275-room hotel. Board member Ann FitzSimmons was the only one to vote against the design plans of the hotel. FitzSimmons voted against the hotel plans primarily because of the height of the building and the shadow it would cast during the winter months.<sup>46</sup>

Just as it seemed plans for the hotel were advancing, the hotel project hit another obstacle. McComic was unable to find financial investors for the hotel project. In April 1990, BURA granted McComic a six-month extension to find financial backing. This was the second extension granted by BURA. McComic eventually found financial backing in October 1990. The French bank Credit Lyonnais of Paris and New York agreed to loan developer Barry McComic \$27 million for the hotel project at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard. News that McComic found financial backing elated most council members

---

<sup>46</sup>Camera, 9 April 1989; Colorado Daily, 23-26 June 1989 and 1-3 August 1989; Camera 3 November 1989.

except for Matthew Appelbaum and Steve Pomerance. Both Appelbaum and Pomerance had reservations about the hotel project from the very beginning, specifically, the city's financing deal of \$6.2 million for the parking garage and the blight designation of the hotel site. Nevertheless, the majority of council members expressed support for the project at its December meeting. Given council's support, approving additional transactions concerning the hotel project next spring seemed favorable. However, before construction of the hotel could proceed, some factors still remained. McComick still had to finalize his loan deal with Credit Lyonnais. McComick also needed the approval of downtown businesses in the Central Area General Improvement District to finance the underground parking garage with their property tax revenue. McComick's loan was to be finalized in February 1991. Petitions signed by CAGID property owners surrendering their property tax revenue to finance the parking garage were forthcoming in the spring of 1991. If the loan and CAGID approval was granted as planned, construction of the hotel and underground parking facility was to be completed by December 1992.<sup>47</sup> In the end, the hotel was never completed as planned. McComick was forced to back out of the hotel project in January 1992. McComick failed to find enough equity to secure the loan.<sup>48</sup> To this day the hotel remains unbuilt, but talk of building a hotel at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard continues.

### **Historic Preservation**

Efforts to preserve historic properties—our American heritage—have been carried out by individuals or agencies at all levels of government—federal, state and local. Historic preservation in Boulder began in 1974 when city council adopted the Historic Preservation Code. The adoption of the Historic Preservation Code established the Landmarks

---

<sup>47</sup>Camera, 17 April 1990 and 9 October 1990; Colorado Daily, 13 December 1990; Camera, 12 December 1990.

<sup>48</sup>Colorado Daily, 17-19 January 1992.

Preservation Advisory Board. This preservation ordinance provided the means to preserve historic buildings, thus preventing the needless destruction of Boulder's heritage. The ordinance helped to prevent the needless destruction of historically significant buildings by the following provisions: designating properties as historic landmarks, reviewing and approving any proposed alterations to the exterior of landmark buildings and listing buildings of historic potential as "structures of merit" in order to encourage their protection. The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board was charged with implementing these provisions and goals of the preservation code. The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board consists of six members, each serving a five-year term. City council appoints five of the six members. Two of the appointed members selected must have either an architectural or urban planning profession background, while the other three members appointed have no specific criteria qualifications to meet. A representative from the city's Planning Board serves as the sixth unappointed member of the board.<sup>49</sup> Although a key element in preserving Boulder's historic properties, the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board was not the only party involved in preserving Boulder's heritage. At times, Federal agencies, local nonprofit groups or members of the community had a hand in preserving Boulder's heritage. These efforts will also be presented in this section.

In 1986, Boulder's main post office, located at 1905 15th Street, was one of thirteen post offices in Colorado designated a historic landmark by the United States Department of the Interior. A bronze plaque commemorating the designation was hung in the post office during a dedication ceremony in May that year. The Boulder Post Office was bestowed the historic landmark designation because the building had both historic and architectural merit. Historically, Boulder was the first Colorado town to establish a post office. The Boulder Post Office was founded in 1860. Additionally, the actual post office building itself had the

---

<sup>49</sup>Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board, Brochure entitled "Preserving Boulder's Landmarks," in Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board file.

distinction of being one of the oldest buildings among the thirteen post offices honored by the U.S. Department of the Interior. Originally built in 1910, the building was seen as an excellent example of simplified Beaux Arts Styling. The building also exhibited a number of features not commonly found in postal buildings of that time period. For instance, the hipped roof and also the iron free standing lamps, which flanked the east and south entrances. Over the years, two additions to the original post office building were added—one in 1933 and the other in 1959. Both additions maintained the architectural style and integrity of the original building.

The Boulder Post Office was not the only building landmarked in Boulder that year. Eight other Boulder buildings were bestowed the honor of city landmark designation by the Boulder Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board. However, unlike the Boulder Post Office building, which was placed on the National Register of Historic Places, these eight buildings were declared city historic landmarks, not national. Nonetheless, this city landmark designation helped to ensure the protection of buildings just as the National Register of Historic Places does. The eight buildings designated as city historic landmarks were as follows: the Terrace Apartments located at 1123 Spruce Street; the Boulder Theater built in 1935 at 2032 14th Street; the Moorhead House at 745 Highland Avenue; the Newland House at 3011 Broadway; the Lennartz House at 655 Arapahoe; the Wolf House at 1235 Elder Avenue; the Spencer House at 427 Pine Street and the Northern Colorado Power Company substation located at Marine and Broadway Streets. These eight buildings were given landmark status.<sup>50</sup> This designation required property owners of landmark structures to submit any building plans that would alter the historic structure's exterior to a design review committee. The design review committee was composed of two Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board members and one staff member of the Boulder Planning

---

<sup>50</sup>Camera, 10 May 1986 (Architecture, Conservation, Restoration file) and 5 June 1986 (Boulder Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board file).

Department. Any changes what so ever—alterations, additions or demolition—had to be approved prior to the changes.<sup>51</sup> This way the city prevented the needless destruction of historically significant buildings.

The following year, the Boulder Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board recognized twenty homes along with the National Center for Atmospheric Research Complex as structures of merit. The Arnett House located at 646 Pearl Street was one of the twenty homes bestowed with the "structure of merit" honor. The Arnett House was both historically and architecturally significant. Willamette Arnett, who was the son of an early Boulder pioneer Anthony Arnett, built the house in 1877. Anthony Arnett was one of the original land donors to the University of Colorado. Architecturally, the Arnett House was significant since it represented the work of Boulder architect, George E. King. Willamette Arnett hired King to design the house. The "structure of merit" recognition basically was an honorary designation on paper, which imposed no additional restrictions on the property. However, the honor could establish the basis for arguing for city historic landmark status at a future date.<sup>52</sup>

In 1990, the "structure of merit" designation for the Arnett House was upgraded to city historic landmark. However, such a change in designation did not come easy. Following the death of the longtime Arnett House owners and residents, the Fullen family, Historic Boulder, a local preservation group, petitioned council to designate the house a historic landmark. The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board backed the landmark designation. However, the Fullen descendants objected to the landmark designation. The decision to landmark the Arnett/Fullen House rested with council. Council had to decide whether or not

---

<sup>51</sup>"Preserving Boulder's Landmarks," Brochure.

<sup>52</sup>Camera, 19 October 1987 and Boulder Planning Department, Historic Building Inventory Record, 1986, in Carnegie Branch Library.

to impose the landmark designation despite objections from the property owner. Such a decision was a first in city historic landmark designation. Council was reluctant to impose the landmark designation outright even though they could have done so. Therefore, council attempted to work out a compromise between the Fullen family and Historic Boulder. With the help of council, a compromise was reached. Although opposed to the designation entirely, the Fullen family agreed to landmark the house, but not to landmark the lot behind the house. Historic Boulder, on the other hand, was hoping to landmark the entire property including the back lot in order to preserve the historical integrity of the property. In August 1990, council designated the 113-year old Arnett/Fullen House a historic landmark, but the back lot along with the tack shed and cast iron fence that surrounds the property was not.<sup>53</sup>

To ensure the preservation of other historic buildings and to further encourage owners to apply for historic landmark designations, the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board presented to council two separate tax break proposals for historic property owners. One of the tax break proposals entailed waiving city sales tax on construction materials used to renovate landmark structures. The other tax break proposal under consideration offered a five-year rebate on city property taxes for building alterations or additions to historic property. Only the taxes on the addition or alterations would be rebated. In December 1987, city council reviewed and considered these two tax break proposals of the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board. Council questioned the rebate proposals since many historic property owners were wealthy. In the end, council approved only one of these tax break proposals. In March 1988, Boulder City Council approved the proposal that waived city sales tax on building materials used to renovate historic landmark property. This new tax incentive program, ordinance number 5092, designed to encourage property owners to apply

---

<sup>53</sup>Camera, 14 August 1990; Colorado Daily, 17-19 August 1990; Camera, 22 August 1990.

for city landmark designations was passed on March 15 1988.<sup>54</sup> The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board designated two more homes city historic landmarks and five others as "structures of merit" in May 1988.<sup>55</sup> The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board was not alone in preserving Boulder's heritage.

In March 1987, a community task force organized to move and renovate a Boulder historic landmark—the train depot. The task force wished to relocate the train depot from its present location at 2275 30th Street back to Central Park, where it would be reunited with the train located in the park. Why did the task force want to move the train depot? The depot had been located at 30th and Pearl Streets since 1973. Prior to 1973, the depot was located near Central Park at Canyon Boulevard and 14th Street, its original home when it was built in 1890. In 1973, the depot was moved from that location to the Pow Wow rodeo grounds at 30th and Pearl Streets by the Boulder Jaycees in order to save the depot from demolition when 14th Street was extended. Now that the pastoral setting of the Pow Wow rodeo grounds had changed to a commercial setting with the development of Crossroad Commons, the depot no longer seemed appropriate in that area. Therefore, the task force wanted to move the depot. The most appropriate place to move the train depot was back to Central Park. The task force's plans to move the depot back to Central Park meant finding a new location for the Central Park band shell. The park was not large enough to house both the train depot and the band shell. The task force had the support of the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board. Plan details were still being hammered out in November 1987, specifically, where to relocate the band shell. One suggestion put forth by the task force was to move the band shell to the Boulder County Fairgrounds in Longmont. Additionally, the task force proposed that the city buy a portable band shell, which could be used in different parks

---

<sup>54</sup>Camera, 1, 2 December 1987 and 17 March 1988 (City Council file).

<sup>55</sup>Ibid., 12 May 1988 (Boulder Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board file).

throughout the city. The Boulder Lions Club, who had donated the band shell to the city in 1938, was receptive to moving the band shell as long as it remained available for public use. Finding another home for the art deco band shell was not the only issue at hand. Moving and refurbishing the depot and band shell was a costly venture. In 1987, the cost of moving and renovating just the depot was estimated at \$250,000. Relocating and refurbishing the band shell would cost another \$115,000 approximately.<sup>56</sup>

The plans to move the band shell to make way for the depot encountered difficulties. Upon further inspection of the band shell it was discovered that the band shell's wooden arches were rotting, weather beaten and laden with asbestos material. The band shell would never withstand a move. Betty Chronic of the Boulder Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board informed the Boulder County Commissioners that the band shell had probably only one or two years left of service before it must be removed. Chronic conveyed to the commissioners that the original plans for the band shell should be preserved so that a replica could someday be built. The train depot task force continued to pursue its plan of moving the depot to Central Park since, in all likelihood, the band shell would be torn down in a couple of years.<sup>57</sup>

The task force hoped to have the train depot moved and refurbished by September 1990—the depot's 100th anniversary. The depot was constructed in 1890 and a dedication ceremony was held that year on September 26, 1890. Raising funds for the depot project got underway in March 1989. Though, the price tag for moving and refurbishing the depot was considerably more than the 1987 estimate. In 1989, the cost of the project was estimated at \$550,000—more than double the 1987 estimate. However, the \$550,000 estimate included

---

<sup>56</sup>Camera, 17 March 1987 and 8 November 1987 and Boulder Courier, 19 November 1987 (Architecture, Conservation and Restoration file).

<sup>57</sup>Times-Call, 19 February 1988 and Camera, 19 February 1988.

not only the moving and renovating expenses, but also the \$80,000 needed to purchase a new portable band shell. Renovation expenses included installing new heating, ventilating and air conditioning systems plus new electrical and plumbing service. A major fund drive for the depot project was held in August 1989. The Boulder Jaycees not only contributed \$7,000 to the fund drive, but also agreed to donate the depot to the city. In return for the Jaycees generosity, the city granted the Jaycees continued use of the depot for office and meeting space.<sup>58</sup>

The fund drive to relocate and restore the train depot by September 1990 was unsuccessful. The depot task force was unable to raise the funds needed in time. By January 1990, less than \$25,000 had been raised since the fund drive kicked off in August 1989. As of May, \$130,000 had been raised thanks in part to the depot project coalition's fundraising efforts, specifically the selling of bricks. The depot project coalition sold bricks for \$35 and \$45. Each brick sold was etched with the donor's name. Those bricks were then going to be used to create a walkway and platform in front of the depot at its new location in Central Park. A donation of \$30,000 to the depot campaign by the Gates Foundation in November 1990 raised the contributions to \$160,000. However, this still was not nearly enough money to accomplish the depot task force's goal of moving and refurbishing the train depot. The following year, the depot project was hampered further when a movement to save the band shell and to keep it in Central Park organized.<sup>59</sup> To this day the depot remains located at 30th and Pearl Streets in the Crossroad Commons shopping development.

Other preservation efforts during this time frame included attempts by downtown merchants and Historic Boulder to preserve historic buildings located along 10th and Pearl

---

<sup>58</sup>Colorado Daily, 3-5 March 1989 and Camera 4 July 1989 and 16 August 1989.

<sup>59</sup>Camera, 27 February 1990 and 14 May 1990 and 22 November 1990 and Colorado Daily, 19-22 July 1991.

Streets and moving two historic houses to make way for the Boulderado Hotel expansion. In the summer of 1988, a group of downtown merchants organized in hope of protecting the historic character of buildings located along 10th and Pearl Streets. The merchants filed an application with the Boulder Planning Department to have that 900 block of west Pearl Street designated a historic district. The reason for the merchants' actions was to prevent developer Robert Shonkwiler from destroying the historic integrity of those buildings. Shonkwiler planned on combining three buildings on that block into one by building two two-story additions. Peter Pollock of the planning department agreed to examine the area's historic merit and inform the city's Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board of the matter. The Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board threw out the merchants' application for historic designation since the application was not from a recognized historic preservation group. However, efforts to declare that area a historic district did not end. Historic Boulder, a recognized historic preservation group, got involved. But before Historic Boulder could file an application for historic designation, Shonkwiler applied for a building permit. Even though it was too late to save those three buildings on the corner, adjacent buildings in that area of 10th and Pearl Streets could still be saved with a historic district designation. Therefore, Historic Boulder filed an application to create a new historic district. City planners disagreed with Historic Boulder's idea to create that 900 block of west Pearl Street a historic district in itself. City planners felt it should be included in the Mapleton Hill Historic District instead of creating that block a historic district in itself. Thus, an application to include that block in the Mapleton Hill Historic District was filed instead. A hearing before the Landmarks Preservation Advisory Board on that application was scheduled for September 26, 1988. However, just days prior to the scheduled hearing, Historic Boulder withdrew its historic designation application. Board members of Historic Boulder felt that the area of 10th and Pearl Streets should not be included in the Mapleton Hill Historic District since the boundaries of that district were already set. Additionally, the withdrawal of the application allowed Historic Boulder more time to investigate and develop a plan to

designate the entire downtown area a historic district at a latter date.<sup>60</sup>

Even though developer Robert Shonkwiler was allowed to proceed with his development plans, he objected to the attempts to designate that block a historic district. He viewed the attempts as interference and believed he should be able to do whatever he wants with his property. In February 1989, Shonkwiler even pondered suing Historic Boulder for construction cost delays over the matter. Instead, Shonkwiler decided to build an additional two-story brick building that would house retail shops and offices. To do that, two turn-of-the-century frame houses would first have to be demolished. At the time, small businesses were located in the two houses—Johnnie Pirate's sandwich shop, Le Parterre Florist and Mysterium. Last fall, city planner Peter Pollock had researched the history of the houses located on the 900 block of Pearl Street. Pollock discovered that some prominent historical figures of Boulder once resided in those houses. For instance, at 935 Pearl Street Boulder's first automobile dealer, Sawyer Downing Clark, use to reside and Boulder's third fire chief, Emil Johnson, once called 941 Pearl Street home. Despite the historic character of these houses, Shonkwiler proceeded with his development plans. To Shonkwiler these buildings were only old not historic.<sup>61</sup> Thus, the historical integrity of that block was destroyed.

While the two frame houses on west Pearl Street were being destroyed, two other turn-of-the-century houses located on Pine Street were being saved. Instead of tearing down houses that lay in the way of new development or progress—Hotel Boulderado owner Frank Day chose to donate and relocate two historic houses before proceeding with his plans to expand the Hotel Boulderado. Day's expansion plans included building 50 additional hotel rooms, creating new banquet and conference space and providing additional underground

---

<sup>60</sup>Camera, 24 July 1988 and 1, 13 September 1988 and Colorado Daily, 23-25 September 1988 (Boulder Planning Board file).

<sup>61</sup>Camera, 19 February 1989; Colorado Daily, 17-19 February 1989 (Land Use file); Camera, 13 September 1988 (Boulder Planning Board file).

parking spaces for at least 26 cars. The city planning board granted Day permission to expand the Hotel Boulderado in July 1988. In April 1989, two houses dating back to the turn-of-the-century were uprooted and moved to other locations in Boulder to make way for the hotel expansion. The Queen Anne style house at 1204 Pine Street and the Victorian style house at 1212 Pine Street were the two houses relocated. Ryberg Construction moved the Queen Anne style house to the corner of Fourth Street and Arapahoe Avenue. The move took place early Thursday morning on April 6, 1989. Day not only donated the house to Historic Boulder and Wonderland Custom Builder Inc. of Boulder, but he also helped pay for part of the moving expenses. Historic Boulder and Wonderland Custom Builders planned to renovate the house and build a 1,000 square-foot addition, then sell the house. Profits from the sale were split between the two parties. The Queen Anne style house was to serve as a model for future housing developments in the new housing project being built by Wonderland along West Arapahoe Lane. The following week, Ryberg Construction moved the Victorian style house to the Naropa Institute campus on Arapahoe Avenue. Naropa planned on using the house for its administrative offices.<sup>62</sup>

In May 1990, council voted 6 to 3 in favor of granting the Alpha Phi Sorority house at 888 13th Street historic landmark status despite objections from neighbors. Neighbors claimed that the owner only wanted historic status in order to increase the occupancy limit. At that time, the current occupancy limit was 23, while the historical maximum occupancy limit of that house was 60. Neighbors feared that raising the occupancy limit would only increase traffic, parking conflicts and noise in that neighborhood. The owner, June Holmes, contended that the application for historic landmark status was submitted because architect Glen Huntington, a renowned Colorado architect, designed the house. Huntington also designed both the Boulder High School and the Boulder County Courthouse. According to a May 3, 1990 article in the Colorado Daily, the historical landmark designation was no

---

<sup>62</sup>Camera, 1, 7, 13 April 1989 (Architecture Conservation Restoration file).

guarantee that the occupancy limit would be increased. Holmes would have to apply to the planning board to increase the occupancy limit of the home. In addition, just prior to designating the sorority house a historic landmark, council passed a motion (ordinance #5285) establishing standards and processes for the restoration of non-conforming uses in landmark structures. Thus, making Holmes' case to change the occupancy limit more difficult. In fact, the Boulder Planning Board ruled against the occupancy expansion in August 1990. Holmes planned to appeal that decision, but later withdrew the appeal for the time being. The appeal was withdrawn since there was no immediate need to increase the occupancy limit since many of the sorority sisters had already signed leases to live elsewhere.<sup>63</sup>

---

<sup>63</sup>Colorado Daily, 3 May 1990 (City Council file) and 3 October 1990 (Zoning file).

## CHAPTER 5 CULTURAL AND OUTDOOR RECREATION

This chapter explores the city's efforts to improve the quality of life for Boulder residents, both culturally and recreationally. Four ways in which the city attempted to improve the quality of life included promoting the arts, expanding the services of the Boulder Public Library, preserving open space and creating new parks and recreational facilities. The city promoted the arts namely through funding. Artists or arts groups were awarded grants or received funding for operational expenses. But the city was not alone in promoting the arts, citizens associations and area residents also took part. For example, the citizens association, Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder, established the Neodata Endowment Fund to support arts groups annually. By expanding the services of the Boulder Public Library and its branches, the city enhanced the cultural activities and knowledge of the Boulder community. Some of the service expansions of the library during this period include computerizing its catalog, offering residents access to the library catalog from home and building a new east branch library and an addition to the main library downtown. By preserving and protecting open space land, the city not only created a buffer around the city, but also preserved Boulder's scenic surroundings for present and future generations. Building new parks or making improvements to existing Boulder Parks and Recreation facilities increased the recreational opportunities for Boulder residents.

The city's efforts to improve the quality of life for Boulder residents did not always come easy and in some instances the city failed. Failed efforts include the city's attempt to build a cultural center and a community park in north Boulder. Challenging but successful efforts by the city include building the new main library addition, making improvements to Chautauqua Park and preserving open space land despite the financial troubles of the Boulder Open Space program. In fact, the financial situation of the Boulder Open Space program nearly cost the program's demise, but the importance of protecting and preserving open space prevailed. The financial troubles were corrected not only by a refinancing plan, but also through a voter approved sales tax increase. Another example of how important it

was to the city to make Boulder a better place to live can be seen in the city's decision not to cut the parks department flower care budget despite the financial troubles of the city in 1987 and 1988. Each year 8 percent of the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department budget was reserved for flower care throughout the city. Besides beauty, the flowers had a positive psychological effect on people according to Park Superintendent Ron Donahue. Therefore, the city decided to save the flower fund and cut the budget for the Volunteer Boulder County program instead for 1988.<sup>1</sup> Let's look in more detail at the city's efforts to improve Boulder residents' quality of life between 1985 and 1990.

### **Visual and Performing Arts**

In April 1985, Boulder voters were asked to approve a \$11.9 million cultural center proposal that would be financed by a 0.1 percent city sales tax increase. The sales tax increase would be in effect for 13 1/2 years—July 1, 1985 through December 31, 1998. The cultural center proposal entailed constructing a new 65,000 square-foot performing arts building on the northeast corner block between 13th and 14th Streets, Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe Avenue, buying and renovating the Midland Federal Savings and Loan Building at 1495 Canyon Boulevard to create 10,000 square-feet of exhibit and meeting room space and also renovating the remaining 4,250 square-feet of the second floor of the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts building located 1750 13th Street. The performing arts building would cater to smaller performing acts. Inside, the new building would offer a 800-seat performing arts hall, an experimental theater with a moveable stage and seating accommodations for 50 to 225 people and three smaller rehearsal performing spaces that would seat 100 to 200 people. There would also be space for offices, dressing rooms, a small reception room and workshops for costume and scenery. The Cultural Center Steering Committee put forth the cultural center proposal. That committee was made up of

---

<sup>1</sup>Camera, 16 October 1987.

representatives from the arts and business community, city council and the University of Colorado. This was the third time Boulder voters were faced with the issue of creating a cultural center. Two previous cultural center proposals had failed—once in 1970 and again in 1980. The 1970 proposal was perceived as too costly and grand in scale, while the 1980 proposal was too elitist and confusing. This new 1985 cultural center proposal put before voters was more broad based in that it would offer not only performing arts space, but also rooms for classes, conferences and meetings as well as exhibit space, all of which were badly needed. The city did have the use of Macky Auditorium for 16 days a year and other University of Colorado facilities like the Old Main Chapel and the Events Center for 75 days a year in exchange for the city's \$375,000 contribution towards the renovations of Macky Auditorium. However, both the Macky and other university facilities were often too large or unsuitable for a particular event. If the proposal was passed, the city intended to conduct a nationwide search for a center director. Instead of creating a new city department, the Boulder Public Library would oversee the cultural center since the library already handled other city arts activities.<sup>2</sup>

Prior to the election, concerns regarding the cultural center proposal were voiced. One of the concerns was over the number of parking spaces. Only 150 parking spaces were planned as part of the cultural center project—100 spaces below grade level and 50 surface spaces. However, the expected patronage of the cultural center was 1500 visitors on weekends and 50 to 100 on weekdays. This discrepancy in the number of parking spaces planned and the expected patronage demonstrated poor planning, considering the fact that parking availability downtown was already a problem. Proponents of the 150 parking spaces plan conjectured that patrons would walk, bus or bicycle to the cultural center. Plus, there were 1,700 other parking spaces downtown that were just a three minute walk from the proposed cultural center and another 5,780 parking spaces within a five minute walk.

---

<sup>2</sup>Ibid., 18 March 1985 and 10 February 1985 and 21 April 1985.

Opponents questioned whether patrons would actually bicycle or bus to attend a concert at the cultural center.

Another concern voiced was in regards to the estimated cost of buying and renovating the Midland Savings and Loan building. Councilman Phil Stern questioned the cost estimates of the proposal. The cultural center proposal estimated the cost of purchasing and renovating the Midland property to be \$1.6 million. The building and property had been unofficially assessed at \$1.6 million by the Boulder County Deputy Assessor's Office. Stern was concerned that the purchase price might end up being \$1.5 or \$1.6 million, which would leave little or no money for the renovations. Renovation costs were estimated to be \$400,000 to \$600,000. Clouding the issue even more was the fact that the city had not yet negotiated a buying price for the Midland property. Without knowing the sale price of the property, how could the cost figures to complete the cultural center project be ascertained? Other city officials were less worried than Councilman Stern. In fact, City Manager Jim Piper was certain that the city would be able to obtain the Midland property for considerably less than the assessor's estimate. In the end, the cost issue of buying and renovating the Midland building or the lack of parking spaces did not matter. Boulder voters defeated the measure by voting down the cultural center proposal 6,776 votes to 3,921.<sup>3</sup>

Following the defeat of the cultural center proposal at the polls, Councilman Phil Stern requested the city account for the money it spent on planning the cultural center proposal and election. Stern was an active opponent of cultural center. Council directed the city manager to ascertain the amount of money spent. Upon review, the city of Boulder spent \$91,619 of the \$100,000 set aside in 1984 for the cultural center proposal. Council decided to give the remaining funds—\$8,381—to theater groups. Attempts by Councilman Spense Havlick to alter council's decision and allow all arts groups to compete for the remaining

---

<sup>3</sup>Ibid., 21 April 1985 and 1 May 1985.

funds were denied. Based on the recommendations of the Boulder Arts Commission, council awarded \$7,381 to the Boulder Theater Producers Consortium and the other remaining \$1,000 to the Nomad Players, Inc.<sup>4</sup>

Other arts groups turned to city council for aide following the defeat of the cultural center. In August 1985, the Boulder Center for the Visual Arts asked the city for \$40,000 to help renovate its building for exhibit space. The group intended to match the city's aide. The director of Upstart Crow Theater Company made a second request for funds. That request was for \$10,000 to \$15,000 to help theater groups rent performance space. Council saw the need for performance space in greater need than exhibit space. Therefore, council decided to grant \$15,000 to performing arts groups to rent performance space.<sup>5</sup>

In the fall of 1985, various theater groups in Boulder joined forces and formed the Boulder Theatre Producers Guild. Theater groups joining forces to form the guild included The Boulder Repertory Theatre, Upstart Crow Co. and the Boulder Civic Opera. The mission of the guild was to raise money for leasing performance space, hence finding an alternative to the failed cultural center. The persistent lack of performance space in Boulder resulted in theater groups booking theatrical productions wherever space could be found. For instance, the Boulder City Council chambers, the Southern Hills Junior High School and even a vacant store at 10th and Walnut Streets were utilized for performance space. The Upstart Crow Theater at 5853 Rawhide Court was forced to shut down for violating Boulder County Fire Codes in October 1985. This caused a further lack of performance space. The theater situation turned hopeful when funding for leasing performance space was made available. As indicated earlier, the city awarded theater groups \$15,000 for renting performance space. The Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder also contributed funds—

---

<sup>4</sup>Ibid., 22, 29 May 1985 and 5, 19 June 1985.

<sup>5</sup>Ibid., 23 August 1985 and 24, 27 September 1985.

\$3,300. With funds now available, the guild started searching for sites to lease performance space.

In December 1985, two theater sites were under consideration by the Boulder Theatre Producers Guild. One site under consideration was 4,000 square-feet of space in the second story of a building located at 1111 Pearl Street and the other site was a 3,600 square-foot warehouse under construction on Sterling Drive near 47th and Pearl Streets. The warehouse site on Sterling Drive was chosen over the Pearl Street site because the warehouse site had less restrictions and lease conditions. The Pearl Street site offered only a 10-month lease maximum with a 90-day vacancy clause if the building was ever sold. In comparison, the guild was able to obtain a three-year lease at \$5 per square-foot per year for the warehouse site. However, before the Boulder Theatre Producers Guild could rent the warehouse space, the city had to grant permission to operate a theater in an industrial zone. In March 1986, city council approved the producers guild's application for a zoning variance. Theaters would be allowed in areas zoned industrial. The Boulder Theatre Producers Guild planned to transform the warehouse into two theaters, both with a seating capacity of 100.<sup>6</sup> The city took other action to promote the arts, namely through the city agency known as the Boulder Arts Commission.

The Boulder Arts Commission, established in 1979 to increase public awareness and support for the arts, continued its tradition of funding local artists of all disciplines of art—visual, literary and performing. In February 1985, the Boulder Arts Commission recommended to council awarding 38 individual artists or artistic groups grants. Total grant money awarded was \$63,000. The following year, Councilman Phil Stern questioned the way in which the city awards the arts grants. Stern objected to the commission's procedure of reviewing grant applications. Evidently, the Boulder Arts Commission was not in habit of

---

<sup>6</sup>Ibid., 5 December 1985; 18 February 1986; 5 March 1986.

informing artists when the commission would be reviewing the artist's application for the grant. City council concurred with Stern's objections and as a result the process of awarding grants was changed. Council directed the Boulder Arts Commission to inform applicants when their projects would be reviewed. In addition, council asked that the Boulder Arts Commission justify its grant recommendations. The city awarded 35 grants to artists in 1986. Stern was one of the 106 grant applicants that year, but his proposal was not awarded funding.

The city's financial crunch in 1987 affected the budget of the art community that year. The 1987 budget of the Boulder Arts Commission totaled \$50,155, less than half of the commission's 1986 budget. After all other operating expenses and regular allocations were distributed, the commission would have approximately \$10,000 to spend on the city arts grants. Therefore, instead of dispersing grants directly to individual artists and artistic groups, the commission chose to channel the available funds into general outreach programs in order to promote the arts as a whole. The commission chose this course of action to avoid creating dissension among local artists and to continue carrying out its mission of promoting the development and public interest of art in Boulder.<sup>7</sup> In 1988, the city was able to return to its usual procedure of dispersing money for the arts. Grants to individual artists and groups were awarded in March 1988. A total of \$60,000 in arts grants were given to 32 artists or groups that year. In 1990, the Boulder Arts Commission recommended that 47 of the 69 grant applicants be awarded grant money. Total grant money awarded that year was \$60,000.<sup>8</sup>

Besides the city, citizens associations helped to promote and finance the arts in

---

<sup>7</sup>Camera, 8 February 1985 and 5 February 1986 and 21 December 1986.

<sup>8</sup>Camera, 2 March 1988 and Public Hearing Motion, 20 February 1990 (Boulder City Council Boards and Commissions-Arts Advisory Commission file).

Boulder. The Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder (AHAB) was one such citizens association. In January 1985, Neodata Services donated its former building at 1255 Portland Place to the Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder. Neodata Services had moved its computerized magazine subscription services to Louisville, Colorado in 1983. Rather than convert the building into a performing arts center, the Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder intended to sell the building and establish an endowment fund for the arts with the proceeds from the sale. The lack of parking and the actual design of the Neodata building made the facility unsuitable for a performing arts center. The instigators behind this deal between Neodata and AHAB were Kurt Burghardt, president of Neodata and Bill Cohen, president of AHAB. The 40,000 square-foot building was appraised at \$2.8 million, thus creating an annual endowment fund of approximately \$250,000. The appraised value was based on the building's development potential. According to the agreement between Cohen and Burghardt, 60 percent of the annual proceeds was uncommitted and was to be dispersed at AHAB's discretion. Thirty percent of the endowment was earmarked for three organizations: the Colorado Music Festival, the radio station KGNU and the Philharmonic Society of Boulder. Each of those organizations would divide the 30 percent of funds equally, thus each would receive 10 percent. The remaining 10 percent of the endowment was to go towards AHAB's operating expenses. The first disbursement of funds from the sale of the Neodata building was expected sometime in 1987.<sup>9</sup>

Although the sale of the Neodata building would bring considerable funding for the arts, not all members of AHAB were pleased with the distribution of funds deal and questioned why those three organizations were guaranteed an equal 10 percent share of the funds. Some AHAB members believed the appointed board selected by Cohen to disburse the money favored certain art groups, specifically music groups. Dissatisfied over how the endowment money was to be distributed led some AHAB members, particularly theater

---

<sup>9</sup>Camera, 18 January 1985.

interest members, to oust AHAB President Bill Cohen from his presidency in October 1985. Leading the dissension to eject Cohen from his position was Carla Selby, Boulder Civic Opera President. At the annual AHAB board meeting Cohen was voted out, despite the fact that he was the one who negotiated the deal with Neodata Services, which created the endowment in the first place. Roger Davidson was elected president of AHAB.<sup>10</sup>

In the fall of 1986, the Neodata building was sold for \$1.5 million to the medical health organization Kaiser Permanete. The first allotment of proceed money from the sale was dispersed in November 1987. A total of \$81,158 was distributed. As stipulated in the terms of the endowment agreement, the Philharmonic Society of Boulder, KGNU public radio and the Colorado Music Festival each received 10 percent of the endowment. A portion of the funds went towards the operating expenses of AHAB, approximately \$20,000. The remaining funds were then distributed among the selected grant applicants.<sup>11</sup> Each year, the Arts and Humanities Assembly of Boulder County continues to distribute grants to artists and arts organizations from the Neodata Endowment.

The stone sculpture of Chief Niwot located along the banks of Boulder Creek near Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard was nearly placed in Denver. However, thanks to a group of Boulder residents, the stone statue of the Southern Arapaho Indian Chief Niwot, who greeted the first settlers in the Boulder area back in 1858, remained in Boulder. The group of Boulder residents organized and called themselves the Citizens Art Committee in March 1985, after the city rejected offers to purchase the sculpture from local artist Tom Miller for \$12,500. The artist Miller worked on the sculpture outside along the banks of Boulder Creek near Ninth Street from late October 1984 until late March 1985. Miller ended up selling the statue to the Citizens Art Committee for less than the original asking price

---

<sup>10</sup>Ibid., 23 June 1985 and 3, 10 November 1985 (Citizens Associations–AHAB file).

<sup>11</sup>Ibid., 3 September 1986 and 14 November 1987.

because of the time and material donated for the project by Boulder residents. Once the Citizens Art Committee raised funds to purchase the statue, the city offered to pay for a permanent base for the sculpture. The sandstone statue of Chief Niwot was placed into the permanent stone base along the banks of Boulder Creek near Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard on February 26, 1986. It was fitting that the sculpture of Chief Niwot found a home in Boulder near the creek not only because Chief Niwot welcomed the first settlers in this area and would winter camp along Boulder Creek, but also because of the alleged quote attributed to him commonly called "Niwot's Curse," which loosely states that once you see Boulder, you'll never want to leave.<sup>12</sup>

In 1990, a group of local artists and art supporters organized in hope of raising money to buy and renovate the old Watts-Hardy Dairy building located at 2590 Walnut Street to house the Flatirons Center for the Arts. Organizers and founders of the Flatirons Center for the Arts group were D.J. Donovan-Johnson and Russ Wiltse. Plans for the arts center included a 700-seat theater with a raked floor, a 300-seat theater, art gallery, two dance rehearsal halls, two arts classrooms, a music practice room, a shop to sell the works of local artists, a tea room and restaurant. The estimated cost of buying and renovating the Watts Hardy facility was \$4 million. Joining the crusade was actress Joan Van Ark. She pledged \$10,000 towards the Flatirons Center for the Arts project. Van Ark grew up in Boulder and had performed locally with the Nomad Players theater group before working on Broadway and becoming a Hollywood actress. To raise additional money and to ascertain public support for the Flatirons Center for the Arts, the group held a 10-day campaign event in the fall of 1990. The group had hoped to raise \$50,000, but only \$17,000 was raised. If the \$50,000 had been raised the group would have hired a firm to conduct a fund raising campaign to raise the needed money for the project. Instead, the group used the money raised to conduct a feasibility study. Enough money had been raised to keep the dream of the

---

<sup>12</sup>Camera, 6 August 1985 and 27 February 1986 and Times-Call, 26 March 1985.

Flatirons Center for the Arts alive.<sup>13</sup> The dream of buying and renovating the Watts Hardy Dairy building eventually became a reality, after years of struggling to secure the financing for the facility.

### HISTORY OF THE DAIRY CENTER FOR THE ARTS

**May 1990:** Organizers announce plans to convert the Watts-Hardy dairy plant into the Flatirons Center for the Arts. Projected cost: \$2 million to \$4 million. Joan Van Ark, former resident and TV star of "Knots Landing," pledges \$10,000.

**September 1990:** 10-day fund-raiser, Jump Start the Arts, held. Proceeds go to feasibility study.

**February 1991:** Study indicates \$3 million could be raised.

**November 1991:** Developer Amory Host, president of Peak Properties Inc., submits plans for an 63-unit apartment complex on half the site. Sinton Dairies will donate the other half to the center.

**January 1992:** Part of the building subleased to Space for Dance, which puts \$40,000 into renovation and begins classes.

**January 1992:** Host's plan approved. Instead of \$2 million or more to buy the entire property, the center gets the land free and needs to raise only \$750,000 for the building by Dec. 31. Boulder City Council agrees to enter a 90-day option to buy the building.

**February 1993:** From sales tax proceeds earmarked for the arts, the city contributes \$150,000, to be matched by the center within a month. The center has 18 more months to raise the additional \$450,000 needed to buy the building.

**April 1993:** The center pays \$300,000 toward cost of building. The city decides to pay a bridge loan of \$450,000 for the rest, and provides up to \$100,000 for safety improvements. Director Russ Wilse says the center intends to raise \$450,000 to repay the city and \$400,000 more for renovations.

**December 1994:** Wilse says the center, now called the Dairy Center for the Arts, has raised \$800,000, half in cash, the rest in in-kind donations and pledges to be paid over the coming years.

**January 1995:** The Dairy has spent only \$64,000 of the \$100,000 the city provided for renovations. The City Council agrees to forgive this loan and to transfer ownership of the Dairy building to the Dairy board at the end of March. The \$450,000 will be repaid over three years. The Dairy also intends to raise \$3.5 million to enlarge and remodel the building.

(Daily Camera, 2 April 1995)

---

<sup>13</sup>Camera, 12 July 1990; Colorado Daily 17-19 July 1990; Camera 23 September 1990 and 10 October 1990.

### **Boulder Public Library**

Advances in technology enabled the Boulder Public Library to expand its services to the public, for both the seeing and the blind. In February 1985, the Boulder Public Library obtained a computerized Braille printer through a grant from the Library Services and Construction Act. The \$22,000 grant also paid for a 10-hour a week employee from the federal Library Services and Construction funds to instruct the library on how to use the new equipment. By obtaining this computerized Braille printer, Boulder Public Library became the first public agency west of the Mississippi River to own such equipment. Additional technological advances came to the library the following year. In February 1986, the Boulder Public Library computerized its card catalog file. The days of the old index cards were over. The new computerized card catalog made searching for library materials easier and faster for patrons. In July 1986, the library offered patrons the luxury of searching the library catalog from home. Three phone lines for home computers to hook up via modem with the library's computer system were set up so patrons could access the library's holdings from home. A \$1,000 grant from the Boulder Public Library Foundation helped pay for the modem and three phone lines necessary for patrons to access the new Dial-PAC system. Patrons could not only access the Boulder Public Library's catalog, but also the catalogs of both the Louisville and Broomfield libraries. Anyone interested in accessing the Dial-PAC system needed to fill out an application form to gain the dialup phone number. As of January 1987, 568 patrons had filled out applications, but library Assistant Director Rick Luce suspected many more people had learned of the dial up number via word of mouth.<sup>14</sup>

The library's efforts to computerize its catalog and to allow patrons access to other library systems besides that of the Boulder Public Library did not come easy. For several years the library attempted to offer the public computerized access to its holdings, but the

---

<sup>14</sup>Camera, 17 February 1985; 14 February 1986; 13 July 1986; 13 January 1987.

computer company Data Phase Corp. located near Kansas City, Missouri failed to deliver the computer hardware and software that functioned as promised. In June 1985, the library severed its contract ties with Data Phase Corp., thus ending six years of frustrations and computer troubles. An undisclosed settlement was reached between the city and the computer company, whereby the library was free to search for another computer company that could deliver a more powerful computer hardware system that functioned as promised. Negotiations with another computer manufacturer, Eyring Research Inc. of Provo, Utah, were taking place at the time the city was severing its ties with Data Phase. As indicated in the settlement, the city funds allocated in 1984 to upgrade the computer hardware system belonging to Data Phase were retained by the city. The city was also allowed to keep and sell the computers and other hardware delivered by Data Phase. The computer software was to be returned to Data Phase. However, until the library completed the negotiations with another computer manufacturer for more powerful computers and a software program that would give the library access to the Colorado Association of Research Libraries system (CARL), the library continued to use Data Phase hardware and software.<sup>15</sup>

Rick Luce, assistant director of the library, was the one instrumental in automating the library check-out system, computerizing the library's catalog and allowing patrons remote access to the library's collection. However, within just a few years of achieving these accomplishments, Luce took a job in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, as executive director of the Southeast Florida Library Information Network. In February 1989, Randy Smith replaced Rick Luce as the assistant director of the Boulder Public Library. Luce's successor, Randy Smith continued working on modifying and expanding the library's computer system known as IRVING.<sup>16</sup>

---

<sup>15</sup>Ibid., 16 June 1985.

<sup>16</sup>Ibid., 2 February 1989.

Advances in technology were not the only way the library expanded its services. In March 1985, the Carnegie Branch Library located at 1125 Pine Street made an agreement with the Boulder Historical Society to house the society's old photograph collection. The Carnegie Branch Library agreed not only to housing the society's photograph collection, but also to sorting, cleaning, mending and indexing the collection. This arrangement not only enabled researchers easier and better access to the society's collection, but also enhanced the holdings of the Carnegie Library as well. Carnegie's mission of preserving and gathering historical resources pertinent to Boulder history for the public was aided by adding this photograph collection to the library's holdings. In 1987, the Carnegie Branch Library was awarded a two-year grant of \$85,000 to organize documents and photos stored at the library from the National Historical Publications and Records Commission of the National Archives. Key reasons why the grant was awarded included the value of the collection, particularly the photograph collection of the Boulder Historical Society, the size of the Carnegie collection and the library's plans to catalog its collection on a statewide computer network. The grant money awarded was to be used to hire an archivist that would process and enter the Carnegie collection into the computer system. Thus, making it easier for patrons to access the holdings of the Carnegie Branch Library.<sup>17</sup> Organizing and cataloging the Carnegie collection was not the only way the library sought to make its collection available.

In December 1985, the main Boulder Public Library announced plans to develop a volunteer literacy program, whereby trained volunteers would offer free and confidential instruction to adults wanting to learn how to read and write. The literacy program helped pave the way for all members of the Boulder community to have access to the library's collection. The literacy program was initiated by the library volunteer coordinator Nancy Morgan and Boulder resident Virginia French Allen, who was a former professor of Temple

---

<sup>17</sup>Ibid., 1 March 1985 and 8 July 1987.

University and an internationally renowned specialist in literacy. In 1986, the library was awarded a \$25,000 federal grant for the literacy program, which came to be called the Learning to Read program. The grant money awarded helped to finance a part-time literacy coordinator and to install a Boulder County Literacy Hotline. Diane Sherry was selected as the literacy coordinator. As of March 1989, the literacy program had helped as many as 143 people learn to read since the program began.<sup>18</sup>

A change in service occurred at the library in December 1988. That winter, the library announced that it would no longer carry federal tax forms. According to Reference Librarian Judy Waller, carrying the tax forms put an unacceptable strain on the traditional library services as well as the staff. The public would ask for assistance filling out their tax forms, but library staff were neither tax experts nor were they suppose to give out tax advice. The library would still carry tax-preparation guidebooks and IRS publications.<sup>19</sup>

Efforts to expand library services continued. What better way to improve on the library's services than by expanding the library building itself? Talk of expanding the Boulder Public Library located at 1000 Canyon Boulevard first surfaced in 1986, as part of the city's proposed election for a capital fund improvements package. This proposed \$22.7 million capital improvements package entailed not only the expansion of the library, but also funding for new city public safety buildings including a fire training facility and police station and a proposal for a third recreational facility and a new senior citizen building. When this capital improvements package was under consideration, a debate of where to build the library expansion quickly ensued. The Boulder Library Commission recommended building the library expansion just to the west of the current building on Canyon Boulevard despite the flood dangers. The lower cost of the expansion there and the proximity to the

---

<sup>18</sup>Ibid., 19 December 1985; 28 September 1986; 22 April 1987; 23 March 1989.

<sup>19</sup>Colorado Daily, 21 December 1988.

downtown area and other municipal buildings played a factor in the Library Commission's decision. City council, on the other hand, was more reluctant to build the library expansion in the floodplain. The contention of where to build the library expansion became a mute point temporarily when the election for the capital improvements package was canceled. City Manager James Piper recommended not holding the election to council since sales tax revenues were less than projected. After sometime, council finally adhered to Piper's advice to cancel the election in August 1986.<sup>20</sup>

The financial situation of the city was just too grave. In fact, the decline in the city sales tax revenue forced city council to cut back every city departmental budget in 1987. The library's \$2.5 million budget was cut by \$125,000. As a result, the library's hours of operation were reduced along with the library's budget to purchase new books and other materials. The record decline in city sales tax revenue had in fact impacted the library as early as the fall of 1986. That fall the library was unable to resume its Sunday hours. Typically, the library closed on Sundays during the summer and was open 7 days a week the rest of the year following Labor Day, but because of the financial situation of the city, the library remained closed on Sundays for the rest of 1986. Public complaints about the library being closed on Sundays prompted the Boulder Library Commission to change the day of the week in which the library would be closed in 1987. The main library at 1000 Canyon Boulevard would be closed on Fridays instead of Sundays. Hours of operation of the two library branches were cut as well in 1987. The George Reynolds Branch and the Carnegie Library remained closed on Sundays and six additional hours of operation were cut. In April 1987, the library indicated it would have to close on Sundays in addition to Fridays during the summer due to budget constraints.<sup>21</sup>

---

<sup>20</sup>Camera, 26 June 1986; Denver Post, 14, 15 July 1986; Camera, 6 August 1986.

<sup>21</sup>Camera, 1 January 1987 and 30 April 1987.

Although the election for the capital improvements was canceled, the idea of expanding the library did not dissipate. Upon learning that the election had been canceled, some Library Commission members vowed not to abandon the idea of expanding the library. With the budget cutbacks and slashing of hours, the library was not meeting the needs of the public it served. In 1987, a group organized to push for an increase in library funding to finance the expansion. The group intended to start a petition that supported holding an election to increase the city's sales tax for the library expansion. The increase in sales tax would generate funds for operating and maintaining the three libraries, plus finance the expansion of the main library and possibly another branch library. The group pushing for the sales tax election and the library expansion was known as the Citizens for the Library. Leading the Citizens for the Library group was Alice McDonald and Doris Hass. Council objected to the library group's petition plans. Council asked if the group would hold off its petition drive until the city had time to develop a tax finance package of its own, one that would include other city improvement projects along with the library. Citizens for the Library agreed to hold off its petition drive until March 17, 1987.<sup>22</sup>

Boulder City Council's opposition to a sales tax increase for just the library as the Citizens for the Library was advocating, resulted in months of debate. Since city council took no definitive action by March 17, the Citizens for the Library started circulating a petition to secure a spot on the November ballot for a 0.4 percent sales tax increase with \$19.5 million in bonding authority. The bonds would finance not only the expansion of the main library, but also the building of other library branches in east and possibly north Boulder. By June 1987, the Citizens for the Library had acquired enough signatures on the petition to force the issue and ensure future funding for the library. Since February, attempts to reach a compromise decision with council had either failed or council took no action to delay the issue. A compromise was eventually reached in the end, but not before council

---

<sup>22</sup>Ibid., 10, 11, 13 February 1987.

threatened placing a second sales tax increase on the November ballot, one that would fund a variety of projects besides the expansion of the library. Council eventually decided against taking that course of action. Placing two competing sales tax increases on the same ballot would, in all likelihood, result in the failure of both measures.<sup>23</sup>

Now that the petition had been turned in, council had to act. At its July 1987 meeting, city council voted 7 to 2 in favor of placing only one sales tax increase issue on the November ballot. Council approved a compromise sales tax increase of 0.38 percent instead of the 0.4 percent. The 0.38 percent sales tax increase had been suggested to council some months back by the library supporters, but council had delayed acting on the measure until the issue was forced. City council imposed two amendments to the November ballot issue regarding the library. One amendment prohibited the main library from expanding westward into the floodplain. The second amendment set a sunset clause on the sales tax for the library to the year 2011. The Citizens for the Library supported the compromise ballot issue of 0.38 percent sales tax increase and agreed to council's two amendments. Even though a compromise had finally been reached, council remained at odds over earmarking the sales tax increase for just the library. In August 1987, a second compromise between council and the Citizens for the Library was proposed and reached. A second ballot issue would appear on the November ballot asking voters whether or not all the money generated from the sales tax increase should be earmarked for the library alone. Boulder voters decided the issue in November. Voters approved the 0.38 percent sales tax increase, but not earmarking the funds from the sales tax increase for just the library.<sup>24</sup>

A few months before the election, the Boulder Library Commission sent council a memo indicating the library may close on Sundays all year-round in 1988. The Boulder

---

<sup>23</sup>Ibid., 15, 18, 31 March 1987 and 19 June 1987.

<sup>24</sup>Ibid., 8 July 1987; 6 May 1987; 5 August 1987; 4 November 1987.

Library Commission had to decide between cutting library hours or book purchases to balance the 1988 library budget. They opted to close the library on Sundays instead of cutting funds for book purchases. It was hinted before and after the election that the Boulder Library Commission's announcement that the library may close on Sundays year-round was a ploy to ensure voters approved the sales tax increase in November. Following the election, the library's normal hours were restored in 1988. The main library was open seven days a week once again and the hours of the library branches that had been cut in 1987 were also restored. The George Reynolds Branch Library resumed its Sunday hours and the Carnegie Library resumed its Monday hours, 11-5pm, as well.<sup>25</sup>

Now that Boulder voters had approved the sales tax increase to help finance a \$12.6 million expansion of the main library, the question arose as to where the new library extension should be built. Following the election, a site-selection committee, comprised of Library Director Marcelee Gralapp, members of the Library Commission, Boulder City Council members Bob Greenlee and Spense Havlick, Public Works Director Dave Rhodes and Boulder resident Harold Short, was organized. The city hired a consultant to narrow the list of library sites compiled by the site-selection committee. The consultant hired was Nolan Rosall of the Rosall Remmen Cares Consulting Firm. The site-selection committee and the consultant were to evaluate the sites based on certain criteria including lot size, price, visibility and proximity to library users and then pass their recommendations onto council. Council was to make the final decision based on those recommendations. By late February 1988, the site-selection committee and the consultant narrowed the list of sites to seven. Four of the sites were in the downtown area and the other three were in the area between downtown and the Crossroads Mall. One of the four downtown locations was on Arapahoe Avenue south of the main library. That list of seven was then narrowed further to only four sites. The final list of the four recommended sites did not include a site near the existing

---

<sup>25</sup>Ibid., 28 August 1987; 20 December 1987; 3 January 1988.

main library. As a result, a group of citizens organized in May 1988 to support expanding the library at its current site along Boulder Creek.
