The group led by John Spitzer tentatively called themselves People to Expand the Boulder Creek Library. The name was later changed to Citizens to Expand the Boulder Creek Library. In addition to the library group, Councilman Steve Pomerance successfully passed a motion to reconsider the hotel land site at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard as a possible location for the library expansion. Now instead of four sites, there were six sites to be considered for the library expansion. The heightened public interest in selecting a site for the new library expansion led Mayor Linda Jourgensen to postpone city council's selection of a library site until July 1988.<sup>26</sup>

A site selection controversy ensued, which turned into a political battle. City departments and officials could not agree on where to build the new library addition. The Boulder Planning Department wished to keep the library in the downtown area and therefore favored the Arapahoe Avenue site. The City Manager Jim Piper, on the other hand, preferred a more eastern site like the Watts-Hardy Dairy land site near Walnut and 26th Streets. The Boulder Library Commission and Library Director Marcelee Gralapp were in concurrence with the city manager that the new library should be built east of downtown, but no specific eastern site was recommended. The consultant Nolan Rosall also recommended building the library east of downtown.<sup>27</sup> The final decision rested with city council.

Over the next few months, the six potential sites were narrowed to three. The three sites in the running included the following: the Watts-Hardy site near 26th and Walnut Streets, the Arapahoe Avenue site located just south of the existing library and the Bradfield site on the northwest corner of Folsom Avenue and Pearl Street. At its July 1988 meeting,

---

<sup>26</sup>Colorado Daily, 13 January 1988 and Camera, 27 February 1988 and 20, 26 May 1988.

<sup>27</sup>Camera, 23 June 1988 and 10 July 1988 and Colorado Daily, 1-4 July 1988.

council rejected the Arapahoe site by a 6 to 3 vote. Reasons why council dismissed the Arapahoe site included cost and location in a flood prone area. Council opted not to make a decision on the two remaining eastern sites at this meeting. That decision would be made at a later date. Council's decision to reject the Arapahoe site prompted the Citizens to Expand the Boulder Creek Library group to survey public opinion and challenge council's decision. The poll conducted by the group indicated the public was in favor of expanding the existing library along Arapahoe Avenue. Despite the poll results, council selected the Watts-Hardy site on August 2, 1988. Unwilling to let that decision stand, the Citizens to Expand the Boulder Creek Library began circulating petitions to ensure that an election would be held on the issue. The people would decide where the library expansion should be built. Former Mayor Ruth Correll and former Colorado Supreme Court Justice Jean Dubofsky joined the crusade to seek a public vote on the issue. In less than three weeks, the citizens group gathered enough signatures to force an election.<sup>28</sup> A special election to decide the matter was held December 6, 1988. Prior to the election, both sides attempted to sway voters to their point of view. Boulder voters selected to build the new Boulder Public Library addition at the Arapahoe site. The vote was 9,576 to 7,203.<sup>29</sup>

With the decision of where to build the library resolved, all that remained was to select an architect to design the new library, acquire the land along Arapahoe where the new addition to the library would be constructed and then build the new library. A five-member committee comprised of Assistant City Manager Dave Knapp, Planning Director Ed Gawf, Library Director Marcelee Gralapp and two architects had the task of selecting the architect to design the new library. In March 1989, the committee selected the Boulder architectural firm of Midyette/Seieroe and Associates to design the library addition. Eugene Aubry of

---

<sup>28</sup>Colorado Daily, 12-14 July 1988 and Camera, 16, 30 July 1988 and 11, 5, 29 August 1988.

<sup>29</sup>Camera, 14 November 1988 and 7 December 1988.

Aubry Architects of Sarasota, Florida was also chosen to work in conjunction with Midyette/Seieroe and Associates as a subcontractor. A contract finalizing the selection of Midyette/Seieroe and Associates to design the library addition was signed in April 1989. The city agreed to pay Midyette/Seieroe and Associates \$703,550 for their design and engineering services and another \$160,000 for renovating and floodproofing the existing library building. As stated in the contract, the design plans for the library addition were to be completed by April 9, 1990. The new addition would add roughly 40,000 square-feet to the current building—thus doubling the size of the library.<sup>30</sup>

During the architect selection process, Library Director Marcelee Gralapp distanced herself from the committee so not to influence their decision since she favored the local architectural firm of Midyette/Seieroe and Associates. Once the decision was made Gralapp became more involved in the project. Gralapp's increased involvement prompted Sally McVey, the project coordinator, to resign in April 1989, after only serving one year as the project coordinator. McVey had been hired in March 1988. McVey felt her authority over the construction of the library expansion had lessened with Gralapp's heightened involvement in the project. McVey's assistant, Chris Powers, took over as the project coordinator.<sup>31</sup>

Wanting public input on the new library addition design, the architects and city staff hosted meetings to gather ideas. The first of these meetings was held on May 8, 1989. Some of the ideas offered by the public included: maintaining the surrounding beauty of Boulder Creek and giving library patrons a view of the creek from inside the library, offering a drive-up window for returning books and adding a young adult book collection. At this meeting, the city expressed its plan to unify or integrate the city's downtown government complex

---

<sup>30</sup>Colorado Daily, 15 December 1988 and Camera 14 March 1989 and 26 April 1989.

<sup>31</sup>Camera, 19 April 1989 and 10 May 1989.

into a municipal-like campus and therefore wanted the new library to maintain architectural compatibility. City buildings bounded by Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe Avenue between Sixth and 14th Streets were to have a common identity and be connected by landscape and pathways marked with signs. A second meeting to welcome the public's ideas for the new library was held in July 1989. Sketches of the new library addition were displayed for public review and comment at this July meeting. Design plans for the new library addition presented included a stone entryway topped with a rounded glass structure, high ceilings and glass windows everywhere to convey an open, airy feeling and to make use of the natural lighting in order to reduce the use of artificial lighting. The heavy use of natural lighting was at first questioned by council. However, once city council found out that the daylighting or natural lighting plan was both energy and cost efficient, council approved the daylighting plan for the library in October 1989.<sup>32</sup>

Details of the library's design and renovations had to be decided and finalized prior to construction. In February 1990, the idea of doubling the size of the library auditorium planned for the renovated library was discussed. Instead of the 225-seat, flat floored auditorium planned, proponents for a larger auditorium proposed a 400-seat auditorium with a sloping floor. In addition, the proponents wanted the larger auditorium equipped with a better lighting and sound system. Cost estimates for enlarging and upgrading the auditorium was \$500,000. The question of where the extra money needed to enlarge and upgrade the auditorium would come from went unanswered. Opponents of this idea to enlarge the library's auditorium claimed the city was attempting to acquire the performing arts center, which the public had voted down in 1985. The decision to increase the size of the library auditorium rested with city council. Council rejected the notion of building a 400-seat auditorium by a 5 to 3 vote in March 1990. Council questioned the impact a 400-seat

---

<sup>32</sup>Colorado Daily, 8 May 1989; Camera, 9 May 1989 and 17, 18 July 1989 and 9 August 1989; Colorado Daily, 5 October 1989.

auditorium would have on downtown parking and also the danger a larger auditorium posed to the public since the auditorium sits in a high risk flood zone. Therefore, the plans for the 225-seat auditorium were to proceed as planned. Library Director Marcelee Gralapp was disappointed, but accepted council's decision. Gralapp hoped private donations would allow the library to improve on the auditorium's lighting and sound systems. Gralapp's hope was answered. Shortly after council made its decision, the design of the library's auditorium was improved thanks to \$100,000 gift from Nell Buckingham. The Library Commission voted to use Buckingham's generous gift to create an auditorium with a sloped floor and better acoustics.<sup>33</sup>

Another detail of the library project that had to be resolved was whether or not to include a drive-up book drop. Plans for a drive-up book drop were dropped from the original plans because of cost and traffic concerns. For those two reasons, the Boulder Planning Board rejected the drive-up book drop plan in October 1989. Instead of the drive-up book drop, a walk-up book drop with some close-in, short term parking nearby was to be built. However, in April 1990 council wished to address the issue again and have staff consider adding a drive-up book drop to the Arapahoe Avenue parking lot. In the end, the views of the Boulder Planning Board prevailed. There would only be a walk-up book drop, not a drive-up book drop.<sup>34</sup> Now that details of the library design were worked out, the construction of the library addition and renovations could begin.

To make way for the library expansion, the first of the five buildings along Arapahoe Avenue scheduled for demolition was torn down on March 27, 1990. The remaining four buildings were torn down over the next several weeks. Construction bids for the library addition were accepted in October 1990. Pinkard Construction Co. of Lakewood, Colorado

---

<sup>33</sup>Camera, 20 February 1990 and 21, 22 March 1990 and 24 May 1990.

<sup>34</sup>Ibid., 13 October 1989 and 4 April 1990.

was awarded the contract after offering the lowest bid of \$9.5 million. Attempts to delay the bidding process for the library expansion project by the Associated General Contractors failed. The Associated General Contractors claimed Boulder included an illegal clause in the contract that was forbidden by state law. The clause in question was "no damage for delay." Boulder District Judge Murray Richtel refused the Associated General Contractors request to delay the bid requests for the library expansion. The judge denied the request primarily because the complaint by the Associated General Contractors to stop the bidding process was late. The "no damage for delay" clause was known at least 3 weeks prior so action by the Associated General Contractors could have been taken earlier. Therefore, the last minute request to delay the bidding was denied by the judge. In addition, Boulder had passed an ordinance granting Boulder home rule power, thus allowing the city the right to include the "no damage for delay" clause in the library contract. The city awarded the contract to Pinkard Construction Co. despite the fact that the firm had been sued by the Boulder Valley School District in 1974 and 1978 over problems with the construction of the Fairview High School roof. The school roof not only leaked, but a portion of the roof had collapsed under a heavy December snowfall in 1978. News of the lawsuit did not waiver the library project coordinator's faith in the company.<sup>35</sup>

Work on constructing the new library addition got underway in the fall of 1990. On November 9, 1990, a ground breaking ceremony for the library expansion was held. The building of the new library addition was officially underway after years of planning, arranging finances, selecting a site and approving architectural designs. The new two-story library addition and basement were to be completed by March 1992. Renovation of the existing library building along Canyon Boulevard was to be completed by October 1992. Once the addition was completed, the Main Boulder Public Library would have an additional 53,585 square-feet of space—a 13,585 square-foot basement and 40,000 square-

---

<sup>35</sup>Ibid., 28 March 1990 and 11, 18, 21 October 1990.

foot two-story addition.<sup>36</sup> Clearly an improvement over what the old existing library had to offer.

In addition to financing the construction of the new addition to the main library, the 0.38 percent sales tax increase approved by voters in 1987 also helped finance the development of a new branch library in east Boulder and renovations to the existing George Reynolds Branch Library. The new east branch library was built in the new Meadows shopping complex. Developer Jim Loftus offered to build the 8,000 square-foot branch library back in 1987 as part of his plans to refurbish the Thunderbird Shopping Center located at Baseline Road and Foothills Parkway. Originally, Loftus intended to build a movie theater, but objections from neighboring Baseline residents convinced Loftus to alter his plans and build the branch library instead. Loftus donated the building and space for the new east branch library to the city. The city only had to finance the interior furnishings of the Meadows Branch Library. Money from the 0.38 percent city sales tax increase financed the interior construction of the new Meadows Branch Library. In June 1989, construction crews were busy working on the exterior and roof of the new branch library being built in the new Meadows shopping complex. The new Meadows Branch Library opened in November 1989. On December 3, 1989, a grand opening celebration was held, whereby Mayor Linda Jourgensen cut the ceremonial ribbon declaring the east branch library officially open. According to the new Meadows Branch Library Manager, Wendy Hall, the new library would offer a unique and creative set-up to display its collection. A bookstore format to tie in with the retail environment was used to display the collection. Window displays highlighted new book arrivals and sloped bookshelves were utilized to display book covers and special interest topics and bestsellers were shelved separately.<sup>37</sup> Renovations of

---

<sup>36</sup>Daily Journal, 25 October 1990.

<sup>37</sup>Camera, 17 June 1989; Colorado Daily, 14 November 1989; Camera, 3 December 1989.

the George Reynolds Branch Library got underway in the summer of 1990. The 0.38 percent sales tax increase helped to finance the \$100,000 renovation project. Renovations included raising the library floor to make it wheelchair accessible, laying new carpets, and adding new furniture and shelves. The renovations to the George Reynolds Branch were completed by October 1990.<sup>38</sup>

### **Open Space and Real Estate**

In March 1986, Boulder bought 697 acres of open space land for \$2.8 million. The land purchased was just south of Marshall Lake, east of Colorado Highway 93 and north of Colorado Highway 128 near the southern county line. The city purchased the land from Jack Varra. This open space land purchase was not only one of the largest, but also one of the most expensive purchases in open space history. Boulder's open space program was established in 1967. According to the terms of the land purchasing agreement, the city was to pay \$4,000 per acre over the next 15 years at a 7.5 percent interest rate. A down payment of \$1.47 million was to be paid to Varra upon closing the deal. The 697 acres purchased was mostly wetlands and tall prairie grasses. Coal Creek and a gravel mining operation were also located on the open space property. As stipulated in the purchase agreement, the gravel mine would remain operational for 15 years with the city receiving royalties. The former owner, Jack Varra, would continue to operate the mine. With this recent purchase, Boulder's open space holdings totaled more than 16,000 acres. Even though the land purchase was outside the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan boundary, most council members not only saw the purchase as a vital buffer zone between Boulder and the Rocky Flats Nuclear Weapons Plant, but also as a barrier to the proposed W-470 highway. Councilman Bob Greenlee disagreed with council's reasoning for approving the purchase and therefore cast the only dissenting vote. Greenlee opposed the purchase since the land was outside the Boulder

---

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 19 July 1990 and 8 October 1990.

Valley Comprehensive Plan. Greenlee also felt that the land was under no threat of development since it lies between Rocky Flats and Marshall Landfill.<sup>39</sup>

The council unanimously approved a second open space land purchase, located southeast of Boulder, in April that same year. The city purchased 107 acres of land located between Cherryvale and Marshall Roads for \$747,600. Bonds issued by council back in 1984 for the sole purpose of purchasing open space land made this land acquisition possible. According to the terms of the purchase, the city was to pay Lela Hogan, the former owner, \$37,380 as a down payment and then make annual payments of \$75,049 for 20 years. Council also granted the Hogan family a 20-year farm and crop lease on the land. In exchange, the Hogan family was to pay Boulder \$10,000 a year for farming privileges.<sup>40</sup>

In June 1987, city council unanimously voted to discontinue or cease the transfer of Boulder Open Space Department money to the city's general operating fund by 1992. Pressure from environmentalists to stop the transfers prompted council's actions. Environmentalists and Open Space Director Jim Crain feared the continuation of the transfers could bankrupt the open space budget by 1989. Therefore, council decided to gradually discontinue the transfers, despite the city's budgetary troubles. The question of how the city would make ends meet without the transfers went unanswered. The city's habit of transferring open space money to the city's general operating fund had been going on for the past 19 years. In addition, those transfers were never itemized in the city's budget. The money being transferred came from a sales tax increase Boulder voters approved back in 1967. From each cent of the new tax, 0.4 cents was earmarked for the acquisition and maintenance of open space land and another 0.6 cents was earmarked for transportation projects. In 1967, to ensure voter approval of the sales tax increase, council pledged to lower

---

<sup>39</sup>Camera, 5, 12, 19 March 1986.

<sup>40</sup>Ibid., 2 April 1986.

city property taxes in exchange for a portion of the new sales tax, which was equivalent to 2 mills of property tax. Although the transfer was deemed legal, such a transfer was ethically questionable. Now that property values were to be reassessed, the amount this transfer would increase sharply. This made the transfer of funds harder for open space proponents to accept. In 1987 alone, approximately \$1 million was transferred from the Boulder Open Space and Transportation sales tax general fund to the city's operating funds.<sup>41</sup> The transfer of money had to end.

A joint city-county venture to purchase open space land was under consideration by council in November 1987. The joint land purchase under consideration was 1,200 acres of agricultural land along the foothills east of U.S. Highway 36 near the Left Hand Valley Reservoir by the Beech Aircraft Corporation building. Total cost of the land purchase was \$3.2 million. Boulder's share of the open space purchase was approximately \$2.7 million. The remaining cost was to be paid by Boulder County. According to the agreement, the county was to pay its entire share before the city would even make a payment. In 1995, after the county finished paying off its share of the land purchase, the city would start making annual payments of \$191,000 until the year 2008. The terms of purchase also stipulated that the city and county would share any future decisions regarding the land even though the city was contributing more money towards the purchase than the county. Moreover, the city was also gaining more of the land—1000 acres of the 1200 acres. Boulder County Commissioners approved its share of the land purchase, 200 acres, in the fall of 1987. Boulder City Council approved the purchase in March 1988. This purchase increased the city's open space holdings to more than 17,000 acres. According to county officials, the land was to officially open to the public for recreational enjoyment on May 27, 1988.<sup>42</sup>

---

<sup>41</sup>Boulder Courier, 18 June 1987 and Camera, 12 April 1987.

<sup>42</sup>Colorado Daily, 13-15 November 1987; 3 March 1988; 27 April 1988.

That same year, the Boulder Open Space Department was at a loss over a 1984 land purchase deal that went sour. Back in 1984, the city of Boulder had purchased 74.5 acres on the western edge of Davidson Mesa just northwest of Louisville with promises from both Louisville and Boulder County to split the purchase three ways. The purchase was done in cooperation with Louisville to create a buffer. However, after the informal land agreement was made, the philosophy and makeup of the Louisville City Council changed. Purchasing open space land became less of a priority. According to Louisville City Administrator Terry Hundley, Boulder had also refused to grant Louisville sole ownership of 7 acres. As a result, Louisville backed out of the deal leaving Boulder with the entire bill. Boulder paid \$472,000 for the land. Since those 74.5 acres of land was not seen as essential to Boulder's open space program as other land and since money was so scarce in the open space program, Boulder Open Space Director Jim Crain announced in January 1988 that he was considering selling the land. There was other land more essential to Boulder's open space. Money from selling that land could be used to purchase other open space land. Crain's announcement was a first in Boulder Open Space Department history. Never before had Boulder been willing to sell open space land. Crain's decision to sell open space land required approval from both council and Boulder residents. The need for public approval to sell open space land stemmed from a 1986 City Charter amendment. According to the amendment, the public had the right to overturn a decision made by the Boulder Open Space Board of Trustees and council regarding the selling, leasing or trading of the city's greenbelt. Boulder residents had 60 days to file a petition opposing the sale once council voted to approve the sale. Crain advised council that approximately 3,100 acres of open space should be purchased in order to close the remaining gaps of greenbelt surrounding Boulder.<sup>43</sup>

Reluctant to sell open space land and perplexed with the current financial situation,

---

<sup>43</sup>Camera, 22 January 1988 and 5 November 1986 and Colorado Daily, 21 January 1988.

the Boulder Open Space Board of Trustees asked Boulder residents for advise in February 1988. Should the open space land near the Louisville Reservoir, the 74.5 acres, be sold? Without additional money, no other open space land could be purchased. However, selling open space land could start an unwelcome precedent and risk the public's trust. In light of the financial situation of the open space program, council voted to stop the transferring of open space funds to the city's operating fund immediately. Council's decision last June to gradually phase out the transfers was overturned. This decision to stop transfers immediately provided the Boulder Open Space Department with \$343,000. That additional money was to be used for maintenance, operation and debt service instead of new land acquisitions.<sup>44</sup> As for the selling of those 74.5 acres known as the Steinbach property, the land was never sold. According to Wally Cameron of the Open Space and Real Estate Department, that land is still preserved as open space.

The recent construction of a 7,300 square-foot house on the mountain ridge next to Chautauqua Park rekindled the city's concern to protect Boulder's mountain backdrop above the city's "Blue Line." The Blue Line is an imaginary border meant to protect the city's western edge from development. The city refuses to provide water and sewer services above that imaginary line as a way to deter development. However, property owners could dig a well and install a septic system themselves, which is what Patricia Finnoff did to build a house along the mountain ridge next to Chautauqua Park. The only way to preserve the mountain backdrop and to ensure no other developments above the Blue Line were built, the city had to purchase the land. According to Open Space Director Jim Crain, the city should purchase 9 to 18 building lots above the Blue Line to protect the city's mountain backdrop. However, funding for those purchases was scarce, mainly because the sales tax revenue had dropped or remained stagnant since 1986. Despite this fact, the city managed to purchase one of the building lots Crain had recommended for purchase. In August 1988, a one acre

---

<sup>44</sup>Colorado Daily, 19-21 February 1988 and Camera, 3 March 1988.

parcel on Flagstaff Mountain, located just below the Flagstaff House Restaurant, was purchased for \$45,000. Money from the Open Space Dakota Ridge account, an emergency purchase fund, was used to make the land purchase.<sup>45</sup>

Still, the future of the city's open space program was in question. The lack of funds for new land acquisitions incited debate. Until the financial situation of the program changed, purchasing additional open space land was unlikely at least until 1992-1993. Why was the open space program financially strapped when Boulder's Open Space Department ranked fourth in spending among other city departments? According to a Daily Camera article dated October 9, 1988, Boulder spent more on open space and parks and recreation combined than it spent on police protection. Despite this fact, the open space program was in financial trouble because of outstanding debt payments for land purchases. Approximately \$4.2 million of the Open Space Department's 1988 \$6.3 million budget went towards paying off debt of previous land purchases. The remaining money was used to finance operations, maintenance of the program and capital improvements. Payments on the bonds issued between 1973 and 1984 for land purchases had strapped the budget of Boulder's Open Space program. Between 1973 and 1984, approximately \$20 million in bonds were issued so open space land could be acquired more quickly. Debt accumulated from those purchases would not be paid off until 2006, thus inhibiting new land acquisitions.

In 1988, the Open Space Board of Trustees considered three ways in which to alleviate the budget woes of the Boulder Open Space Department and to ensure additional land purchases. One option considered was to refinance the Boulder Open Space Department's existing debt by turning in the old bonds of \$20.2 million and re-issuing new bonds for \$21.5 million. The new bonds would be repaid over a 24 year period instead of 15. By re-issuing new bonds, the open space program would save approximately \$7.3 million in

---

<sup>45</sup>Camera, 11 July 1988 and Colorado Daily, 22 August 1988.

interest payments over the next six years. A second option was to expand the open space bonding capacity by asking Boulder voters to approve a charter amendment that would increase the length of time in which the open space program could take to pay back bonds. The length of time would be increased to 30 years. That way more bonds could be issued to purchase additional open space. According to the brokerage firm of Dain Bosworth Inc., increasing the length of time to 30 years coupled with the refinancing plan, the Boulder Open Space Department could pay off an additional \$30 million worth of debt. With additional bonds, the Open Space Department could purchase land considered essential to protecting the city's boundaries from its encroaching neighboring cities. A third option considered by the Open Space Board of Trustees, but not recommended at the time, was to increase the sales tax by 0.35 percent. The board realized the political realities of trying to pose a new tax increase. Therefore, the board favored the first two options to help alleviate the financial troubles of the city's open space program and to provide the funds necessary to purchase additional open space land. The board outlined the first two options to the council in November 1988. However, before agreeing to the additional debt that the two recommendations may accrue, council wanted additional information about the refinancing plan. Council also instructed the Open Space Board to identify and prioritize open space purchases on the basis of the projected available funds.<sup>46</sup>

The city-owned Boulder Valley Ranch, located north of Boulder just east of US Highway 36 off of Longhorn Road, came under new management in December 1988. The city selected Bob Lover to manage the ranch. Under the new management, the ranch continued to offer both grazing and boarding for horses, but hay rides and a livery stable renting riding horses was no longer offered. In addition, the new ranch manager decided to discontinue operating the Silver Nickel, a barn rented to the public for parties, because it was

---

<sup>46</sup>Colorado Daily, 15 September 1988 and Camera, 9 October 1988 and 28, 30 November 1988.

not profitable. Love also intended to standardize the boarding prices and fix up the barn. The former manager, Bob Stave, left with bitter feelings towards the city over losing the ranch managership. Stave claimed the city had refused him a 10-year lease and had wanted to raise his rent. The city only wanted to offer a two-year lease to remain more flexible about how the ranch was operated. When Stave refused the city's terms, the city had no choice but to take bids.<sup>47</sup>

In April 1989, the Open Space Board of Trustees returned to council with a slightly revised refinancing plan. This time council approved restructuring the Open Space Department's debt-service payments. The plan called for refinancing the department's \$20.7 million debt with new lower-interest bonds and extending the repayment schedule of the new bonds by 11 years. This refinancing enabled the Open Space Department to resume purchasing open space. In June 1989, city council authorized the purchase of 3.2 acres of land on Flagstaff Mountain to preserve as open space. The land purchased was located just below the Flagstaff House Restaurant. The city paid \$180,000 for the 3.2 acres.<sup>48</sup>

That same summer, the Boulder Open Space Board of Trustees proposed placing a sales tax increase on the November 1989 ballot. Refinancing the Open Space Department's debt alone would not provide funds soon enough to purchase additional open space land. The proposed sales tax increase would provide the funds needed to purchase more open space land. With the additional land purchases, Boulder could protect itself from the neighboring cities to the east and the annexation wars taking place. The request for the sales tax increase by the Open Space Board of Trustees came after the recent defeat of a countywide ballot initiative that proposed raising the sales tax 0.1 percent in Boulder County to finance open space purchases. Boulder voters had supported the ballot proposal in the

---

<sup>47</sup>Camera, 19 December 1988.

<sup>48</sup>Colorado Daily, 4, 6 April 1989 and Camera, 18 June 1989.

May special election, but other Boulder County cities did not support the sales tax increase for open space purchases. The support for the sales tax increase by Boulder residents prompted the Open Space Board to request a new sales tax proposal for just the city of Boulder in June 1989. Upon review, council approved placing the sales tax increase on the November ballot. Boulder voters were asked to raise the sales tax by 0.33 percent for 15 years. If approved, the sales tax would generate approximately \$41.7 million over the 15 year period. Voters approved the sales tax increase on November 7, 1989. The new tax went into effect January 1, 1990.<sup>49</sup>

In July 1989, council authorized another open space purchase. The city purchased 173 acres of farm land located one mile east of 75th Street on Arapahoe Road. This new purchase linked two existing open space parcels and solidified the buffer greenbelt around the city. Boulder paid \$1,169,000 for the 173 acres. That price included water rights and the city would also continue to grow alfalfa there. The former owner, George Autry, kept 10 acres for two homes and some out-buildings.<sup>50</sup> Purchasing additional open space land continued. The recent sales tax increase approved by Boulder voters allowed Boulder to purchase an additional 1,106 acres of open space in December 1989. The price tag of this open space land was \$5.8 million. This new acquisition brought Boulder's total open space land holdings to nearly 19,000 acres. This purchase not only provided a land buffer between Boulder and the Louisville/Lafayette area, but also preserved land around the Boulder Reservoir as well as the Gunbarrel and Heatherwood neighborhoods. Boulder had no intention of stopping there. Boulder anticipated it would purchase another 8,000 acres of open space over the next two years. In order to do that, Boulder intended to sell \$12 million in bonds next year rather than wait for those tax dollars to accumulate. That way the city

---

<sup>49</sup>Camera, 23, 28 June 1989 and 2 August 1989 and 8 November 1989.

<sup>50</sup>Ibid., 17 July 1989.

could acquire the desired open space land sooner and also at a cheaper rate. Some of that desired open space land was bought the following year as part of Boulder's accelerated purchase program for open space. In September 1990, Boulder City Council authorized three open space purchases totaling 1,200 acres. Total cost of the 1,200 acres was more than \$3 million. Boulder County intended to pay the city \$1.1 million for a 408 acre portion of one of the parcels just purchased by the city. The three properties purchased included the 70 acre Kolb property as well as three shares of the Butte Mill Ditch, two separate portions of the Lindsay property totaling 940 acres and the 163 acre Knaus property off of Lookout Road.<sup>51</sup>

### **Parks and Recreation**

A summary of what facilities the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department had to offer as of February 1985 was reported in a Daily Camera article dated February 24, 1985. The list of facilities included the following: 55 city parks, 26 playgrounds, 24 soccer fields, 23 tennis courts, 19 ball-fields, 8 basketball courts, 2 outdoor and 2 indoor swimming pools, 7 ponds and lakes and 2 recreation centers.<sup>52</sup> Between 1985 and 1990, the Parks and Recreation Department added to that list of facilities. New parks were created and a third recreation center was under construction. The Parks and Recreation Department not only added to its list of facilities, but also made changes and improvements to existing parks as well.

Improvement plans for Chautauqua Park, located on the western end of Baseline Road, were presented to the public in March 1985. The Boulder Parks and Recreation Department intended to spend approximately \$370,000 in improvements. The city's improvement plans included the following: constructing a 2,400 square-foot building that

---

<sup>51</sup>Colorado Daily, 21 December 1989 and Camera, 6 September 1990.

<sup>52</sup>Camera, 24 February 1985.

would serve as both a ranger station to welcome visitors and a storage shed to house equipment used to fight grass fires; easing parking and traffic hassles by creating angle parking spaces around the park's perimeter and by making Clematis Drive around the grassy commons a one-way road; replacing the old public restrooms and the sidewalk east of the Dining Hall with a wheelchair accessible sidewalk. The ranger station to be constructed would offer winterized public restrooms with running water. The Parks and Recreation Department's improvement plans for Chautauqua were not well received, especially by Chautauqua area residents.

Residents of that area objected to building the ranger station and also the parking improvement plan. They felt the ranger station would not fit the historic setting of the park or harmonize with the surroundings. Residents felt that adding more parking spaces would not only clutter the park by making it more accessible, but also would endanger the children playing in the park. Residents also claimed the relaxed setting and beauty of Chautauqua would be destroyed if the parking improvements went ahead as planned. The public objections of the proposed improvements resulted in a considerable debate between the city and Chautauqua area residents. In an attempt to resolve the points of contention, the city held as many as 20 meetings. In the end, city council approved the plans to refurbish Chautauqua Park in April 1986, but a modified version of the plans presented last March. The improvement plans still included the controversial ranger station and parking reconfiguration. However, a 900 square-foot ranger station was to be built instead of the 2400 square-foot station first proposed and the parking plan was altered as well. Parking spaces were added to the park's two eastern lots and on the north side of Clematis Drive, while spaces in the parking lot west of Kinnikinick Road were eliminated. As a result, the park actually was left with 10 to 15 fewer parking spaces than before the improvements, but the parking was now more organized.<sup>53</sup> By March 1987, the majority of the improvements

---

<sup>53</sup>Ibid., 14 March 1986 and 30 September 1985 and 23 April 1986.

were completed. The new ranger station was built and the parking lots were paved. The controversy surrounding the improvement plans still lingered, but the public's reaction to the new ranger station was mostly favorable. The new ranger station blended with the style of the Chautauqua buildings already in existence and was seen as an attractive building.<sup>54</sup>

Chautauqua Park was not the only recreational facility making improvements. Improvements at the Boulder Reservoir, paid for by a 1981 bond issue, were finally completed in June 1985. The \$2.8 million improvements at the Boulder Reservoir included building a concession house, a bath house with hot showers, a boat rental facility and restrooms with running water. The renovations to the Boulder Reservoir were to be completed last summer, but bad weather coupled with soil problems forced the design plans for the improvements to be modified. Delivery delays of construction materials also prevented the renovations from being completed on time. Even though the improvements were not complete in 1984 as scheduled, the city sponsored a grand opening celebration anyway. Now that all the improvements were actually completed one year later, the city held another grand opening celebration to mark the completion of the improvements. At this second grand opening celebration held in June 1985, Mayor Linda Jourgensen along with Parks and Recreation Board Chairman Bruce Alexander cut the ribbon marking the occasion. Other grand opening activities included volleyball games, greased watermelon contests, tug-of-war contests, music entertainment, face painting and a barbecue.<sup>55</sup>

Over the next few years, changes in the activities allowed at the Boulder Reservoir took place. In March 1986, windsurfers were granted access to the reservoir during the winter months to enjoy windsurfing. Normally the reservoir would close between October and March, denying cars access to the park by locking the entrance gates. To reduce the

---

<sup>54</sup>Ibid., 1 March 1987.

<sup>55</sup>Ibid., 7 June 1985.

city's vulnerability to lawsuits now that the reservoir would be open in the winter, warning signs were posted indicating that no lifeguards or security personnel were on duty. The Parks and Recreation Department placed a fence along the reservoir road to ensure cars parked only in the parking lots. The cost of the fence was about \$7000.<sup>56</sup> Another change at the Boulder Reservoir occurred in 1989. The tradition of waterfowl hunting at the reservoir, started back in 1957, came to an end. Since 1983, when the city annexed the reservoir property, hunting at the reservoir had become illegal. City ordinances prohibit hunting within the city limits. Until 1989, both the Boulder City Attorney's office and the Parks and Recreation Department were unaware that hunting at the reservoir had become illegal when the property was annexed. The decision to legalize hunting at the Boulder Reservoir rested with city council. Boulder City Council favored enforcing the law of no hunting anywhere within the city limits. In July 1990, efforts to reinstate hunting at the reservoir failed. Urgings from the State Division of Wildlife to reinstate hunting at the reservoir prompted the Parks and Recreation Department to ask council to reverse its decision. The Parks and Recreation Department proposed amending the city ordinance to allow hunting at the reservoir on a permit basis. Arguments that hunting helped control the waterfowl population and that the city should offer affordable hunting opportunities did not sway council. The "No Hunting" signs remained at the Boulder Reservoir.<sup>57</sup>

Improvements to another Parks and Recreation Department facility occurred in 1990. The city spruced up its swimming pool facility located at 2102 Spruce Street with a mural. The city's search for artists to paint the mural at the swimming pool was launched in January 1990. The city advertised the mural project nationwide, but the prize money of only \$5,000 no doubt impeded the number of out-of-state applicants. In May 1990, work on painting the

---

<sup>56</sup>Ibid., 20 March 1986.

<sup>57</sup>Ibid., 29 August 1989 and 7 September 1989 and 25 July 1990.

mural at the Spruce Street swimming pool got underway. The mural depicted a playful underwater fantasy theme of indigenous aquatic species. Local artists Randi Eyre and Linda Lowry were selected by the Boulder Arts Commission and the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board to paint the mural as part of the Art-in-the-Park program.<sup>58</sup>

The Art-in-the-Park program was an event sponsored by the Parks and Recreation Department. One percent of the parks department capital improvements fund was earmarked for the program. Working in collaboration with the Boulder Arts Commission, an artist would be selected for the project advertised. The Parks and Recreation Advisory Board reviews the applicant's artwork proposal for safety, maintenance and site suitability, while the Arts Commission reviews and selects the artwork based on aesthetics. At times the two groups have disagreed over the selection or have faced public criticism for their selection. A case in point was the selection of New York artist Andrea Blum's sculpture that was to be placed at the corner of Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard back in 1983. Public outcry led the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board to reverse its decision and dismiss Blum's proposal. The Arts Commission continued to back Blum, but the lack of a consensus resulted in the rejection of Blum's proposal. In 1985, bitter feelings between the Arts Commission and the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board still lingered when it came time to select more artwork for the Art-in-the-Park program. Works of art were commissioned for three separate park locations in 1985: the Boulder Reservoir, the North Boulder Recreation Center and Scott Carpenter Park. During the selection process, the Arts Commission felt the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board had overstepped its bound of authority when the board recommended including another art piece in the Boulder Reservoir competition and dismissing another. The Arts Commission had rejected the inclusion of that art work in the reservoir competition because the artist had entered it only in the Scott Carpenter Park contest. The two committees eventually agreed and picked the artwork now visible at those

---

<sup>58</sup>Colorado Daily, 19-21 January 1990 and Camera, 21 May 1990.

three locations. The Art-in-the-Park projects commissioned that year included the aluminum sculpture of a sunbather sitting on a bench by W.R. Vielehr for the Boulder Reservoir, the wall mural depicting gymnasts in motion by Lisa Reasoner at the North Boulder Recreation Center and the "Continuous Form" sculpture by John Haertling placed near Scott Carpenter Park. In May 1987, the sculpture park located at Ninth Street and Canyon Boulevard was officially named The Charles A. Haertling Sculpture Park, after the well-known Boulder architect. Sculptures created by local artists, who had been awarded city grants, were on display at the sculpture park.<sup>59</sup>

Building new parks in Boulder proved as challenging as renovating or making changes to Boulder's existing parks. A case in point was the city's efforts to build two new community parks—one in north Boulder on the west end of Locust Avenue and the other in east Boulder at the southern end of 55th Street. By building these two new community parks, Boulder's total number of community parks would increase to three. The only community park in existence at the time was Harlow Platts Park located at 1360 Gillaspie Drive. The money to purchase the sites for these two community parks came from a \$7.4 million Parks and Recreation bond issue approved by Boulder voters in 1981. The city revealed its plans for the two community parks in 1985. The 57 acre park planned for north Boulder included constructing two pavilions to house basketball, tennis, racquetball and volleyball courts, creating seasonal ponds and building a small neighborhood park within the larger park that featured a playground, a smaller pond and tennis courts. The city's plans for the park in north Boulder also took into account the park's proximity to a tract of city open space. The city intended to blend the two environments by planting natural grasses in the park and placing large rocks throughout the park. Paths in the park were to be connected with the open space trails as well. The 52.8 acre park planned for east Boulder included developing soccer fields, hard surfaced basketball, tennis, volleyball and racquetball courts

---

<sup>59</sup>Camera, 28 August 1985 and 19 December 1985 and 2 May 1987.

and constructing a recreation center with a gymnasium, indoor swimming pool, playing courts, nursery and meeting rooms. Plans for the community park in east Boulder also included creating a six-acre lake and a two-acre flower garden, plus a picnic and performance area. The park in east Boulder would connect with city owned open space land along South Boulder Creek. The city held two meetings to gather public input and to ascertain what activities or sports the parks should offer.<sup>60</sup> The city soon learned that designing and planning the two new parks was not an easy task. Both park proposals stirred public controversy that had to be resolved before the city could proceed with its plans to develop the community parks.

At the meeting to gather public input on the community park planned for north Boulder, residents expressed interest in everything from a frisbee golf course to an indoor dance center, but mostly residents complained about and discussed the proposed subsidized housing project known as White Owl. Residents of north Boulder objected to that housing project, claiming it was too dense for that neighborhood. Residents also questioned how the land for the housing project was obtained. The Parks and Recreation Department had bought the land with bond money approved by voters in 1981, but now was considering selling a portion of that land, approximately 13.3 acres, to the Boulder Housing Authority for a subsidized housing project. The land was in fact sold to the Boulder Housing Authority in 1985. As a result of the controversy surrounding the proposed housing project, the community park planned for north Boulder at Locust Avenue and 6th Street never materialized as planned during this time frame.<sup>61</sup>

Plans for the community park in east Boulder were also surrounded in controversy.

---

<sup>60</sup>Camera, 24 February 1985.

<sup>61</sup>Camera, 28 February 1985 and Suzanne Hudson, A History of Boulder's Parks and Recreation (Boulder, Colorado: Boulder Parks and Recreation Department, ca. 1990-1993), 54-55.

However, unlike the park planned for north Boulder, the park planned for east Boulder was built, but only after overcoming numerous obstacles. Obstacles encountered in building the East Boulder Community Park included the destruction and compensation of wetlands and the 55th Street extension controversy. When planning the East Boulder Community Park, the city learned that the Environmental Protection Agency considered approximately 12 acres of the proposed 53 acre park site as wetlands. As required by law, the city had to compensate for the destruction of those wetlands before the city could proceed with the park's development. The city chose to compensate for the wetlands destroyed by creating over 11 acres of wetlands around Coot Lake, located on 63rd Street west of IBM. The actual soil from the wetlands at the park site was transported to Coot Lake and used as seed source. The city also planted trees around the lake. In exchange, gravel and dirt from the lake area was used to build up the park site and fill-in low areas of the park.<sup>62</sup>

The second and biggest obstacle surrounding the community park development in east Boulder was the 55th Street extension issue. Boulder's Transportation Department had plans to extend 55th Street in order to connect South Boulder Road to Baseline Road. This proposed future leg of 55th Street would run right through the proposed park, thus dividing the park in half. Not surprisingly, residents of that area objected to placing a road through the middle of the park not only for safety reasons, but also for noise problems. Despite the public's objections, the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board approved the conceptual plan of the park with 55th Street running through the middle of the park in March 1985. However, the board reserved the right to alter its decision if the design of the 55th Street extension significantly interrupted the park plan. Approval of the 55th Street extension plan itself was still forthcoming. The Boulder Planning Board reviewed the Transportation Department's plans for the 55th Street extension in December 1985. The Boulder Planning Board voted 5 to 2 in favor of building the 55th Street extension, but it

---

<sup>62</sup>Camera, 15 May 1989.

would not cut through the planned community park at Baseline Road and 55th Street. Instead, 55th Street was to be rerouted to run along the eastern edge of the park. The Boulder Planning Board placed other restrictions on the 55th Street extension including limiting the road to two lanes, closing the road to truck traffic and creating the bike path separate from the road.<sup>63</sup>

Once the obstacles hindering the development of the community park in east Boulder were resolved, work on the first phase of the park's development got underway in May 1989. Phase one included developing a soccer field, walkways and a parking lot on the western 10 acres of the park site. The city expected to have phase one of the park completed later that same year. Once that was completed, the park's centerpiece—construction of Boulder's third recreational facility—was to be built. Construction of the recreation center got underway in 1990. The new recreation center was scheduled to open for business by the spring of 1992. The Aurora construction firm of Adolfson & Peterson, Inc. was selected to build the East Boulder Community Recreation Center. The cost of building the new recreational facility was approximately \$6 million. Money to finance the project came from the voter approved 1987 sales tax increase of 0.38 percent. This same tax increase also helped finance the Boulder Public Library addition. The \$6 million, 50,000 square-foot East Boulder Community Recreation Center was to house not only recreational activities, but also house another city senior center in a separate wing of the facility. Recreational activities to be offered at the new facility included a gymnasium with two basketball courts, a weight room, an aerobics room, a climbing wall for rock climbers and two swimming pools—one 25-yard lap pool and the other a leisure pool with a waterslide.<sup>64</sup>

Although the recreational facility was offering a variety of activities, some Boulder

---

<sup>63</sup>Ibid., 26 March 1985 and 20 December 1985.

<sup>64</sup>Ibid., 15 May 1989; 17 February 1991; 11 February 1990.

residents were not pleased with the center, specifically the size of the lap pool. Many Boulder area swimmers claimed the 25-yard lap pool was inadequate; a 50-meter pool would suit the community's needs better. Despite the people's urging to create a larger pool, the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board approved plans for the recreation center that called for the 25-yard, six-lane lap pool in January 1990. An appeal to alter those plans and install a 50-meter pool was made to city council. Council rejected the idea of creating an Olympic sized 50-meter pool at the East Boulder Community Recreation Center on account of the price tag associated with the larger pool. However, city council did approve two additional swim lanes to create an eight-lane lap pool instead of a six-lane in March 1990. The city celebrated the grand opening of the East Boulder Community Recreation Center in May 1992. Finishing touches to park itself were to continue over the next five years. Finishing touches included adding five tennis courts, two basketball courts, two outdoor racquetball and squash courts, four sandlot volleyball courts and a children's playground.<sup>65</sup>

In May 1988, the city of Boulder, the Boulder Sister City Project, four University of Colorado environmental design students and the local architect James Copeland collaborated on creating a "peace garden" park along Boulder Creek between the Municipal Building and the Boulder Public Library. The peace park to be created here in Boulder was part of the Sister City Project exchanges agreed upon last year by Boulder Mayor Linda Jourgensen and Maksud Ikramov, the mayor of Boulder's Soviet sister city of Dushanbe in Tajikistan. In exchange, a children's playground dedicated to Boulder was being created in Dushanbe. The city signed a sister-city contract, formalizing its relationship with the Soviet city of Dushanbe, in May 1987. The design of the Peace Garden Park consisted of two semi-circular amphitheatres on either side of the creek facing each other to form a circle separated only by the creek. The amphitheatres, approximately 30-feet in radius, provided space for individual contemplation or meditation and even small performances. A variety of plants and

---

<sup>65</sup>Ibid., 23 January 1990; 11 February 1990; 10 May 1992.

vegetation native to the sister city of Dushanbe were to be planted around the amphitheaters. Originally, the Colorado University students' designs for the park included placing a sculpture in the middle of Boulder Creek between the two amphitheaters, but the city's flood regulations prevented that idea from materializing.<sup>66</sup> According to the architect James Copeland, the park's design was meant to symbolize the two portions of the world divided by water, but together the two portions form a whole. The Boulder Creek running between the two halves represented the "...geographical distance, political conflict and ethnic boundaries."<sup>67</sup>

The estimated cost of the Peace Garden Park project was \$30,000. The Boulder Parks and Recreation Department agreed to contribute \$15,000 towards the park's construction provided the Sister City Project matched that amount. Construction of the Peace Garden Park did not get underway for two years, until the spring of 1990. Reasons why construction was delayed until 1990 included the inability of the Sister City Project to raise the matching funds until then, the issuing of a floodplain permit and finalizing the park's design. A Floodplain Development Permit was required since the park site was located within the 100 year floodplain of Boulder Creek. The permit was granted in March 1990. However, before construction of the stone amphitheaters could begin as planned, an old willow tree located along the banks of Boulder Creek had to be removed. If the tree was not removed the design plan for the two stone amphitheaters on either side of the creek could not be built. According to the Parks and Recreation acting director Ken Ramsey, the tree trunk was cracked and rotting. Leaving the tree would not only alter the park's design, but also any landscaping and masonry foundation work done would, in all likelihood, cause the tree to fall over risking injury to the public. Therefore, council voted 7 to 1 in favor of removing the tree so the

---

<sup>66</sup>Ibid., 23 May 1988 and 19 December 1988

<sup>67</sup>Colorado Daily, 7 September 1988.

building of the Peace Garden Park could proceed as planned. Twelve new trees were incorporated into the park's design and planted. The masonry work on the amphitheaters started on Monday April 30, 1990 and was scheduled for completion by May 11, 1990, provided the good weather held out.<sup>68</sup>

In the fall of 1988, the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board addressed the issue of selecting a new site for the city's ball-field. Stazio softball park, located between 30th Street and Foothills Parkway, had to be abandoned after the 1989 ball season due to the development of the Colorado University Research Park, specifically the building of U.S. West Advanced Technologies. Until the new fields were selected and built, a temporary channel was devised to keep the old Stazio fields in operation for the 1989 softball season. The channel was built so that the rerouting of Skunk Creek for the construction of the U.S. West Advanced Technologies building would not interfere with the softball season. Engineers managed to build a channel without shortening the length of fields as originally anticipated, thus denying ballplayers any easy homeruns. Two sites for the new softball fields were under consideration by the board. One of the sites under consideration was located in north Boulder at 26th Street and Violet Road. The other site was located in east Boulder just south of Valmont Road on the west side of 63rd Street. The north Boulder site was 77 acres of land owned by Midsummer Associates. The east Boulder site was 42 acres owned by the Public Service Company of Colorado. Although both sites would serve adequately, the north Boulder site was preferred because of its larger size. However, development of the ballpark there would involve more complications. Complications included making changes to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan land designation and addressing public objections to the ballpark. Before the ballpark could be built in north Boulder, changes to the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan were required since the land

---

<sup>68</sup>Camera, 19 December 1988; Parks and Recreation Public Hearing Motion, 3 April 1990; Colorado Daily 5 April 1990; Camera, 4 May 1990.

was designated as Area III, land not slated for annexation or development. Amending the Boulder Valley Comprehensive Plan could be a lengthy process since approval by four city and county boards was required. In contrast, the eastern site was already designated as Area II, i.e., land ready for development. In addition, residents in north Boulder, specifically residents of the Orange Orchard neighborhood, objected to the city building the ballpark in their neighborhood. Residents argued that the pastoral setting of the area would be ruined if the ballpark were built in north Boulder.<sup>69</sup>

The objections of Orange Orchard residents were herd by council and, as a result, council voted unanimously to abandon the plans for the north Boulder softball fields. The new softball fields would be built at the eastern site near Valmont Road and 63rd Street. The new ball-fields retained the name of Stazio in honor of the late Gerald Stazio, founder of Boulder's recreational softball program. The new softball complex offered four diamonds initially with room for six additional fields, more parking and warm-up areas for athletes, as well as shade for softball fans. Construction of the \$3.5 million softball complex was financed primarily through the 0.38 percent sales tax approved by voters in 1987. According to a Daily Camera article dated April 8, 1990, the new Stazio ball-fields were to be completed by May 14, 1990—just in time for the 1990 softball season—provided it did not rain until then.<sup>70</sup>

Efforts to build a skateboard park in Boulder began in 1990. A group calling themselves P.O.S.S.E.—People Organized For a Safe Skateboarding Establishment—pressured the city of Boulder into building a skateboard park. The group P.O.S.S.E. was comprised of mostly kid skateboarders and their parents and also Ray Mueller who was the Youth

---

<sup>69</sup>Camera, 27 September 1988; 15 August 1988; 18 October 1988.

<sup>70</sup>Colorado Daily, 20 October 1988 and Camera, 12 December 1988 and 8 April 1990.

Services Director at the YMCA. A formal request to build an indoor-outdoor skateboarding park was made by the group to the Parks and Recreation Department in the summer of 1990. The group proposed building the skateboard park along the Boulder Creek Path either near the Boulder Reservoir or Countryside Village at Violet and 19th Streets in north Boulder. P.O.S.S.E. asked that the funding for the park come from the Parks and Recreation Citizen Initiated Projects program fund of \$10,000. The city conducted a study to determine public support for a skateboard park and also the feasibility for such a project. The study indicated public support for the project, but details of the project like liability insurance, funding and operating the park remained in question. The staff of the Parks and Recreation Department estimated that it would cost more than \$30,000 to build the skateboard park. As a result, the request for the \$10,000 Citizen Initiated Projects fund was denied by the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board since the cost of building the skateboard park exceeded that amount. Instead, the board decided to allot \$30,000 from the Parks and Recreation general fund for the skateboard park. As the plans and cost of the park became clearer, the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board allotted \$50,000 instead in order to improve the design of the skateboard park.<sup>71</sup>

It was not until 1992-1993 that the details of the skateboard park were worked out and actually built. The 8,500 square-foot outdoor skateboard park was built east of the ball-fields at Scott Carpenter Park located at 30th Street and Arapahoe Avenue instead of along Boulder Creek Path as originally proposed. A lease between the city of Boulder and YMCA officials was signed in April 1993. Under the lease terms, the YMCA agreed to manage the park and provide liability insurance while the city furnished the funding needed to build the skateboard park. The skateboard park was scheduled to open in June 1993.<sup>72</sup>

---

<sup>71</sup>Camera, 28 July 1990 and 26, 27 November 1990 (Parks and Recreation Dept. file) and 18 April 1993 (Citizens Association YMCA file).

<sup>72</sup>Colorado Daily, 9 April 1993 (Citizens Association YMCA file).

Building new parks or renovating existing parks was not the only improvements or changes taking place with the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department during this time frame. In April 1988, the Parks and Recreation Department announced plans to offer a new adventure program for teens that summer. The new program would offer youth between the ages of 13 and 18 an opportunity to go rock climbing, canoeing and hiking. Each week-long session would begin with three days of instruction and then end with a three-day expedition to such places as Rocky Mountain National Park or Mount Evans in Colorado or a rock climbing place in Wyoming called Vedaauwoo. The cost for each session would range between \$105 and \$146. The program grew out of the Outdoor Program offered by the University of Colorado.<sup>73</sup>

Another change taking place during this time was the location of the Parks and Recreation Department offices. The Parks and Recreation Department needed a new facility to house its offices since its facility, dubbed the Treehouse, located northeast of the main library was going to be torn down to make way for the library expansion. In 1990, the Parks and Recreation Department began searching for a new facility. One facility under consideration was the Church of Christ building and its grounds located at 3198 Broadway next to the North Boulder Recreation Center. Buying the church building would not only provide the space sought to house the Parks and Recreation Department offices, but also the much needed additional parking spaces for the North Boulder Recreation Center. A slight drawback of this facility was the location since the building was not located downtown. The majority of city offices were located in the downtown area and the city wanted to keep all city offices close together. Nevertheless, the former church was renovated to house the Parks and Recreation Department offices. However, when the Church of Christ building was initially approved for purchase by council in July 1990, the church building was seen as a

---

<sup>73</sup>Camera, 18 April 1988.

temporary solution to the housing needs of the Parks and Recreation Department. The church building was expected to house the Parks and Recreation offices for no more than five years, until space closer to the other city offices downtown could be found. Council granted final approval to purchasing the Church of Christ property in January 1991. The Parks and Recreation Department moved into the new offices at 3198 Broadway, the renovated church, in October 1991.<sup>74</sup>

In addition to finding new office space, the Parks and Recreation Department came under new leadership in 1990. A new director of the department was selected to replace Mike Segrest that year. Segrest had been named the new assistant city manager. The city selected Chris Dropinski to become the new Parks and Recreation Department Director in April 1990. Dropinski was chosen out of the 100 candidates who had applied for the position. Dropinski became the fourth female to head a city department. Dropinski took charge of the Parks and Recreation Department on May 14, 1990.<sup>75</sup>

---

<sup>74</sup>Ibid., 10 July 1990 and 9 January 1991 and 25 October 1991.

<sup>75</sup>Ibid., 28 April 1990.

## Chapter 6 HOUSING AND HUMAN SERVICES

The social needs and essential requirements of the Boulder people have been of major importance for the city. The city agency charged with addressing the social needs of Boulder residents is the Department of Housing and Human Services, which was formerly called Human Resources Development when the agency was first created in 1973. Some of the human services that the city helps provide include supplying adequate affordable housing, protecting human rights, mediating differences, educating parents on quality day-care, counseling youth and providing programs and services for the senior citizen population. Often joining in partnership with other agencies in the delivery of those human services, the city has helped to provide for the social needs and aspirations of its community. One such agency working with the city to improve Boulder's housing situation and to provide low-income residents housing is the Boulder Housing Authority. The Boulder Housing Authority, a quasi-governmental entity created under state law, has the responsibility to provide safe and sanitary housing conditions for low-income Boulder residents. To carry out that responsibility, the Boulder Housing Authority acquires, builds and manages low-income housing projects within the city.<sup>1</sup> At times, the Boulder Housing Authority and the city disagree on how to provide low-income housing units, but certainly not the need to provide low-income housing in Boulder. In addition to looking at the housing situation in Boulder and the city's efforts to provide adequate housing, this chapter will look at the services provided by the multidimensional organization known as the Department of Housing and Human Services. Divisions of this organization other than housing include Children's Services Center, Youth Services, Senior Services, the Office of Human Rights and the Community Mediation Service.

---

<sup>1</sup>Louise Smart, Housing Authority Chair, to David Potter, Chairman of Parks and Recreation Advisory Board, 3 January 1990, Letter in Boulder Housing Authority file.

### Housing

According to a Daily Camera article dated October 3, 1985, Boulder was the most costly city in the county to buy a home. However, the other Boulder County cities were gaining ground in housing costs. Over the past 12 years, housing costs in Boulder had risen 8 percent over inflation. In comparison, housing costs in neighboring cities had risen between 15 to 40 percent over the same 12 year period. Nonetheless, housing still remained cheaper in those neighboring cities than in Boulder. Rising housing costs was not the only problem facing Boulder residents. Boulder residents also found it difficult to find low-income housing units within the city. In June 1985, the results of a four-month study on Boulder's housing situation were revealed to city council. The housing study was conducted by Marshall Kaplan, who was dean of the Graduate School of Public Affairs at the University of Colorado in Denver. The report concluded that overall Boulder excelled at providing a wide-range of housing, except for low-income residents. In fact, the city's efforts to provide low-income housing were considered weak. The city of Boulder's inability to provide sufficient low-income housing units was a problem, but it was one that could be corrected. With federal housing funds dwindling, Kaplan recommended the city establish a Housing Trust Fund to help low-income residents afford housing in Boulder. Kaplan offered suggestions as to how the city could raise money to establish such a trust fund. Kaplan's suggestions included charging development or permit fees, increasing the city's sales tax, or allowing residential developers to give the city money for low-income housing projects in lieu of building the required 15 percent moderately priced units—hence a developer buy out. All residential developers, including developers of smaller housing projects of 10 or less units, would be affected if the city passed an ordinance allowing the developer buy out. Council favored the developer buy out option. The other suggestions of Kaplan were either rejected or not discussed by council.<sup>2</sup>

---

<sup>2</sup>Camera, 3 October 1985 and 17, 18 June 1985.

In August 1985, council passed an ordinance (Ordinance #4929) whereby developers of small housing projects of 10 or less units were required to either provide moderate or low-income housing or make a payment to the Boulder Housing Authority. Before this ordinance was passed, small developers were exempt from providing a percentage of moderate or low-income housing units unlike larger developers, who were required.<sup>3</sup> The developer buy out was just one way the city set out to correct its weakness of not providing enough low-income housing units for Boulder residents. Another way the city attempted to correct its weakness was by continuing to finish two low-income housing projects started at the time the report was released. One of the low-income housing projects was known as Bridgewalk and the second as White Owl.

After three years on the drawing board, construction of the Bridgewalk housing project started in May 1985. The location of this housing project was just south of Table Mesa Drive and west of the Boulder Turnpike. The city of Boulder and the private developer Wonderland Hill Development Co. joined forces to build the low-income housing project. This was the first joint low-income housing project built using both public and private funds. The Bridgewalk project consisted of 124 units spread through 21 buildings of condominiums and townhouses. According to the Boulder Housing Authority, 25 of the 124 units were low-income units, with monthly rent ranging between \$250 and \$350. The other units rented between \$350 and \$650 depending on the size of the unit. The 25 low-income units were scattered throughout the project so no one could differentiate. Besides the residential units, the Bridgewalk project also included a park with a pond, a commercial building occupied by a law firm and pedestrian friendly streets, whereby pedestrians had the right of way and cars were forced to slow down by curves and other barriers. Total cost of the Bridgewalk housing project was \$7.2 million. The project was built on city-owned land. The Housing Authority

---

<sup>3</sup>Camera, 21 August 1985.

and Wonderland Hill Development Co. split construction costs. Once built, the Housing Authority intended to manage the project and rent those 25 low-income units. After 10 years, the two project partners—the Housing Authority and Wonderland Hill Development Co.—planned on selling all units except for the 25 low-income units and then splitting the profits. A grand opening ceremony marking the near completion of the Bridgewalk housing project was held the last weekend in May 1986. Some tenants had already moved in, but the majority of the apartments were not yet finished. Full occupancy was expected by September 1986 when the entire project would be completed.<sup>4</sup>

The Housing Authority's second low-income housing project known as White Owl was to be built on 13.3 acres of land located in north Boulder at the west end of Locust Avenue. The White Owl project was to include both attached and detached houses of 900 to 1200 square-feet ranging in price from \$50,000 to \$57,000. Therefore, individuals or families earning between \$16,000 and \$27,000 could afford one of the houses. However, unlike the Bridgewalk housing project, the White Owl housing project was so embedded in controversy that the project eventually died. Plans for the White Owl housing project began in 1984, when the Boulder Housing Authority learned that the Parks and Recreation Department was considering selling approximately 14 acres of land. The prospect of those 14 acres put the White Owl project in motion. The Housing Authority had been searching for potential housing sites for sometime. In fact, the Housing Authority had a standing request with the Real Estate Office to keep an eye out for potential housing sites. Here's where the controversy started. The Parks and Recreation Department had purchased those 14 acres of land now for sale with a portion of the \$7.4 million Parks and Recreation Bond issue approved by voters in 1981. The Parks and Recreation Department intended to build community parks among other things with that \$7.4 million. When the land was purchased

---

<sup>4</sup>Camera, 30 May 1985; Colorado Daily, 31 May - 1 June 1985; Camera, 25 October 1985 and 2 June 1986.

for the community park planned for north Boulder, the city was forced to purchase those 14 acres along with the 57 acres it wanted for the park. The owners refused to sell the land otherwise. By selling those unwanted 14 acres to the Housing Authority, the Parks and Recreation Department would have money to develop the community park in north Boulder. Not surprisingly, residents in north Boulder questioned the legality of using bond funds to purchase a site and then selling a portion of that land to the Housing Authority. Voters had approved the bond issue for the acquisition of land intended for a park not a housing project.<sup>5</sup>

Despite objections and opposition towards selling the land to the Housing Authority, the Boulder Parks and Recreation Advisory Board granted initial approval to sell the land in June 1985. The board voted 5 to 2 in favor of selling the 13.3 acres to the Boulder Housing Authority. Final approval by the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board was granted on July 10, 1985. That decision to sell the land went before city council. Council delayed making a decision until November 1985. The Boulder City Council approved selling the 13.3 acres of land to the Housing Authority for \$350,000. The vote was 5 to 4 in favor. Those voting in favor of the land sale were Mayor Ruth Correll and council members Homer Page, Linda Jourgensen, Roger Olson and Bev Sears. Council members opposing the motion were Phil Stern, Annette Anderson, Bob Greenlee and Spense Havlick.<sup>6</sup>

The legality of selling the land to the Boulder Housing Authority was not the only point of contention surrounding this White Owl project. A second point of contention was the proposed density of the White Owl housing project. The Housing Authority originally planned a 120 unit development with a density of 8.5 units per acre. In comparison, the average density of the nearby neighborhoods was only 3 units per acre, although the

---

<sup>5</sup>Camera, 24 June 1985 and 27 February 1985.

<sup>6</sup>Ibid., 25 June 1985 and 11 July 1985 and 13 November 1985.

Cottages condominiums located just south of the proposed housing project had a density of 6.9 units per acre. North Boulder residents complained about the project's density. In efforts to appease north Boulder residents, the Housing Authority lowered the number of units to 100 with a density of 7.5 units per acre. Residents in north Boulder were still dissatisfied and continued to complain of the project's density of 7.5.<sup>7</sup>

Plans for the White Owl project quickly came to a halt the following year when new council members were elected. Two people who had favored the White Owl project were no longer in office in 1986. Mayor Ruth Correll retired at the end of 1985 and Councilman Roger Olson was not re-elected in November 1985. The two newly elected council members—Cindy Carlisle and Steve Pomerance—opposed the White Owl project. In January 1986, an unofficial vote by council members on the project was taken at the council's weekend retreat at the National Center for Atmospheric Research.
