That unofficial vote was 6 to 3 in favor of killing the White Owl project. Instead of building new housing, council favored using existing buildings to house low and moderate-income residents. This shift in policy to use existing buildings for Boulder's housing needs reflected the anti-growth sentiment of the newly elected council members.<sup>8</sup>

In February 1986, council outlined several approaches as to how the city could meet the housing needs of the poor without building new subsidized housing projects. Those approaches were as follows: "Converting existing housing from rentals to owner-occupied; Increasing incentives for landlords to renovate housing; Investigating mobile homes as a low-cost housing source; Providing financial aid to help the poor buy existing homes."<sup>9</sup> The Housing Authority Director John Martinez disagreed with the new council's approach to

---

<sup>7</sup>Ibid., 24 June 1985 and 27 February 1985.

<sup>8</sup>Ibid., 12 January 1986.

<sup>9</sup>Ibid., 9 February 1986.

solving Boulder's housing needs of the poor. According to Martinez, major problems result from focusing only on the existing housing stock. Problems included financing, maintenance costs and the relocating costs of higher income residents evicted if a property is bought with federal funds and used for low-income housing. By law, the Housing Authority would have to finance the relocation costs. According to a Daily Camera article dated February 12, 1986, the Boulder Housing Authority intended to challenge council's decision of only using existing buildings. The Housing Authority had a waiting list of 535 people qualified for low-income housing units. Focusing on only existing housing would not be enough to meet the city's need. After a few months of being at odds with city council, the Housing Authority backed down and conceded to council's wishes.<sup>10</sup> The Housing Authority agreed to a two-year moratorium on new construction projects. Projects already underway were exempt from the moratorium. The Housing Authority also agreed to dropping their "...goal of building 50 units of low-income housing a year and replacing it with a goal of maintaining what exists."<sup>11</sup> Focus turned to utilizing existing housing units for the city's low-income housing needs.

One source of low-income housing explored by the city of Boulder during this time was mobile home parks. The city viewed mobile home parks as a possible solution to the city's low-income housing shortage. As early as 1985, the city explored ways to preserve mobile home parks as a source of low-income housing. Five mobile home parks within the city limits were in existence at the time. Those five mobile home parks were: Branding Iron Mobile Park at 2685 28th Street, Boulder Mobile Manor at 2637 Valmont Road, Mapleton Park at 2635 Mapleton Avenue, Orchard Grove at 3003 Valmont Road and Vista Village at 5000 Butte Street. In 1985, the city grew concerned over the possibility of losing these

---

<sup>10</sup>Ibid., 9, 12 February 1986 and 28 May 1986.

<sup>11</sup>Ibid., 10 June 1986.

mobile home parks as a low and moderate-income housing source if the mobile home park owners ever decided to redevelop the park. If a redevelopment did occur, mobile home residents would be displaced and homeless. The city's concerns were warranted since a threat of redeveloping a mobile home park in Boulder did exist. Boulder Mobile Manor was the park threatened. The new owner of that mobile home park, C.S. Company, intended to raze the park and build condominiums. As a result of this threat, city council created a new zoning classification for mobile homes in May 1985. With the new zoning classification—Mobile Home-Established (MH-E)—mobile home park owners were prevented from redeveloping the land, thus preserving the mobile home parks as a low-income housing source. However, before the city rezoned any of the five mobile home parks to the new mobile home zoning classification, the owner of Boulder Mobile Manor and the mobile park tenants attempted to work out a deal with the help of the city, whereby the tenants would buy out the owner and stop the redevelopment. With the threat of redevelopment dissipated, the city planning staff questioned the need to rezone Boulder Mobile Manor with the new Mobile Home-Established zoning classification. Instead, the planning staff recommended rezoning three of Boulder's other mobile home parks—Orchard Grove, Vista Village and Mapleton Park. Branding Iron Mobile Park was not recommended for rezoning. The planning staff felt that the Branding Iron Mobile Park lots were too small, plus many of the trailers were obsolete and half of the park was situated in the path of a proposed flood channel. Residents of Branding Iron Mobile Park feared losing their home if the park was not rezoned. The final decision of rezoning the mobile home parks rested with city council.

Council intended to address the issue at its June meeting. The rezoning issue turned tense. Some mobile home park owners threatened to raise the rent if tenants spoke in favor of rezoning. As a result, some mobile home residents, who attended the June 18, 1985 city council meeting, asked council not to rezone their mobile home park. The array of mixed feelings on the subject led council to postpone the mobile home rezoning issue for three of

the parks—Branding Iron, Orchard Grove and Mapleton Park. Council delayed its decision, not only to diffuse the situation, but also to allow more time for the city to negotiate a deal with the mobile home park owners to guarantee rent would not be raised and that the parks would not be redeveloped. Council did approve rezoning the Vista Village Mobile Park for the new zoning classification—Mobile Home-Established—at that June meeting.<sup>12</sup>

After numerous delays, council finally made a decision regarding the other three mobile home parks in December 1985. Council voted 6 to 3 in favor of rezoning Branding Iron Mobile Home Park to the zoning classification Mobile Home Established (MH-E). The three council members opposing the zoning change felt the land should not be rezoned for residential use since the park was situated in the floodplain. As for the other two mobile home parks—Mapleton Park and Orchard Grove—council worked out a five-year agreement with the owner of those two mobile home parks. Council voted unanimously to pay the owner Lu Nuttal \$6,000 a year per park to ensure that rent increases would be limited, that mobile home owners would be able to sell their trailers on the same lot and that the city would be given the first opportunity to purchase the mobile home parks if the owner ever decided to sell the park.<sup>13</sup>

In August 1988, city council announced that the city was prepared to encourage new mobile home parks as a source of affordable housing. The continued fear that mobile home park owners would convert their properties to more profitable uses, thus displacing several hundred mobile home residents, prompted the city's exploration of developing a mobile home subdivision. The city received a \$20,000 federal Community Development Block Grant to fund a study to explore the feasibility of a mobile home subdivision. A survey was conducted to determine the public's perception of mobile home parks and to establish

---

<sup>12</sup>Ibid., 16, 19 June 1985.

<sup>13</sup>Ibid., 17 July 1985 and 18 December 1985.

guidelines for developing an attractive mobile home subdivision. University of Colorado Professor Lou Sauer and his students, along with the city's research and evaluation division, conducted the survey and established the guidelines for the mobile home subdivision. The city then selected a task force and consultants to conduct the feasibility study. The feasibility study entailed surveying land availability and determining the cost for such a project. The results of that feasibility study were revealed to council in June 1989. The task force concluded that such a project was both needed and economically feasible for a private developer. However, according to the study very few 10-acre sites for the mobile home subdivision were available or suitable for the project. One site had possibility. A 9.5 acre site, owned by the Boulder Parks and Recreation Department, located at Baseline Road and 55th Street. Council planned to assess if private developers would be interested in creating a mobile home subdivision.<sup>14</sup> No new mobile home subdivision was built during this time frame—1985-1990.

In February 1989, city council appointed an Affordable Housing Task Force to evaluate the city's housing program. The task force reported back to council that fall. The task force concluded that Boulder's subsidized housing programs assisted mostly families with moderate incomes and well-off singles. Boulder was not doing enough to help the truly poor. To correct this problem and to better serve the poor, the Affordable Housing Task Force searched for ways to turn the subsidized housing program around. One suggestion of the task force was to establish a housing trust fund that could be used to build or buy units to rent to the poor. Revenue sources to set up this housing trust fund under consideration by the task force were as follows: creating a new tax on commercial development, requiring employees to pay fees, establishing a real estate transfer tax, increasing the sales tax or

---

<sup>14</sup>Ibid., 22 August 1988 and 21 June 1989.

establishing a fee to be paid by out-of-town landlords.<sup>15</sup>

Over the next couple of months, the Affordable Housing Task Force along with Boulder's Department of Housing and Human Services developed a plan that would increase subsidized rental housing for low-income people in Boulder. That plan developed was presented to council in February 1990. The plan proposed raising the level of subsidized rental units for low-income people from 3.3 percent to 5 percent by the year 2017. To reach that 5 percent goal, a total of 63 new or renovated units would have to be added each year over a 25 year period. Total cost of the plan was estimated to be \$3.2 million a year. To finance this plan, the task force proposed an increase in property taxes and development fees. Council supported the goal of the plan, but not how to finance the plan. Council directed the group to find alternatives to financing the plan other than an increase in property tax. In May 1990, council was still struggling with how to finance more subsidized housing for those earning 30 to 60 percent of the median income. Council decided the city should raise \$1 million annually for its new subsidized housing program—Community Housing Assistance Program (CHAP). The \$1 million raised would establish a Housing Trust Fund which both private and nonprofit organizations could use to construct affordable housing for low-income families. However, how the city intended to raise that \$1 million to establish the Housing Trust Fund remained uncertain. The earlier recommendations made by the task force to increase property taxes and new development fees to finance the city's subsidized housing efforts were under consideration once again. A final decision on how to finance the city's Community Housing Assistance Program was put off until the fall, when council would decide the 1991 budget.<sup>16</sup>

In October 1990, council approved both a property tax increase and a tax on new

---

<sup>15</sup>Ibid., 23 October 1989.

<sup>16</sup>Camera, 11, 14 February 1990 and Colorado Daily, 29-31 May 1990.

buildings based on square-footage to finance the Community Housing Assistance Program. Approximately 70 percent of the \$1 million housing program would be financed through the 0.8-mill increase in property taxes. The remaining cost of the housing program would be financed from the excise tax on new buildings. The taxes were to go into effect November 5, 1990, before voters decided the fate of a ballot initiative (Amendment 1) that would limit Colorado local governments ability to raise taxes or fees without getting approval from voters first. Boulder residents voted down the amendment measure in November, therefore council pushed back the starting date of new taxes until 1991.<sup>17</sup>

In December 1990, concern over the 13.3 acres in north Boulder belonging to the Boulder Housing Authority resurfaced when the city planned to annex approximately 384 acres in north Boulder. Those 13.3 acres, the intended location of the White Owl housing project before it were abandoned in 1986, were included in the 384 acres the city planned to annex. Residents of north Boulder were concerned over how the city would zone those 13.3 acres. The Boulder Planning Board had recommended the land be zoned High Density Residential. Not surprisingly, north Boulder residents objected to the high density zoning. As a result, council delayed its decision on how to zone that land until March 1991. Council zoned those 13.3 acres of land for medium density. At the time the land was annexed, the Housing Authority Director Karen Thoreson informed north Boulder residents that the Housing Authority had no current plans for those 13.3 acres, but that the Housing Authority intended to use that site for subsidized housing someday. That someday has arrived. The Housing Authority has plans in the works to develop a housing project for low and moderate-income families at that site. That project is known as Foothills Community. The current plans include building apartments, townhouses and carriage houses ranging in size from one-bedroom to three-bedroom. The Housing Authority plans to build a total of 75 units on those 13.3 acres. Work on developing the Foothills Community started back in

---

<sup>17</sup>Camera 24 October 1990 and 21 November 1990.

1995.<sup>18</sup>

### **Human Services**

Charged with the task of evaluating, researching, planning and providing for the city's social needs, the Boulder Department of Human Resources Development, now called Department of Housing and Human Services, released its 1985 Social Report in August. The report, entitled "Family, Children, & Municipal Policy, examined the declining number of families with children living in Boulder and what steps, if any, the city could take to stop the exodus. According to the report, the number of children living in Boulder had dropped 37 percent between the 1970 and 1980 census. Despite this decrease, the report indicated that Boulder still had plenty of children. More middle-income families were living in Lafayette and Louisville because they could not afford to live in Boulder. However, the report still considered those families as Boulder families since many of those parents worked, shopped and played in Boulder. The report concluded that Boulder needed to take a "regional view" of the child shortage in Boulder. That the city should look at regional statistics when it comes to counting the children population of Boulder. In addition, the report indicated that Boulder needed to improve its image as a good place to raise a family. Reasons for the family exodus from Boulder were exhibited in the report also. Those reasons for the Boulder family exodus included Boulder's high housing costs, the economy and Boulder's image as an elitist community. Critics of the report claimed the report's conclusion to include regional statistics when counting children dismissed the fact that Boulder was losing families and children to nearby communities. Although under fire and criticized, no one suggested discontinuing these social reports. Instead, it was suggested that the reports be structured differently, that perhaps city council and the city manager participate and decide each year's

---

<sup>18</sup>Ibid., 19 December 1990; 6 March 1991; 2 December 1990 (Annexation Municipal Government file); 12 October 1999 (Boulder Housing Authority file).

topic for the social report.<sup>19</sup>

In March 1986, Melba Shepard resigned as the director of Boulder's Human Resources Development Department. After serving for 12 years as the Human Resources Development director, Shepard wished to experience more personal growth by exploring her spirituality. When Shepard became the director, back in 1974, the department consisted of only Youth Services, the Office of Human Rights and a small crime prevention program. The department had only six employees. Both the Housing and Senior Services divisions were added later. According to Shepard, the directorship had become more administrative over the years rather than working directly with the public who needed the social services the department helped provide. In May 1986, Susan Purdy was selected to replace Shepard as the Human Resources Development director. Purdy was chosen from the 80 candidates who had applied for the position. Purdy would be responsible for directing a department with a budget of approximately \$2.6 million and a work force of 100 employees. Before taking the position, Purdy had worked in the Boulder Planning Department as director of community development.<sup>20</sup>

In November 1988, Purdy, along with the Housing Authority Board Chairman David Hill, had the task of selecting a new Housing Authority director to replace John Martinez. Martinez quit as Boulder's Housing Authority director to take a new job as executive director of the Kentucky Housing Corporation in Frankfort, Kentucky. Martinez served as Boulder's Housing Authority director for four years. Martinez was only the second individual to hold that position of director since the Housing Authority was established in Boulder 21 years ago. In December 1988, Karen Thoreson was selected by Purdy and Hill to succeed Martinez. Prior to becoming Boulder's new Housing Authority director, Thoreson

---

<sup>19</sup>Camera, 20 August 1985 and Colorado Daily, 30-31 August 1985.

<sup>20</sup>Camera, 22 March 1986 and 28 May 1986.

had been the deputy housing director.<sup>21</sup>

In February 1987, the Boulder Child Care Support Center, a division of Boulder's Housing and Human Services Department, announced that it was going to move to a new location in the Crossroads Mall and expand its services there. Besides moving to a new location and expanding its services, the Boulder Child Care Support Center changed its name to Children's Services Center. At its new location in the Crossroads Mall, Children's Services Center occupied the rent-free space in the mall belonging to the city. The city acquired the rent-free space in the mall when MaceRich Co. expanded the Crossroads Mall in the early 1980s. Originally, a drop-in baby-sitting service occupied that space, but it closed after the first year. Other attempts to run a day care center also failed. The Children's Services move to the Crossroads Mall occurred in March 1987. According to Grace Hardy, the director of the Children's Services Center, moving the Children's Services Center into Crossroads Mall would make the center more visible and accessible to parents. Upon moving to the Crossroads Mall, the Children's Services Center took over the United Way First Call For Help program, which was a referral service for parents looking for day care. Other services the Children's Services Center provides include training and support for child care providers, educating parents on what to look for in quality day care, and tracking and maintaining information on all licensed day care facilities in Boulder County. The city's Youth Services, also a division of the Housing and Human Services, moved to 2160 Spruce Street, the former location of the Boulder Child Care Support Center. At its new location, the Youth Services division would house its meetings, counseling sessions and its help phone-line called Youthreach.<sup>22</sup> In June 1987, Grace Hardy was appointed by Governor Roy Romer to become project director of a new state program on early childhood

---

<sup>21</sup>Ibid., 25 November 1988 and 20 December 1988 (Boulder Housing Authority file).

<sup>22</sup>Times-Call, 8 April 1987 (Boulder Children's Services file) and Camera, 3 February 1987.

development called First Impressions. Hardy left her city position as Children's Services Center director in August 1987 to take her state post.<sup>23</sup> Sandy McCarthy was selected to replace Hardy as the director of Children's Services.

The city's Community Mediation Service, another division of Boulder's Housing and Human Services, helped residents resolve their differences more amicably through mediation rather than small claims court. According to the Community Mediation Service Director Kon Damas, 95 percent of cases mediated were resolved. The mediation program evolved out the city's Landlord Tenant Mediation Project of the early 1980s. This free mediation service offered by the city was not limited to landlord and tenant disputes. Other issues handled included roommate disputes, neighbor disagreements and noise and disturbance matters. Out of the 2,400 phone calls the Community Mediation Service received in 1987, 150 resulted in requests for mediation. The peak time of the year for complaints and calls to the Community Mediation Service was late August and early September when the university students returned to town. Not surprising, since university students comprise about one-quarter of the program's disputes.<sup>24</sup>

In addition to helping residents resolve their differences, the city helped to protect the human rights of Boulder residents. Since Boulder's gay rights ordinance went into effect December 1987, five individuals had filed formal complaints of discriminatory practices by Boulder businesses in 1989 to the city's Office of Human Rights. Two of the complaints were aimed at health clubs for denying non-heterosexual couples joint membership. Another was aimed at a hotel for refusing to rent its ballroom to a lesbian organization. The remaining two complaints alleged discriminatory hiring practices. As of June 1989, two of the complaints were still being investigated, one had been withdrawn and the one aimed at

---

<sup>23</sup>Camera, 20 June 1987.

<sup>24</sup>Colorado Daily, 26-28 August 1988.

the Flatiron Athletic Club had been settled. The Flatiron Athletic Club changed its policy on couple's membership, allowing gay couples residing at the same residence joint membership. In the case aimed at the hotel, the hotel capitulated. Judy Dixon, director of the Boulder Human Rights division, and her staff had the task of determining whether or not the gay rights ordinance had been violated and if so how to remedy the violation.<sup>25</sup>

In January 1988, Senior Center Director John Torres took a new job as executive director for the Colorado Association of Homes and Services for the Elderly. Torres had served as the director of the Senior Services Center since 1976. When Torres started, the Senior Center was located in a 4,000 square-foot basement room of a county-owned building on 13th Street across from the Boulder County Courthouse. The Senior Center moved to its current location at 909 Arapahoe Avenue in 1979. In February 1988, Tom Trujillo was named to replace Torres as the director of the Senior Services Division and Diane Hilliard was named assistant director of Senior Services. In September 1990, the Senior Center celebrated the completion of a 1,400 square-foot expansion of the 14,500 square-foot Senior Center at 909 Arapahoe Avenue. The expansion included creating a spacious and sunny lobby and lounge, a larger dining room, and additional bathrooms, storage and office space as well as more space for adult day care. The 1,400 square-foot addition was funded in part by the 0.38 percent sales tax increase approved by voters in 1987. The sales tax contributed \$250,000 towards the \$350,000 addition to the Senior Center. Federal grants and donations from the Senior Center Foundation and other city departments helped cover the remaining cost of the expansion. On average, approximately 450 Boulder seniors 55 and older visit the

---

<sup>25</sup>Camera, 25 June 1989.

Senior Center.<sup>26</sup> The expansion was greatly needed.

---

<sup>26</sup>Camera, 9 January 1988; Camera Business Plus, 23 February 1988; Camera, 7 September 1990.

## CHAPTER 7 PUBLIC SAFETY

Boulder, wanting to provide its residents with a safe environment in which to live, implemented several measures to ensure the public's safety between 1985 and 1990. For instance, the city adopted the use of the Enhanced-911 system, strengthened both floodway and fire code regulations and increased police presence on the Pearl Street Mall and the University Hill area. The Enhanced-911 system allowed emergency response personnel to trace where an emergency call was being made in order to quicken the response time of emergency personnel. Stricter floodway regulations in conjunction with a flood control plan reduced the area and number of properties at risk during a flood, thus protecting human lives. By revising the fire code regulations to require the installation of sprinklers in high-rise buildings, the city wished not only to protect the residents of those high-rise buildings, but also to prevent or curtail the spread of fire. The stepped up police presence on the mall not only helped to combat the aggressive panhandling of transients, but also the drug dealings that took place in the downtown area. With police foot-patrols on the Hill, the city wanted to deter the uncongenial behavior of college students like underage drinking, fighting, drunkenness and disorderly conduct. In addition to increasing police presence and adopting new emergency systems and regulations, the city targeted traffic problems like speeding motorists, pedestrian-vehicle accidents and bicycle safety. Boulder's Police and Fire Departments, along with the Boulder Regional Communication Center help to ensure the public safety of Boulder residents. How these agencies and the city carried out that responsibility of protecting its citizens during this time period will be the focus of this chapter.

### Emergency Communications

In November 1985, Boulder's emergency communications system broke down over the weekend of November 22nd. Evidently, the Boulder Regional Communications Center's

10 year-old microwave radio system went haywire. The breakdown hampered radio contact between police, fire and ambulance services. As a result, Mountain Bell was contacted to run emergency phone lines through the phone company's central office and connect them to the phone lines already running into the Justice Center, where Boulder's Regional Communications Center was located. The cost of setting up the emergency phone lines was \$4,000. The Boulder Regional Communications Center was able to transmit messages to all areas of Boulder County by the following Tuesday. However, according to the Communications Director David Atherton, dispatchers still had a problem receiving transmissions from remote areas in the mountains and from agencies outside Boulder County along the Front Range. The break down of the microwave radio system had been handled, but the city and county had to decide on a permanent solution to the problem.

Options considered included repairing the faltering microwave system at a cost of \$5,000 to \$7,000 or purchasing a new microwave system that would cost approximately \$150,000. If city and county officials decided on the later option, there would be additional expenses like the cost of the interim phone lines while the new microwave system was installed. A third option considered, but not favored, was to expand the telephone hookup. This option would render a nearly completed 18-month long improvement of the Boulder Regional Communications Center useless. According to Atherton, moving the communications center farther east would eliminate the long-standing problems with the microwave system. Whatever option the city and county decided to take, both would share the financial costs of that option, except for a small portion of the tab. Both Lafayette and Louisville were to help pay a portion of the tab.

A decision was made in June 1986. The city and county decided to spend \$66,445 to improve the existing microwave communications system. The city's portion of the improvement bill was \$31,740, while the county's was \$34,705. A consultant hired to assess the communications system recommended repairing the microwave system and making

electrical improvements to protect the equipment from power surges and power failures. The consultant also recommended installing an air conditioning and heating system to minimize temperature extremes in the antenna buildings located throughout the county. According to the consultant, making those improvements would add another 5 to 10 years to the life of the microwave system.<sup>1</sup>

In October 1986, the cities of Boulder County and the county government were considering a new emergency phone system called Enhanced 911. With the Enhanced 911 system, calls would be traced, thus giving police, fire and ambulance dispatchers instant access to the address from which an emergency call was made. Not only was the caller's telephone number and address displayed on the dispatchers' computer screens, but also the address and telephone numbers of the police, fire and medical help serving that jurisdiction. The response time would be reduced by half the average response time with this new Enhanced 911 system (E-911). The current 911 system in use at that time did not have the technology to trace the source of a call. Another advantage of the E-911 system was that it would bring in those rural areas not currently covered by a 911 system like Erie, Lyons, Allenspark and Nederland. To further ensure help would arrive when needed, emergency telephones were to be installed along the Peak-to-Peak Highway and along Boulder Canyon Drive between Boulder and Nederland.

To finance the new E-911 system, Boulder County residents would be charged a monthly fee on their phone bills they receive from Mountain Bell. Approximately 28 to 30 cents per month would be charged the first two years. After two years the charge per month would be less, somewhere between 23 and 25 cents. The higher charge the first two years would cover the installation cost of the E-911 system. However, before the Enhanced 911 system could be implemented, the county and all cities in the county along with the fire

---

<sup>1</sup>Camera, 27 November 1985 and 5 June 1986.

districts of that region served by the system were required to sign an intergovernmental agreement. That agreement would establish an E-911 five member authority board that would oversee the installation and operation of the E-911 system. The money collected by Mountain Bell would be turned over to that authority board. The fire districts and most of the cities and towns in Boulder County were interested in the Enhanced 911 system except for Ward. Ward residents held a public vote and rejected the E-911 system. Representatives from Erie, Louisville, Lafayette and Jamestown signed the intergovernmental agreement, as did the Boulder County Fire Districts. In July 1987, the Boulder County Commissioners tabled a vote on whether or not to approve the E-911 system. The commissioners wanted to allow the public more time to consider the issue and to gather more information. The county commissioners also had a problem with the makeup of the E-911 authority or governing board that would be controlling the purse strings.<sup>2</sup>

The proposed makeup of the five member E-911 governing board was the Boulder County Sheriff, the Boulder Police Chief, the Longmont Director of Public Safety, the President of the Boulder County Firefighters' Association and one member chosen by the other Boulder County municipalities on a rotating basis. The county commissioners disliked the idea of having the sheriff act as the authority's voice for unincorporated Boulder County and wanted to appoint a county representative. Commissioner Josie Heath filed a motion to add another governing E-911 board position that the commissioners would appoint, but that motion failed. Therefore, despite objections over the makeup of the governing board, the Boulder County Commissioners unanimously approved the Enhanced 911 system on July 21, 1987. The city of Longmont also approved joining the E-911 system just the week before. Only the city of Boulder had yet to approve the E-911 system. After debating the costs of the E-911 system, Boulder City Council voted 4 to 3 in favor of joining the countywide Enhanced 911 phone system on August 18, 1987. Council members who voted

---

<sup>2</sup>Camera, 17 October 1986; Times-Call, 18 January 1987; Camera, 8 July 1987.

against joining because of the cost of the E-911 system were Cindy Carlisle, Phil Stern and Spense Havlick. In September 1987, an intergovernmental agreement was signed establishing the Boulder Regional Emergency Telephone Service Authority that would oversee the installation and operation of the Enhanced 911 system. The following fall, council accepted a \$5.7 million budget for the Enhanced 911 phone system over the next 7 years. Residents with telephones were to pay 38 cents a month for the E-911 service. The fee was to go in effect January 1989. The E-911 system was to be operational by 1990.<sup>3</sup>

However, various circumstances prevented the E-911 system from being totally completed on time. Delays in the contractor's schedule moved the start-up date to December 5, 1990. But even that start-up date was suspect, according to Boulder County Undersheriff Al Staehle, since the system still needed to be tested first. In fact, the Enhanced 911 system did not become operational until March 14, 1991. The Enhanced 911 phone system was installed by the Boulder company Systems Concepts of Colorado. Total cost of the Enhanced 911 system dispatch hardware and software, installation and telephone subscriber data was \$1,835,000. One element of the Enhanced 911 phone system that was completed as scheduled was the installation of emergency telephones along Boulder County roads. The installation of emergency telephones took place in September 1990. Motorists could use those emergency telephones to call for help. The phones automatically dialed 911 when activated and connected the caller to the Boulder Regional Communications Center or an operator that would contact the communications center. The emergency telephones were placed every five to seven miles along lonely stretches of road where there was no access to public phones. Signs alerting motorists of the emergency phones were to be posted as part of the project also.<sup>4</sup>

---

<sup>3</sup>Camera, 22 July 1987; Times-Call 22 July 1987; Camera, 19 August 1987 and 16 November 1988.

<sup>4</sup>Times-Call, 19 November 1990 and Camera, 21 March 1991 and 1 September 1990.

In December 1987, Sheriff Brad Leach and Police Chief Jay Propst considered splitting the Boulder Regional Communications Center and creating separate communication operations for the city and county. The Boulder Regional Communications Center had been a joint city and county communication operation since 1972. Leach and Propst explored the idea of splitting the communications operation because of their differences of opinion and philosophy. Police Chief Propst wanted to move his dispatchers out of the floodplain and into a new police building. However, moving to a new location and building outside of the floodplain depended on council's approval of a building purchase for the police department's new headquarters. The building being considered was the former Mountain Bell building at 1805 33rd Street. Sheriff Leach, on the other hand, was more concerned about dispatch time rather than the moving the communications center out of the floodplain, since the center was located on the second floor of the Justice Center building. Plus, emergency generators were located on the roof of the Justice Center. Sheriff Leach considered splitting the communications center operation to gain direct management control over the dispatch lag-time problem. As long as the communication center remained a joint operation Leach would have to share authority with city officials. Even though the dispatch time would improve once the Enhanced 911 phone system was installed, Leach still wanted quicker response time.

Propst and Leach not only held differences of opinion, but also differences in philosophy, specifically on what a communications center should do. Police Chief Propst held the philosophy that all communications for the city including public works calls should be handled by the communications center, while Sheriff Leach believed the center should focus on public safety communications only. Other factors influencing and impacting the decision of whether or not the Boulder Regional Communications Center should remain a joint operation included the differing emergency agencies' dispatching procedures, and the

new added responsibilities the communications center had taken on in recent years like monitoring alarms for banks and schools, paging governmental agencies and taking alarm calls. Complicating a continued joint communications center operation further was the fact that city dispatchers received higher wages and better benefits than county dispatchers doing the exact same job. Sheriff Leach intended to hire the consultant Kenneth Fieler of Fieler & Associates of Sun Valley, California to analyze the situation.<sup>5</sup>

After considerable time and deliberation, a decision was reached. In June 1990, the city and county agreed to continue operating the Boulder Regional Communications Center jointly, despite the long-standing problems. This decision to continue a joint communications operation not only facilitated the installation schedule and operation of the E-911 phone system, but also saved city taxpayers \$150,000 a year. Had the city and county split the operation, city taxpayers would of paid an additional \$150,000 a year. A written agreement to resolve the long-standing problems with running a joint operation was to be composed. The written agreement would strengthen the role of the communications director, equalize pay for dispatchers, and establish a joint handling of job related employee appeals by both the police chief and sheriff. The joint-operated Boulder Regional Communications Center moved to a new location—the Public Safety Center at 1805 33rd Street—on September 10, 1990. At the time of the move, city and county officials were still working on a solution to solve those long-standing problems, specifically the pay discrepancy and procedural problems between city and county dispatchers. One possible solution considered was to make the communications center an independent operation that was separate from both city and county guidelines. According to the Communications Center Director Dave Atherton, having one set of policies for all dispatchers, both city and county, was key to solving the

---

<sup>5</sup>Camera, 20 December 1987.

problems.<sup>6</sup>

### **Flood Control and Protection**

In January 1985, the Blue Ribbon Panel gave council its preliminary report on ways to reduce flood hazards posed by Boulder Creek and its tributaries. The Blue Ribbon Panel was a panel of local flood experts appointed by council back in 1984 to develop a master plan for Boulder Creek tributaries. Council wanted the panel to further study and review an earlier study of Boulder Creek and its 11 tributaries by Greenhorne and O'Mara. The Greenhorne and O'Mara study had favored non-structural solutions to tributary flooding. The panel suggested the city change its strict definition of a floodway on some of Boulder Creek tributaries to avoid costly and unnecessary flood control improvements. The city defined a floodway as two feet of depth, or flow of two feet per second. This definition was stricter than either federal or metropolitan regulatory agencies standards. Based on the panel's assessment, council directed city utilities staff to develop other definitions for the 12 creeks that run through the city except for Boulder Creek. The new definition would not separate the elements of depth and speed to define a floodway. Council also directed city staff to develop an ordinance that would require property owners along the creeks to keep the creek channels clear.

Besides changing the definition of a floodway, the panel's initial report recommended improvements for just three creeks—Goose Creek, Elmer's Two Mile Creek and Two Mile Creek. These three creeks posed the most flood threat. Recommendations for the other creeks were still being prepared. Cost estimates for the improvements to those three creeks were \$16.7 million. The following month, the panel reported back to council with more recommendations on the remaining tributaries. The report recommended \$31.5 million in improvements to reduce flood hazards on 11 tributaries. Improvements

---

<sup>6</sup>Camera, 25 June 1990 and Colorado Daily, 18 September 1990.

recommended included creating detention basins and building small floodwalls to allow shallow waters to flow unimpeded. Council endorsed the \$31.5 million improvement recommendations of the panel in February 1985. However, before council would take any formal action, the city intended to hold a series of public hearings to gather public opinion and input. Some council members questioned the panel's heavy use of structural solutions for flood control.<sup>7</sup>

The Blue Ribbon Panel released its final report in April 1985. The final report of the panel recommended spending \$50 million in private and public money to reduce the flood danger on the tributaries of Boulder Creek. Sources for funding the \$50 million improvements included Boulder's Flood Utility fund, departmental funds of Open Space, Parks and Recreation as well as Transportation, the State Highways Division, ditch companies, railroads and private property owners. The panel felt top priority should be given to making structural improvements like detention ponds, culverts, floodwalls and channels along Goose Creek, creating a detention pond behind the K-Mart store located on 28th Street for Elmer's Two Mile Creek and enlarging the crossing of Bear Canyon Creek at Broadway since these areas posed the most severe flood threat to human lives. Prior to taking any action, various city departments were to review the report before passing the report on to city council. City departments to review the report included the Open Space Board of Trustees and the Parks and Recreation Advisory Board. If the city accepted the recommendations of the panel, public hearings would be held in the summer.<sup>8</sup>

The Blue Ribbon Panel's report triggered considerable debate. The debate centered on whether the flood waters should be channeled away from people and property or should people and property be moved away from the flood path. Approximately 600 households

---

<sup>7</sup>Camera, 9 January 1985 and 27 February 1985.

<sup>8</sup>Ibid., 6 April 1985.

and more than 100 acres of undeveloped land occupying the floodway of the 11 tributaries would be effected in the city's flood improvement efforts. But to what extent those 600 households and 100 plus acres would be affected depended on which approach the city opted to take. A number of public agencies and private groups questioned the recommendations of the panel and the heavy reliance on structural solutions for flood control. Agencies and organizations that disagreed with the panel's recommendations included the Federal Emergency Management Agency, the Boulder Open Space Board of Trustees and the environmental organization PLAN-Boulder County. Even flood expert Gilbert White, who was the former director of the Natural Hazards Institute at the University of Colorado, considered the panel's recommendations as "...too costly, too reliant on structural solutions, and too vague."<sup>9</sup>

Three public hearings were held in August 1985. At those hearings the public could voice their opinion and learn about the recommendations from the panel. Residents attending the first public hearing were not overly receptive to the costly flood control proposal of the Blue Ribbon Panel. Residents wanted additional information. The predominant message of residents attending the first hearing was to let the owners living in the floodplain buy flood insurance. At the last public forum held August 22, 1985, north Boulder residents disapproved using channels as a way to reduce flooding. Instead, residents favored non-structural solutions to control flooding offered by the engineering firm of Greenhorne and O'Mara. Residents felt the city's strict definition of a floodway should be loosened. In addition, residents felt that the estimated \$48 million the Blue Ribbon Panel was recommending for flood control improvements could be better spent on more common life threatening hazards like rock climbing and automobile accidents. All public comments were forwarded to various city departments for review and additional assessment before passing the information to city council. Public comments were forwarded to the following city

---

<sup>9</sup>Ibid., 11 August 1985.

departments: Parks and Recreation Advisory Board, Open Space Board of Trustees and the Boulder Planning Board. Both the Planning Board and the Open Space Board of Trustees favored the non-structural approach to flood control. City planners thought the city should focus more on buying land in the floodplain to prevent people from living there. The Open Space Board was in concurrence and felt that the property purchased in the floodplain should be preserved as open space. To finance such purchases, the Open Space Board recommended increasing flood control utility fees.<sup>10</sup>

At council's study session in March 1986, council favored the less costly approach to control flooding, hence keeping development out of the path of a flood. When property in the floodplain became available, the city would buy the property and restore the land as a park or natural area. Identifying specific costs and finding alternative funding methods was the next step in carrying out the city's flood control plan. Favoring the less costly approach to limit potential flooding on Boulder Creek's 11 tributaries, council adopted the first phase of the Greenhorne and O'Mara floodplain study on May 27, 1986.<sup>11</sup>

The ever present threat of the next 100-year flood required action to be taken to reduce the hazards of flooding. An article in the Daily Camera dated July 27, 1986, reiterated that ever present threat. The article reported that Boulder was the "...highest risk city in the entire state..." for a flash flood just like the one to hit Big Thompson on July 31, 1976.<sup>12</sup> That summer, the city held a seminar on the impact of a Boulder Creek flood for emergency response personnel. The prevailing question of the seminar was could the county handle a flash flood? The answer—emergency personnel were probably prepared to handle a flash flood, but the public probably was not prepared. Boulder County has a sophisticated

---

<sup>10</sup>Ibid., 13, 23 August 1985 and 20 December 1985.

<sup>11</sup>Ibid., 26 March 1986 and 28 May 1986.

<sup>12</sup>Ibid., 27 July 1986.

system that utilizes rain and stream gauges to detect the possible threat of flooding, but the system for warning the public was less sophisticated or comprehensive. The county uses public address systems, sirens, and radio and television announcements to warn the public. The decision of when and how to warn the public was up to the city manager or other government representatives.<sup>13</sup> With that in mind, it should come to no surprise that the city wanted to put less people at risk.

In January 1987, Boulder considered strengthening its floodway regulations by completely banning all construction of new buildings along Boulder Creek and its 11 tributaries. Residential construction in the floodway had been banned in Boulder since 1975. However, commercial construction continued to be allowed provided buildings were flood proofed or elevated two feet above projected flood water levels. Now council was considering banning commercial construction too. Until council decided whether or not to change its floodway regulations, council placed a temporary ban on building in the floodway. Floodway permit applications placed after January 6 were put on hold. Some council members questioned this moratorium on construction, particularly changing the rules in mid-stream without giving the public proper notice. Public Works Director Dave Rhodes recommended council change Boulder's floodway definition to match that of the Urban Drainage and Flood Control District's definition before banning construction. Boulder's floodway definition was based on projected water level, velocity and depth during a major flood, while the district's definition was only based on water level.<sup>14</sup>

On March 17, 1987, council unanimously passed, by an emergency vote, an ordinance that suspended construction in the floodway for up to a year. This basically was an extension to the ban enacted by council earlier in January 1987. The temporary ban only

---

<sup>13</sup>Ibid., 24 August 1986.

<sup>14</sup>Ibid., 18 January 1987.

applied to buildings intended for human occupancy, not to improvements like utility lines, bike paths, sidewalks and bridges. Improvements valued up to 50 percent of the existing building were also permitted. The ban allowed the Department of Public Works time to prepare a new definition of "high hazard" areas and to fully study the impact of a permanent construction ban. City utility staff were directed by council to evaluate the correlation between Boulder's floodway boundaries and high-danger flood zones. After several months of study, city staff could find no correlation. Therefore, in October 1987, staff recommended redefining the boundaries of the Boulder Creek floodway based on a study by David J. Love & Associates. That study presented a more accurate profile of the most dangerous areas during a flood. City staff asked council to adopt a high-hazard flood zone based on a "product number" of four to define Boulder's floodway. The use of a product number went beyond using the rise in water level to define a floodway. The flood waters velocity and depth were used also. By redefining Boulder's floodway using the product number of four, Boulder's current floodway boundaries would extend beyond that defined by the Urban Drainage and Flood Control District. The change in definition would alter Boulder's restrictions on commercial and residential building along Boulder Creek and its tributaries because the floodway boundaries would change. The Urban Drainage and Flood Control District defined Boulder's floodway as the area where a six-inch rise in water level would occur in the 100-year floodplain, approximately 189 acres. In comparison, the high-hazard flood zone covered approximately 213 acres in the city of Boulder. That meant the size of the designated floodway would increase by nearly 13 percent. According to City Attorney Joseph de Raismes, adopting the high-hazard flood zone could strengthen the construction codes in some areas along the creek and loosen them in other areas. Council initially approved redefining Boulder's floodway and mapping the high-hazard flood zone in October 1987.<sup>15</sup> This new method of defining the floodway using the product number of four meant

---

<sup>15</sup>Colorado Daily, 19 March 1987; Camera, 20 March 1987; Colorado Daily, 27 October 1987.

"...any area where the floodwater would be expected to flow 2-feet-deep and at a rate of 2 feet per second, or 4-feet deep and 1 foot per second would be considered dangerous to human safety."<sup>16</sup> Boulder's new flood plan emphasized human safety and preservation of life, not property.

In December 1988, when the public learned of the city's flood plan and the impact it would have in regards to new construction in the floodway, the plan came under fire over the next several months. Many property owners living along Boulder Creek or its tributaries were opposed to the floodway building restrictions and feared a loss in property value. As indicated earlier, the new floodway regulations not only banned new construction, but also prevented property owners, both residential and commercial, from expanding or rebuilding damaged property if their buildings were damaged beyond 50 percent of their market value. Under the old floodway regulations, residential structures were banned in the floodway and had been ever since 1975, but commercial or industrial structures could still be built as long as the buildings were flood proofed or elevated two-feet above the projected flood height. Also under the old floodway regulations, residential buildings already in existence in the floodway could not be expanded or replaced if more than 50 percent of the property was damaged. The only change or difference in the new proposed floodway regulations compared to the old was that now commercial construction would be banned in the floodway, particularly in the high-hazard flood zone. The public outcry over the new floodway regulations, which had been in effect since January 1987, stemmed from the fact that many residential owners did not realize that their property was already in the existing floodway or that they were not allowed to expand their property or even rebuild if a flood destroyed their home.

---

<sup>16</sup>Boulder Courier, 29 October 1987.

How much land would be affected with the new floodway regulations? Computer models showed the high-hazard flood zone encompassing 778 acres along Boulder Creek and its 14 tributaries, which put 90 commercial and industrial buildings in the high hazard zone along with 11 public buildings and 822 residential units. City staff had wanted the new regulations in place before the moratorium on construction expired in March 1989, but council directed staff to slow down given the public reaction to the proposed floodway regulations. City officials were still assessing how many people would be affected by the new floodway regulations and how to lessen the impact the new regulations would impose. Property owners and developers were not the only ones to question the new floodway regulations being considered. Some Boulder Planning Board members felt the restrictions were too strict, that the city had overreacted in determining the threat or risk to human life in the high-hazard flood zone.<sup>17</sup>

Proponents of the flood plan and new regulations, like city planner Peter Pollock, perceived the changes in the floodway regulations as opening up land for development since less property would actually be classified as high-hazard. Granted, restrictions would be more severe in the high-hazard flood zone, but under the new proposal some properties currently banned by the temporary moratorium imposed in 1987 would be freed for use. In February 1989, council voted 8 to 1 in favor of continuing the moratorium on new construction in the floodway. Council extended the moratorium to allow the city more time to develop a floodplain ordinance, one that both city officials and Boulder residents could live with. To address the public's concerns, the Public Works staff held a workshop to answer questions and explain the new ordinance as well as to discuss mitigation strategies. Once residents realized that the new floodway regulations were not that much different from the floodway regulations that have been in place since 1975, staff felt the public's concerns would be alleviated. Public comments made at the workshop were forwarded to city

---

<sup>17</sup>Camera, 1, 13 January 1989.

council.<sup>18</sup>

In March 1989, council followed the advice of City Manager Jim Piper to proceed with the new floodway regulations banning all development in the high-hazard flood zone. The city wanted to have the new regulations in place by September 1989, when the moratorium would expire, even though ways of easing the impact the regulations would present probably would not be in place by then. Saving lives was the focus of these new floodway regulations. Commercial and industrial property owners would be the ones most impacted by the new floodway regulations. How to finance flood control measures such as drainageway changes, channels and purchasing flood-prone properties once they became available still had to be found. Some ways to finance flood control measures council considered included charging residents a monthly flood control fee, using parks and recreation funds, lottery funds or open space funds to purchase flood-prone property. In May 1989, council opted to increase Boulder residents' monthly utility charges. Boulder residents would pay an extra \$1.33 a month in utility fees starting July 1, 1989 to finance \$11.5 million in storm drainage and flood control improvements along Boulder Creek and its tributaries. Prior to the increase, residents paid \$1.67 in monthly flood control fees. Starting July 1, 1990, the rate would increase to \$4.03 a month. Flood control improvements along Goose Creek were to be given top priority with the new funding that would become available with the utility fee increases.<sup>19</sup>

According to the floodway coordinator, Alan Taylor, the flood control improvements the city planned to undertake would remove land from the high-hazard flood zone, thus minimizing the impact of the new floodway regulations the city was proposing. Also, over time the public's interest and concern over the new floodway regulations had waned

---

<sup>18</sup>Denver Post, 29 January 1989 and Colorado Daily, 23 February 1989.

<sup>19</sup>Camera, 4 March 1989 and 4, 17 May 1989.

considerably. As the public learned the new floodway regulations were not that much different than the existing regulations or that their property would not be affected by the new regulations, concern lessened so fewer people attended public hearings on the issue. With that in mind, the Boulder Planning Board approved the new floodway regulations that banned new construction in the high-hazard flood zone. However, the board asked that the restrictions limiting additions to buildings in the floodplain to 10 percent of their floor area be reconsidered since the size of a building did not necessarily correlate into an increase in occupancy. Having been approved by the planning board, the floodway regulations were forwarded to city council. On June 27, 1989, council voted 7 to 1 in favor of adopting the floodway regulations, which prohibited commercial development in the high-hazard flood zone. Councilwoman Cindy Carlisle cast the only dissenting vote. Carlisle believed the new regulations took a more anti-development posture than a life safety one. At that same meeting, council also voted to require people living in the high-hazard flood zone to buy flood insurance. The vote was 6 to 2 in favor of this requirement. Once again Carlisle cast a dissenting vote along with Councilman Steve Pomerance. According to the Assistant Public Works Director Bob Wheeler, the flood insurance requirement would not be actively enforced. Only when someone applied for a building permit would proof of flood insurance have to be presented. The city also intended to launch an education program promoting the importance of having flood insurance. According to Wheeler, there were 2,691 buildings in Boulder's floodplain and only 700 of those buildings were insured against flooding.<sup>20</sup>

Now that the new floodway regulations were adopted, the city focused on developing and implementing flood control measures to reduce the high-hazard flood zone and the properties within that zone. One property within the high-hazard flood zone was Boulder High School. Just prior to the city's adoption of the new floodway regulations, the Boulder Valley School District had passed a bond election for making improvements to the schools

---

<sup>20</sup>Ibid., 19 May 1989 and 28 June 1989.

in Boulder Valley, which included a new addition and an improved parking lot for Boulder High School. Wanting to work cooperatively with the city to reduce the flood hazard for Boulder High students, the city and the Boulder Valley School District began working out an agreement in January 1990. That agreement entailed the city acquiring and razing three other high-hazard flood zone properties west of Boulder High School and a fourth empty lot that would ultimately remove Boulder High School and other residential structures from the high-hazard flood zone. The city would then give the Boulder Valley School District that empty property along with \$344,320. In exchange, the city would acquire Ropper Fields, which was 35 acres along Jay Road east of 30th Street, from the Boulder Valley School District. Finalization of this land trade deal between the city and the Boulder Valley School District occurred in June 1990. This was just the first phase of a flood plan that would reduce the flood hazard downtown. Council had yet to decide whether or not to proceed with two more phases of this flood hazard reducing plan that would cost the city a total of \$5 million. If council decided to proceed with the two other phases, a total of 20 buildings along Boulder Creek would be removed.<sup>21</sup>

In September 1990, council approved the second phase of the Boulder Creek flood reducing plan. Total cost of the second phase was \$2.4 million. The second phase involved the city acquiring and removing nine buildings south of Arapahoe Avenue between Boulder Creek and Boulder High School. Those nine buildings situated in the high hazard flood zone contained 47 dwelling units. The second phase removed 14 acres containing properties in the downtown area from the high-hazard flood zone. Boulder High School was among the downtown properties removed from the high-hazard flood zone. Council's vote was 5 to 2 in favor of carrying out the second phase. Councilmen Bob Greenlee and Jim Topping opposed the plan. Greenlee opposed the plan because he felt Boulder High School should bear the

---

<sup>21</sup>Memorandum, Alan Taylor to City Manager Dave Knapp, 31 January 1990 and Camera, 20, 19 June 1990.

brunt of the project's cost since it was the high school that benefited from the project. Topping agreed with Greenlee, but Topping was also dismayed over removing 47 medium to low income dwelling units. By removing those residential units, the city was contradicting its policy to improve low-income housing. Council members were not the only ones to oppose the second phase of the flood plan. Residents, particularly those of Terrace View Apartments at 1645 14th Street, opposed the plan also. Terrace View Apartments was one of the nine buildings the city intended to raze as part of the second phase of the flood reducing plan. According to Alan Taylor of the city's Public Works Department, removing Terrace View Apartments was vital to the flood reducing project. Without its removal, the reduction of the high-hazard flood zone would not be realized. Terrace View residents wanted more time to find an alternative to demolishing their homes and had pleaded with council to delay its decision of approving the second phase, but to no avail. Residents argued that the building was strong enough to withstand a flood and that the city did not properly notify them about the flood reducing project. The city had contacted the building's owner in May, but residents were not contacted until July or August. The city assured the residents that they had two or three more years in the apartment complex before the city would tear it down. This would allow residents time to adjust and seek alternative housing. The city wanted to make the transition as smooth as possible. The residents' complaints over the notification process prompted the city to assess its notification process. Each city department was to report to council on how they notified the public of city projects.<sup>22</sup>

As for phase three of the flood reducing plan, council decided to cancel that phase. Council felt the \$1 million price tag of phase three was not worth the benefits of the project. Phase three would have involved the city acquiring and removing seven more structures north of Arapahoe Avenue between 13th and 14th Streets. Instead of phase three, council

---

<sup>22</sup>Camera, 5 September 1990; Colorado Daily, 6 September 1990; Camera, 27 September 1990.

opted to have city staff assess how to best resolve the acquisition and gradual relocation of Branding Iron Mobile Home Park located at 2685 28th Street. Branding Iron Mobile Home Park was situated in the high-hazard flood zone of Goose Creek. City staff were to report back to council in mid-November with a plan for the mobile home park. Work on clearing the flood channel planned for Goose Creek was not to start for at least seven years. In effort to ease the concerns of Branding Iron Mobile Home residents, the city entered into negotiations to try and purchase the mobile home park in November 1990. City staff assured the residents of Branding Iron that the city would help them find new homes when the time came. Staff also indicated that the city might help pay moving expenses or buy the aging mobile homes that would not hold up moving to another park. To finance the purchase of Branding Iron Mobile Home Park and the relocation of its residents as well as the acquisition and removal of the nine buildings along Boulder Creek for the second phase of the flood plan, the city approved the issuance of flood management bonds to raise \$3.7 million. Bids from bond purchasers were taken on December 4, 1990. Council was to then select the best interest rate.<sup>23</sup>

### **Fire Department**

To increase safety and the Boulder Fire Department's response time, a new remote-control device that triggers traffic signals to turn green as fire trucks approached the intersections were installed in August 1986. The new device installed was called Opticom. Opticom was installed on five stop and go signals along where Broadway intersects with Pine Street, Spruce Street, Walnut Street, Canyon Boulevard and Arapahoe Avenue. How did the Opticom system work? Fire trucks would have an emitter installed that would flash a strobe light and send a signal to receivers mounted on the traffic signals at those five major

---

<sup>23</sup>Colorado Daily, 6 September 1990; Camera, 20 November 1990; Colorado Daily, 23-25 November 1990; Camera, 3 December 1990.

intersections on Broadway. The traffic signals would then either extend the length of the green light or speed the transition from a red light to a green light allowing the approaching fire trucks to travel safely through an intersection. City council's goal to have firefighters respond to a fire call anywhere in the city within six minutes would be more attainable with this new device, especially in west Boulder which was outside that six minute response goal area. Any aid that would quicken the Boulder Fire Department's response time was vital considering the fact that the Boulder Fire Department employed 0.94 fire department employees per 1,000 population. In comparison, the national average of fire department employee per 1,000 population was 1.64 according to the 1986 Municipal Year Book. In fact, the Boulder Fire Department had fewer firefighters than most U.S. cities of similar size.<sup>24</sup> Given that fact, the Boulder Fire Department held the philosophy that adding more firefighting equipment, personnel and fire stations was more expensive than controlling fires before firefighters arrived on the scene. Therefore, it should come to no surprise that the Boulder Fire Department focused on preventing and controlling fires before help arrived.

In April 1987, Boulder Fire Department officials pushed for implementing a mandatory sprinkler systems ordinance on all new buildings built except for single-family houses and duplexes in Boulder. In addition, the fire department wanted sprinklers installed in some existing buildings, specifically motels and high-rises. Not surprisingly, this last aspect of installing sprinklers in existing buildings drew fierce criticism. The main reason for the criticism was cost. Approximately seven high-rise structures would be affected by this proposed mandatory sprinkler systems ordinance. Many of those high-rise residents were elderly and therefore could not afford to install a sprinkler system. Given the criticism of the fire department's proposal, the Boulder Planning Board recommended that a task force be formed to review the proposal requiring the installation of sprinklers. The task force was comprised of various city department representatives, planners, architects, landscape

---

<sup>24</sup>Camera, 28 August 1986.

architects and buildings owners. After reviewing the proposal and addressing the public's concerns, the task force was to report back to the planning board with a recommendation.

Almost one year later, the task force reported back to the planning board. Based on the recommendations of the task force, the planning board approved a modified version of the sprinkler systems fire code ordinance in February 1988. This modified version of the sprinkler systems ordinance differed from what the fire department had first proposed. This new fire code ordinance required all buildings over 55 feet high to install a "modified" sprinkler system within five years. Under this modified sprinkler system, sprinklers were to be installed in all common areas rather than in all units as first proposed. Just one sprinkler had to be installed per apartment on the ceiling just inside the door. Fire Marshall Cliff Harvey acknowledged that this probably wouldn't save the life of the person trapped inside, but it would prevent the fire from spreading to the hall. The overall cost of installing a modified sprinkler system was considerably less than the standard sprinkler system installation first proposed, as much as 50 percent less. All new construction projects were still required to install a sprinkler system. However, the fire code ordinance made several exceptions to this last compliance based on the size of the building being constructed and also the building's access to fire protection equipment. Council approved this modified fire code ordinance in May 1988 despite objections from seniors and business owners, who would be the most affected by the ordinance. The complaints and objections did not go unacknowledged. Council set in motion plans for the city to help pay for the installation of sprinklers in high-rise buildings. Council appointed a committee to figure out how the city could help pay for the installation of sprinklers. That committee was also to address other questions raised by citizens like what was the actual cost of a modified sprinkler system? and how would the installation disrupt the lives of the elderly? and what asbestos problems the installation of sprinklers may cause?<sup>25</sup>

---

<sup>25</sup>Ibid., 3 April 1987 and 5 February 1988 and 4 May 1988.

In March 1989, council announced that the city would help pay for the installation of sprinklers in four of the seven high-rise buildings affected by the new fire code ordinance. Those four buildings were as follows: Golden West Manor at 1955 Adams Circle; Good Samaritan retirement home at 2525 Taft Drive; Presbyterian Manor retirement home at 1050 Arapahoe Avenue; and Walnut Place, the city-owned elderly housing complex located at 1940 Walnut Street. Residents of the other three high-rise buildings would have to finance the sprinkler installation cost themselves. Those other three buildings were San Marco North condominiums at 850 N. 20th Street, Bramford Apartments at 500 Mohawk Drive and Horizon West condominiums at 1850 Folsom Street. Council directed city staff to develop funding options for installing sprinklers in 4 of the 7 high-rise buildings. Council also indicated that \$120,000 in federal Community Development Block Grant funds and another \$100,000 in general funds would be utilized to help cover the sprinkler installation costs.

High-rise buildings were not the only properties affected by the changes to Boulder's fire code regulations. Existing commercial buildings, particularly those located on University Hill were also affected. Many of the businesses on the Hill were in violation of the fire code and had been for decades. The most prevalent violations included having no sprinkler systems and blocking fire trucks and equipment access in alleys either with electrical lines, parked cars or trash containers. According to the fire code regulations, a building's secondary or rear access required the width of alleys to be at least 20 feet wide, with no overhead power lines lower than 55 feet so fire trucks could have easy access. Businesses unable to meet this rear access provision of the fire code were required to install sprinklers. The Boulder Fire Department conveyed its concerns and the Hill's fire hazards to council. As a result, the city established deadlines for business owners to comply with Boulder's fire code regulations in March 1989. University Hill businesses had two years to fix their fire code violations. Complaints of the city's fire code particularly the provision that

---

required the installation of sprinkler systems in high-rises and existing commercial buildings that restricted fire trucks access, persisted. As a result, in June 1989, city council recommended limiting fire code enforcement activities over the next several months. Council also extended for one year the deadline for complying with the city's new fire sprinkler ordinance as long as there was no imminent threat to life or property. This moderate approach to fire code enforcement did not apply to new buildings being constructed.<sup>26</sup>

Despite the city's efforts to moderate or limit the enforcement of the fire code, criticism of Boulder's fire code regulations continued. Therefore, the city held a public hearing on August 29, 1989, so citizens could voice their opinion on the fire code. Complaints included the rear access requirements of the fire code and the enforcement techniques of some fire department personnel. Business owners compelled to install sprinklers because they failed to meet the rear access requirement criticized that provision of the fire code at the public hearing. They claimed that the financial burden of installing a sprinkler system was too much and that a retroactive requirement was unreasonable. Some business owners also complained that the fire department used aggressive and heavy-handed tactics when enforcing the fire code regulations. All the comments made at the public hearing were forwarded to city council. Council intended to address the controversial fire code and the comments made at the hearing at its next study session scheduled for September 12, 1989.

Following the public hearing, some business owners accused fire inspectors of targeting owners who had criticized the fire code regulations at the meeting. However, upon review of the inspection records, Fire Chief Chuck Boyes found no indication that the

---

<sup>26</sup>Camera, 9 March 1989 and 4 April 1989 and Memorandum, City Manager to the Mayor and City Council, 29 June 1989.

properties of people who had spoken out at the public hearing were inspected in the last week. Some business owners also alleged the inspectors were using "Gestapo tactics." In light of the complaints, Acting City Manager Dave Knapp ordered the Boulder Fire Department to stop fire code violation inspections. The city took the accusations seriously. Council directed the city manager to investigate the allegations. All inspections were suspended until council could review the controversial fire code. At council's September 12th study session, council voiced support for easing the compliance standards regarding sprinkler systems requirements. City staff were directed to revise the fire code regulations. As for the hard-line approach supposedly used by fire inspectors enforcing the fire code, council directed fire department personnel to develop a more customer-service attitude. Fire department personnel were to attend training programs that encouraged a more service-oriented approach to enforcing the fire code regulations.<sup>27</sup>

On September 19, 1989, council decided that fire safety inspections should resume immediately in spite of the allegations that fire department personnel were using Gestapo tactics. Council and the mayor realized that suspending fire safety inspections was endangering public safety. Besides, the fire department personnel had been directed to be more customer-service oriented when enforcing the fire code. As for the controversial provisions of the fire code, those were not to be enforced until council decided the matter. Council continued to study the fire code. Investigation of fire department personnel accused of using heavy-handed tactics to enforce the fire code also continued. In September 1989, Assistant Fire Chief Clifford Harvey was reassigned to a new job within the Boulder Fire Department pending an investigation into charges of misconduct. Property owners alleged that Harvey's enforcement of the fire codes was heavy-handed and selective. In February 1990, the Boulder Police Department investigation of Assistant Fire Chief Harvey cleared Harvey of all citizens' allegations of any wrongdoing in enforcing the fire code. Not

---

<sup>27</sup>Colorado Daily, 29 August 1989; Camera, 8 September 1989; Colorado Daily, 13 September 1989.

surprisingly, some of the citizens who had made the allegations questioned the accuracy and thoroughness of the police report that cleared Harvey. Many of those residents called the police report a whitewash. Having been cleared, Harvey was reinstated to his former position within the Boulder Fire Department.<sup>28</sup>

The battle over Boulder's fire code involved not only public safety and money, but also politics. The Boulder Chamber of Commerce involved itself in the fire code controversy. The chamber sponsored a committee that recommended council review the fire code point by point. That committee believed the new fire code sprinkler requirement was excessive and that the code was more concerned about the protection of property than human lives. Feeling the pressure from all sides, city staff recommended to council lifting the secondary access requirements on existing buildings in October 1989. Those existing buildings not meeting the secondary access requirement would be exempt from having to install a sprinkler system. Fire chief Chuck Boyes supported the recommendation of city staff even though the narrow alleys would delay the fire department's response. Alley ways and power lines were beyond the control of property owners and therefore owners should not have to install sprinklers for something they could not control. Council approved the recommendation of city staff on October 17, 1989. Council voted 9 to 0 in favor of applying the secondary access requirement to only new buildings or to existing buildings if owners did large-scale remodeling. Council also reassured business owners and the Chamber of Commerce that city staff would continue to review the fire code.
