Other issues council planned to examine included the creation of a citizens' board to hear appeals of Boulder Fire Department orders and deciding which buildings need 24-hour monitoring of fire alarms.<sup>29</sup>

---

<sup>28</sup>Camera, 21 September 1989 and 16 February 1990.

<sup>29</sup>Ibid., 1, 11, 18 October 1989.

Within a few months of revising the fire code on December 11, 1989, a fire occurred at the Stratford Park West Condominium building located at 3030 O'Neal Parkway. Two lives, a mother and daughter, perished in the fire. Had sprinklers been installed in the 24-unit building, Edith Mueller and her 12 year-old daughter Natasha Pettus might not of perished in the fire. Another tenant of the condo, George Ince, started the fire by using Coleman fuel to start a fire in his fireplace. Ince was later tried in the Boulder County District Court and convicted of two counts of criminally negligent homicide in September 1990. This fire caused council to reconsider or re-evaluate Boulder's fire code, specifically whether or not to mandate the installation of sprinkler systems in residential complexes despite the high cost to property owners.

Despite the tragic outcome of the fire at the Stratford Park West Condominium, that may of been prevented if the building had been equipped with a sprinkler system, many residents' associations continued to object to the mandatory installation of sprinklers in buildings higher than 55 feet. Residents of high-rise buildings continued to pressure council in March and April 1990. Those residents appealed to council for a study session on the city ordinance that requires sprinklers to be installed in buildings over 55 feet, seven buildings in all. Residents and owners argued that installing sprinklers would disturb asbestos and be too costly an undertaking. Some residents also claimed that installing sprinklers was unnecessary in some of the buildings, particularly the San Marco North and the Horizon West buildings, which had concrete and steel construction. The appeal to council was over a recent report supporting the installation of sprinklers by city staff. Residents claimed that report was inaccurate and biased. Council directed city staff to look again at whether the city should modify or rescind that provision of the fire code despite urgings from Fire Chief Boyes not to back down on the fire code provision that requires high-rise buildings to install

sprinklers.<sup>30</sup>

Some surprising information was revealed during the trial of George Ince regarding Boulder's fire safety regulations, particularly the Life Safety Code. The trial not only revealed that the Stratford Park West Condominium did not meet the Life Safety Code, but also that as many as 300 other buildings in Boulder did not meet Boulder's fire safety regulations. The problem dates back to the 1970s. During the 1970s, the city manager decided that apartments and multi-family residences should be inspected by only the city's rental housing division and not by the Boulder Fire Department. The Boulder Fire Department would inspect hotels and high-rise apartment buildings, but not other apartment complexes. Inspections of rental units by the rental housing division occur once every four years and do not include inspections for violations of the Life Safety Code or the inspection of condominiums.<sup>31</sup>

Ironically, the need to improve the enforcement of fire safety regulations and how inspections were conducted came at a time when the city was proposing to cut two fire inspection positions and one training officer of the Boulder Fire Department as part of the 1991 city budget. In exchange, the city proposed increasing the fire suppression staff. Not surprisingly, Boulder firefighters were not happy with the city manager's 1991 budget recommendation to eliminate two fire prevention positions and one training officer. Boulder firefighters argued that it was the prevention and training positions that allowed the city to cover the increased calls without increased staffing. Cutting those prevention and training positions would affect public safety.

In Boulder, fire calls had increased from 2,966 in 1977 to 5,004 in 1989, but the

---

<sup>30</sup>Colorado Daily, 18 December 1989 and Camera, 12 December 1989 and 3, 4 April 1990 and 28 March 1990.

<sup>31</sup>Camera, 29 September 1990.

number of firefighters had remained constant. The National Fire Protection Handbook recommends 4 pumper trucks, 2 ladder trucks, 24 firefighters and 2 officers respond to high-hazard occupancy buildings such as hotels. According to the firefighters local 900 union officials, understaffing made meeting those recommended standards impossible. The city should be adding positions, not cutting them. Compared to other cities of similar size, the Boulder Fire Department was undersized by one-third. The Boulder Fire Department was not only understaffed, but also under equipped according to the National Fire Prevention Association's recommended standards. The National Fire Prevention Association recommends a minimum of four firefighters per truck. The association also did not recognize mini-engine trucks as fire engines. Boulder had a total of 7 fire engines, 3 of which were minis each with only a two-person crew. The other four fire engines each had a three-person crew. In addition, the fire trucks in use were old. Only one fire engine was a 1990 model, the others were built in 1976 and 1977. Boulder firefighters recommended the following to the city: replacing the three minis with regular fire trucks at a cost of approximately \$300,000 each, boosting the three two-person crews to three-person crews at a cost of approximately \$450,000 per year, and building and staffing a new east Boulder fire station to improve response time to that part of town. In response, City Manager Joe de Raismes claimed the 1991 budget proposal was taking steps toward those recommendations by adding 3.6 new firefighters in 1991. The city's budget cycles would address adding another firefighter to each of the three two-person crews. However, those new firefighter positions would be paid with the money saved by eliminating the two fire prevention positions and one training officer. The city manager also pointed out that his budget recommendations actually increased the Boulder Fire Department's budget overall by \$78,000. In the end, council decided not to cut the fire department's budget for training and prevention as the city manager had proposed. Council awarded the Boulder Fire Department \$20,000 for training and overtime and also \$55,000 for continuing one full-time fire prevention officer position. Despite council's efforts, the firefighters were not pleased with

the fire department's budget. Firefighters wanted additional funding for the already understaffed Boulder Fire Department. Council approved the \$92 million city budget in October 1990.<sup>32</sup>

Over the course of discussing the 1991 budget in August and September 1990, city council learned that the Boulder Fire Department was conducting very few fire code inspections. The department was inspecting only new buildings and by complaint according to Fire Chief Chuck Boyes. Council was not pleased with this news. The inspections were to have resumed last fall, but the contested portions of the fire code were not to be enforced until the city resolved the issue. The city was still negotiating with the Chamber of Commerce committee over changes to the fire code. In October 1990, the city manager informed council that firefighters would resume conducting inspections of commercial buildings and that building inspectors of the rental housing division had been reminded to check for fire violations also during their inspections of rental units.<sup>33</sup>

In addition to the changes taking place with Boulder's fire code, changes within the Boulder Fire Department were taking place as well. In February 1985, the city of Boulder asked the Colorado Supreme Court to determine if a Boulder firefighter may claim his pension if he retires early. At issue was a state law that affected both firefighters and police officers hired before 1978. According to the state law, employees must work 20 years and be at least 50 years old before one could retire and receive pension payments. The city wanted the state Supreme Court to overturn a 1983 District Court ruling that favored the early retirement of Boulder firefighter William Traxler and awarding Traxler his pension payments once he turned 50 years old. Traxler who had worked 20 years by the time he was

---

<sup>32</sup>Camera, 22 August 1990 and 11 September 1990 and 10 October 1990 and Colorado Daily, 11 October 1990.

<sup>33</sup>Camera, 12 September 1990 and 3 October 1990.

43 years old wanted to retire early, but the law prevented that. Nevertheless, in 1980 Traxler told the Boulder pension board that he wished to retire early and receive his pension payments once he turned 50. The six-member pension board split on their decision whether or not to allow Traxler to retire early and receive his pension. In 1983, Traxler took his case to the District Court and won. The District Court Judge Michael Enwall ruled that the state law was ambiguous and should be interpreted in favor of Traxler. It was that ruling the city was trying to have the state Supreme Court overturn in 1985. In July 1985, the Colorado Supreme Court refused a hearing and offered no explanation as to why. Traxler was allowed to retire in 1985 at the age of 48. To avoid future deadlock decisions by the pension board, the rules governing how the pension board operates were changed.<sup>34</sup>

In September 1989, the city of Boulder and the Boulder Fire Department reached a tentative contract agreement on a new one-year contract for 1990. The agreement stipulated that fire department personnel would receive a 2.2 percent pay increase for the first 6 months of 1990, after July 1, 1990, a second pay increase of 2.6 percent would be awarded. The city also agreed to pay 100 percent of firefighters' health insurance costs. Firefighter union members were to vote on ratifying the contract next month.<sup>35</sup>

In June 1990, Fire Chief Chuck Boyes announced that he intended to retire on January 25, 1991. Boyes had worked for the Boulder Fire Department for 22 years. Boyes was appointed fire chief after only working with the Boulder Fire Department for 10 years. Prior to going to work for the fire department in 1968, Boyes had been a funeral director for Geddes-Hibbard Mortuary. The city began searching for a new fire chief to replace Boyes in the fall of 1990. Upon request from the city, the 15 finalists competing for the job of Boulder's new fire chief submitted a videotape of their replies to five questions about

---

<sup>34</sup>Ibid., 15 February 1985 and 20 July 1985.

<sup>35</sup>Ibid., 20 September 1989.

themselves and the job of fire chief. Boulder staff were to watch the videotapes and pick 6 to 8 finalists for interviews scheduled to be held December 10, 1990. The city hoped to have the new fire chief in place by mid-January 1991. The city selected Larry D. Donner to replace Fire Chief Chuck Boyes. Donner was a Colorado native and former Fort Collins firefighter. At the time Donner was hired to replace Boyes, he was serving as the fire chief in Iowa City, Iowa. Donner held that position of fire chief in Iowa since 1986. Donner was expected to start work in Boulder on January 28, 1991.<sup>36</sup>

With talk of the city annexing land in north Boulder, concern for providing a higher level of fire protection and suppression service arose in 1989 and 1990. The city discussed plans to expand the existing Fire Station Five, located at 2200 Norwood Avenue, in order to provide that higher level of service. At a public meeting held in May 1989, north Boulder residents strongly opposed the expansion of Fire Station Five. As a result, council directed city staff to examine potential sites for relocating Fire Station Five. When selecting a site, council insisted that the new location site would not cause an interruption in the six-minute response time of firefighters. After some debate, the city selected the corner of 19th Street and Violet Avenue for the new location of Fire Station Five. Construction of the new Fire Station Five got underway in August 1992. The station was scheduled to be operational by December 1992.<sup>37</sup>

### **Police Department**

Numerous complaints about motorists speeding prompted city council to earmark approximately \$130,000 for an additional police car and three police officers in July 1985 so that the Boulder Police Department could crackdown on speeding motorists. The Boulder

---

<sup>36</sup>Camera, 2 June 1990 and 17 November 1990 and Times-Call, 6 January 1991.

<sup>37</sup>Memorandum, Fire Chief Chuck Boyes to City Manager Joseph de Raismes, 3 May 1990, in Boulder Fire Department file and Camera, 27 August 1992.

Police Department created a three-member Neighborhood Traffic Enforcement Unit to target speeding motorists in residential areas. Using Radar to catch speeders, the Neighborhood Traffic Enforcement Unit monitored various residential areas for speeding motorists based on the complaints of residents and observations of police officers. When school started in the fall, additional police officers were to assist in monitoring school speed zones. In just the first three days in operation, the Neighborhood Traffic Enforcement Unit issued 132 tickets. The majority of the tickets issued those three days were for speeding. Stop sign violations made up nearly the rest of the tickets issued. In the fall, monitoring for speeders in the 20 mph school speed zones was also successful. In 1985, sixteen tickets were issued the first day school started. The following fall, the Neighborhood Traffic Enforcement Unit issued more than 70 speeding tickets in school zones the first two days school was back in session.<sup>38</sup>

In January 1986, with the support of CU administrators, the Boulder Police Department announced its intention to crackdown on CU fraternity and sorority parties that attempt to sell alcohol without the proper permit. The crackdown came as a result of a recent alcohol related death of a CU student, Sherri Ann Clark. Clark had fallen off a bridge after attending a sorority party in Eldorado Springs. The city sent a representative to each sorority and fraternity house in Boulder to inform them of the state liquor code. The state liquor code required sponsors of a party where alcohol was sold or served as part of an admission price to obtain a temporary liquor license from the city clerk's office.<sup>39</sup>

That same year in February, Boulder County authorities announced its new domestic abuse policy, one that lent aid to the victims and punished the abusers. The new policy would allow police officers to arrest and prosecute domestic batterers when officers found

---

<sup>38</sup>Camera, 25, 27 July 1985 and 27 August 1985 and 28 August 1986.

<sup>39</sup>Colorado Daily, 22 January 1986.

probable cause even when the victims refused to press charges. According to the new policy, abusers were to receive psychological counseling along with jail time following the mandatory arrest of suspected abusers. Other counties in Colorado followed Boulder County's lead and established similar domestic abuse policies over the next few years. In 1989, a study conducted on the Boulder Police Department enforcement of the domestic abuse policy indicated that the police officers were enforcing the policy. In addition, the study indicated that at least one-third of the police officers interviewed were enthusiastic about the domestic abuse policy. The study was conducted to dispel several concerns held by both the victims of domestic abuse and critics of the policy. Those concerns dispelled included the notion that the police were not sympathetic to victims and that the police were resentful of a policy which took away the police officers freedom of discretion or that the police preferred punitive action rather than psychotherapy for perpetrators. The author of the study was Roger Wade, who was a sociology professor at Metropolitan State College.<sup>40</sup>

In August 1986, Police Chief Jay Propst announced that the Boulder Police Department was going to change how the department handled routine cases. Routine non-emergency cases would be dealt with over the telephone instead of sending officers to the scene, unless the caller insisted an officer be sent. Non-emergency cases that could be handled over the telephone included obscene telephone calls and auto thefts. By handling routine, non-emergency cases over the telephone, the police department could devote more time to solving major crimes and be less hindered by the proposed city wide budget cuts for 1987.<sup>41</sup>

In January 1987, a new crime unit charged with fighting crime trends, like the recent

---

<sup>40</sup>Camera, 4 February 1986 (Domestic Abuse file) and Colorado Daily, 15 December 1989.

<sup>41</sup>Camera, 27 August 1986.

increase in burglaries in Boulder, was created. The new crime unit was called the Tactical Patrol Team. The Tactical Patrol Team consisted of four police officers and one police sergeant from the patrol division. The new unit was put to the test early. A recent series of armed robberies of local businesses located along 28th Street was first on the Tactical Patrol Team's agenda. Crime analysis and strategic placement aided the new unit in apprehending a robber trying to rob the Kentucky Fried Chicken at 3235 28th Street.<sup>42</sup>

In addition to solving and stopping crimes, the Boulder Police Department was concerned with public safety. That concern prompted the police department to institute a pedestrian safety program in April 1987. Police officers intended to enforce pedestrian violations by issuing tickets to violators in order to curb auto-pedestrian accidents. The number of accidents involving motorists and pedestrians was increasing. Between January 1, 1987 and March 16, 1987, there were fifteen reported pedestrian-motorists accidents compared to nine accidents for that same period last year. Sergeant Tom Kilpatrick, head of the police department's traffic section, wanted to decrease the number of accidents. To do that, the police would have to enforce the traffic laws. The pedestrian safety program implemented would keep pedestrians law abiding and safe by reminding them to observe the traffic laws. Bicyclists and joggers were included in the program. They too would be reminded to observe the traffic laws and would be ticketed for violations. Not surprisingly, pedestrians felt targeted and believed the police should be cracking down on the motorists violating the law rather than pedestrians. The Boulder Police Department was bombarded with telephone calls complaining about the new pedestrian safety program. Because of the negative public reaction to the new program, Kilpatrick decided to have his officers concentrate on issuing warnings to pedestrians rather than ticketing them. However, tickets would be issued when warranted. The most common pedestrian violations included crossing the street against the light, jaywalking, and jogging or walking in the street when there was a

---

<sup>42</sup>Ibid., 22 February 1987.

sidewalk available.<sup>43</sup>

A first in Boulder Police Department history occurred in 1987. Molly Bernard became the first female police officer to be promoted to sergeant, making her the highest ranked woman officer on the police force. Bernard's firsts did not stop there. In 1989, Bernard became the first woman to join Boulder's SWAT Team. Another first in Boulder Police Department history occurred in 1988. Police officer Bob Thomas Sr. was awarded the Medal of Valor for apprehending a suspect attempting to rob the Buster-Yerby Uhaul Company in Denver. Thomas was off-duty when he apprehended the suspect back in 1979.<sup>44</sup> The following year, two other police officers were awarded the Medal of Valor in May 1989 for rescuing an elderly couple from their burning home on December 1, 1988. The two police officers awarded were Donald R. Knight and John Ross. The two police officers were honored with this award at the Boulder Police Department's annual awards ceremony. Only one other Boulder Police officer had received the Medal of Valor before Knight and Ross.<sup>45</sup>

A new Boulder Police Department squad of non-commissioned employees was formed in March 1988. The new squad was charged with such duties as investigating traffic accidents, serving municipal subpoenas, providing traffic control and taking telephone calls on abandoned cars. This non-commissioned squad allowed commissioned police officers to devote more time on the criminal case load of the police department. The new squad was funded in part by a \$115,000 grant from the State Division of Highway Safety. The grant not only paid the annual salaries of three squad members, but also for a training seminar on accident investigation and for one of the two fully equipped accident investigation vans used

---

<sup>43</sup>Ibid., 28 April 1987 and 17 May 1987.

<sup>44</sup>Ibid., 1 December 1987 and 6 March 1989 and 3 May 1988.

<sup>45</sup>Ibid., 5 May 1989.

by the squad team.<sup>46</sup> One criminal case the police could now devote more time to was stopping the drug deals taking place on the Pearl Street Mall. In the summer of 1988, the Tactical Patrol Team went undercover to put a stop to the drug dealings. The police were alerted to the drug dealing problem by mall merchants. The Tactical Patrol Team made 18 arrests for suspected drug dealing. The police department kept officers assigned to the downtown mall area to discourage and possibly catch other drug dealers. Efforts to rid the mall of drug dealings continued the following summer. Once again police officers went undercover on the mall. The undercover operation, code named OMAR—On the Mall Anticipating Reefer, resulted in the arrest of nine drug dealers in July 1989. Four of the nine arrested were Boulder residents. During the undercover operation, the police learned that people were using the pay phones on the mall to arrange drug deals. To combat that, the police intended to change the pay phones so that only outgoing calls could be made. By not allowing incoming calls on the pay phones, the police hoped to deter drug dealings. The Boulder Police Department planned on repeating operation OMAR later in the year to further discourage drug dealings on the mall.<sup>47</sup>

According to a factfinder ruling in September 1988, Boulder police received a 2 percent pay hike in 1989 instead of the 4.5 percent raise desired by the police force. Besides the raise, police salaries also increased because of lower pension contributions from each employee's salary. Police officers hired before 1978 received a 2.8 percent increase and those hired after 1978 received a 1.8 percent increase as a result of the decreased pension contributions.<sup>48</sup> In January 1989, the Boulder Police Department was granted a permit to build an explosives storage and disposal bunker on a 20-acre parcel located at the north end

---

<sup>46</sup>Ibid., 21 March 1988.

<sup>47</sup>Ibid., 16 July 1988 and 18 July 1989.

<sup>48</sup>Ibid., 20 September 1988.

of 26th Street, just south of the Boulder Rifle Club. The Boulder County Commissioners unanimously approved the permit. The police department had been without a place to dispose of explosives for the past year. At this new bunker site, explosives confiscated would either be burned in a pit six feet underground or detonated within a police bomb trailer. No hazardous materials or permanent storage of explosives would be allowed at the bunker site. Traffic on the north end of 26th Street would be stopped whenever the bomb disposal site was in use and warning signs would be posted.<sup>49</sup>

That same year, a rash of car thefts and break-ins were reported. Between January 1 and March 24, 46 automobiles were reported stolen. The number of car thefts had increased more than 70 percent in 1989 compared to the same period last year. Reported break-ins were up 46 percent from last year. The police suspected car theft rings were in operation in the Boulder area. To deter or possibly catch the car thieves, the Boulder Police Department increased patrols of parking lots and apartment complexes, where most break-ins and thefts occurred.<sup>50</sup> Preventing car thefts and break-ins was not the only concern of the Boulder Police Department. Police officers were concerned about bicycle safety. As a way to promote cycling courtesy and safety, police officers started patrolling the Boulder Creek Path and other bikeways in town. The bike paths were patrolled seven days a week. The popularity of the new bike path along Boulder Creek prompted the patrolling by police. The bicycle patrol would also enforce a 15 mph speed limit on the Boulder Creek Path. Police officers doing bicycle patrol carried bike path maps and information on bicycle and pedestrian safety to distribute to bicyclists and pedestrians using the paths. In addition to patrolling the bike paths, the police also patrolled the University Hill area due to the heavy

---

<sup>49</sup>Ibid., 25 January 1989 (Boulder County Commissioners file).

<sup>50</sup>Ibid., 3 April 1989.

use of bicycles by students.<sup>51</sup>

That same summer of 1989, Boulder police increased foot patrols of the downtown mall. City council voted 9 to 0 in favor of paying police officers overtime in order to increase police patrols on the mall. In addition, council allocated \$7,500 to hire private security guards to assist police officers in patrolling the Pearl Street Mall through the fall. With the increased budget, police officers were able to maintain a presence on the mall until 11pm every night. Council's actions were in response to merchants' complaints of aggressive panhandlers, drunks and transients on the mall, which impeded businesses from operating and endangered shoppers and tourists. An article in the Louisville Times-Call dated August 17, 1989, reported the city's crackdown on transients resulted in many of the drifters to move west to the town of Nederland.<sup>52</sup> The police department wished to increase police patrols in other areas of town as well. In August 1989, police patrol division chief Hal Nees, proposed council allocate another \$8,000 to increase police presence on University Hill. Nees proposed paying two off-duty officers overtime to patrol that area from 7pm till 3am to act as a deterrent and to guard against typical offenses like disorderly conduct, drinking and noise disturbance. If effective, the police intended to put a request for continued funding for the foot patrol of University Hill in 1991. Council held off approving that allocation until the fall when council decided the 1990 budget.<sup>53</sup>

Overwhelmed by citizen complaints, the police department stepped up enforcement of bike violations on the Hill during the fall of 1989. As many as 87 accidents involving bicyclists and motorists had already occurred in 1989 prior to the police officers vigorous

---

<sup>51</sup>Ibid., 2 July 1989.

<sup>52</sup>Camera, 2 August 1989 and Colorado Daily, 23-26 June 1989 (Shopping Malls file) and Louisville Times-Call, 17 August 1989 (Homelessness file).

<sup>53</sup>Colorado Daily, 24 August 1989.

enforcement of bicycle violations. According to police sergeant Mike Root, students were to be warned first before the actual police crackdown on violations started. Warnings were issued until September 21, 1989. After that date, the police issued tickets for violations. Fines for violations by bicyclists ranged from \$25 to \$100. Under Colorado Law, bicyclists were subject to the same rules of the road as motorists. The most prevalent violations committed by bicyclists included riding on the sidewalk in congested areas, riding the wrong way on one-way streets and running both stop signs and stop lights. Locking bicycles to trees was also a city violation. Bicycles found locked to trees after September 21 were impounded. Pedestrians and motorists on the Hill were also to be monitored, warned and ticketed for violations along with the bicyclists.<sup>54</sup>

In May 1990, the Boulder Police Department opened a substation on 14th Street just north of Walnut Street in the Continental Building. Police officers patrolling Pearl Street Mall and the downtown area needed the police substation or annex now that the main police headquarters were located in a newly remodeled building on 33rd Street. The offices of the police substation were to be staffed only when police officers were doing paperwork. The office space was obtained at a reduced rate of \$350 per month for six months thanks to the Downtown Management Commission and Downtown Boulder Inc. Both agencies wanted a stronger police presence on the mall. Downtown Boulder Inc. loaned the police department the furniture for the offices at the police substation. At the end of six months, the police department planned to evaluate and decide if the annex was worthwhile to maintain. In addition to increasing police presence on the mall, the police made their presence known on the Hill. Once again the police department geared up for the return of CU students. The Tactical Patrol Team was assigned to supplement the standard police patrol on the Hill during the first few weekends of the school year. In just the first weekend of operation, the Tactical Patrol Team made more than 150 arrests on the Hill. The majority of the arrests

---

<sup>54</sup>Camera, 13 September 1989 and Colorado Daily, 21 September 1989.

were alcohol related violations like selling alcoholic beverages without a permit and selling alcohol to minors.<sup>55</sup>

Boulder police detective John Luebke was named Crime Stopper of the Year at the annual Boulder County Crime Stoppers luncheon in October 1990. Luebke was honored with the title for tracking down a suspected burglar. At that same Crime Stoppers luncheon, Police Chief Jay Propst and Boulder County Sheriff Brad Leach were honored for their service. Propst had served 11 years as police chief and Leach had served 20 years as sheriff. Leach did not intend to run for re-election as sheriff in the fall. Rumors that Propst intended to retire as police chief in 1991 were also confirmed. At the Crime Stoppers lunch, Propst announced his plans to retire in March 1991. The city launched a national search to fill Propst's position. Applications were accepted through November 8, 1990. Candidates interested in applying for the position of police chief had to meet certain requirements. Each candidate was required to have a bachelor's degree plus a minimum of five years of progressive command and administrative experience, and to have a ranking of captain or higher. In addition, the candidate had to possess complete integrity as well as a high sense of personal and professional ethics. Only three Boulder police officers were qualified to apply – Glenn Kaminsky, Hal Nees and Joe Pura. As of October 21, 1990, only Nees, who headed up the patrol division, had applied for the position. Applicant finalists were also required to take a polygraph and psychological tests. Background checks on the applicants were to be conducted as well by Police Chief Propst before he retired. In April 1991, the city selected Thomas Koby to replace Propst as Boulder's new police chief. Before being hired as Boulder's new police chief, Koby was an assistant police chief in Houston, Texas.<sup>56</sup>

Propst's replacement would head up the Boulder Police Department in new police

---

<sup>55</sup>Camera, 11 May 1990 and Colorado Daily, 28 August 1990.

<sup>56</sup>Camera, 17, 21 October 1990 and 30 April 1991.

headquarters located at 1805 33rd Street in the former Mountain Bell building. The Boulder Police Department moved to its new headquarters in January 1990. Plans to acquire a new building to house the Boulder Police Department originated back in March 1987. At that time, the city initiated negotiations to acquire the vacant old Mountain Bell building to house the police department. The main reason for acquiring a new building was for safety purposes. The police department, located in the Justice Center on Canyon Boulevard and 6th Street, was situated in a floodplain. It was not prudent to have emergency services in a floodway and therefore the police department had to be moved out of the floodplain. Moving the police department out of the floodplain was not the only advantage of the new location. Other advantages to the 1805 33rd Street location included centralization, proximity to major thoroughfares, and closer proximity to the new Boulder County Jail under construction near the Boulder Municipal Airport. The closer proximity to the county jail made transporting criminals to the jail more convenient. In addition, the move consolidated police operations into one building. Prior to the move, the police department had been operating out of several buildings. The police department had offices in both the Randolph Building and Washington School in addition to the offices at the Justice Center. The possibility that the Boulder Police Department would be vacating space in the Justice Center peaked the interest of Boulder County. The county considered purchasing the police department's space in the Justice Center to house county agencies and courtrooms. This in turn would help the police department finance the purchase of the old Mountain Bell building. In December 1987, Boulder City Council approved a purchase-option contract for the Mountain Bell building. Council authorized a down payment of \$25,000. Total cost of purchasing and renovating the Mountain Bell building was estimated at \$5.7 million. Revenue from the recent voter approved sales tax increase of 0.38 percent helped to finance the purchase and renovation of the Mountain Bell building. Work to be done on the building before the police department could occupy it included remodeling the interior of the building, and resurfacing both the building's roof and parking lot. Prior to remodeling, the

former owner of the building, Franklin Burns, had to remove the asbestos-contaminated materials discovered in the building. The Boulder Police Department was not the only city agency that would occupy the new Public Safety Center. Other occupants included the Boulder Fire Department's administrative staff and fire prevention bureau and the Boulder Emergency Communications Center.<sup>57</sup>

In March 1988, city council approved the sale of \$6.2 million in bonds to buy and renovate the old Mountain Bell building. The purchase plan called for dedicating \$2.7 million for purchasing the 45,892 square-foot building and 4.17 acres of land. Another \$3.25 million was used to cover the renovation costs. According to the purchase agreement, the city agreed to pay for the property over the next 18 years at an interest rate of about 7.1 percent. The first year's payment of \$600,000 had already been appropriated as part of the city's 1988 budget. A dedication ceremony for the new Public Safety Center was held on December 15, 1989. Boulder residents were able to tour and view the newly remodeled building that was to house the entire Boulder Police Department and the administrative staff of the Boulder Fire Department. The Boulder Police Department would now be operating under one roof instead of four. Both the police department and fire officials were expected to move into the new facility in early January 1990.<sup>58</sup>

However, problems with the electrical subcontractor hired to install the electrical wiring at the new Public Safety Center building pushed back the moving in date towards the end of January. Evidently, the electrical subcontractor Michael Chester of D'Meco Electric in Fort Collins absconded with funds, leaving the electrical work unfinished and workers unpaid. The added expense of repairing and finishing the electrical work fell to the general

---

<sup>57</sup>Camera, 19 March 1987; Boulder Courier, 3 December 1987; Camera, 6 January 1988.

<sup>58</sup>Colorado Daily, 17 March 1988 and Camera, 15 December 1989.

contractor of the project, not Boulder taxpayers. The general contractor RJC (Ringland, Johnson, Crowley) Inc., had to straighten out the mess left by Chester, which included paying the workers and the cost of finishing the electrical work. To complete the electrical work left unfinished, RJC Inc. vice president Jeff Nading hired Chester's electrical workers to continue the job plus other electrical subcontractors including Louisville Electric, Walck Electric and Yes Electric. Fort Collins Police arrested Michael Chester in late December 1989. Police suspected Chester forged the signature of Colotex Electric Supply Co. of Loveland on a joint check issued by RJC Inc. Shortly after Chester deposited the check in his bank account he closed that account. Colotex Electric Supply Co. never received its share of the payment. Colotex had supplied the electrical material used in the construction of the new Public Safety Center. Chester was to appear for a preliminary hearing in the Larimer County Court.<sup>59</sup>

---

<sup>59</sup>Camera, 16 January 1990 and Times-Call, 16 January 1990.

## APPENDIXES

### WHERE EXTREMELY HAZARDOUS MATERIALS ARE STORED IN COUNTY

The following is a list of places in Boulder County that store what are considered "extremely hazardous" materials, as defined by the federal Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act.

The evacuation distance covers the area that would be evacuated in the event of a "worst-case scenario" accident involving the chemicals, such as a major spill and uncontrollable fire. Places listed in the evacuation "hazard zone" are those where lots of people likely would be.

**American Pride Fertilizer & Chemicals**, Sugar Mill Road, Longmont  
(Information not yet compiled.)

**Ball Aerospace Systems Division**, 1600 Commerce St., Boulder

**WHAT:** Up to 500 gallons of caustic material; 55 gallons of various chemicals; up to 1,500 gallons of isopropyl alcohol; up to 40 55-gallon drums of waste solvents, caustics; small quantity of toxic gasses.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** Half-mile; likely confined to plant grounds.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Eisenhower Elementary School; Syntex Chemicals.

**Beech Aircraft Corp.**, 6859 N. Foothills Highway

**WHAT:** Hydrazine-MAF 4; inhibited red fuming nitric acid.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** No off-site area affected.

**Boulder's 75th Street Wastewater Treatment Plant**, 4049 75th St.

**WHAT:** 20,000 pounds of chlorine; 5,000 pounds of sulfur dioxide; 10

gallons of sodium hydroxide.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Heatherwood Elementary School.

**Boulder's Betasso Water Treatment Plant**, 1094 Betasso Road.

**WHAT:** 20,000 pounds of chlorine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** No group facilities or schools.

**Boulder's Diagonal Water Treatment Plant**, 5605 N. 63rd St.

**WHAT:** 30,000 pounds of chlorine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** IBM plant; Gunbarrel shopping area.

**North Boulder Recreation Center Pool**, 3170 N. Broadway, Boulder.

**WHAT:** 300 pounds of chlorine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Child care facilities — Alaya Preschool, The Elm Tree, Make A Mess & Make Believe Preschool, Morning Star Infant Care, Mount Zion Lutheran School, Jack & Jill Preschool; hospitals — Boulder Community Hospital, Boulder Medical Center, People's Clinic; schools — Foothills Elementary; Washington Community School.

**South Boulder Recreation Center Pool**, 1360 Gillaspie Drive, Boulder.

**WHAT:** 300 pounds of chlorine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Schools — Southern Hills Junior High, Fairview High School, Paddock School; retirement facility — Meridan Retirement Community.

**Boulder's Spruce Pool**, 21st and Spruce streets, Boulder.

**WHAT:** 300 pounds of chlorine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1 mile.  
**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Child care facilities — Charlie's Place Day

Care, First Presbyterian Preschool, Honey Bear Toddler Workshop, Boulder Day Nursery, Little Lambs Christian Preschool, Walnut Street School; schools — Whittier Elementary, Boulder High School; group facilities — YWCA.

**Cord Laboratories Inc.**, 2555 W. Midway Blvd., Broomfield.

**WHAT:** Methanol.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 200 feet to 1,000 feet.

**Frontier Fertilizer & Chemical Co.**, 4401 Highway 66, Longmont.  
(Information not yet compiled.)

**IBM**, 6300 Diagonal Highway, Boulder.

**WHAT:** Sulfuric acid; ammonia; ethylenediamine.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** Half-mile.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** No group facilities or schools.

**Kryptonics**, 5660 Central Ave., Boulder.

(Information not yet compiled.)

**Longmont's North Water Treatment Plant**, 4651 Ute Highway, and the **South Water Treatment Plant**, 4562 Ute Highway.

**WHAT:** Between 1 ton and 4 tons of chlorine.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1.5 miles.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Lyons Junior-Senior High School.

**Longmont's Wade Gaddis Water Treatment Plant**, 7024 Ute Highway.

**WHAT:** Between 1 ton and 4 tons of chlorine.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1.5 miles.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Rocky Mountain Elementary School.

**Longmont's Wastewater Treatment Plant**, 501 E. First Ave., Longmont.

**WHAT:** Between 1 ton and 6 tons of chlorine; between one-half ton and 5 tons of sulfur dioxide.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 1.5 miles.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Rocky Mountain Elementary School.

**Longmont Turkey Processors Inc.**, 150 Main St., Longmont.

**WHAT:** Ammonia, chlorine, sodium hydroxide, sulfuric acid, carbon dioxide, aluminum sulfate.

**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** Half-mile.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Longmont Civic Center, Police Department and Library; Central Elementary; downtown business district.

**Public Service Co.'s Valmont Power Plant**.

(Information not yet compiled.)

**Scott Specialty Gases**, 500 Weaver Park Road, Longmont.

**WHAT:** Chlorine, ammonia.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 150 feet.

**Storage Technology Corp.**, 2270 S. 88th St., Louisville.

**WHAT:** Hydrogen fluoride.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 150 feet to 1.5 miles.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** Centennial Peaks Hospital.

**Syntex Chemicals**, 2075 N. 55th St., Boulder.

**WHAT:** Various chemicals.  
**EVACUATION DISTANCE:** 2,000 feet.

**WITHIN HAZARD ZONE:** No group facilities or schools.

### Appendix B

### PREVIOUS MAJOR COUNTY FIRES

Here is a recap of previous significant forest fires in Boulder County dating to 1974:

June 25, 1990 — Two fires sparked by lightning burn seven acres in Sunshine and Fourmile canyons.

July 9-13, 1989 — Fire on Sugarloaf Mountain burns 2,086 acres, destroying 39 homes and damaging 20 others, making it the most destructive forest fire in county history.

Sept. 8, 1988 — Fire in Lethand Canyon burns 2,456 acres, making it the biggest forest fire in county history at the time.

Sept. 7, 1988 — Fire north of Ward burns more than 300 acres.

Feb. 21, 1988 — A fire started by a hiker's campfire and fueled by 90 mph winds in Sunshine Canyon threatens homes and chars 200 acres. Wet snow helps firefighters contain the blaze before it hits the western edge of downtown Boulder.

Nov. 4, 1987 — Fire burns 100 acres of Boulder open space southwest of U.S. 36.

Aug. 1, 1987 — Three lightning-sparked forest fires in the foothills between Boulder and Lyons burn more than 50 acres.

Sept. 1, 1985 — Fire started by lightning burns six acres of city open space below Devil's Thumb in the foothills south of Boulder.

Aug. 28, 1985 — Fire caused by lightning burns four acres north of Lethand Canyon.

Aug. 26, 1985 — Fire burns two acres on Flagstaff Mountain.

Aug. 11, 1985 — Fire caused by lightning burns three acres at the base of the first Flatiron.

Sept. 21, 1984 — Sixty acres of U.S. Forest Service land are burned 6½ miles west of Lyons, caused by an abandoned campfire.

Sept. 3, 1984 — Fires burn 41 acres of U.S. Forest Service land near Sugarloaf Road five miles west of Boulder, also blamed on an abandoned campfire.

June 23, 1981 — Thirty acres burn in the Indian Peaks Wilderness area northwest of Boulder; the blaze is started by lightning.

Oct. 6, 1980 — 150 acres and a \$150,000 house in Pine Brook Hills burn, caused by an arsonist.

August 1979 — Fifty acres burn in Coal Creek Canyon, possibly caused by sparks from a train.

Sept. 4, 1978 — Twenty acres of U.S. Forest Service land burns near Gross Reservoir, caused by careless campers.

Aug. 9, 1978 — Fire caused by lightning burns more than 1,000 acres in Rocky Mountain National Park in northwestern Boulder County.

June 1976 — 256 acres burn on Comforter Mountain, seven miles west of Boulder, cause by careless campers.

May 28, 1974 — 160 acres burn near Gold Hill, forcing evacuation of the Summerville settlement.

(Camera, 25 November 1990)
